         <tei:p>Observations on the systematics of the<tei:lb/>genus Difflugia in Britain (Rhizopoda,<tei:lb/>Protozoa)</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>The Bulletin of the British Museum (Natural History), instituted in 1949, is issued in four<tei:lb/>scientific series, Botany, Entomology, Geology (incorporating Mineralogy) and Zoology, and<tei:lb/>an Historical series.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Papers in the Bulletin are primarily the results of research carried out on the unique and<tei:lb/>ever-growing collections of the Museum, both by the scientific staff of the Museum and by<tei:lb/>specialists from elsewhere who make use of the Museum's resources. Many of the papers are<tei:lb/>works of reference that will remain indispensable for years to come.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parts are published at irregular intervals as they become ready, each is complete in itself,<tei:lb/>available separately, and individually priced. Volumes contain about 300 pages and several<tei:lb/>volumes may appear within a calendar year. Subscriptions may be placed for one or more of<tei:lb/>the series on either an Annual or Per Volume basis. Prices vary according to the contents of<tei:lb/>the individual parts. Orders and enquiries should be sent to:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Publications Sales,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>British Museum (Natural History),<tei:lb/>Cromwell Road,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>London SW7 5BD,<tei:lb/>England.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>World List abbreviation: Bull. Br. Mus. nat. Hist. (Zool.)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Trustees of the British Museum (Natural History), 1983</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Zoology Series is edited in the Museum's Department of Zoology<tei:lb/>Keeper of Zoology : Dr J. G. Sheals<tei:lb/>Editor of Bulletin : Dr C. R. Curds</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Assistant Editor: Mr C. G. Ogden</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ISSN 0007- 1 498 Zoology series</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Vol 44 No 1 pp 1-73<tei:lb/>British Museum (Natural History)<tei:lb/>Cromwell Road<tei:lb/>London SW7 5BD Issued 27 January 1983</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>Observations on the systematics of the genus<tei:lb/>Difflugia in Britain (Rhizopoda, Protozoa).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>F GENERAL</tei:p>
         <tei:p>28 JANW83</tei:p>
         <tei:p>^4L</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Colin G. Ogden</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London<tei:lb/>SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Contents</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Synopsis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Materials and methods</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Systematic descriptions</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pyriform and elongate species . . . .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pointed species or those with protruberances .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ovoid or spherical species</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Compressed species</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discussion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>References</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Index .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1<tei:lb/>1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2<tei:lb/>2<tei:lb/>2</tei:p>
         <tei:p>27<tei:lb/>45<tei:lb/>59<tei:lb/>66<tei:lb/>69<tei:lb/>73</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Synopsis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Detailed descriptions of the shell structure in thirty-eight species of Difflugia are given, and<tei:lb/>further information on one species which has been redescribed in an earlier report (Ogden &amp;<tei:lb/>Fairman, 1979) is included. Two new species, Difflugia hiraethogii and D. stoutii, are described,<tei:lb/>and other systematic changes include the following new combinations: D. cylindrus (Thomas,<tei:lb/>1953), D. lacustris (Penard, 1899), D. microclaviformis (Kourov, 1925), D. microstoma (Thomas,<tei:lb/>1954), D. parva (Thomas, 1954), D. tennis (Penard, 1890), D. tricornis (Jung, 1936), D. venusta<tei:lb/>(Penard, 1902) and D. distenda nom, nov., D. gassowskii nom, nov., D. paulii nom. nov., D. rotunda<tei:lb/>nom. nov. Seventeen of these redescriptions are new records for the British Isles. The structure of the<tei:lb/>shell is discussed and the patterning of the organic cement which binds the particles together is shown to<tei:lb/>be a useful taxonomic character.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The taxonomy of specimens belonging to the genus Difflugia is based mainly on<tei:lb/>differences in size and shape of the agglutinated shells constructed by these animals.<tei:lb/>Comparison of cytoplasmic features are either difficult or impossible because most<tei:lb/>of it is encased by the shell which is often opaque. Differences in the shell features<tei:lb/>have resulted in about three hundred named species, varieties and forms being<tei:lb/>currently attributed to the genus. This proliferation of species is due to a combination of the<tei:lb/>lack of good diagnostic features and inadequate descriptions. The problem is clearly shown<tei:lb/>in the only comprehensive survey of the genus, based on African specimens, by Gauthier-<tei:lb/>Lievre and Thomas (1958). In this work the authors had difficulties with several groups of<tei:lb/>individuals which shared common features, and as a result, about half of the 129 species<tei:lb/>described were designated as varieties or forms.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Some earlier studies (Ogden, 1979, 1980; Ogden &amp; Fairman, 1979) were con-<tei:lb/>cerned with the variation of specimens having a pyriform shell, and the surface</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. nat. Hist. (Zool.)44 (1): 1-73</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Issued 2 7 January 1983</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>2 C. G. OGDEN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ultrastructure as revealed by the scanning electron microscope. The results showed<tei:lb/>that there was usually a limited variation in size, shape and composition of the shell<tei:lb/>within a species, whilst in some instances the patterning of the organic cement which<tei:lb/>binds the particles together was a reliable specific character. The present account<tei:lb/>uses the experience gained from these previous studies to extend the examination of<tei:lb/>shell structure in Difflugia, and to establish specific features for ten of the varieties of<tei:lb/>Difflugia oblonga listed by Gauthier-Lievre and Thomas (1958). Thirty nine species<tei:lb/>are described, of which some are new records for the British Isles. Some 600<tei:lb/>specimens have been examined and over 3000 micrographs representing different<tei:lb/>aspects of the shells are retained in the Protozoa Section, Department of Zoology as<tei:lb/>part of the study collection.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Materials and methods</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Samples have been collected from several localities in England and Wales during the last<tei:lb/>four years. The material gathered varied from mosses, water plants including the substrate<tei:lb/>associated with the roots, and clumps of algae. Type of habitat ranged from areas of bog,<tei:lb/>banks of streams and small ponds in the New Forest, Lake District and North Wales to the<tei:lb/>dykes and rivers of Norfolk and Suffolk. Information relating to locality, date and type of<tei:lb/>sample is given with the description of each species as several have been found in more than<tei:lb/>one habitat.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Specimens of Difflugia were selected by searching through small isolates of material in a<tei:lb/>petri dish. Specimens were extracted using a glass micropipette, washed in several transfers<tei:lb/>through distilled water, and then individual shells were manipulated with a single-hairbrush<tei:lb/>onto a small drop of Araldite on a previously cleaned cover slip. When about twenty<tei:lb/>specimens were positioned on each cover slip it was glued with Araldite onto a standard<tei:lb/>aluminium stub. In a few instances the very small delicate specimens collapsed before or<tei:lb/>during manipulation, this problem was overcome by transferring the washed specimens in a<tei:lb/>small drop of water onto cleaned cover slips and allowing it to dry. Prepared stubs were<tei:lb/>coated evenly with gold or gold/palladium, using a conventional sputter coating device, and<tei:lb/>examined in a Cambridge Stereoscan SI 80 operating at lOkV. The results were recorded on<tei:lb/>Ilford HP5 film.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Systematic descriptions</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In a previous review (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958) of the genus the species were<tei:lb/>divided into ten groups, namely; lobed, collared, compressed, urceolate, globose, ovoid-<tei:lb/>globose, elongate, acute angled, horned and pyriform, but no particular significance was<tei:lb/>attached to these groupings. However, it does show the diversity of shell shape that has been<tei:lb/>included in the genus. Similarly in this report the species have been grouped together, the<tei:lb/>pyriform and elongate species are described first, followed by those which are pointed or<tei:lb/>have aboral protruberances, then the ovoid or spherical and finally the two compressed<tei:lb/>species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pyriform and elongate species<tei:lb/>Difflugia bryophila (Penard, 1 902) Jung, 1 942</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia pyriformis var. bryophila Penard, 1 902</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia oblonga var. bryophila (Penard, 1902) Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is brown, pyriform, with the sides usually tapering evenly to the<tei:lb/>aperture (Figs, la &amp; d), although the occasional specimen may be slightly mis-aligned or</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>DIFFLUGIA IN BRITAIN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 1 Difflugia bryophila: a, lateral view of shell with large particles obscuring the basic outline<tei:lb/>x780; b, apertural view x760; c, portion of shell surface showing the distribution of organic<tei:lb/>cement (arrowed) x 5100; d, lateral view of specimen with typical basic outline x440; e, detail<tei:lb/>of organic cement x 24 000.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>4 C. G. OGDEN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Table 1 Range of measurements (in um) of pyriform specimens.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>*measurements quoted from an earlier report (Ogden &amp; Fairman, 1979)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>have a large particle obscuring the even-tapering (Fig. la). It is composed mainly of a<tei:lb/>mixture of small to medium pieces of quartz and the occasional diatom frustule or siliceous<tei:lb/>flagellate cyst. In common with most rough, thick shells, organic cement is seen infrequently<tei:lb/>(Fig. Ic), it appears as an open network, with a mesh about 350-450 nm in diameter. The<tei:lb/>walls of the mesh are not pronounced but blend with the matrix, the mesh openings are often<tei:lb/>covered by inner strands of cement which appear to form a smaller mesh (Fig. le). The<tei:lb/>aperture is circular and surrounded by small particles (Fig. Ib), in several of the specimens<tei:lb/>examined small flagellate cysts had also been incorporated to give an irregular margin.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED Specimens were collected from samples of Sphagnum moss gathered at<tei:lb/>Mately Bog, Lyndhurst, New Forest, Hampshire in March, 1980 and at Mynnd Hiraethog,<tei:lb/>Denbigh, Clwyd, North Wales in August, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Algeria (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Belgium<tei:lb/>(Chardez &amp; Gaspar, 1976; Couteaux, 1969), British Isles (Cash el al, 1919), Chile (Jung,<tei:lb/>1942), Congo (Chardez, 1964), Czechoslovakia (Rosa, 1957), France (Thomas, 1954),<tei:lb/>Gambia (Decloitre, 1947), Germany (Jung, 1936), Roumania (Godeanu et al., 1973), Russia<tei:lb/>(Kourov, 1925), Spain (Gracia, \912a), Switzerland (Penard, 1902), Tunisia (Gauthier-<tei:lb/>Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), West Africa (Decloitre, 1948).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This description is in good agreement with Penard (1902) who stated that the sides<tei:lb/>were rather straight and that it was formed of stones which were usually large and angular.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Amongst the sample from Matley Bog were specimens of Pontigulasia which were<tei:lb/>indistinguishable in size and shape from those of D. bryophila. Observations of the former<tei:lb/>specimens by optical microscopy to determine the presence of an inner diaphragm, the<tei:lb/>distinguishing generic character for Pontigulasia, are often difficult. However, detailed<tei:lb/>examination by scanning electron microscopy showed that, unless the apertural opening is<tei:lb/>blocked, it was easy to identify the inner diaphragm and that additionally there are differ-<tei:lb/>ences in their organic cement patterns (a review of the genus Pontigulasia is in preparation).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>DIFFLUGIA IN BRITAIN 5</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The random selection of these specimens from the sample gave a ratio of 3 : 1 in favour of<tei:lb/>D. bryophila.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia cylindrus (Thomas, 1953) comb. nov.<tei:lb/>Difflugia oblonga var. cylindrus Thomas, 1953</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is usually opaque, cylindrical, tapering evenly from the aboral<tei:lb/>region to the aperture (Fig. 2a). It is composed mainly of medium to large pieces of quartz<tei:lb/>with the occasional diatom frustule on the rough surface, but the latter are seldom<tei:lb/>incorporated into the thick structure. Small areas of organic cement in the form of a network<tei:lb/>are seen infrequently (Fig. 2d). Each mesh of the network is between 300-500 nm in<tei:lb/>diameter, has thick walls and is covered by a thin perforated layer of cement, the pores of<tei:lb/>which are evenly distributed and are about 30 nm in diameter (Figs. 2e &amp; f)- The<tei:lb/>aperture is irregular in both outline and composition, being roughly circular and usually<tei:lb/>surrounded by small particles but often incorporating medium particles that produce a<tei:lb/>jagged margin (Fig. 2b &amp; c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from a sample of aquatic plants taken at the<tei:lb/>banks of the River Brett, near Hadleigh, Suffolk in August, 1979.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Algeria (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Austria<tei:lb/>(Laminger, 19736, 1975, 1976), Belgium (Chardez, 1980; Chardez &amp; Caspar, 1976), Congo<tei:lb/>(Chardez, 1964), France (Thomas, 1953, 1954; Thomas &amp; Mabille, 1956), Germany<tei:lb/>(Voeltz-Hohn, 1971), Ivory Coast (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Morocco<tei:lb/>(Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Poland (Moraczewski, 1965).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species was initially described by Thomas (1953) as a new variety of D.<tei:lb/>oblonga, he also listed what he considered to be previous descriptions of this variety under<tei:lb/>the general descriptions of D. oblonga given by earlier workers. These are not repeated here,<tei:lb/>but suggest that this variety may be found throughout Europe. The shape, structure and size<tei:lb/>of the shell, together with the distinct patterning of the organic cement matrix as described<tei:lb/>above, are considered to be good specific characters sufficient to warrant the raising of this<tei:lb/>variety to species rank.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia gassowskii nom. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia pyriformis longicollis Gassowsky, 1936</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia longcollis (Gassowsky, 1936) Ogden &amp; Hedley, 1980</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is pyriform, with a distinct short neck about one-third of the body<tei:lb/>length, and a rounded aboral region (Fig. 3a). It is rough and composed of small to medium<tei:lb/>angular pieces of quartz, small areas of organic cement are sometimes seen between particles<tei:lb/>(Fig. 3b). The cement is in the form of a network, the mesh of which is about 400-550 nm in<tei:lb/>diameter and the walls 125-200 nm thick, each mesh enclosure has a covering with small<tei:lb/>perforations about 50 nm in diameter (Fig. 3c). The aperture is circular.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from samples of Sphagnum moss gathered<tei:lb/>at Holmsley Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire in March, 1980 and at Clocaenog<tei:lb/>Forest, Denbigh, Clwyd, North Wales in August, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Belgium (Chardez, 1980; Couteaux, 1969), British Isles<tei:lb/>(Ogden &amp; Hedley, 1980), Germany (Voeltz-Hohn, 1971), Netherlands (Hoogenraad &amp;<tei:lb/>Groot, 1940a), Nigeria (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Poland (Moraczewski, 1961,<tei:lb/>1965), Roumania (Godeanu et al, 1973), Russia (Gassowsky, 1936), Spain (Gracia, 1972a;<tei:lb/>Margalef, 1955).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species has been redescribed recently (Ogden &amp; Hedley, 1980), but is<tei:lb/>included here on two counts, the added detail of the organic cement and the change of name.<tei:lb/>The name D. longicollis was used initially by Ehrenberg (1854) to describe specimens which<tei:lb/>now are not considered to belong to the genus Difflugia, nevertheless, under the Rules of<tei:lb/>Zoological Nomenclature the name is preoccupied. I am grateful to Dr. E. G. Merinfeld,<tei:lb/>Dalhousie University, Halifax, Nova Scotia, for drawing my attention to this point.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REMARKS.          <tei:p>Difftugia lacustris (Penard, 1 899) comb. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia pyriformis var. lacustris Penard, 1899<tei:lb/>Difflugia oblonga var. lacustris Cash &amp; Hopkinson, 1 909</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent or hyaline, elongate, cylindrical or slightly pyriform<tei:lb/>(Figs. 5a &amp; b). It is composed of small to medium pieces of quartz, diatom frustules and small<tei:lb/>siliceous flagellate cysts blended together to form a thin structure intermediate between<tei:lb/>smooth and rough. Only small areas of organic cement occur at the junction of the shell<tei:lb/>components (Fig. 5d). The cement is in the form of thick-walled rings, between 700-800 nm<tei:lb/>in diameter, perforated with either three or four holes, 120-160 nm in diameter, which gives<tei:lb/>these units a similar shape to a button (Fig. 5e). The cement may occasionally be seen either<tei:lb/>as rings with a slight indentation or as a network of joined rings. When organised as a<tei:lb/>network the walls of individual rings may be fused together but the typical button-like<tei:lb/>form are usually seen at the edges. The aperture is usually circular and surrounded by small<tei:lb/>particles so that the margin is smooth (Fig. 5c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from three samples in the same locality,<tei:lb/>aquatic plants at the edge of a pond in Burley, and two gatherings of Sphagnum from<tei:lb/>opposite banks of a small stream at Holmsley Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire in<tei:lb/>March, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Algeria (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Argentina<tei:lb/>(Dioni, 1970; Lena &amp; Zaidenwerg, 1975), Austria (Laminger, 19736 1974, 1975), Belgium<tei:lb/>(Chardez, 1980; Chardez &amp; Gaspar, 1976; Oye, 1953), British Isles (Cash &amp; Hopkinson,<tei:lb/>1909), Congo (Chardez, 1964; Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Czechoslovakia<tei:lb/>(Stepanek, 1967), France (Thomas, 1954; Thomas &amp; Mabille, 1956), Germany (Jung, 1936;<tei:lb/>Schonborn, 1962a &amp; b), Guatemala (Laminger, 1973a), Italy (Rampi, 1950), Ivory Coast<tei:lb/>(Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Mexico (Laminger, 1973a), Morocco (Gauthier-Lievre<tei:lb/>&amp; Thomas, 1958), Poland (Moraczewski, 1961, 1965), Roumania (Godeanu et al., 1973),<tei:lb/>Russia (Kourov, 1925), Switzerland (Penard, 1902), United States of America (Laminger<tei:lb/>etal., 1979;Wailes, 1912).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The specimens described here agree well with the original description (Penard,<tei:lb/>1899) shell long, cylindrical, larger in the rear, rarely with a small constriction of the<tei:lb/>neck and those given later by Penard (1902) and Cash &amp; Hopkinson (1909). Nevertheless,<tei:lb/>it would appear that these earlier descriptions were based on groups of similar specimens,<tei:lb/>hence Penard's reference to a constriction of the neck and the diverse illustrations provided<tei:lb/>by Cash &amp; Hopkinson. As a result of the latter diagrams, two specimens were tentatively<tei:lb/>identified as D. lacustris in a previous publication (Ogden, 1980). Additional specimens to</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia lanceolata Penard, 1 890</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is yellow or hyaline, lanceolate, tapering from the widest diameter<tei:lb/>situated about two- thirds of the body-length from the aperture, to give a clean outline that is<tei:lb/>rounded aborally and evenly angled towards the aperture (Fig. 6a). It is composed of small<tei:lb/>to medium flattish pieces of quartz and some flat diatom frustules so arranged that the shell is<tei:lb/>thin and smooth, the surface frequently appearing as though it had been polished, a feature<tei:lb/>that often permits easy identification. An angular piece of quartz may occasionally protrude<tei:lb/>from the surface but these are uncommon and limited to one or two in any one shell. As the<tei:lb/>shell components are usually arranged so that they are in close contact with each other there<tei:lb/>are no large areas of organic cement, nevertheless, a network of small rings of organic cement<tei:lb/>may be seen between these particles (Figs. 6d, e, f)- The rings are 240-300 nm in diameter<tei:lb/>and have a distinct wall about 1 50-200 nm in thickness with a smooth membrane over the<tei:lb/>mesh. When several rings fuse to form a sheet the thick wall is still usuallv apparent. The<tei:lb/>aperture is circular and well defined because the rim has a thin covering of organic cement<tei:lb/>(Figs. 6b &amp; c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Variation appears to be limited to cigar-shaped specimens which have almost parallel<tei:lb/>sides, one such specimen is illustrated by Ogden &amp; Hedley (1980).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED The majority of specimens came from a sample of Sphagnum moss<tei:lb/>collected close to a small stream at Holmsley Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire, a few<tei:lb/>came from a similar sample on the opposite bank where they were equally abundant, both<tei:lb/>samples were taken in March, 1 980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Argentina (Vucetich, \913a, /?), Austria (Laminger, 1972c),<tei:lb/>Belgium (Chardez, 1961; Oye, 1953), Brazil (Green, 1975), British Isles (Cash &amp; Hopkinson,<tei:lb/>1909; Ogden &amp; Hedley, 1980), Canada (Decloitre, 1965), China (Decloitre, 1965), Congo<tei:lb/>(Chardez, 1964), Czechoslovakia (Opravilova, 1974), France (Thomas, 1954; Thomas &amp;<tei:lb/>Mabille, 1956), Germany (Schonborn, 1975), Hungary (Gal, 1969), Java (Bartos, 1963a),<tei:lb/>Morocco (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Poland (Golemansky, 1970; Moraczewski,<tei:lb/>1961, 1965), Roumania (Godeanu et al, 1973), Sudan (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958),<tei:lb/>Switzerland (Penard, 1902), Venezuela (Grospietsch, 1975), West Africa (Decloitre, 1965),<tei:lb/>United States of America (Decloitre, 1965).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The organic rim surrounding the aperture is shared with one other pyriform<tei:lb/>species of Difflugia, namely D. rubescens where the cement is in the form of tooth-like<tei:lb/>projections (see PI. 66 in Ogden &amp; Hedley, 1980). The only other species of which we are<tei:lb/>aware that has an organic rim is D. oviformis, but this was transferred to a new genus Netzelia<tei:lb/>by Ogden, 1979. D. lanceolata is characterized by its uniform size and outline.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia manicata Penard, 1 902</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is yellow or brown, pyriform, tapering evenly and gradually from a<tei:lb/>rounded aboral extremity towards the aperture (Fig. 8a). The surface is rough and<tei:lb/>composed mainly of small to medium pieces of quartz, although the occasional specimen<tei:lb/>may have large particles added. Small areas of organic cement are seen infrequently, due to<tei:lb/>the close packing of the shell material, but when present they appear either as strands or as<tei:lb/>pores in a matrix (Fig. 8c). These pores are about 300 nm in diameter and have an ill-defined<tei:lb/>inner structure which appears to have smaller pores about 1 30 nm in diameter (Figs. 8c &amp; d).<tei:lb/>The aperture is circular and surrounded by a distinct pattern of small particles<tei:lb/>(Fig. 8b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from samples taken at three sites,<tei:lb/>Sphagnum moss gatherings at Holmsley Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire in March,<tei:lb/>1980 and Mynnd Hiraethog, Clwyd, North Wales in August, 1980, and from aquatic plants<tei:lb/>taken at the banks of the River Brett, near Hadleigh, Suffolk in August, 1979.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Austria (Laminger, 1971, 1972c), Belgium (Chardez, 196 \b\<tei:lb/>British Isles (Cash el al, 1919; Ogden &amp; Medley, 1980), Congo (Stepanek, 1963), Italy<tei:lb/>(Grandori &amp; Grandori, 1934), Poland (Moraczewski, 1965; Pateff, 1926, 1927), Switzerland<tei:lb/>(Penard, 1902).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. Although this species was thought to be uncommon by Cash, Wailes &amp;<tei:lb/>Hopkinson (1919), it is suggested that this has been one of the overlooked species and that it<tei:lb/>will probably be found as one of the most commonly distributed. The wide range of habitat<tei:lb/>already reported, lake, river and Sphagnum moss would appear to support this opinion.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia minutissima Penard, 1904<tei:lb/>Sexangularia minutissima (Penard, 1 904) Deflandre, 1931</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent, elongate or ovoid, rounded aborally and tapering<tei:lb/>slightly towards the aperture (Figs. 9a &amp; c). It is composed of thin flattish pieces of quartz,<tei:lb/>and the occasional diatom frustule, which are arranged so that they overlap, but the result is<tei:lb/>still a fragile structure. Only small strands of organic cement have been seen at some<tei:lb/>junctions. The aperature is basically circular, any variation is usually due to the irregular<tei:lb/>arrangement of particles around the opening (Fig. 9b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in urn). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIALS EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from a sample of Sphagnum moss gathered<tei:lb/>at Mynnd Hiraethog, Clwyd, North Wales in August 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION Switzerland (Penard, 1904)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species appears to be known only from the initial description (Penard, 1 904),<tei:lb/>although Deflandre (1931) using Penard's description suggested that it should be transferred<tei:lb/>to the genus Sexangularia Awerintzew, 1906. The main features of this latter genus are<tei:lb/>'chitinous shell, with or without the addition of extraneous particles; polygonal in transverse<tei:lb/>section, most often hexagonal'. At present it is considered that these features are insufficient<tei:lb/>to differentiate this genus from Difflugia, and therefore refer the included species to<tei:lb/>Difflugia-D. minutissima Penard, 1904; D. parvula (Awerintzew, 1906) and D. polydera<tei:lb/>Deflandre, 1931.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The specimens described here were found adhering to extraneous particles when prepared<tei:lb/>by air-drying and because of this it is not possible to examine them in transverse section,</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>DIFFLUGI A IN BRITAIN 17</tei:p>
         <tei:p>nevertheless, they do not appear to be polygonal. The value of this feature in relation to a<tei:lb/>fragile shell is questionable, especially as Penard's observations would have been carried out<tei:lb/>on specimens under a cover slip, which would allow a degree of compression. The specimens<tei:lb/>share similar dimensions to those given by Penard for D. minutissima and are so designated.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia parva (Thomas, 1954) comb. nov.<tei:lb/>Dijjlugia oblonga var. parva Thomas, 1954</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is pyriform, tapering evenly from the swollen and rounded aboral<tei:lb/>third, to the aperture for the remaining two-thirds (Fig. lOa). It is composed mainly of a<tei:lb/>mixture of small to medium pieces of quartz, often with the addition of two or three large<tei:lb/>pieces. Organic cement is seen between particles usually as a series of single units (Fig. 1 Oc),<tei:lb/>which may overlap but are seldom fused to form a network. Each unit is a ring about<tei:lb/>650-750 nm in external diameter, 300-380 nm internal diameter, with walls about 150 nm<tei:lb/>thick. A small mesh covers the inner portion of each ring (Fig. lOd). The aperture is circular<tei:lb/>and surrounded mainly by small particles (Fig. lOb).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in urn). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from Sphagnum moss gathered at Matley<tei:lb/>Bog, Lyndhurst, New Forest, Hampshire in March, 1980; Mynnd Hiraethog, Denbigh,<tei:lb/>Clwyd, North Wales in August, 1980 and aquatic plants from a pond at Hurley, New Forest,<tei:lb/>Hampshire in March, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Algeria (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Argentina (Lena<tei:lb/>&amp; Zaidenwerg, 1975), Austria (Laminger, 19736), Belgium (Chardez, 1980; Chardez &amp;<tei:lb/>Caspar, 1976; Couteaux, 1969), Bulgaria (Golemansky, 1967), France (Thomas, 1954;<tei:lb/>Thomas &amp; Mabille, 1956), Germany (Schonborn, 1965; Voeltz-Hohn, 1971), Ivory Coast<tei:lb/>(Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Poland (Golemansky, 1970; Moraczewski, 1965),<tei:lb/>Venezuela (Grospietsch, 1975).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. There have been several reports of D. oblonga var. parva since Thomas (1954)<tei:lb/>described his new variety, although size was the only diagnostic feature used and illustrated<tei:lb/>(PI. Ill, Fig. 1) by him to differentiate this variety from D. oblonga. The figure given also<tei:lb/>shows the difference in shell composition, described below as a specific feature.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This variety is considered as a distinct species from Z&gt;. oblonga because of its clean outline,<tei:lb/>relatively smooth surface and detailed cement pattern.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia paulii nom. nov.<tei:lb/>Difflugia oblonga var. elongata Oye, 1953</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent, slim and elongate, tapering evenly from just anterior<tei:lb/>of the mid-body region towards the aperture, the even-tapering is more apparent in Fig. lib<tei:lb/>whereas the upper side of Fig. 1 la has a misleading hump, the posterior region is slightly<tei:lb/>swollen, curving sharply and smoothly at the extremity (Fig. 1 la). It is composed of flattish<tei:lb/>pieces of quartz to give a smooth appearance, with small areas of organic cement often<tei:lb/>apparent as part of the matrix (Fig. lie). The cement is in the form of small perforated cones<tei:lb/>about 600 nm in diameter, the perforations being about 100-1 50 nm in diameter (Fig. 1 Id).<tei:lb/>The aperture is circular and surrounded by small pieces of quartz (Fig. 1 Ib).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Austria (Laminger, 19736), Belgium (Oye, 1953), Congo<tei:lb/>(Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Spain (Gracia, 19720).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species has been described on two occasions, from Belgium by Oye (1953)<tei:lb/>and from Africa by Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas (1958). Oye (1953) stated that these<tei:lb/>specimens were slender in comparison with D. lacustris, and considered that this feature and<tei:lb/>the limited grains of quartz in the shell was sufficient to warrant a new variety. Gauthier-<tei:lb/>Lievre and Thomas (1958) agreed with this earlier description and designation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Of the more elongated pyriform species, D. paulii in body length appears to occupy a<tei:lb/>position mid-way between D. linearis and D. lacustris, the present work shows that it is<tei:lb/>distinct from these two species in outline, elemental composition and patterning of the</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>20 C. G. OGDEN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>organic cement, in addition, it can be differentiated from D. linearis by the size of the<tei:lb/>aperture (Table 1).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ETYMOLOGY. This species is named after Dr Paul van Oye whose original description was<tei:lb/>based on specimens from a pond in Belgium.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DifflugiapetricolaCash, 1909</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This species has been redescribed recently by Ogden &amp; Fairman (1979). It is reported here<tei:lb/>only to demonstrate the regularity of dimensions between specimens from different<tei:lb/>localities, see Tables 1 and 2, because these are often used as taxonomic features.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from Sphagnum moss gathered at two sites,<tei:lb/>Holmsley Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire in March, 1980 and Mynnd Hiraethog,<tei:lb/>Denbigh, Clwyd, North Wales in August, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dijflugia pristis Penard, 1902</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is brown or opaque, ovoid, tapering from the mid-body position<tei:lb/>towards the aperture and gracefully curved aborally (Fig. 12a). It is thin, smooth and<tei:lb/>composed of flattish pieces of quartz (Fig. 1 2a-c) or infrequently including flat pieces of<tei:lb/>diatom frustule. The particles are packed closely together and only small connections of<tei:lb/>organic cement are visible (Figs. 12d &amp; e). The aperture is circular with a regular margin<tei:lb/>(Fig. 12b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in urn). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from samples of Sphagnum moss gathered<tei:lb/>at Holmsley Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire in May, 1978 and at Myndd Hiraethog,<tei:lb/>Denbigh, Clwyd, North Wales in August, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. British Isles (Cash &amp; Hopkinson, 1909), Czechoslovakia<tei:lb/>(Stepanek, 1967), Hungary (Varga, 1963), Italy (Grandori &amp; Grandori, 1934), Java (BartoS,<tei:lb/>1963a), Poland (Moraczewski, 1965), Roumania (Godeanu et al, 1973), Switzerland<tei:lb/>(Penard, 1902).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. Cash and Hopkinson (1909) considered their specimens to be in good agreement<tei:lb/>with Penard's (1902) description, except for the absence of refractive particles that made the<tei:lb/>shell appear opaque or black. This they attributed to the difference in habitat of their<tei:lb/>samples, the former being found amongst floating vegetation in clear water and Penard's<tei:lb/>from a muddy lake bottom. Some differences in size have been reported since Penard who<tei:lb/>gave 45-65 um as the range of length, Cash and Hopkinson gave 60-65 um, whilst more<tei:lb/>recently Varga (1963) found specimens measuring 44-48 um in length.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The specimens reported here are smaller than any previously described, although in all<tei:lb/>other respects agree with Penard's description. The main features that distinguish D. pristis<tei:lb/>from D. pulex are the darker colour and the more regular shape.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A single larger specimen, 56 um long, 31um wide, aperture 10 um in diameter, with the<tei:lb/>typical pyriform shape (Fig. 13a &amp; b) was found in the sample from Wales. This is reported<tei:lb/>here as being questionably D. pristis (?), being atypical in having a definite neck, the<tei:lb/>discovery of more specimens may result in a more accurate identification. This specimen is<tei:lb/>similar to those identified by Cash &amp; Hopkinson (1909) as (?) D. pulex which they described<tei:lb/>as being 'oval, tapering suddenly to a short neck'.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia pulex Penard, 1902<tei:lb/>Difflugia minuta minor Godeanu, 1972</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent, elongate or ovoid (Figs. 14a &amp; d). It is composed<tei:lb/>mainly of a mixture of small thin pieces of flat quartz and pieces of diatom frustule, often<tei:lb/>with whole frustules or round flagellate cysts adhering to the surface (Figs. 14a-e). The<tei:lb/>arrangement of these particles is such that only small strands of organic cement are visible.<tei:lb/>The aperture is usually circular (Fig. 14c) but may vary due to the arrangement of the<tei:lb/>surrounding particles (Fig. 14b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in urn). See Tables 1 &amp; 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED Specimens were collected from samples of Sphagnum moss gathered at<tei:lb/>Subberthwaite, Broughton in Furaess, Cumbria in June 1979 and at Mynnd Hiraethog,<tei:lb/>Denbigh, Clwyd, North Wales in August, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION Argentina (Lena &amp; Zaidenwerg, 1975), Australia (Playfair,<tei:lb/>1918), Austria (Laminger, J9736), Belgium (Chardez, 19616), British Isles (Cash &amp;<tei:lb/>Hopkinson, 1909), Congo (Stdpanek, 1963), Czechoslovakia (Stepanek, 1967), Germany<tei:lb/>(Schonborn, \962a &amp; b), Italy (Grandori &amp; Grandori, 1934; Rampi, 1950), Java (BartoS,<tei:lb/>1963a), Netherlands (Hoogenraad &amp; Groot, 1940), Roumania (Godeanu et al, 1972), Spain<tei:lb/>(Gracia, 19726), United States of America (Laminger et al, 1979), West Africa (Decloitre,<tei:lb/>1948).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. Penard's original description stated that the shell was-' pyriform, with or without<tei:lb/>narrowing of the mouth, chitinoid, slightly yellow, covered with small scales or particles of<tei:lb/>amorphous silica, plates, sufficiently transparent to examine the contents' and that<tei:lb/>specimens rarely exceeded 30 urn in length. Cash &amp; Hopkinson (1909) described specimens<tei:lb/>between 65-70 urn in length that they tentatively identified as D. pulex, but on the basis of<tei:lb/>both Penard's and the present description it is now suggested they should be reassigned.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The description of D. minuta minor given by Godeanu (1972), shell colourless circular in<tei:lb/>cross section but with an irregular outline because of added quartz particles and a circular<tei:lb/>aperture, is so similar to that of D. pulex that it must be considered a synonym. The range of<tei:lb/>measurements given are also similar to those quoted here (see Table 1 ).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although Penard gave 30 urn as the maximum length for this species, in the described<tei:lb/>sample two specimens were just in excess of 40 urn and two 30 urn, but in all other respects<tei:lb/>were similar to the smaller specimens, and all are considered to represent D. pulex.</tei:p>

      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>DIFFLUGIA IN BRITAIN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 14 Difflugia pulex: a, lateral view x 1800; b, apertural view of specimen with irregular<tei:lb/>apertural opening x 1300; c, apertural view of specimen (a.) with circular aperture x 1500; d,<tei:lb/>lateral view of specimen mainly made of flattish particles x 1600; e, shell surface showing<tei:lb/>mixture of flat components x 5 100.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia tenuis (Penard, 1 890) comb. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia pyriformis var. tenuis Penard, 1890<tei:lb/>Difflugia oblonga var. tenuis Wailes &amp; Penard, 1911</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is usually transparent, cylindrical or slightly pyriform, composed of<tei:lb/>a mixture of mainly small to medium pieces of angular quartz, but with an occasional large<tei:lb/>particle added (Figs. 15a &amp; b). Sometimes additional particles give a pointed outline to the<tei:lb/>aboral region, instead of the usual rounded contours. Organic cement is seen frequently as<tei:lb/>single units between shell components (Fig. 15d), and occasionally in small patches where<tei:lb/>these units are in a network with a mesh diameter of 350-400 nm and walls 180-220 nm<tei:lb/>thick (Fig. 1 5e). The mesh surface is characterised by a thin, usually central, inner ring about<tei:lb/>1 50-200 nm in diameter (Fig. 15e). The aperture is roughly circular, often having an<tei:lb/>irregular outline because of the mixture of particles surrounding it (Fig. 1 5b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>One specimen with projections similar to the rigid 'filament' structures described by<tei:lb/>Penard (1890), occurred in the present sample (Fig. 15c). These projections usually arise<tei:lb/>from a common base, on the surface of the quartz particles, and vary in size and number (Fig.<tei:lb/>16a). The projections are about 250 nm in diameter and may be up to 7 um in length, as<tei:lb/>many as fifteen have been seen sharing a common base (Fig. 1 6b). On the present evidence<tei:lb/>they seem to be rosettes of bacterial rods rather than the parasitic organisms suggested by<tei:lb/>Penard (1890).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in urn). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from Sphagnum moss gathered at Holmsley<tei:lb/>Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire in March, 1980 and Mynnd Hiraethog, Denbigh,<tei:lb/>Clwyd, North Wales in August, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION Argentina (Dioni, 1970), British Isles (Cash et al, 1919),<tei:lb/>Germany (Penard, 1 890), Poland (Golemansky, 1970), Venezuela (Grospietsch, 1975).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. Although the specimens described here are a little longer than those reported by<tei:lb/>Penard (1890) they are otherwise in good agreement with his description. This species has<tei:lb/>been identified previously on only a few occasions, but this may be due to its being mistaken<tei:lb/>for the more common species in this size range, for example D. penardi and D. rubescens.<tei:lb/>There remains the question of specific biological requirements, food, temperature, pH etc.,<tei:lb/>which may be the reason why although the site at Holmsley has been sampled regularly for<tei:lb/>five or six years, March 1980 was the first time that this species has been seen there and then<tei:lb/>it appeared in significant numbers. This species is distinguished by its shape, size of aperture<tei:lb/>and the pattern of the organic cement.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia viscidula Penard, 1 902</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. This species has been redescribed recently by Ogden &amp; Hedley (1980) but is<tei:lb/>reported again with some additional information.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The shell is opaque, pyriform or elongate ovoid, aborally it is usually rounded (Fig. 17a)<tei:lb/>but may occasionally be pointed. It is composed of a mixture of different sizes of angular<tei:lb/>quartz, with organic cement seen infrequently as part of the surface matrix. The cement<tei:lb/>appears as single units squashed together so that they are adjacent or overlap (Fig. 1 7c). The<tei:lb/>aperture is circular and usually surrounded by small particles which give it a characteristic<tei:lb/>well-defined outline (Fig. 17b). The illustrated aperture has a cyst plug, which although<tei:lb/>broken at one edge has in general a smooth surface, suggesting that it is mainly organic but<tei:lb/>reinforced by particles of quartz. About 10% of the examined specimens had an apertural<tei:lb/>cyst plug.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). See Tables 1 and 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>

         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED Specimens were collected from a sample of Sphagnum moss gathered<tei:lb/>at Holmsley Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire in March, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Argentina (Boltovskoy &amp; Lena, 1974), Austria (Laminger,<tei:lb/>1971), British Isles (Ogden &amp; Hedley, 1980), Germany (Schonborn, \962a, 1965, 1975), Java<tei:lb/>(BartoS, 1963a),Roumania(Godeanu^0/., 1973), Switzerland (Penard, 1902).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The present material extends the measurements we gave previously (Ogden &amp;<tei:lb/>Hedley, 1980) to encompass those given by Penard (1902). There remains the problem of<tei:lb/>priority of names for these specimens because Penard (1902) described two distinct species,<tei:lb/>namely D. lemani Blanc, 1892 and D. viscidula sp. nov., which he differentiated mainly on<tei:lb/>size; the former being 50-85 um long (three individuals were 100-200 um) and the latter<tei:lb/>180-260 um long. When he later found (Penard, 1905) that he had used incorrect measure-<tei:lb/>ments for D. lemani he suggested that D. viscidula should be regarded as a synonym.<tei:lb/>However, this proposal left his description of specimens under the name D. leman /-Penard,<tei:lb/>1902 p. 249, without a proper designated name. They were divided into two series, the first<tei:lb/>slim, between 75-85 um long and the second wider, but smaller about 50 urn long. It would<tei:lb/>appear that no subsequent report has rectified this situation, and it would seem that now is<tei:lb/>too late to change this because of the proliferation of species that have since been described<tei:lb/>and fall within the size range of the earlier description. Furthermore, it appears that Blanc's<tei:lb/>(1892) original description of D. lemanii might contravene Article 8 of the International<tei:lb/>Code of Zoological Nomenclature, because when first issued it would seem that it was not<tei:lb/>available by purchase or free distribution. There are no records of this publication in either<tei:lb/>this Museum's Libraries nor in the British Library, although I have recently obtained a<tei:lb/>photocopy from the Universitaire Lausanne. In Blanc's initial description of D. lemanii the<tei:lb/>shell dimensions varied as follows: body length between 180-310 um, breadth 130-150 um.<tei:lb/>and the aperture diameter was given as 1 lOum, it had a cylindrical body with almost parallel<tei:lb/>sides and the composition was such that it was friable and delicate. These measurements are<tei:lb/>in good agreement with Penard's 180-260 um body length for D. viscidula, and those given<tei:lb/>here in Table 1 except for the diameter of the aperture, this latter feature is given as being<tei:lb/>equal to about half the breadth of the shell by Penard (1905). The diameter of the aperture<tei:lb/>and the pyriform rather than cylindrical shape of the body are considered sufficient to<tei:lb/>differentiate the present specimens and those of Penard (1902, 1905) from D. lemanii Blanc,<tei:lb/>1892. This therefore leaves Penard's designated name of D. viscidula as valid and the<tei:lb/>specimens described here are so named.</tei:p>

It is composed mainly of medium pieces of quartz with<tei:lb/>small pieces mixed and so arranged to produce an intermediate smooth surface. Organic<tei:lb/>cement is seen in small patches between particles (Fig. 19d) and appears as rings fused to</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REMARKS. The structure of the shell in the present specimen differs from the original (Cash &amp;<tei:lb/>Hopkinson, 1909) by being composed mainly of quartz particles, rather than 'amorphous<tei:lb/>(?siliceous) scales'. However, it should be noted that the specimens described by Leidy (1879)<tei:lb/>and quoted as synonyms of this species by Cash &amp; Hopkinson (1909) are also composed<tei:lb/>mainly of 'quartz sand'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia bicruris Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is elongate ovoid, the sides being almost parallel with a slight<tei:lb/>tapering towards the aperture and the aboral extremity, the latter is rounded and has two<tei:lb/>small, equally spaced, protruberances or horns (Figs. 20a &amp; c). It is composed of medium to<tei:lb/>large pieces of angular quartz, with some small particles being used in shaping the horns (Fig.<tei:lb/>20d). The surface is rough but patches of organic cement are seen to form part of the shell<tei:lb/>matrix (Fig. 20e). Organic cement is arranged in the form of a regular network whose mesh<tei:lb/>has a diameter of about 300-350 nm and the distance between each enclosure is about<tei:lb/>300 nm (Fig. 20f). The aperture is circular and surrounded by an even arrangement of small<tei:lb/>particles (Fig. 20b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). Two specimens: body length 202-207, breadth 95-1 1 5, diameter of<tei:lb/>aperture 4 1-5 8.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from aquatic plants taken at the banks of the<tei:lb/>River Brett, near Hadleigh, Suffolk, in August, 1979.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION Ivory Coast (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Poland<tei:lb/>(Golemansky, 1970).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. Slight differences exist between the present specimens and those described by<tei:lb/>Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas (1958). They have slightly larger general body measurements,<tei:lb/>although the proportions are directly comparable, and the horns are reduced in length,<tei:lb/>20 urn here compared with 30-33 um in the African specimens. Nevertheless, the<tei:lb/>descriptions are in good agreement showing that D. bicruris is distinctly ovoid with two<tei:lb/>aboral spines or horns.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia distenda nom. nov.<tei:lb/>Difflugia acuminata var. inflata Penard, 1899</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. It has an intermediate smooth surface and thickness, being<tei:lb/>composed mainly of small to medium pieces of quartz, with occasional diatom frustules<tei:lb/>added. Areas of organic cement are sometimes seen in the shell matrix as a network (Fig.<tei:lb/>21c), with a mesh 350-400 nm in diameter and walls 150-200 nm thick (Fig. 21d). The</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DIFFLUGIA IN BRITAIN 33</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Algeria (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Austria<tei:lb/>(Laminger, 1971, 19736), Belgium (Chardez, 196 la), British Isles (Cash &amp; Hopkinson,<tei:lb/>1909), Congo (Chardez, 1964; Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), France (Deflandre, 19626;<tei:lb/>Thomas &amp; Mabille, 1956), Gabon (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Ivory Coast<tei:lb/>(Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Mexico (Laminger, 19736), Morocco (Gauthier-Lievre<tei:lb/>&amp; Thomas, 1958), Poland (Golemansky, 1970; Moraczewski, 1965), Switzerland (Penard,<tei:lb/>1902), Tashkent (Pashintowa, 1967).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The variations of D. acuminata, and its twelve varieties has recently been<tei:lb/>illustrated by Chardez (1961). More recently it has been shown (Ogden, 1979) that D.<tei:lb/>acuminata Ehrenberg, 1838 has a distinctive elongate shape and an apparently unique<tei:lb/>organic cement pattern. Specimens described as var. inflata differ from this species in shape,<tei:lb/>size and organic cement pattern, the former is clearly demonstrated when the measurements<tei:lb/>are expressed as ratios, the comparable ratios for D. acuminata are B/L 0-36 0-03 and d/L<tei:lb/>0-1 5 0-03 (Ogden, 1979).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The specimens described here agree well with Penard's (1899, 1902) original descriptions,<tei:lb/>and the differences from D. acuminata are considered sufficient to warrant specific<tei:lb/>designation. Our normal practise is to raise the variety name to specific rank, but on this<tei:lb/>occasion it is impracticable as the terminology inflata has been used several times in<tei:lb/>connection with specimens of Difflugia, for example D. curvicaulis var. inflata Decloitre,<tei:lb/>1951.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ETYMOLOGY The specific name has been selected to reflect the inflated condition of the shell<tei:lb/>(L. distenda = swell out).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia labiosa Wailes, 1919<tei:lb/>Difflugia amphora Leidy 1879 of Penard, 1901; 1902 &amp; 1905</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is opaque or dark brown, ovid or elongate ovoid, tapering from the<tei:lb/>mid-body region sharply to the rounded or pointed aboral extremity and more gradually<tei:lb/>towards the aperture (Fig. 22a). It is relatively thick and composed of quartz pieces so<tei:lb/>arranged that small particles fill the interstices between the larger pieces which form a<tei:lb/>mainly smooth surface (Fig. 22d). Organic cement is seen infrequently but is in the form of a<tei:lb/>network, each mesh being about 250-350 nm in diameter and is usually covered by a smooth<tei:lb/>membrane (Fig. 22e). The aperture is roughly circular but is sinuous with as many as six or<tei:lb/>seven undulations or lobes (Fig. 22b). It is slightly recessed into the main body, as though it<tei:lb/>was surrounded by a groove, the margin or lips, are thin and bordered by tiny particles of<tei:lb/>quartz. (Fig. 22b &amp; c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). Based on five specimens: body length 150-21 1, breadth 112-158,<tei:lb/>diameter of aperture 50-63.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from aquatic plants taken at the banks of the<tei:lb/>River Brett, near Hadleigh, Suffolk in August, 1979.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Austria (Laminger, 1971, 1975), Belgium (Chardez, 1980),<tei:lb/>British Isles (Cash et al, 1919; Ogden &amp; Hedley, 1980), Czechoslovakia (Ertl, 1965;<tei:lb/>Stepanek, 1952, 1967), France (Thomas, 1954; Thomas &amp; Mabille, 1956), Germany<tei:lb/>(Grospietsch, 1958; Schonborn, 19620, &amp; 6), Netherlands (Hoogenraad &amp; Groot, 1940),<tei:lb/>Poland (Moraczewski, 1961, 1965), Russia (Kourov, 1925), South Africa (Oye, 1931),<tei:lb/>Switzerland (Penard, 1902), Venezuela (Deflandre 1926a; Grospietsch, 1975).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. There has been some confusion over the correct name for this species, most recent<tei:lb/>authors using D. amphora Leidy, 1879, basing their identifications on the description given<tei:lb/>by Penard (1902). However, this name is preoccupied as it was used by Ehrenberg (1854,<tei:lb/>1872) to describe a specimen which is no longer considered to be a species of Difflugia. It</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difftugia mamillaris Penard, 1 893</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is colourless or hyaline, ovoid elongate, swollen or arched in the<tei:lb/>mid-region but tapering at both extremities, to give a rounded protruberance aborally and<tei:lb/>gradually near the aperture to give a slightly pronounced neck (Fig. 23a). Irregularities in<tei:lb/>general shape are not uncommon, for example one specimen although tapered did not have<tei:lb/>an aboral protruberance, whilst another (Fig. 23c) tapered markedly from the mid-body<tei:lb/>region. It is composed mainly of small to medium pieces of quartz so arranged that the larger<tei:lb/>particles tend to be in the mid-body region whilst the extremities have the smaller particles,<tei:lb/>overall it usually produces an intermediate thickness of a single layer and a relatively smooth<tei:lb/>outline. Organic cement occurs in small patches as a network, which has walls about<tei:lb/>80-1 30 um thick between each mesh but sometimes 230 um thick at junctions. Each mesh is<tei:lb/>about 250nm in diameter and is covered by a smooth membrane which is distinct in having<tei:lb/>three or four small white spots on the surface of each enclosure (Figs. 23d &amp; e). The aperture<tei:lb/>is circular, composed of small particles, and roughly finished so that the margin appears<tei:lb/>uneven or serrated (Fig. 23b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). Based on twenty-three specimens: body length 93-111, breadth<tei:lb/>54-72, diameter of aperture 23-3 1 .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from a sample of aquatic plants taken at the<tei:lb/>banks of the River Brett, near Hadleigh, Suffolk in August, 1979.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Austria (Laminger, 1975), Congo (Chardez, 1964),<tei:lb/>Czechoslovakia (St6panek, 1967), Germany (Grospietsch, 1957), Switzerland, (Penard,<tei:lb/>1901), Venezuela (Grospietsch, 1975).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species was initially described by Penard (1893) and redescribed in more<tei:lb/>detail by the same author (Penard, 1 902). The present specimens are in good agreement with<tei:lb/>the latter description, including measurements which gave the body length as being generally<tei:lb/>between 90-110 um with a few large specimens up to 130 um long. More recently,<tei:lb/>Grospietch (1957) has shown a similar regularity in size of specimens from Lake Maggiore,<tei:lb/>giving length 90-1 13 um and breadth 48-63 urn. Penard (1902) suggested that this species<tei:lb/>was rare in Swiss Lakes, and this appears to be the case in other localities judging by its<tei:lb/>reported incidence. Nevertheless, if the two recent reports (Grospietch, 1957 and the<tei:lb/>present) are used as indicators, it would appear that when present this species is usually<tei:lb/>abundant.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>D. mamillaris is distinct in outline, even though the thin structure may be subject to<tei:lb/>distortion, and the unusual organic cement pattern.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia molesta Penard, 1902</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is brown, ovoid or ovoid-elongate, sometimes with a small apertural<tei:lb/>collar and arched aborally (Fig. 25a). It is composed of a mixture of quartz particles and<tei:lb/>diatom frustules, the former usually being predominant. Organic cement is seen as a network<tei:lb/>between particles, either as part of the shell matrix or occasionally at junctions. The rings<tei:lb/>that form the network are about 650-750 nm internal diameter with the dividing wall being<tei:lb/>100-1 50 nm thick, a second thin, inner wall lies close to the main wall and in the illustrated<tei:lb/>specimen the covering membrane is either holed or figured (Fig. 25c). The aperture is<tei:lb/>roughly circular, irregular in outline, and mainly surrounded by small particles (Fig. 25b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). Based on four specimens: body length 106-114, breadth 61-87,<tei:lb/>diameter of aperture 28-43.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from Sphagnum moss gathered at Holmsley<tei:lb/>Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire in March, 1980 and Mynnd Hiraethog, Denbigh,<tei:lb/>Clwyd, North Wales in August, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Roumania (Godeanu et al, 1973), Russia (Kourov, 1925),<tei:lb/>Switzerland (Penard, 1902).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. These specimens are tentatively identified here as D. molesta, the query arising<tei:lb/>due to their similarity with D. amphoralis. Of the four specimens examined, the smallest<tei:lb/>(Fig. 25a) bears the closest resemblance being slim, with a small aperture and differing in the<tei:lb/>absence of a pointed aboral protruberance. The three broader specimens have a wider<tei:lb/>aperture, a narrow neck and are arched aborally. In addition, there are differences in the<tei:lb/>organic cement patterns between the two species, but altogether there is insufficient<tei:lb/>information to satisfactorily resolve the problem.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia tricornis (Jung, 1936) comb. nov.<tei:lb/>Difflugia elegans forma tricornis Jung, 1936</tei:p>

The surface is rough and<tei:lb/>mainly composed of a mixture of medium and large pieces of angular quartz, the spines have<tei:lb/>medium particles at their wide bases but small particles are used progressively as they taper<tei:lb/>to a point. Small areas of organic cement are seen between the particles (Fig. 26c), usually in<tei:lb/>the form of a regular network (Fig. 26d), having a mesh between 350^00 nm internal</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia ventricosa Deflandre, 1926</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is colourless, elongate, with a slight swelling in the aboral half of the<tei:lb/>body which then tapers to a sharp point (Fig. 27a). It is composed of a mixture of quartz,<tei:lb/>diatom frustules and flagellate cysts to give a thin, irregular surface. Organic cement is<tei:lb/>frequently seen between particles in the form of a network (Fig. 27c), made of rings about<tei:lb/>600-680 nm in diameter with walls 1 80-250 nm thick (Fig. 27d). In some instances the rings<tei:lb/>are fused and appear to have lost or merged their walls (Fig. 27e). The aperture is circular<tei:lb/>and usually surrounded by small particles (Fig. 27b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in urn). Two specimens: body length 177-199, breadth 64-66, diameter of<tei:lb/>aperture 30-3 1 .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. The specimens were collected from a sample of Sphagnum moss<tei:lb/>gathered at Holmsley Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire in March, 1 980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Belgium (Chardez, 1973), Congo (Gauthier-Lievre &amp;<tei:lb/>Thomas, 1958), France (Thomas &amp; Mabille, 1956), Ivory Coast (Gauthier-Lievre &amp;<tei:lb/>Thomas, 1958), Venezuela (Deflandre, 1926a).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. A redescription of D. ventricosa has recently been given by Chardez (1973) who<tei:lb/>compared it with other species having a pointed aboral extremity. It is interesting to note<tei:lb/>that he made no comparison with D. venusta, although from the description given here<tei:lb/>(below) there would seem to be some similarities. The tabulated measurements given by<tei:lb/>Chardez (1973) from earlier descriptions, are in good agreement with the exception of those<tei:lb/>given by Thomas &amp; Mabille (1956).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This species is distinct in having a thin, elongate outline which is sharply pointed aborally.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION.          <tei:p>Difflugia ampullula Playfair, 1918</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is hyaline, ovoid and circular in cross section (Fig. 30a). It has a<tei:lb/>medium thickness and is composed mainly of small to medium pieces of quartz, arranged to<tei:lb/>give a clean outline with a smooth surface. Small areas of organic cement in the form of a<tei:lb/>network, are often seen as part of the shell structure (Fig. 30d). The mesh of the network is<tei:lb/>small about 300-350 nm in diameter with thin walls 50-100 nm thick, and an even smaller<tei:lb/>distinctive network covering each mesh enclosure (Figs. 30e &amp; f)- The aperture is circular,<tei:lb/>surrounded by a slightly raised collar of small particles, and the edge of the collar is often<tei:lb/>irregular (Figs. 30b &amp; c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in urn). Range of 39 specimens: body length 54-95, breadth 35-72,<tei:lb/>diameter of aperture 16-29.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from aquatic plants taken at the banks of the<tei:lb/>River Brett, near Hadleigh, Suffolk in August, 1979.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Australia (Playfair, 1918).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS.          <tei:p>Dijflugia angulostoma Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent, spherical and composed mainly of diatom frustules<tei:lb/>(Fig. 3 la &amp; b). The particles are packed close together with many overlapping, to give a<tei:lb/>rough surface. Organic cement is seen infrequently as small strands between particles (Fig.<tei:lb/>3 Ic). The aperture is usually circular (Fig. 3 la), but it may have irregularities depending on<tei:lb/>the arrangement of surrounding diatom frustules.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Two groups of specimens with identical shell features but differing dimensions were<tei:lb/>examined, the 'a' specimens are from all four listed localities and 'b' specimens are from</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species was described by Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas (1958), from specimens<tei:lb/>found in Algeria, who considered that it differed from D. minuta by the large size of the<tei:lb/>aperture, quoted as being about one-third of the breadth in diameter, and the covering<tei:lb/>diatoms. The<tei:lb/>'b' specimens share almost similar dimensions to D. angulostoma, the latter having a body<tei:lb/>length 60-95 um and aperture 30-45 um, whereas the 'a' specimens are generally smaller.<tei:lb/>The difference in size between specimens 'a' and 'b' is more apparent when the ratios d/L are<tei:lb/>compared, such differences are usually significant. However, lack of similar data from the<tei:lb/>original description of D. angulostoma does not allow a comparison to be made. In the<tei:lb/>absence of this information and the otherwise similarity of the two groups of specimens, they<tei:lb/>are both designated as D. angulostoma.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. A network of organic cement is seen at most<tei:lb/>junctions of these particles (Fig. 32c). The mesh is about 280-350 nm in diameter and the<tei:lb/>walls 350 nm thick (Fig. 32d). The aperture is circular with often a rugged outline due to the<tei:lb/>placement of the flattish particles (Fig. 32b).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia masaruzzi Oye, 1958</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION.          <tei:p>Difflugia microstoma (Thomas, 1954) comb. nov.<tei:lb/>Difflugia globularis var. microstoma Thomas, 1954</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is ovoid or subspherical, composed mainly of a mixture of small to<tei:lb/>medium pieces of flattish quartz and diatom frustules.          <tei:p>Difflugia minuta Rampi, 1950</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia stoutii sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is ovoid or ovoid elongate, composed of mainly small, flattish<tei:lb/>particles of siliceous material, including quartz, diatom frustules and shell plates from<tei:lb/>smaller testate amoebae (Figs. 38a &amp; d). It is extremely fragile, several specimens having<tei:lb/>collapsed in preparation, and hence the apparent lateral flattening of the specimen shown in<tei:lb/>Fig. 38c. Organic cement is seen only as small threads due to the regular overlapping of the<tei:lb/>shell components (Fig. 38e). The aperture is roughly circular, small and appears to be<tei:lb/>recessed, but this latter feature may be due to structural fragility (Figs. 38b &amp; d).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). Based on four specimens: body length 47-59, breadth 33-36,<tei:lb/>diameter of aperture 9-12.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from a sample of Sphagnum moss gathered<tei:lb/>at Myndd Hiraethog, Denbigh, Clwyd, North Wales in August, 1979.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The present specimens are similar to three species recently described from<tei:lb/>Germany, namely D. stechtinensis Schonborn, 1962, D. sudiformis Schonborn, 1966 and D.<tei:lb/>szczepanskii Schonborn, 1965. They differ from the two former species in general<tei:lb/>dimensions, D. stechtinensis is almost spherical, with an aperturual diameter equal to half<tei:lb/>the body breadth, whilst D. sudiformis is an elongate, very slender species. D. szczepanskii is<tei:lb/>a slightly larger species but differs mainly in having an aperture size two-thirds of the body<tei:lb/>width. All three species are described as having a hyaline shell covered with a meagre<tei:lb/>scattering of particles.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>D. stoutii is distinct in having a fragile, elongate ovoid shell composed of flattish particles<tei:lb/>and a small aperture.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ETYMOLOGY. This species is named after the late Dr John Stout in recognition of his<tei:lb/>contributions to recent advances in protozoology.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DIFFLUGIA IN BRITAIN 59</tei:p>
         <tei:p>aboral protruberances, and a pronounced apical rim or collar (Fig. 39a). The rim has a<tei:lb/>recurved appearance the edge of which is usually well denned (Figs. 39b &amp; c), with an<tei:lb/>abundance of organic cement apparent as part of the rim matrix (Fig. 39e). The body is<tei:lb/>composed of small to medium particles of quartz, blended together so that the smaller<tei:lb/>particles and organic cement fill the gaps between the larger particles and give a relatively<tei:lb/>smooth surface. Diatom frustules or parts of them are occasionally included in the structure.<tei:lb/>Organic cement in the form of a network is seen as part of the matrix (Fig. 39d), the mesh has<tei:lb/>a diameter of about 240-290 nm with walls 100-180 nm thick (Fig. 39f). The aperture is<tei:lb/>usually circular (Fig. 39a).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Variation in this species is not uncommon. Although usually limited to the presence or<tei:lb/>absence of aboral protruberances, an occasional deformed shell may be seen. The specimen<tei:lb/>illustrated here (Figs. 40a &amp; b) has a depressed apical rim, malformed body and defined<tei:lb/>aboral protruberances are absent.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in jim). Based on twenty-one specimens: body length 204-398, breadth<tei:lb/>193-^26, diameter of aperture 87-198; B/LO-920-10,d/L 0-44 + 0-06.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Specimens were collected from samples of Sphagnum moss gathered<tei:lb/>at Holmsley Lodge, Burley, New Forest, Hampshire on several occasions, May, 1977; 1978<tei:lb/>and March, 1980.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Algeria (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Argentina<tei:lb/>(Boltovskoy &amp; Lena, 1971, 1974; Dioni, 1970; Vucetich, 19730 &amp; b), Australia (Playfair,<tei:lb/>1918), Austria (Laminger, 1972c, 19736), Belgium (Chardez, 1960, 19616; Chardez &amp;<tei:lb/>Gaspar, 1976), Brazil (Green, 1975), British Isles (Cash &amp; Hopkinson, 1909; Ogden &amp;<tei:lb/>Hedley, 1980), Chile (Decloitre, 1954), China (Decloitre, 1954), Congo (Chardez, 1964;<tei:lb/>Stepanek, 1963), Czechoslovakia (Stepanek, 1952), France (Deflandre 1962b; Thomas,<tei:lb/>1954), Germany (Jung, 1936), Hungary (Bereczky, 1973), Italy (Grandori &amp; Grandori,<tei:lb/>1934), Java (Bartos, 1963a), Netherlands (Hoogenraad &amp; Groot, 1940), Poland<tei:lb/>(Moraczewski, 1965), Russia (Kourov, 1925), Spain (Margalef, 1955), Sudan (Gauthier-<tei:lb/>Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1 958), Switzerland (Penard, 1 902), United States of America (Laminger et<tei:lb/>al, 1979), Venezuela (Deflandre, 19260;Grospietsch, 1975).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This is one of the most widely reported specimens ofDifflugia, probably due to its<tei:lb/>size and distinctive shape. However, variation in shell construction has led to the description<tei:lb/>of several varieties. Thomas (1954) used the presence of aboral protruberances to<tei:lb/>differentiate the variety olla Leidy, 1879; whilst Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas (1958) list four<tei:lb/>which differed in rim construction, namely lageniformis (Wallich), lageniformis forma<tei:lb/>minor forma nov., minor Deflandre and sphaerica Playfair; and more recently descriptions<tei:lb/>of specimens which differed in shape and material have produced two more-var. chayuensis<tei:lb/>Wang Jiagi, 1977 and forma subureceola Chardez &amp; Gaspar, 1976.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>These reports of natural variation can have little value until they are thoroughly<tei:lb/>investigated, and the present specimens are therefore referred to D. urceolata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Compressed species</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difftugia himethogii sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is light yellow or transparent, thin pyriform with a distinct neck or<tei:lb/>collar which often has parallel sides (Figs. 4 la &amp; b). The neck region is made of angular<tei:lb/>quartz and usually has a rough appearance (Fig. 41b), whilst the remainder of the body is<tei:lb/>composed of small to medium pieces of flattened quartz and has a smooth appearance.<tei:lb/>Organic cement is frequently seen in small areas as part of the shell matrix (Fig. 4 Id). It is in<tei:lb/>the form of a network, made of fused rings each having an internal diameter of about<tei:lb/>250-320 nm and walls 200-260 nm thick (Fig. 41e). The aperture is circular and surrounded<tei:lb/>by assorted particles of quartz to give it an irregular outline (Fig. 41c).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species is similar to two other compressed species namely, D. compresscf!<tei:lb/>and D. lingula Penard, 1911. Complications regarding the species D. compressa Carter, 1 864<tei:lb/>should have been resolved by Cash &amp; Hopkinson (1909) who suggested that from Carter's<tei:lb/>figures he was 'beyond question' referring to a species of Pontigulasia. Nevertheless, the<tei:lb/>name has been used subsequently to refer to compressed specimens of Difflugia, either as D.<tei:lb/>compressa or D. oblonga/pyriformis var compressa. Whether or not there are some genuine<tei:lb/>specimens of Difflugia amongst these descriptions is difficult to know, but the name<tei:lb/>compressa is preoccupied by Carter's description and is no longer valid, and most refer to<tei:lb/>much longer, broader specimens than those described here. The present specimens are<tei:lb/>distinct from D. lingula Penard, 1911 and D. lingula var regularis Gauthier-Lievre &amp;<tei:lb/>Thomas, 1958 because these have a more rounded shape which tapers sharply from the<tei:lb/>mid-body region to the aperture, and D. lingula also has an aboral horn.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>D. hiraethogii can be recognised by its lateral compression, distinct circular collar and<tei:lb/>aperture.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ETYMOLOGY. This species is named after the area of North Wales in which it was found.</tei:p>
