         <tei:p>REMARKS. Some differences in dimensions are worth noting from earlier descriptions; Penard<tei:lb/>(1 890) gave a range of body length 50-70 um, but later stated that specimens ranged between<tei:lb/>50-60 and rarely greater than 65 um (Penard, 1902), Cash &amp; Hopkinson quoted 60-80 um,<tei:lb/>whilst Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas (1958) suggested that there might be three groups (a)<tei:lb/>44-50 (b) 55-70 (c) 83-90. In the present group of specimens only seven are outside of the<tei:lb/>range 70-80 um and they are remarkable for their similarity.<tei:lb/>This species is distinct in having a well defined shape and by being evenly compressed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discussion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>One of the main difficulties encountered in trying to identify specimens ofDifflugia is due to<tei:lb/>the irregular shape of the shell. It is a problem shared with other agglutinate species of<tei:lb/>protozoa, such as the foraminifera. In general most species have a regular basic outline,<tei:lb/>which may be altered by either natural variation or obscured by the addition of extraneous<tei:lb/>material. Both natural and additional variation are probably related to the composition of<tei:lb/>the shell, fragile shells being more likely to be influenced by disturbances in the environment<tei:lb/>during or after construction, whilst robust shells may be so encrusted by particles that any<tei:lb/>resemblance of a specific shape is lost.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fragile shells are usually made of small particles arranged in a single layer and often have<tei:lb/>organic cement as a major component of the shell matrix. In some species a smooth surface<tei:lb/>composed of flattish particles is constructed, for example D. mamillaris, where variation in</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>DIFFLUGIA IN BRITAIN 67</tei:p>
         <tei:p>general shape is frequently seen especially in the aboral region (see Fig. 23). Nevertheless, in<tei:lb/>a species with a similar surface but different shape, D. lanceolata which is rounded rather<tei:lb/>than pointed in the aboral region, there is a relatively constant shape. In the present report a<tei:lb/>third of the specimens of D. mamillaris differ from the basic outline given in Fig. 29, whilst<tei:lb/>all of the specimens of D. lanceolata agree with the outline in Fig. 1 8. Although robust shells<tei:lb/>are usually made of angular quartz which do not lend themselves to being arranged in a<tei:lb/>regular manner, if enough specimens of a species is present in a sample it is possible to<tei:lb/>illustrate a basic outline. The problems of subsequent recognition of such species from the<tei:lb/>basic outline is complicated when the diagnostic feature is obscured by the arrangement of<tei:lb/>particles. For instance the diagnostic feature may be the presence of a neck, but if this is<tei:lb/>hidden it may be identified incorrectly. Amongst species with this type of shell, examples of<tei:lb/>specimens incorporating a single large particle with similar dimensions to the whole shell<tei:lb/>have been observed and a not infrequent sight is to see two similar shells united. In these<tei:lb/>latter instances the shell is usually of similar size and composition, but these are not<tei:lb/>necessarily species of Difflugia but can be other agglutinate forms like Pontigulasia (pers.<tei:lb/>observation).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>To assist in resolving the question of what represents the basic outline in the species<tei:lb/>described here, three sets of line drawings are provided (Figs. 18, 29, 45) which are based on<tei:lb/>the average dimensions of the specimens examined.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the previous studies on pyriform species of Difflugia it has been suggested (Ogden,<tei:lb/>1979) that measurements are useful in distinguishing species, with the body length and<tei:lb/>diameter of aperture perhaps being the more stable dimensions. However, it was emphasised<tei:lb/>that these features alone are not usually sufficient to warrant specific diagnoses. The problem<tei:lb/>of using dimensions as a diagnostic character is that they may be valid for a proportion of<tei:lb/>species in a genus, but do not hold for all especially in the present instance with a genus<tei:lb/>supposedly comprised of over three hundred species. Possibly this is best illustrated by the<tei:lb/>variability in size exhibited within a species of testate amoebae, the smallest often being<tei:lb/>reported as half the size of the largest, which does not pose problems of identification when<tei:lb/>the body length is under 80 um, but for those of larger dimensions the difference between<tei:lb/>200 jim and 400 urn can often be interpreted as representing two separate species. The<tei:lb/>extreme example is as we have noted previously (Ogden &amp; Fairman, 1979) the range of<tei:lb/>measurements quoted for the body length of D. oblonga, 60-580 um, which is so variable<tei:lb/>that it could embrace most of the genus or almost all of the described testate amoebae.<tei:lb/>Nevertheless, there are examples of consistent dimensions within a species, for instance<tei:lb/>those of D. lanceolata and D. mamillaris described here are in good agreement with<tei:lb/>previously published results (Penard, 1902; Grospietsch, 1957). The regularity of body<tei:lb/>length in D. lanceolata is such that over 87% of the specimens fall within a range of 10% of<tei:lb/>the average value given in Table 2, whilst in D. mamillaris 96% fall within the same range.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ovoid or spherical specimens of Difflugia present the same problem. In certain cases<tei:lb/>groups of similar species may only be distinguished by dimensions, for example D. anchlora,<tei:lb/>D. gramen and D. lobostoma (see Ogden, 1980) which may represent a phylogenetic series.<tei:lb/>Whilst is other cases, like the D. globulosa/globularis species complex, size variation is so<tei:lb/>great that it is difficult not to include any ovoid or spherical specimen between 50-1 50 um in<tei:lb/>this complex. Part of the difficulty is illustrated by the two groups of specimens described<tei:lb/>here as D. angulostoma (p. 47), where the shells are identical in construction and essentially<tei:lb/>they share the same shape, but there are differences in dimensions especially the diameter of<tei:lb/>the aperture. It is possible to separate some of these small spherical species using the latter<tei:lb/>feature in addition to other differences, as shown in the descriptions of D. minuta, D. mica<tei:lb/>and D. microstoma (see p. 53, p. 50 &amp; p. 53). Structural differences in basic outline as<tei:lb/>mentioned earlier are mainly related to shell components and deformities, the former<tei:lb/>concerns the choice of materials and will be dealt with later, but the latter using D. urceolata<tei:lb/>as an example may be due to its large size. Perhaps it is easier to understand if one considers<tei:lb/>that in all probability the shell components are not cemented together until the final shape</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>68 C. G. OGDEN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>has been moulded by cytoplasmic movements. As this process takes about sixty minutes in a<tei:lb/>small siliceous species (Ogden, 1981), it will probably take considerably longer in a larger<tei:lb/>animal, during which time in a natural environment there is a continual motion<tei:lb/>and hence a possibility of disruption. The result of such a disturbance may produce the shell<tei:lb/>illustrated in Fig. 40, sufficiently different from normal but not enough for the animal to<tei:lb/>abhort and discard the shell prior to the final stiffening of the cement.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Studies on clonal cultures of both siliceous and proteinaceous species (Ogden, 1981 &amp;<tei:lb/>pers. observations) show that variation in dimensions are small, differences outside the norm<tei:lb/>usually being attributed to abnormal development and even here it is usually below 5%.<tei:lb/>Such abnormal development is thought to be associated with cultural differences and not a<tei:lb/>frequent natural occurrence. One feature of siliceous species behaviour which may explain<tei:lb/>some changes in dimensions, is the occasional production of a shell having a double<tei:lb/>complement of shell plates (Hedley &amp; Ogden, 1973). However, this results in an increase in<tei:lb/>volume of an ovoid structure, which means that the enlargement in body length is probably<tei:lb/>no greater than a third.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>At present there are only two reports (Jennings, 1916, 1937) on the development of<tei:lb/>Difflugila corona in the laboratory. Both have shown that there can be some variation in<tei:lb/>shell construction and until further observations are available on other species of Difflugia,<tei:lb/>the question of shell size and composition as diagnostic features will remain a subject of<tei:lb/>speculation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Mention has already been made in the literature of differences in shell construction and<tei:lb/>the three categories which are readily identified, robust, intermediate and fragile (Ogden,<tei:lb/>1980). But the choice of materials, other than a comment (Ogden, 1980) on the influence of<tei:lb/>pH, has not been discussed. That a system of choice is available to the animal is clearly<tei:lb/>demonstrated by the composition of certain shells. For example, D. minutissima, D.<tei:lb/>lanceolata, D. mamillaris and D. decloitrei all use flattish pieces of quartz, in some instances<tei:lb/>small flat particles of diatom frustules may be substituted, but the components used appear<tei:lb/>to be restricted in size and thickness. The function of the cytoplasm to identify and select<tei:lb/>these particles may appear to be extreme. Nevertheless, it can be measured against the ability<tei:lb/>of siliceous testate amoebae to hold each shell plate during shell construction, place it in<tei:lb/>position so that there is an even amount of overlap between plates and in some specimens<tei:lb/>manipulate spines into definite positions (Ogden, 1981). This selectivity is not restricted to<tei:lb/>flat particles, but probably includes the choice of diatoms or angular particles, as well as<tei:lb/>mixtures of all types in the composition of Difflugia shells. An additional factor that<tei:lb/>influences the choice of particles is undoubtedly the structure and extent of organic cement<tei:lb/>in the shell matrix.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It has already been suggested (Ekert &amp; McGee-Russell, 1974) that the organic cement<tei:lb/>which binds the shell particles together in Difflugia lobostoma imparts both strength and<tei:lb/>flexibility to the structure. This may seem obvious from the different type of shells<tei:lb/>constructed by these animals, but it has an importance related to the material used. For<tei:lb/>instance, when the cement becomes part of the surface matrix, usually in species with flat<tei:lb/>particles, it is found at each facet as part of the shell wall. The strength of the shell is then<tei:lb/>directly related to the tenacity of the cement at these junctions.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Furthermore, in species where there is some overlapping of particles the cement is<tei:lb/>interwoven with the material and can be likened to the structure of a brick wall, in which the<tei:lb/>strength is dramatically increased by the combination of bricks and mortar beyond the<tei:lb/>strength of the individual materials assessed on their own. The importance of the organic<tei:lb/>cement in shell structure is easily demonstrated by treating a robust individual with either a<tei:lb/>chelating agent or concentrated sulphuric acid, in each case within a short time it is reduced<tei:lb/>to a small residue of particles. Strength is not directly due to the composition of the acid<tei:lb/>mucopolysaccharide material that forms the basic organic cement, but to the properties of<tei:lb/>this material. It has already been shown that inorganic elements incorporated with this type<tei:lb/>of material in the proteinaceous shells of testate amoebae (Hedley et al, 1976; pers. observ.)</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>DIFFLUGIA IN BRITAIN 69</tei:p>
         <tei:p>and areanceous foraminifera (Hedley, 1963) are thought to strengthen the shell. Some<tei:lb/>specimens of Difflugia have been examined by X-ray microanalytical techniques (pers.<tei:lb/>observ.), and found to have a significant amount of ferrous iron associated with areas of<tei:lb/>organic cement. This probably accounts for the reports of yellow or brown specimens, the<tei:lb/>degree of colouration being proportional to the amount of inorganic elements bound to the<tei:lb/>organic cement. There is no doubt that the density of this colouration in proteinaceous<tei:lb/>specimens can be used as a measure of the degree of reinforcement that the inorganic<tei:lb/>elements impart to the structure, newly formed shells being light coloured and fragile, whilst<tei:lb/>older shells are dark and strong. This measure can probably be applied to agglutinate forms<tei:lb/>as well.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The differences in the network structure of the organic cement are harder to understand,<tei:lb/>especially as the examination is limited to surface detail. A need for porosity in some of the<tei:lb/>individual organic cement units is puzzling. If they are definite pores to the interior of the<tei:lb/>shell they might function as pressure valves for aqueous interchange, because often when the<tei:lb/>animal is moving or feeding the apertural opening is completely blocked by cytoplasmic<tei:lb/>extrusions. Such a scheme could ensure that the internal volume unoccupied by cytoplasm is<tei:lb/>not isolated and allowed to stagnate. Alternatively it may be associated with the hardening<tei:lb/>process by inorganic elements. This process seems to be directly related to the environment<tei:lb/>and the chemical composition of the cement, the activities of the animal apparently having<tei:lb/>no effect on this association. The strengthening process has some degree of justification<tei:lb/>because the pores often seen between individual proteinaceous units is newly-formed, light<tei:lb/>coloured, shells ofArcella, are not seen in older darker specimens. Examination of the walls<tei:lb/>of such specimens show that they are thick and stronger, the implication being that the<tei:lb/>inorganic elements have strengthened the shell not the deposition of further organic material<tei:lb/>(pers. observ.).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The diversity in the construction of the organic cement units is considered to be a good<tei:lb/>taxonomic feature, although they are beyond the limit of optical microscopy, the appearance<tei:lb/>of some being particularly unique for example the button-type of D. lacustris (see Fig. 5e<tei:lb/>p. 9). The sharing of the same type of unit between different species may suggest some<tei:lb/>phylogenetic relationship, possibly linked to the type of shell construction whether smooth,<tei:lb/>rough, fragile or strong. However, at present only a quarter of the described species have<tei:lb/>been examined and it is too early to make proposals on such relationships. That is apart from<tei:lb/>the apparent sharing of the same organic cement pattern between most ovoid species. It is<tei:lb/>hoped that further studies in progress on this genus will help to unravel the complications<tei:lb/>attributed to describing so many different shapes and forms, and allow a comprehensive<tei:lb/>division based on shell structure.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>An Atlas of Freshwater Testate Amoebae</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. G. Ogden &amp; R. H. Medley</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1980, Hardcovers, 222pp, 17,50 (18.00 by post). Co-published by British Museum<tei:lb/>(Natural History) and Oxford University Press.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This book illustrates, using scanning electron micrographs, most of the common<tei:lb/>species of testate amoebae that are found in freshwater habitats. Information on<tei:lb/>the biology, ecology, geographical distribution and a classification are followed by<tei:lb/>descriptions of ninety-five species. Each of these is illustrated by several views of<tei:lb/>the shell.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The text is designed not only to enable biologists to identify species of testate<tei:lb/>amoebae, but to serve as an introduction to students interested in the taxonomy<tei:lb/>and biology of these freshwater protozoa. It will be of special interest to<tei:lb/>protozoologists, ecologists, limnologists, water treatment specialists and<tei:lb/>micropalaeontologists interested in recent sediments.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>British Museum (Natural History)<tei:lb/>Publication Sales,<tei:lb/>Cromwell Road,<tei:lb/>London SW7 5BD.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>The Bulletin of the British Museum (Natural History), instituted in 1949, is issued in four<tei:lb/>scientific series, Botany, Entomology, Geology (incorporating Mineralogy) and Zoology, and<tei:lb/>an Historical series.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Papers in the Bulletin are primarily the results of research carried out on the unique and<tei:lb/>ever-growing collections of the Museum, both by the scientific staff of the Museum and by<tei:lb/>specialists from elsewhere who make use of the Museum's resources. Many of the papers are<tei:lb/>works of reference that will remain indispensable for years to come.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parts are published at irregular intervals as they become ready, each is complete in itself,<tei:lb/>available separately, and individually priced. Volumes contain about 300 pages and several<tei:lb/>volumes may appear within a calendar year. Subscriptions may be placed for one or more of<tei:lb/>the series on either an Annual or Per Volume basis. Prices vary according to the contents of<tei:lb/>the individual parts.          <tei:p>Cirolana cranchi Leach, 1818 (Crustacea: Isopoda:<tei:lb/>Cirolanidae) redescribed, with notes on its<tei:lb/>distribution</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Niel L. Bruce</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, University of Queensland, St Lucia, Queensland, 4067, Australia</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Joan Ellis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana cranchi is considered to be the type of the genus Cirolana Leach (by monotypy,<tei:lb/>Bruce, 1981) and as Cirolana is the type genus for the family Cirolanidae (Harger, 1880;<tei:lb/>Hansen, 1890), the species is of some significance. It comes therefore as some surprise to find<tei:lb/>that C. cranchi has not been effectively described since the work of Hansen (1890). The<tei:lb/>species has frequently been considered to be indistinguishable from Cirolana parva Hansen<tei:lb/>1890 (Stebbing, 1917; Nordenstam, 1946; Monod, 1976), and though these authors have<tei:lb/>discussed at some length the similarities of the two species, none resorted to redescription.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hale (1925) described a variety of Cirolana cranchi from South Australia. Examination of<tei:lb/>that species suggested that not only was Hale's variety quite distinct from C. cranchi, but that<tei:lb/>several closely similar species exist in the seas around Australia. It was therefore necessary to<tei:lb/>examine the holotype and specimens of Cirolana cranchi from England in order to be certain<tei:lb/>that none of the Australian species is C. cranchi.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>To prevent any further confusion of Cirolana cranchi with C, parva and similar related<tei:lb/>species, a new description is given here with full figures, from specimens in the British<tei:lb/>Museum (Natural History) collections. The holotype, initially stored in the British<tei:lb/>Museum's dry collections (Ellis, 1981) is in extremely poor condition, lacking most<tei:lb/>appendages, setae and spines, and is in two pieces. The holotype, taken from Cornwall was<tei:lb/>closely compared to Norman's (1904) specimen to assure conspecificity, and the description<tei:lb/>and drawings are taken from the latter.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana cranchi Leach<tei:lb/>(Figs 1-3)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana cranchii Leach, 1818 : 347; Gosse, 1855 : 134, Fig. 230; Hesse, 1866 : 257; Bate &amp; Westwood,<tei:lb/>1867:296, Fig. 5; Delages, 1881 : 156; Chevreux 1884:519; Koehler, 1886:25, 61; Bonnier,<tei:lb/>1887: 134;Heape, 1888: 1 76; Robertson, 1888 : 76; Hansen, 1890 : 341, PI. 3, figs 3-3i; 1905:350,<tei:lb/>PI. 33. fig 3a; Stebbing, 1893 : 343; 1906 : 275; Norman, 1904 : 438; 1907 : 362; Norman &amp; Scott,<tei:lb/>1906:40, PI. 4; Monod, 1923: 14; 1930: 137, 145, Figs 2, 5B; 1976: 151; Larwood, 1940:33;<tei:lb/>Barrett &amp; Yonge, 1958 : 99, Fig. 59; Crothers, 1966 : 58; Naylor, 1972 : 28, Fig. 9A-C; Ryland &amp;<tei:lb/>Nelson-Smith, 1975 : 252; Kussakin, 1979: 191, Figs 71, 72; Bruce. 1981 : 949.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Nelocira swainsonii Leach, 1818 : 347; Desmarest, 1825 : 302, PI. 48, fig. 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Eurydice swainsonii: Milne-Edwards, 1840 : 236.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Conilera grampoides Gourrett, 1 89 1 : 1 1 , PI. 1 , fig. 7, PI. 3, figs 4-1 1 .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana cranchi: Marine Biological Association, 1931 : 183; 1957 : 195; Ellis, 1981 : 123.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana borealis: Clarke, 1971 : 103 (Non Natatolana &amp;0ra2//s(Lilljeborg)).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Part Cirolana cranchii, Nordenstam, 1946:3, Figs 1-5. [More than one species is involved in<tei:lb/>Nordenstam's description.]</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Non Cirolana cranchii, Barnard, 1920 : 346; 1940 : 392, 49, 499, Fig. 66; Kensley, 1978 : 65, Fig. 27B,<tei:lb/>C.[= Cirolana vicina Barnard, 1914].</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE. The holotype is held by the British Museum (Natural History).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. nat. Hist. (Zool.) 44(2): 75-84 Issued 24 February 1 983</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Fig. 3 Cirolana cranchi: all figs from cf 14-0 mm. (a) maxilliped; (b) maxillule; (c) right mandible;<tei:lb/>(d) antenna, flagellar articles 9-11; (e) antennule, flagellar articles 4-7; (f) left mandible, incisor;<tei:lb/>(g) mandibular palp; (h) maxilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE LOCALITY. Leach (1818) gives 'Grande Bretagne' as the source of his specimens. Ellis<tei:lb/>(1 98 1 ) records the locality as Falmouth, Cornwall.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL. 2cf (17-0, 13-3 mm), Polperro, Cornwall. Coll. A. M. Norman. BM(NH) Reg<tei:lb/>1911. 11. 8:7840-49. 2rf(14-0, 9-5 mm) 9 (12-6 mm), Plymouth, Devon. Coll. A. M.<tei:lb/>Norman. BM(NH) Reg 191 1. 11.8: 7828-30. &lt;S (17-2 mm), 2 9 (13-0, 13-5 mm), Torquay,<tei:lb/>Devon. Coll. A. M. Norman. BM(NH) Reg 191 1. 1 1. 8: 7831-33. These specimens form part<tei:lb/>of those reported on by Norman (1904).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION OF MALE. Body about 2-75-3 times longer than wide. Cephalon without rostral<tei:lb/>process, interocular carina on anterior margin, dorsal interocular furrow extending from the<tei:lb/>dorsal medial margin of each eye; posterior margin of cephalon with groove on each side<tei:lb/>indicating presence of maxillipedal somite. Pereonite 1 longest, with 2 horizontal furrows on<tei:lb/>each side; pereonites 2-7 approximately subequal in length; coxal plates each with distinct<tei:lb/>carina, posterior margins of coxae 5-7 straight, projecting beyond posterior margin of<tei:lb/>segment. Pleonite 1 entirely concealed by pereonite 7; pleonite 3 with posterolateral margins<tei:lb/>moderately produced, those of pleonite 4 rounded; dorsal surfaces of pleonites with posterior<tei:lb/>margins minutely crenulate, pleonite 5 with additional small tubercles. Pleotelson slightly<tei:lb/>more than 0-66 as long as greatest width, lateral margins sinuate, converging to narrowly</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>CIROLANA CRANCHI 79</tei:p>
         <tei:p>rounded apex; posterior margins densely setose, with about 21 spines; dorsal surface flat<tei:lb/>except for two oblique anterolateral ridges.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Antennule peduncle Inarticulate, articles 1 and 2 appearing fused, although suture<tei:lb/>evident; peduncular article 3 equal in length to combined lengths of articles 1 and 2;<tei:lb/>flagellum extends to posterior margin of eye. Antenna with peduncular articles 1 and 2 short,<tei:lb/>peduncular article 3 about half as long as 4, which in turn is half as long as 5; flagellum<tei:lb/>composed of about 38 articles, extends to pereonite 4.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Frontal lamina pentagonal, apex not overlapped by rostral process, lateral margins slightly<tei:lb/>concave, diverging slightly, anterior margins straight; about 0-5 times as long as greatest<tei:lb/>width. Clypeus about 5-75 times wider than long. Mandibles with asymetrical incisors, that<tei:lb/>of right mandible with 3 distinctly formed subequal cusps, that of left mandible with<tei:lb/>posterior cusp prominent, central cusp broad and shallow; molar process with about 25<tei:lb/>teeth, inferior distal margin setose, lacina mobilis with about 7 spines; mandibular palp with<tei:lb/>terminal article curved ventrolaterally, lateral margins with numerous stiff setae. Maxillule<tei:lb/>with about 10 stout spines on gnathal surface of exopod, 3 robust plumose setae on proximal<tei:lb/>half of medial margin. Maxilliped with continuous marginal setae on palp articles 3-5,<tei:lb/>marginal setae on distal margins only of palp article 2; endite with 3 terminal and 3 lateral<tei:lb/>plumose setae, and with 2 coupling hooks.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pereopod 1 with slender spines at posterior distal angle of basis; ischium with 2 setae on<tei:lb/>posterior margin and 3 setae at anterior distal angle; merus with about 6 setae at anterior<tei:lb/>distal angle, posterior margin with 3 acute spines and 5 tubercular submarginal spines;<tei:lb/>carpus with a single spine on posterior margin, set within a conspicuous indentation;<tei:lb/>propodus with 2 acute spines on palm, each spine set distally to tooth like projection, third<tei:lb/>robust spine opposes dactylus; margin of propodus minutely denticulate between spines.<tei:lb/>Pereopods 2 and 3 similar, pereopod 2 with 3 acute spines at anterior distal angle of ischium,<tei:lb/>2 blunt spines at posterior distal angle, and third spine on the distal lateral margin; merus<tei:lb/>with 5 spines at anterior distal angle, posterior margin bisinuate, with 8 stout spines; carpus<tei:lb/>with a single stout spine and single seta on posterior distal angle, spine present on lateral<tei:lb/>distal margin; propodus with 3 spines on palm, fourth spine opposing the dactylus; dactylus<tei:lb/>with weakly developed but distinct secondary unguis, as in all pereopods. Pereopods 5-7<tei:lb/>similar, pereopod 4 intermediate in form between anterior (1-3) and posterior (5-7)<tei:lb/>pereopods. Pereopod 7 with about 2 setae and anterior distal angles of propodus, otherwise<tei:lb/>without setae; distal angles of ischium, merus and carpus each with a group of spines;<tei:lb/>posterior margin of ischium and produs with further 3 groups of 1-4 spines; posterior<tei:lb/>margins of merus and carpus with further group of spines; propodus has spine opposing<tei:lb/>dactylus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Penes set together on posterior of sternite 7, separated from each other by about 0-05 the<tei:lb/>width of the sternite; penes are not robust, but rather lamellar flaps of cuticle which originate<tei:lb/>posteriorly, and project anteriorly, lying against sternite.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pleopods 3-5 with exopods with partial suture. Peduncles of pleopods 1-5 becoming<tei:lb/>progressively shorter towards posterior, peduncle of pleopod 1 twice as wide as long,<tei:lb/>peduncle of pleopod 4 3-5 times as wide as long; lateral distal angles each with a single spine,<tei:lb/>medial margin 3-5 coupling hooks on pleopods 1-4, pleopod 5 without coupling hooks.<tei:lb/>Pleopod 1 with rami subequal in length, endopod with margins parallel, exopod with spine<tei:lb/>at proximal lateral angle. Pleopod 2 with endopod fractionally longer than exopod; appendix<tei:lb/>masculina arises basally, extends beyond endopod by 0-1 of its length, narrows smoothly to<tei:lb/>an acute apex.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Uropods extend distinctly beyond apex of pleotelson. Exopod slightly shorter than<tei:lb/>endopod, lateral margin smoothly convex, with continuous marginal setae and about 9<tei:lb/>spines, distal half of lateral margin with dense mass of setae extending on to dorsal surface;<tei:lb/>medial margin with distinct angle half way along its length, distal half with 6 spines and<tei:lb/>dense mass of marginal setae; apex not bifid. Endopod with lateral margin angled at about<tei:lb/>0.33 of the way along its length, proximal 0-66 densely setose, setae extending onto dorsal<tei:lb/>surface; medial margin convex with dense marginal setae and about 8 spines; apex not bifid.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>80</tei:p>
         <tei:p>N. L. BRUCE &amp;J. ELLIS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 4 Map showing the distribution of C. cranchi. Type locality of Nelocira swainsonii Leach,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>18 18 given as Sicily.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>FEMALE. The only differences from the male are in the shape of the pleotelson which is wider,<tei:lb/>in the shape of the uropods which are not angled, and the lack of the dense setae on the<tei:lb/>pleotelson and uropods.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DEVELOPMENT. Young males are similar to females, and the characteristic shape and setation<tei:lb/>of the uropods of large males is acquired gradually. One female has oostegites, and measured<tei:lb/>13-3 mm.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>CIROLANACRANCHI 81</tei:p>
         <tei:p>VARIATION. From the specimens examined, it would appear that the minute crenulations on<tei:lb/>the pleonites are not visible in large males. The presence and distribution of tubercles on<tei:lb/>the dorsal surface of the pleonites is erratic.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>SIZE. Hansen (1905) records the largest specimen as a female of 18.0 mm. Museum material<tei:lb/>had adult males from 9-0-1 9-1 mm, females ranged from 9-6-1 9-2 mm.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COLOUR. In alcohol, all a pale tan. Barrett and Yonge (1958) describe the colour as 'very pale<tei:lb/>grey, minutely dotted on first three segments; rear edge of first seven segments marked by<tei:lb/>transverse line'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The shape and setation of the pleotelson and uropods are unique, and<tei:lb/>immediately separate Cirolana cranchi from all other species of Cirolana. Other characters<tei:lb/>useful in separation include the shape of the frontal lamina, the shape of the posterolateral<tei:lb/>margins of the pleonites, the length and shape of appendix masculina, the shape of the<tei:lb/>endopod of pleopod 1 , and the form of the penes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana cranchi can be separated from C. parva by the lack of a rostral point, by not<tei:lb/>having the frontal lamina overlapped by a rostral projection, and the very different shape,<tei:lb/>setation and spination of the pleotelson and uropods.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION. Reliably recorded only from the eastern North Atlantic and Mediterranean.<tei:lb/>These records are summarized here together with new records from the collections of the<tei:lb/>British Museum. Fig. 4 illustrates the present distribution of Cirolana cranchi.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Firth of Clyde: Cumbrae (Bate &amp; Westwood, 1867); Fairland Point, Cumbrae (Robertson,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1888).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Galway Bay, Eire: Spiddal (Ryland and Nelson-Smith, 1975); near Galway (Naylor, 1972);</tei:p>
         <tei:p>North Sound (Clark, 197 1 , as Cirolana borealis).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Nymphe Bank: Ballycotton, County Cork, Eire (BM(NH) Collections).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>St George's Channel: Dale, Pembroke (Crothers, 1966).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>English Channel: Falmouth (BM(NH), holotype); Gwyllyn Vase, Falmouth (Stebbing,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1906); Polperro, Cornwall; Torquay (Norman, 1904); Plymouth, outside breakwater (Bate &amp;</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Westwood, 1867); Batten, Mewstone Ledge, Tinside and Stoke Point, Plymouth (Marine</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Biological Association, 1957); Knapp Buoy, Plymouth (Heape, 1888); Torbay (BM(NH)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>collections); Anstis Cove, near Torquay (Stebbing, 1893); Jersey (Koehler, 1886); off St</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sampson's Harbour, Guernsey (Norman, 1907); Roscoff(Delages, 1881).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bay of Biscay: Minou, Brittany (Hesse, 1866); Concarneau (Bonnier, 1887);</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Grands-Carneaux and east of Belle-Isle, Croisic (Chevreux, 1884); Le Croisic; Belle Isle; He</tei:p>
         <tei:p>d'Yeu; Guethery, near Biarritz (Hansen, 1905); Capbreton (Norman, 1904).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Mediterranean: Toulon; Cannes; Porto Vecchio, Corsica; Gabes, Tunis (Hansen, 1905);</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Rade d'Hyeres (BM(NH) collections); Brusq, Var (Gourret, 1891, as Conilera grampoides);</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Villefranche (Hansen, 1890); Monaco (Monod, 1923); Sicily (Leach, 1818, as Nelocira</tei:p>
         <tei:p>swainsonii)', Alexandria (Larwood, 1940).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discussion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Leach (1818) recorded Cirolana cranchi, the second species of what was to become<tei:lb/>the family Cirolanidae. The species then received little attention up to the revision of<tei:lb/>the family by Hansen (1890). In that publication and a later one Hansen (1905) reviewed all<tei:lb/>previous records. Records published since Hansen's two publications have basically con-<tei:lb/>tributed little towards an increased knowledge of the species. Monod (1930) figured the<tei:lb/>pleopods, and Kussakin (1979) gave new figures for the antenna, pereopods 1 and 7 and the<tei:lb/>male second pleopod, his other figures being taken from Hansen (1890). From this it can be<tei:lb/>seen that although the figures given by Hansen (1890) are of a high standard, no modern<tei:lb/>description has been given.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>82 N. L. BRUCE &amp;J. ELLIS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As a consequence of the lack of detailed description Cirolana cranchi has become<tei:lb/>confused with Cirolana parva. The initiator of this confusion was Stebbing (1917)<tei:lb/>who considered C. cranchi and C. parva as likely to be synonymous. This opinion<tei:lb/>was later followed by Nordenstam (1946) who went to some length to demonstrate<tei:lb/>that the two species were one. Unfortunately, he chose to do this by illustrating<tei:lb/>pereopod characteristics, the one character likely to lead to the conclusion to which<tei:lb/>he came. Pereopods in the genus Cirolana (sens. str. Bruce, 1981) vary very little<tei:lb/>between species. Monod (1976) entered the argument in describing a Cirolana sp.<tei:lb/>from Togo, West Africa. In this paper Monod reviewed the arguments of previous<tei:lb/>authors, and discussed Hansen's (1890) diagnoses. Monod came to the conclusion<tei:lb/>that the West African species could be assigned neither to cranchi nor to parva and<tei:lb/>nor could they be said not to belong to those species. In effect, that parva and cranchi<tei:lb/>are not separable.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>When reviewing this debate, the most surprising aspect is that the problem existed<tei:lb/>at all. Hansen's (1890) figures clearly separate the two species. The differences in<tei:lb/>frontal lamina shape, pleotelson and uropods, presence and absence of rostral process are all<tei:lb/>clearly shown. Hansen also states that in parva the uropod apices are bifid, and in cranchi<tei:lb/>entire. It also seems remarkable that no author encountering this problem has sought to<tei:lb/>redescribe the species involved as a solution. Comparison of the figures, description and<tei:lb/>remarks given here to those of Bruce &amp; Bowman (1982) show that Cirolana cranchi and<tei:lb/>Cirolana parva are two readily separable species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As there has been some confusion over the species included under the synonymies<tei:lb/>of Cirolana cranchi, the most important of these are now listed and discussed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana swainsonii: Miers, 1881. Examination of Miers' specimens shows that they</tei:p>
         <tei:p>represent a species related to, but distinct from cranchi that has yet to be described.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana vicina Barnard, 1914. This species is very similar to C. cranchi and should</tei:p>
         <tei:p>be redescribed before final judgement on its status is passed. From Barnard's (1914)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>description it differs in having 'sub-bifid' uropod apices, and lacks the dense mass of setae on</tei:p>
         <tei:p>the uropods.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana cranchii var. australiense Hale, 1925. This species is in no way a race or</tei:p>
         <tei:p>variety of C. cranchi. It differs in the shape of the frontal lamina, pleotelson and</tei:p>
         <tei:p>posterolateral margin ofpleonites2-4, and has a distinct rostral process.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana cranchii: Nordenstam, 1946. Nordenstam's material came from Europe, South</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Africa and the Pacific, and consisted of C. cranchi., C. vicina and a species of</tei:p>
         <tei:p>unknown identity from the Pacific.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana australiense Naylor, 1961. This species is closest to the variety described</tei:p>
         <tei:p>by Hale (1925), but may be a distinct species. Naylor's specimens were from the</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Chatham Islands, New Zealand.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cirolana sp.: Monod, 1976. Monod figures two 'Cirolana sp', one from Togo, and</tei:p>
         <tei:p>one from the Congo. One of these is Cirolana chaloti Bouvier, 1901 (Bruce, in press),</tei:p>
         <tei:p>and the other species may well be new. Monod's (1931) record of C. cranchi may be</tei:p>
         <tei:p>of one of these species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In conclusion, it should be emphasized that in identifying or describing species<tei:lb/>related to C. parva and C. cranchi particular care and attention should be given to<tei:lb/>details of the frontal lamina, the relative position and shape of the penes, the shape<tei:lb/>of the posterolateral margins of pleonites 2-4, the first and second pleopods of the<tei:lb/>male, and most importantly details of the pleotelson and uropods.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Acknowledgements</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>Valettietta, a new genus of deep-sea amphipod<tei:lb/>(Gammaridea: Lysianassidae) with descriptions of<tei:lb/>two new species from the North Atlantic Ocean</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Roger J. Lincoln</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London<tei:lb/>SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Michael H. Thurston</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Institute of Oceanographic Sciences, Brook Road, Wormley, Godalming, Surrey GU8 5UB</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Within the gammaridean family Lysianassidae the combination of a strongly toothed<tei:lb/>mandibular incisor and unspecialized gnathopods is shared by only 4 genera, Valettia<tei:lb/>Stebbing, 1888, Alicella Chevreux, 1899, Valettiopsis Holmes, 1908 and Valettiella<tei:lb/>Griffiths, 1977. Each is monotypic with the exception of Valettiopsis which comprises 4<tei:lb/>species, dentata Holmes, 1908, macrodactyla Chevreux, 1909, anacantha Birstein &amp;<tei:lb/>Vinogradov, 1963 and multidentata Barnard, 1961. Barnard (1969) included one other<tei:lb/>genus, Onesimoides Stebbing, 1888, in his key to lysianassids having a toothed incisor, but<tei:lb/>there is some doubt as to the true nature of the mandibular margin. Stebbing (1888), in his<tei:lb/>description of O. carinatus says '. . . cutting edge . . . seemingly of the usual form . . .'. In O.<tei:lb/>cavimanus, the mandible has '. . . bord tranchant presque simple, renforce a chacun de ses<tei:lb/>angles par des bourrelets de chitine. . . .' (Pirlot, 1933), while in O. chelatus the cutting edge<tei:lb/>is simple (Pirlot, 1933).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Those genera with a strongly dentate incisor share a broadly similar facies and can be<tei:lb/>regarded as forming a natural group, with the omission of Valettia coheres which has a<tei:lb/>quite different morphology, especially in the structure of the mouthparts. The choice of<tei:lb/>names for these genera has turned out to be rather unfortunate since the genus name Valettia<tei:lb/>was used as the stem term for the later taxa Valettiopsis and Valettiella with which it does not<tei:lb/>have close affinity.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Valettiopsis and its allies live at moderate to great ocean depths and have as a conse-<tei:lb/>quence been infrequently recorded. In fact, all six species were first described from unique<tei:lb/>types, and only dentata and macrodactyla have since been redescribed from additional<tei:lb/>material (Barnard, 1967; Chevreux, 1935). Recent deep-water collections from the North<tei:lb/>Atlantic made during cruises of RRS Discovery and RRS Challenger have produced 5<tei:lb/>mature individuals of this rare lysianassid group, one belonging to Valettiopsis<tei:lb/>macrodactyla, the other 4 representing two species new to science. The combination of<tei:lb/>characters shared by the two new species puts them close to Valettiopsis, but with<tei:lb/>sufficient disparity in the configuration of the coxal plates, pereopodal bases, and<tei:lb/>mandibular palp armature to justify the erection of a new genus for which we propose the<tei:lb/>name Valettietta gen. nov. One existing species of Valettiopsis, namely V. anacantha from<tei:lb/>the Philippine Trench in the Pacific, is transferred to the new genus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Systematics<tei:lb/>Family LYSIANASSIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus VALETTIOPSIS Holmes, 1908</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REMARKS. Three of the Valettiopsis species names (dentata, anacantha, multidentata) have<tei:lb/>been corrected to give the epithets feminine terminations. Holmes (1908) derived the name<tei:lb/>Valettiopsis '. . .from Valettia, a genus of Amphipods, and 6vj/is, appearance'. Greek nouns<tei:lb/>ending in ops, genitive opsis, are feminine and adjectival specific names must agree in gender<tei:lb/>with their genus name (International Code of Zoological Nomenclature, Articles 1 1 (g) (i)<tei:lb/>(D,34(b)).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>VALETTIETTA gen. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. Body robust, compressed; pleosome well developed; urosome segment 1 with<tei:lb/>weak dorsal process, segment 3 broad and flattened dorsally with lateral margins raised.<tei:lb/>Antenna 1 and 2 elongate, slender, about equal length; peduncle articles 2-3 of antenna 1<tei:lb/>compressed, flagellum article 1 conjoint, accessory flagellum well developed, multi-<tei:lb/>articulate. Upper lip weakly notched. Lower lip without inner lobes, mandibular lobes<tei:lb/>prominent. Mandible with robustly dentate incisor, spine row strong, interspersed with<tei:lb/>plumose setae, molar large and triturative, palp attached level with molar, article 2 elongate<tei:lb/>with proximal and distal margin setose. Maxilla 1 inner plate densely setose along entire<tei:lb/>inner margin, palp robust, 2-articulate. Maxilla 2 inner and outer plates subequal length,<tei:lb/>inner plate with dense mediodistal and facial setae. Maxilliped basic; outer plate with short<tei:lb/>stout inner marginal spines grading distally to robust elongate spines. Coxal plates 1-4<tei:lb/>forming continuous series; plate 4 with deep posterior emargination. Coxal plate 5 anterior<tei:lb/>lobe not deeper than posterior lobe. Gnathopod 1 subchelate; palm oblique; gnathopod 2<tei:lb/>subchelate or simple. Pereopods 5-7 basis expanded with prolonged rounded posterodistal<tei:lb/>lobe. Uropods biramous, lanceolate, spinose. Telson triangular, deeply cleft. Branchial lobes<tei:lb/>with small accessory lobe close to base.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE SPECIES. Valettietta lobata sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ETYMOLOGY. The affinity of the new genus to Valettiopsis is recognized by adding the<tei:lb/>diminutive ending -etta to the common stem. Gender feminine.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REMARKS. The paratype male, body length 18 mm, has essentially similar morphology to the<tei:lb/>holotype; antenna 1 flagellum 25-articulate with especially obvious tooth on peduncle<tei:lb/>article 1; antenna 2 flagellum 36-articulate bearing calceoli. The structure of the calceolus<tei:lb/>(Fig. 10) was examined by SEM and was found to approximate to the basic lysianassid form<tei:lb/>described by Lincoln &amp; Hurley (1981), but with certain unique features that set it apart from<tei:lb/>all other lysianassid calceoli described to date. These include the 4 broad crescentic plates<tei:lb/>that form the distal element and the lack of a cuticular pit at the point of origin of the stalk<tei:lb/>from the flagellar article.</tei:p>

Gnathopod 2<tei:lb/>with characteristic twist in appendage between basis and merus that reverses the orientation<tei:lb/>of the distal articles.          <tei:p>DISCUSSION. The new genus Valettietta shares a general appearance and many special<tei:lb/>features with Valettiopsis, but is characterized by the following combination of characters:<tei:lb/>fully developed coxal plate 1; obtuse epimeral plate 2; produced posterodistal lobes on<tei:lb/>pereopods 5-7 bases; groups of proximal setae on article 2 of mandibular palp. The species<tei:lb/>anacantha described by Birstein &amp; Vinogradov from a deep-sea station in the Pacific south of<tei:lb/>the Philippines is transferred to Valettietta; it is very close to gracilis but can be distinguished<tei:lb/>by the rounded shape of coxal plate 4 and the presence of a short palm on the propodus of<tei:lb/>gnathopod2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Key to species of Valettiopsis and Valettietta gen. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 Coxal plate 1 reduced; urosome with strong acute tooth . . . (VALETTIOPSIS) 2<tei:lb/>Coxal plate 1 not reduced; urosome lacking strong acute tooth (VALETTIETTA gen. nov.) 4</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2. Pereon segments 5-7 and pleosome segments 1-3 dorsally dentate .... multidentata<tei:lb/>Pereon segments 5-7 and pleosome segments 1-3</tei:p>
         <tei:p>not dorsally dentate 3</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3. Gnathopod 2 propodus elongate, tapering dentata</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gnathopod 2 propodus stout, ovo-rectangular macrodactyla</tei:p>
         <tei:p>4. Gnathopod 2 simple, or with very small palm, propodus slender, tapering .... 5<tei:lb/>Gnathopod 2 subchelate, propodus not slender, ovo-rectangular .... lobata sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5. Gnathopod 2 palm oblique, coxal plate 4 distal margin convex anacantha</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gnathopod 2 lacking palm, coxal plate 4 distal margin straight .... gracilis sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Valettietta gracilis and V. anacantha can be regarded as vicarious species having disjunct<tei:lb/>distributions, one from the Atlantic Ocean and the other from the Pacific Ocean. The<tei:lb/>differences separating them, although considered valid at species level, are of a minor nature,<tei:lb/>and are much less marked than those separating either species from Valettietta lobata.<tei:lb/>Within Valettiopsis, the Pacific species dentata and the Atlantic macrodactyla form a similar<tei:lb/>species pair. Other Atlantic/Pacific species pairs are known; Paracallisoma alberti<tei:lb/>Chevreux, 1903 and P. coecum (Holmes, 1908), and Crybelocephalus birsteini Thurston,<tei:lb/>1976 and C. obensis Birstein &amp; Vinogradov, 1964. The separation of the Atlantic and Pacific<tei:lb/>elements of these species pairs may have occurred in the geologically recent past. All are<tei:lb/>meso- to abyssopelagic, and have been found in areas that were contiguous prior to the<tei:lb/>emergence of the Isthmus of Panama about 3-5 x 10 6 yearsB.P. (Keigwin, 1978).</tei:p>

         <tei:p/>
         <tei:p>Three new genera of misophrioid copepods from the<tei:lb/>near-bottom plankton community in the North<tei:lb/>Atlantic Ocean</tei:p>
         <tei:p>G. A. Boxshall</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London<tei:lb/>SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Only three species of the copepod order Misophrioida have been described, Misophria<tei:lb/>pallida Boeck 1864, Benthomisophria palliata Sars 1909 and B. cornuta Hulsemann &amp; Grice<tei:lb/>1964. Despite the small number of species the misophrioids have attracted interest because<tei:lb/>of the combination of characters that they exhibit, drawn from both the gymnoplean and<tei:lb/>podoplean lineages within the Copepoda. Attention has recently been drawn to the unique<tei:lb/>characters that misophrioids display; the possession of a carapace-like posterior extension of<tei:lb/>the cephalosome, the lack of a nauplius eye in a free living copepod, the distensibility of the<tei:lb/>gut and the retention of the antennary glands as the functional excretory organs of the adult<tei:lb/>(Boxshall, 1982). These characters, together with the abbreviated lecithotrophic nauplius<tei:lb/>phase of the life cycle, can be interpreted collectively as evidence of a bathypelagic origin of<tei:lb/>the Misophrioida. This interpretation is supported by the discovery of several new<tei:lb/>misophrioid taxa from the deep North Atlantic Ocean, which was reported at the First<tei:lb/>International Conference on Copepoda held at Amsterdam in August 1981 (Boxshall, in<tei:lb/>press).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A total of 37 misophrioids was taken in a single haul fished near the bottom in 3000 m of<tei:lb/>water to the south west of the Azores. Fourteen of these were B. cornuta, 8 were B. palliata<tei:lb/>and 15 represented previously undescribed taxa. Three new genera and species are here<tei:lb/>described on the basis of 13 of these specimens, the remaining 2 specimens being too badly<tei:lb/>damaged for description. The new genera are of great phylogenetic significance as they<tei:lb/>exhibit some very plesiomorphic characters which provide fresh insights on the nature of the<tei:lb/>appendages of the common ancestor of the Copepoda as a whole. The new records of B.<tei:lb/>cornuta and B. palliata further extend their known geographical ranges.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Family MISOPHRIIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus ARCHIMISOPHRIA nov.<tei:lb/>DIAGNOSIS. As for type species.<tei:lb/>TYPE SPECIES. Archimisophria discoveryi gen. et sp. nov.</tei:p>

Cone organs not observed but large mass of glandular tissue present on sides of<tei:lb/>cephalosome beneath usual location of cone organs. Urosome 6-segmented (Fig. 1C). Surface</tei:p>

         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. Holotype 9, 3 paratype cfd 1 , 19,2 Copepodid IV and 3 Copepodid III<tei:lb/>stages all from Discovery Stn 10379*37 (345T N 3255' W) in the North Atlantic to the<tei:lb/>southwest of the Azores. Collected in RMT1+8M net system fished 23 to 56 m off the<tei:lb/>bottom in a water depth of about 3000 m. BM(NH) Registration Nos Holotype 9 1982.128,<tei:lb/>paratype 9 1982.129, rfrf 1982.130-132,Co. IV 1982. 133-134 and Co. Ill 1982.135-137.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The new genus differs from all known misophrioids, including those described<tei:lb/>herein, in the possession of an anteriorly directed rostrum. In other genera the rostrum is<tei:lb/>either ventrally directed (Misophria and Misophriopsis gen. nov.) or postero ventral ly<tei:lb/>directed and fused to the labrum (Benthomisophria and Misophriella gen. nov.). Another<tei:lb/>remarkable feature of this genus is the large number of segments in the antennules of both<tei:lb/>sexes. The twenty-seven segments found in the female is the largest number recorded for any<tei:lb/>copepod, including the calanoids in which 25 is the largest number known. The<tei:lb/>phylogenetic significance of the multi-segmented antennules is discussed below.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The developmental stages of A. discovery! will not be described as only the third (Co. Ill)<tei:lb/>and fourth (Co. IV) copepodid stages have been found. As in other misophrioids (Boxshall &amp;<tei:lb/>Roe, 1980) the copepodid stages can be determined by the number of urosome somites, the<tei:lb/>Co. Ill having 3 and the Co. IV having 4. It is interesting to note that the segmentation of the<tei:lb/>antennule is complete (27 segments) at the Co. IV stage whereas in Benthomisophria palliata<tei:lb/>the complete complement of 1 8 segments is not achieved until the last moult into the adult.<tei:lb/>The third copepodid of A. discoveryi has a 24-segmented antennule.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The presence of a 3-segmented leg 5 in female and 4-segmented leg 5 in male A. discoveryi</tei:p>

         <tei:p>is the first documented example of sexual dimorphism in the fifth leg in misophrioids. In<tei:lb/>Misophria and Benthomisophria sexual dimorphism is restricted to the antennules and sixth<tei:lb/>legs. The presence of modified setae on the inner margin of the third exopod segment of leg 4<tei:lb/>in male A. discoveryi may also represent sexual dimorphism but this cannot be confirmed<tei:lb/>until more material is obtained, as the only 2 females in the present material had incomplete<tei:lb/>fourth legs.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REMARKS. The new genus differs from all known misophrioids, including those described<tei:lb/>herein, in the form of the maxillule (which has a 3 -segmented endopod, no outer lobe and an<tei:lb/>elongate exopod bearing only 4 setae), in the presence of only a single endite on the second<tei:lb/>segment of the maxilla and in the number of segments in the antennule. This genus exhibits a<tei:lb/>general reduction in the numbers of armature elements on most of the mouthparts, particu-<tei:lb/>larly on the maxillulary palp and the maxilla. In addition to these quantitative differences<tei:lb/>there are also qualitative differences, such as the spiniform nature of 4 setae on the proximal<tei:lb/>segments of the antennule and the presence of articulated setae on the maxilliped.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The armature elements of the fifth legs provide some indication of the homology of the<tei:lb/>segments. The second segment carries an outer seta at its distal angle and it is also much<tei:lb/>wider than the other segments. It probably represents the basis. Segment 1 therefore<tei:lb/>represents the coxa, and segments 3 and 4 the 2-segmented exopod. The holotype exhibits<tei:lb/>bilateral asymmetry in the armature of leg 5 but it is assumed that this is an aberrant con-<tei:lb/>dition and is not indicative of a true asymmetry as displayed by the fifth legs of many<tei:lb/>calanoids.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REMARKS. The new genus differs from all known misophrioids, and from all known<tei:lb/>podopleans, in the possession of a biramous fifth leg. It also differs from other misophrioid<tei:lb/>genera in the presence of a pair of large spinous processes on the posterior margin of the<tei:lb/>labrum. The arrangement of the mouthparts (Fig. 12) is very similar to that of<tei:lb/>Benthomisophria palliata. The antennae and mandibular palps are both reflexed so that their<tei:lb/>setae will sweep over the areas of cone organs located laterally on the cephalosome as in<tei:lb/>Benthomisophria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Key to genera and species of the Misophrioida</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Leg 5 uniramous, with 1 to 4 segments 3</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3. Leg 5 4-segmented, antennule with 19 segments (9) . . . Misophriellatetraspina<tei:lb/>Leg 5 with less than 4 segments, antennule with less than 1 9 segments (9) .... 4</tei:p>
         <tei:p>4. Leg 5 3-segmented; antennule with 1 3 segments (d) or 1 6 segments (9) Misophria pallida<tei:lb/>Leg 5 2-segmented, comprising a triangular proximal segment and short distal segment;</tei:p>
         <tei:p>antennule with 18 (9) or 16 (d 1 ) segments Benthomisophria palliata</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Leg 5 1 -segmented; antennule with 16 segments (9 &amp; rf) B.cornuta</tei:p>

         <tei:p>MISOPHRIOIDCOPEPODS 121</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discussion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>These three new genera exhibit between them an unusual array of plesiomorphic characters,<tei:lb/>many of which are present in a state approaching that attributed to the hypothetical ancestor<tei:lb/>of the Copepoda as a whole (see discussion in Boxshall et al., in press). The antennae, for<tei:lb/>example, are biramous with an 8-segmented exopod and a 3-segmented endopod in<tei:lb/>Archimisophria, and the mandibles have a well developed gnathobase plus a biramous palp<tei:lb/>with a 5 -segmented exopod and 2 -segmented endopod in Misophriopsis. The basic structure<tei:lb/>of both these limbs is the same as that proposed for the ancestral copepod. The detailed<tei:lb/>structure of the maxillule was not considered in the discussion reported by Boxshall et al. (in<tei:lb/>press) but in my opinion the misophrioid pattern of large gnathobase, 2 other endites, 1<tei:lb/>setose outer lobe, a 1 -segmented exopod and a 3-segmented endopod, with the first segment<tei:lb/>fused to the basis, may well be similar to that possessed by the ancestral copepod. The<tei:lb/>misophrioid maxilla comprises 6 segments, the first 2 each bear a pair of setose endites, the<tei:lb/>third a claw-like endite, and the fourth to sixth variable number of inner and distal setae.<tei:lb/>This is close to what may be considered to be the ancestral copepod pattern. The<tei:lb/>8-segmented maxilliped of Archimisophria consisting of a 3-segmented protopod and<tei:lb/>5-segmented endopod is also very similar to the 9-segmented basic copepod maxilliped<tei:lb/>favoured by Gurney (193 1) in his analysis of copepod appendages.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>All these misophrioid features closely approximate to those exhibited by the<tei:lb/>plesiomorphic calanoids. It is the common possession of these calanoid-like<tei:lb/>gnathostomatous mouthparts and the possession of a heart that indicates that the<tei:lb/>Misophrioida diverged from the podoplean lineage soon after its separation from the<tei:lb/>gymnoplean lineage. The discovery of a 27-segmented antennule in Archimisophria and of a<tei:lb/>biramous fifth leg in Misophriopsis clearly demonstrates that the Misophrioida has diverged<tei:lb/>less from the common ancestral stock of the Copepoda than any other podoplean group.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The possession of a biramous fifth leg is of great phylogenetic significance. The difference<tei:lb/>between the normal biramous fifth swimming leg of gymnopleans and the reduced<tei:lb/>uniramous fifth leg of podopleans led Giesbrecht (1899) to suggest the possibility that they<tei:lb/>are not homologous. Gurney (1931) rejected this and suggested that the typical uniramous<tei:lb/>leg of podopleans represents the exopod of an originally biramous limb. This interpretation<tei:lb/>has been widely adopted and the presence, in Misophriopsis, of a biramous fifth leg in which<tei:lb/>the endopod is reduced to a single segment bearing a single seta provides further<tei:lb/>confirmation. The fifth leg undergoes considerable reduction within the Misophrioida. In<tei:lb/>Misophria the endopod is represented by a single median seta on the distal margin of the<tei:lb/>unsegmented protopod, although the exopod is similar to that found in Archimisophria. In<tei:lb/>Benthomisophria cornuta the fifth leg is reduced to a single segment.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The 27-segmented antennule of female Archimisophria is of interest because of the con-<tei:lb/>siderable controversy that exists (see Boxshall et al, in press) concerning the nature of this<tei:lb/>limb in the ancestral copepod. Giesbrecht (1892 &amp; 1899) analysed the segmentation and<tei:lb/>armature of the antennules of many calanoid and other copepods in an attempt to reduce the<tei:lb/>antennule of all copepods to a common type. Giesbrecht's basic copepod antennule was<tei:lb/>25-segmented and by studying the arrangement of the armature elements he was able to<tei:lb/>determine which segments had fused in those forms with fewer segments. This basic limb<tei:lb/>closely resembles that ofCalanusfinmarchicusGunnerus, 1770 both in number of segments<tei:lb/>and in setation. The typical armature present on each antennulary segment is 2 setae and 1<tei:lb/>aesthetasc, at least in the female, although one or more of these elements is often lost, most<tei:lb/>commonly the aesthetasc. Even the arrangement of these 3 elements, which Giesbrecht<tei:lb/>called a 'trithek', follows a constant pattern. One seta, the proximal seta, is positioned about<tei:lb/>midway along the anterior margin of the segment whereas the other seta, the distal seta, and<tei:lb/>the aesthetasc are positioned close together at the distal angle of the anterior margin. The<tei:lb/>typical trithek may have been different for the male, because a proximal and a distal seta<tei:lb/>plus 2 distal aesthetascs are commonly found, as for example, in Eucalanus attenuatus<tei:lb/>Dana, 1849. Some of the more distal segments have modified tritheks. In female Calanus</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>122</tei:p>
         <tei:p>G. A. BOXSHALL</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>MISOPHRIOID COPEPODS 123</tei:p>
         <tei:p>finmarchicus segments 20 and 21 have no proximal seta, segments 22, 23 and 24 have no<tei:lb/>proximal seta but have instead a distal seta on the posterior margin, and the terminal<tei:lb/>segment 25 has an increased armature of up to 6 setae and an aesthetasc (see Table 1). As<tei:lb/>Gurney (193 1) noted, many calanoids possess 3 complete tritheks on the second segment and<tei:lb/>a single proximal seta plus a distal trithek on the first segment. He interpreted this as<tei:lb/>evidence that the second segment of calanoid antennules is derived from 3 fused segments<tei:lb/>and that the first segment may be derived from 2 fused segments. On the basis of this<tei:lb/>interpretation he postulated that the ancestral copepod antennule comprised 27 or possibly<tei:lb/>28 segments. The discovery of Archimisophria with its 27-segmented antennules provides a<tei:lb/>remarkable corroboration of Gurney 's hypothesis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Comparison between the antennules of female Calanus finmarchicus and Archimisophria<tei:lb/>discoveryi is made in Table 1. The precise correspondence in the position of typical tritheks,<tei:lb/>denoted by T or t, and of modified tritheks, denoted by D or d and P or p, in these two taxa is<tei:lb/>remarkable as they are drawn from the 2 main copepod lineages, Gymnoplea and Podoplea.<tei:lb/>It is possible from the comparison in Table 1 to identify a common pattern from which both<tei:lb/>may be derived by reduction. I suggest that this pattern may well represent that found in the<tei:lb/>common ancestor of the Copepoda.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Functional interpretation of the unique characters exhibited by misophrioids (Boxshall,<tei:lb/>1982 &amp; in press) suggests that the ancestral misophrioid stock became adapted to a<tei:lb/>bathypelagic existence and to gorging as a feeding strategy. The group appears to have<tei:lb/>radiated in the deep-sea near-bottom environment and it is probable that many new<tei:lb/>misophrioid taxa will be discovered as the near-bottom community is subject to more<tei:lb/>intense study. Despite their obvious specializations the misophrioids also retain many<tei:lb/>characters of the presumed ancestral copepod stock and it is clear that they diverged from the<tei:lb/>ancestral podoplean stock soon after it had attained its characteristic division into prosome<tei:lb/>and urosome.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Acknowledgements</tei:p>

I am also grateful to Dr Roger Lincoln for reading and commenting on<tei:lb/>the manuscript.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Larval development of British prawns and shrimps<tei:lb/>(Crustacea: Decapoda: Natantia) 4. Palaemon<tei:lb/>(Palaemon) serratus (Pennant, 1777) and<tei:lb/>functional morphology of swimming</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. A. Fincham</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London<tei:lb/>SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Palaemonid shrimps are widely distributed in the N.E. Atlantic being found in fresh water,<tei:lb/>in estuaries, intertidally and down to 40 m offshore, but their larvae are rare in the plankton.<tei:lb/>All decapod larvae have a natural tendency to sink (Foxon, 1934) and in order to maintain<tei:lb/>position at a particular depth (Savage, 1926) and perform daily vertical migrations (Russell,<tei:lb/>1925, 1927; Hardy &amp; Bainbridge, 1954), active upward swimming is necessary also. Foxon<tei:lb/>measured rates of movement in various decapods including pandalid carideans and several<tei:lb/>authors have noted the effect of light and gravity on the orientation and movement of<tei:lb/>decapod larvae (Sollaud, 1921;Gurney, 1942; Forward &amp;Cron in, 1978).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The aim of this paper is to review the larval development of Palaemon (Palaemon)<tei:lb/>serratus (Pennant, 1777) and report on morphological adaptations, and a mechanism using<tei:lb/>many-jointed plumose setae fringing the thoracic exopods, for larval swimming.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Materials and Methods<tei:lb/>Rearing</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ovigerous Palaemon (Palaemon) serratus were trawled from 12 m in April 1979 from<tei:lb/>Plymouth Sound, Devon (Grid reference: SX 475512). Similar rearing techniques to those<tei:lb/>reported previously (Fincham 1977, 1978, 1979) were used with the following modifications:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 . The controlled temperature room was at 1 4 C.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2. Antibiotics were used for the first three stages only (Fincham, 1979).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Larval material has been deposited in the Crustacea collection of the BM(NH), registration<tei:lb/>number 1982 : 186.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Telson morphology</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The telson was removed from larvae by a cut at the narrow junction with the abdomen,<tei:lb/>rinsed in distilled water, freeze dried, mounted end-on with Araldite on stubs, coated with<tei:lb/>gold and examined with a scanning electron microscope.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>In the following short descriptions of the key characters of the larval stages, setal counts<tei:lb/>have been omitted usually, but they are recorded in Table 1 .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Description of larval stages</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Key characters are printed in italic type and are useful for separating stages in British species.<tei:lb/>ZOEA 1 (Fig. 1)3-3 mm (3-2-3-5 mm)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Head (Figs la, b): eyes sessile.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Carapace (Figs la, b): without spines, rostrum straight or downcurved at tip, tapering</tei:p>
         <tei:p>distally, ventral margin with minute retrorse teeth distally, equal to, or greater than, length of</tei:p>
         <tei:p>peduncle of antenna 1 but not reaching to end of antenna I (excluding terminal aesthetascs</tei:p>
         <tei:p>and setae).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Antenna 1 (Fig. Ic): peduncle bearing single flagellar segment with three aesthetascs distally,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>usually two narrow and one wide, occasionally 1 narrow and 2 wide.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Antenna 2 (Fig. Id): exopodite as a broad lamina divided into 5 short segments distally, with</tei:p>
         <tei:p>9 + 2 plumose setae on inner and distal margins. Endopodite of one segment (0-67 length of</tei:p>
         <tei:p>exopodite), with terminal plumose seta and short spine.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Mandibles (Fig. le): asymmetrical.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Maxillipeds 1-3 (Figs Ih-j): with natatory exopodites.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pereiopods 1,2 (Figs Ik, 1): rudimentary, biramous.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pereiopods 3-5: absent.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Abdomen (Figs 1 a, b): somite 5 with posterior margin rounded, not produced into spines,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>somite six continuous with telson. No trace of pleopods.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Telson (Fig. Ip): fans out distally, posterior margin bears 7 + 7 plumose spines, with minute</tei:p>
         <tei:p>spines between four innermost spines.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ZOEA 2 (Fig. 2) 3-7 mm (3-5-3-9 mm)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Head (Figs 2a, b): eyes stalked.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Carapace (Figs 2a, b): one dorso-medial and a pair of supra-orbital spines all bent forward</tei:p>
         <tei:p>with small retrorse teeth, rostrum without teeth, downturnedat end to form small hook.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Antenna 1 (Fig. 2c): two peduncle segments, stylocerite forming on proximal external margin</tei:p>
         <tei:p>of first segment; single flagellar segment with four terminal aesthetascs, two wide and two</tei:p>
         <tei:p>narrow.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Antenna 2 (Fig. 2d): exopodite with 4 or 5 short segments distally.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pereiopods 1 , 2 (Figs 2k, 1): developed with natatory exopodite.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pereiopods 3, 4 (Figs 2m, n): rudimentary, biramous.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pereiopod 5 (Fig. 2o): rudimentary, uniramous.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Abdomen (Figs 2a, b): somite 5 with posterior margin produced into a pair of conspicuous</tei:p>
         <tei:p>spines, somite 6 continuous with telson.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Telson (Fig. 2p): developing uropods visible beneath exoskeleton alongside telson proper; in</tei:p>
         <tei:p>central group of small spines, one pair longer than others.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Discussion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Most of the specimens reared by Sollaud (1912) at the Marine Laboratory in Roscoff reached<tei:lb/>metamorphosis after 8 larval stages. But he found also '. . . quelques individus, en effet,<tei:lb/>peuvent presenter un stade IX supplementaire avant de se transformer. . .' He concluded,<tei:lb/>however, that the normal larval development for Palaemon serratus was eight stages<tei:lb/>(Sollaud, 1923). In the present work the differences between stages 8 and 9 were slight and<tei:lb/>confirm, therefore, the conclusion reached by Sollaud. The insertion of extra moults which<tei:lb/>probably represents an adaptation to sub-optimal conditions by prolonging larval existence<tei:lb/>is a recurrent feature of the development of Palaemoninae (Fincham, 1977, 1979). Sandifer<tei:lb/>and Smith (1979) indicated that in addition to being affected by environmental factors the<tei:lb/>tendency of individual palaemonid larvae to pass through a given number of larval stages<tei:lb/>may be inherited. They suggested also that variation in development may enhance the<tei:lb/>general advantages of an extended planktonic larval phase, a common feature of 'r'<tei:lb/>strategists. These include greater potential for dispersion and the ability to colonize new<tei:lb/>habitats quickly if favourable conditions occur. A variable planktonic existence may<tei:lb/>produce a wider spread of individuals of a given brood and average the risks of survival. At<tei:lb/>the population level Sandifer and Smith emphasize that early metamorphosis will enhance<tei:lb/>the possibility of gene flow between populations.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Many environmental factors affect development including photoperiodicity. Wickins<tei:lb/>(1972) reported work on larval Palaemon serratus in which growth was improved and<tei:lb/>metamorphosis reached sooner in those reared in continuous light. Eight hours dark and 16<tei:lb/>hours light produced improved growth compared with 8 hours light and 16 hours dark;<tei:lb/>continuous darkness produced slowest growth and development. Dalley (1979) working with<tei:lb/>Palaemon elegans concluded that greatly increased mortality during larval development in<tei:lb/>non-circadian light regimes was due to desynchronization of the circadian rhythms of<tei:lb/>metabolic processes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>One feature commonly found in exuviae of the various larval stages are thin strands<tei:lb/>extending from the bases of sensory hairs (see Figs 17d, e). In a paper on the ultrastructure of<tei:lb/>the antennal sensilla of the shrimp Acetes Ball &amp; Cowan (1977) describe dense strands of<tei:lb/>unknown composition crossing the base of their type 1 seta, which they consider<tei:lb/>uninnervated. Tracts leading from the bases of their other four types of seta contain axons.<tei:lb/>The exact nature of the strands in the exuviae of Palaemon serratus is not clear and will be<tei:lb/>examined further.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Swimming: adaptations and a mechanism</tei:p>
         <tei:p>When palaemonid larvae first hatch they usually swim upside down and telson first. At rest<tei:lb/>the larvae tend to sink and there are several adaptations which slow the rate of descent. At<tei:lb/>either end of the body plumose setae or spines increase drag. Fringing plumose setae<tei:lb/>effectively double the area of the broad exopodite (scaphocerite) of antenna 2 at the anterior<tei:lb/>end of the body (Figs la, 2a, 3a, 5a, 7a, lOa, 13a, 16a, 20a). These antennal setae are<tei:lb/>thickened at the base (Fig. 4c) and their rigidity increases drag when the exopodites are<tei:lb/>spread.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>At the posterior end the telson bears stout spines which are ornamented with rows of small<tei:lb/>spines and are also plumose (Figs 27a-d). The function of the ornamentation is uncertain but<tei:lb/>might further slow the rate of sinking. Body size increases as larval development progresses<tei:lb/>and the importance of even the large spines on the telson for increasing drag, diminishes<tei:lb/>when the sixth abdominal segment develops its appendages. The exopodites of these uropods</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>LARVAL DEVELOPMENT OF P. (P.) SERRA TVS \ 59</tei:p>
         <tei:p>appear at zoea 3 (Fig. 41) and endopodites at zoea 4 (Fig. 6k) and are broad and flat and<tei:lb/>fringed with plumose setae. The spreading of these uropods slows the rate of sinking during<tei:lb/>periods when the larva is not swimming.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The telson, however, retains its important function as a stabilizing hydrofoil throughout<tei:lb/>larval life and is supplied with powerful muscles that enable it to assist in orientation control.<tei:lb/>Its gradual transformation from a triangular shape at zoea 1 with a 7 + 7 spine formula (Figs<tei:lb/>1m, 27a-d) to the narrow shape at the last larval stage with spine formula 4 + 4 and three<tei:lb/>pairs of lateral spines (Fig. 23h) accompanies the gradual development of more thoracic<tei:lb/>natatory exopodites.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the present rearing programme food is supplied to the larvae in the form of Anemia<tei:lb/>nauplii. In the wild it is likely that copepods and other small planktonic organisms form the<tei:lb/>main source of food (Sollaud used copepods in his rearing work at Roscoff). The pursuit of<tei:lb/>prey- Anemia or copepods - necessitates accurate orientation and direction control. The<tei:lb/>propulsive locomotory force is provided by the natatory exopodites developed sequentially.<tei:lb/>Zoea 1 has only three natatory exopodites - those of the maxillipeds-but biramous<tei:lb/>pereiopods are developed later and are also integrated into a regular beating pattern of the<tei:lb/>limbs. Pereiopod 5 is in fact developed by stage 4 before pereiopod 4 in Palaemon serratus<tei:lb/>but has no exopodite and is therefore not involved in larval swimming. The effective area of<tei:lb/>the propulsive exopodites is extended by the fringing plumose setae. Preliminary analysis of<tei:lb/>the swimming action from cine film shows that there is a power stroke and a recovery stroke.<tei:lb/>The morphological adaptations of these plumose setae and their role in swimming behaviour<tei:lb/>was determined following the routine examination by light microscopy of hundreds of<tei:lb/>moults during the course of the rearing programme.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The shaft of the plumose setae fringing the natatory exopodites is a differentially thickened<tei:lb/>cylinder with flexible cuticular hinges at intervals along its length (Fig. 28a). The hinges only<tei:lb/>permit bending towards the unthickened side of the shaft. During the propulsive power<tei:lb/>stroke the setae remain straight with the two rows of thin, lateral setules set at an obtuse angle<tei:lb/>to the shaft, thus providing maximum surface area and purchase in the water. On the<tei:lb/>recovery stroke the flexible exopodite bends and the marginal plumose setae fold back along<tei:lb/>the many hinge lines with their thin side branches streaming out behind. This offers the least<tei:lb/>possible resistance by the exopodite to the water and repositions the limb ready to begin the<tei:lb/>next power stroke.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The rhythmic beating of the six pairs of setose thoracic exopodites in the three larval<tei:lb/>stages, and swimmerets or pleopods in the post larvae of lobsters, has been analysed from<tei:lb/>cine film by Neil et al. (1976), Macmillan et al. (1976) and Laverack et al. (1976). No<tei:lb/>comparable study has been made for a caridean with regular sequential addition of limbs (in<tei:lb/>lobsters all limbs are present on hatching). The rarity of palaemonid larvae in the plankton<tei:lb/>remains an enigma especially as they are apparently so well adapted for swimming. Detailed<tei:lb/>analyses of cine film of swimming in larval palaemonids, together with plankton sampling<tei:lb/>using a static bottom net and experimental work on the periodicity of larval swimming, all of<tei:lb/>which are in progress, should shed some light on the problem.</tei:p>
