      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>The larval development of the Angular Crab,<tei:lb/>Goneplax rhomboides (Linnaeus) (Decapoda:<tei:lb/>Brachyura)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>R. W. Ingle &amp; Paul F. Clark</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London<tei:lb/>SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Angular Crab, Goneplax rhomboides (Linnaeus) occurs in the Eastern Atlantic Ocean<tei:lb/>and Mediterranean Sea. It has been reported from the northern Irish Sea to as far south as the<tei:lb/>Cape region of South Africa (see Barnard, 1950 : 285; Ingle, 1980 : 109); the species occurs<tei:lb/>from the lower shore to depths of about 700 m.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Capart (1951 : 1 69) discussed regional variation of this species with respect to the degree of<tei:lb/>development of the posterior pair of carapace anterio-lateral teeth and differences in shapes<tei:lb/>of the male first pleopod. He suggested two 'varieties' may exist. Specimens attributed to G.<tei:lb/>rhomboides (L.) have a very rudimentary pair of posterior teeth and are reported only from<tei:lb/>the Mediterranean Sea, coast of Mauritania and the Canary Islands whereas material<tei:lb/>assigned to G. angulata (Pennant) has been recorded from various Atlantic Ocean localities<tei:lb/>(see Manning &amp; Holthuis, 1981 : 164).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The larval stages of G. rhomboides have been described previously (see larval and<tei:lb/>post-larval references below), but these accounts are generally inadequate for use in detailed<tei:lb/>comparative studies of larval morphology. The recent rearing of G. rhomboides to third crab<tei:lb/>stage has provided sufficient material for redescribing the complete larval development of<tei:lb/>this species and an account is given here of the four zoeal and megalop stage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Materials and Methods</tei:p>
         <tei:p>After several unsuccessful trawling attempts, SCUBA diving was used to collect ovigerous<tei:lb/>material. On the advice of Alan Howard (MAFF) members of the BM(NH) Diving Unit<tei:lb/>searched the sandy-mud substrate to a depth of 18m off Shoalstone Point (SX937568),<tei:lb/>Brixham, Devon. An ovigerous crab was collected on 10 July 1981 and transported to the<tei:lb/>rearing laboratory of the Crustacea Section, BM(NH). The larvae were reared using methods<tei:lb/>described by Rice &amp; Ingle (1975) and Ingle &amp; Clark (1977), except that sea water was<tei:lb/>untreated. Drawings and measurements were made with the aid of a camera lucida.<tei:lb/>Measurements are as follows: total lengths of zoeae (T.T.) measured from tip of dorsal to tip<tei:lb/>of rostral spine and carapace length (C.L.) measured from between eyes to posterio-lateral<tei:lb/>margin of carapace (for zoeae) and from rostral tip for megalop. All material was fixed in<tei:lb/>Steedman's preservative (Steedman, 1976 : 148) and later transferred to 70% ethanol<tei:lb/>alcohol. The female and larval stages are deposited in the collections of the BM(NH),<tei:lb/>accession numbers 1981 : 540 &amp; 1982 : 55 respectively.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Descriptions</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Goneplax rhomboides (Linnaeus, 1 758)</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mm. not. Hist. (Zool.) 44(2): 1 63-1 77 Issued 24 February 1983</tei:p>

         <tei:p>SECOND ZOEA</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Remarks</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The present laboratory reared material of G. rhomboides differs in a number of features from<tei:lb/>the accounts given by Bourdil Ion-Casanova (1960) and Rice &amp; Williamson (1977). These<tei:lb/>differences are tabulated below.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bourdillon-Casanova</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ZOEA!</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Denticles on proximal part of posterio-lateral<tei:lb/>margin of abdominal segments very pronounced.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEGALOP</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Disto-lateral margins of rostrum very acute.<tei:lb/>Protogastric spines of carapace stout and straight.<tei:lb/>A pair of widely spaced tubercles on meta-<tei:lb/>branchial-intestinal regions.<tei:lb/>Groups of setae on posterior region of carapace</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Exopod of uropod with 8-9 setae</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Rice &amp; Williamson</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ZOEA III<tei:lb/>Antennal exopod with a single mid-point seta.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Scaphognathite of maxilla with 1 8-19 setae.<tei:lb/>Endopod of 2nd maxilliped with 1,1,5 setae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ZOEAE 1-IV</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Exopod terminal segment of 2nd maxilliped with<tei:lb/>4, 7,9,11 setae in respective stages.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Present material</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ZOEA I</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Denticles on proximal part of posterio-lateral<tei:lb/>margin of abdominal segments very minute.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEGALOP</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Disto-lateral margins of rostrum not acute.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Protogastric spines of carapace thin and curved.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A pair of tubercles placed near to median line on</tei:p>
         <tei:p>cardiac region.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Without groups of setae on posterior region of</tei:p>
         <tei:p>carapace.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Exopod of uropod with 7-8 setae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ZOEA III</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Antennal exopod with more than one seta/spinule<tei:lb/>at mid-point.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Scaphognathite of maxilla with 20 setae.<tei:lb/>Endopod of 2nd maxilliped with 1,1,6 setae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ZOEAE I-IV</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Exopod terminal segment of 2nd maxilliped with<tei:lb/>4, 7, 8, 10-1 1 setae in respective stages.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With the exception of Geryon tridens (Kroyer), the zoeae of Goneplax rhomboides can<tei:lb/>be distinguished from those of other known brachyrhynchs occurring in the N.E. Atlantic sea<tei:lb/>area (see Ingle, 1980) by the following combined features: (1) A pair of small but prominent<tei:lb/>dorso-lateral processes on the 4th segment of the abdomen and sometimes a minute pair on<tei:lb/>the 5th segment in the 3rd and 4th stages. (2) The antennal exopod with spinules and setae<tei:lb/>sub-terminally placed. Features separating zoeae of G. rhomboides from those of Geryon<tei:lb/>tridens were tabulated by Ingle (1979 : 229). The following amendments must now be made<tei:lb/>to this table with respect to G. rhomboides: (1) The antennal exopod-the spinous process is<tei:lb/>longer than the exopod in all stages. (2) Maxilla of ZHI-scaphognathite with a maximum of<tei:lb/>20 setae on margin. (3) 1st maxilliped-endopod setae of ZIII, G. tridens 2, 2, 1, 2, 5+ 1 and<tei:lb/>G. rhomboides 3, 2,1,2,5+1 respectively.</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>The larval and first crab stages of three Inachus<tei:lb/>species (Crustacea: Decapoda: Majidae); a<tei:lb/>morphological and statistical analysis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Paul F. Clark</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London<tei:lb/>SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Several authors have acknowledged difficulties in distinguishing between congeneric<tei:lb/>brachyuran crab- larvae (see Lebour, 1928 : 546; Hartnoll, 1961 : 181; Christiansen, 1969;<tei:lb/>Rice &amp; Ingle, \915a &amp; 19756; Ingle, 1982). These observations were based on a limited<tei:lb/>amount of material that did not permit statistical analysis of larval characters. Rearing large<tei:lb/>numbers of crab larvae (see Ingle &amp; Clark, 1977) provided sufficient material for this<tei:lb/>statistical study. A multivariate technique was used to examine the larvae and first crab stage<tei:lb/>of three species of spider crabs belonging to the genus Inachus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Material &amp; Methods</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Berried females of Inachus dorsettensis and /. phalangium were collected by trawl from<tei:lb/>localities off Port Erin, Isle of Man, and Plymouth and /. leptochirus was trawled from<tei:lb/>Modiolus beds four miles south of Spanish Head, Isle of Man. The adult females, together<tei:lb/>with the larvae used in this study are deposited in the BM(NH). Larvae were reared using<tei:lb/>methods described by Ingle &amp; Clark (1977), then fixed and preserved in 80% alcohol. Twenty<tei:lb/>specimens of each stage were dissected and mounted as permanent slide preparations in<tei:lb/>lignin pink/poly vinyl lactophenol. For the multivariate study 43, 142 &amp; 178 characters were<tei:lb/>used for zoeal, megalop and first crab stages respectively. The majority of these characters<tei:lb/>are meristic, e.g. setal counts on appendages, but a few were present or absent scores.<tei:lb/>Accurate measurements of spines and carapace dimensions, as well as carapace setal counts<tei:lb/>of megalops and crab stages proved to be impracticable and were not used. Setal counts were<tei:lb/>scored for one side of the body although on occasions it was necessary to combine parts of<tei:lb/>both left and right appendages to form a complete score. The data was subjected to Principal<tei:lb/>co-ordinate analysis according to Gower (1966). This method summarizes similarities<tei:lb/>between OTUs as a 2-dimensional plot. The computation was carried out using a varian V<tei:lb/>72 computer. Each larval stage was analysed separately to avoid major differences between<tei:lb/>stages swamping any specific variation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Results<tei:lb/>Statistical Analysis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>First and second principal co-ordinates were plotted for zoea II, megalops and first crab stage<tei:lb/>of each species. The zoea I stage data was not computed because only two characters were<tei:lb/>considered significant on inspection. The OTUs in zoea II (Fig. 1) can be separated into two<tei:lb/>groups, /. leptochirus and /. dorsettensis/I. phalangium whereas in both megalops (Fig. 2)<tei:lb/>and first crab stage (Fig. 3) they are clearly separated into three groups which correspond to<tei:lb/>the three species.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Fig. 2 A plot of the 1 st &amp; 2nd Principal co-ordinates of megalops using 68 variates. OTUs 1-20 /.<tei:lb/>leptochirus, 21-40 /. dorsettensis, 41-60 /. phalangium. These OTUs fall into 3 distinct groups<tei:lb/>which correspond to the three British Inachus species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The second zoeal stages can also be split into the same two groups (Fig. 1). The three<tei:lb/>characters showing variation were the number of terminal aesthetascs on the antennule, the<tei:lb/>numbers of setae on the margin of the maxillary endopod and on the posterio-dorsal margin<tei:lb/>of the first abdominal somite. The scores of these three characters are shown respectively on<tei:lb/>Fig. 1 in bold type, adjacent to their respective OTUs. Separation of the two groups shown by<tei:lb/>the dotted line is determined only by one character, the number of posterio-dorsal marginal<tei:lb/>setae on the first abdominal somite. /. leptochirus has 2 setae (Fig. 4c) whereas /. dorsettensis<tei:lb/>and /. phalangium have none (Fig. 4d).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The megalops can be divided into three groups which correspond to the three species using<tei:lb/>only 68 out of the original 142 characters (Fig. 2). Group separation remains constant how-<tei:lb/>ever, if only nine characters are used.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>By using combinations of the means of these nine characters it seems possible to separate<tei:lb/>the megalops of the three species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Nevertheless, their overlapping distribution (Table 2) makes separation very difficult in<tei:lb/>practice as no single character separates all three species. For example, OTU 21 (I.<tei:lb/>dorsettensis) is grouped with OTUs 52 and 53 (I. phalangium) on its overall similarity as<tei:lb/>they have identical scores for all nine characters. Overlapping ranges of variation in numbers<tei:lb/>of setae makes it impossible to differentiate all three species with absolute confidence at the</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Morphology</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The general morphology of/, dorsettensis was described and illustrated by Ingle (1977).<tei:lb/>Most of the setal counts fall within the variation recorded during the present study. This<tei:lb/>indicates little or no temporal variation. Some of the discrepancies may be due to the<tei:lb/>difficulty of classifying and objectively defining elements, as between a seta and a spine,<tei:lb/>when the structures grade one into the other (Gurney, 1931 : 38). However, some disparity<tei:lb/>between Ingle's study and the present work could not be accounted for.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discussion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Williamson (1965 : 390) listed the presence of a seta on the outer margin of the maxillule<tei:lb/>basal endite as one often characters for separating brachyuran larvae from anomuran larvae.<tei:lb/>In previous descriptions of zoea II in majids this character is shown as present, for example,<tei:lb/>Ingle (1977) records this seta as present in zoae II and megalops of Inachus reared from<tei:lb/>Plymouth material this was confirmed by re-examining Ingle's material and by rearing<tei:lb/>fresh material from the Plymouth area. In this study the seta was absent from zoea II of/.<tei:lb/>dorsettensis reared from the Isle of Man and from all three megalops.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lebour* (1928) suggested that larvae of the three Inachus species could be separated on<tei:lb/>size, chromatophore patterns and length of dorsal spines, but none of these claims could be<tei:lb/>verified. Only the megalops and first crab stage of/, leptochirus in the present study proved to<tei:lb/>be larger than those of the other two species. Larval inachinids have apomorphic zoeal<tei:lb/>characters which were listed by Rice (1980 : 307), to which can now be added the absence of</tei:p>
         <tei:p>*Lebour's material is no longer extant; Ingle, pers. comm.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>

         <tei:p>STATISTICAL ANALYSIS OF CRAB LARVAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus INACHUS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>189</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus MACROPODIA</tei:p>
         <tei:p>group 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>I. leptochirus<tei:lb/>I. thoracicus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>group 2</tei:p>
         <tei:p>I. dorsettensis<tei:lb/>I. phalangium</tei:p>
         <tei:p>M. tenuirostris</tei:p>
         <tei:p>M. rostrata</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2 setae present<tei:lb/>on first<tei:lb/>abdominal somite</tei:p>
         <tei:p>First abdominal somite<tei:lb/>without setae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Zoea I without a<tei:lb/>seta on basis of<tei:lb/>2nd maxilliped</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Distal endopod<tei:lb/>segment of 2nd<tei:lb/>maxilliped with<tei:lb/>3 terminal setae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Distal endopod segment of 2nd maxilliped<tei:lb/>with h setae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 seta present on basis of 2nd maxilliped in zoea I<tei:lb/>and absent in zoea II.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Zoea II with mandible palp and antennule endopod absent.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 6 Cladogram of known Inachus &amp; Macropodia larval descriptions.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>a mandibular palp and endopod bud on the antennule of zoea II. Similarly, when compared<tei:lb/>with other majid larva characters the loss of paired dorsal setae on the first abdominal somite<tei:lb/>and the absence of setae on the basis of the 2nd maxilliped in /. dorsettensis and /.<tei:lb/>phalangium, can be considered as derived traits. The present study failed to reveal characters<tei:lb/>which separate the larvae of/, dorsettensis from /. phalangium, but demonstrated that the<tei:lb/>larvae of/, leptochirus can be easily recognized.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Adult males of/, leptochirus share one important feature with two other species of Inachus<tei:lb/>(i.e. /. thoracicus &amp; I. aquiarii) in having a sternal callosity, a character that is absent in<tei:lb/>males of/, dorsettensis, I. phalangium and /. communissimus. Such a separation of Inachus<tei:lb/>species into two groups, those with and those without a sternal callosity, is supported by the<tei:lb/>present study. Heegaard (1963) studied the zoeae of/, thoracicus and clearly figures two setae<tei:lb/>on the first abdominal somite (p. 475, Fig. 83), but not a seta on the basis of the 2nd<tei:lb/>maxilliped (p. 475, Fig. 82). Unfortunately Heegaard's material is no longer extant.<tei:lb/>Re-examination of /. thoracicus zoeae may well show that they are inseparable from /.<tei:lb/>leptochirus, adding support to the suggestion that there are two natural groups in the genus<tei:lb/>Inachus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Present larval evidence supports the view that Inachus and Macropodia are the most<tei:lb/>derived of all majids since they show the greatest reduction in numbers of setae; considered<tei:lb/>by Rice (1980) to be the derived condition. A suggested phylogeny of well documented<tei:lb/>larvae from the genera Inachus and Macropodia is shown in Fig. 6.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Setal studies of other brachyuran genera have shown that the larvae of species accepted as</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>190 P. F. CLARK</tei:p>
         <tei:p>closely related are not usually separable on quantitative characters. Therefore meristic setal<tei:lb/>incongruities within genera, as shown here for Inachus, may be the only morphological<tei:lb/>evidence of phylogenetic non-homogeniety.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This project formed part of an M.Sc. degree in Modern Taxonomy. All relevant data and<tei:lb/>larval figures were deposited in the Crustacea Section, BM(NH) and the Library of the<tei:lb/>Polytechnic of Central London.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>British Museum (Natural History)<tei:lb/>British Marine Amphipoda: Gammaridea</tei:p>
         <tei:p>by R. J. Lincoln</tei:p>
         <tei:p>658pp 2,300 figures 4to hard bound<tei:lb/>ISBN 565 00818 50.00</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Amphipods are both numerous and diverse in numbers of genera and species in<tei:lb/>British coastal waters, but in the absence of any form of modern systematic synopsis<tei:lb/>or key this group of crustaceans has acquired the reputation of being notoriously<tei:lb/>difficult to identify. This monograph, which is the first comprehensive and illustrated<tei:lb/>text on British gammaridean amphipods to be published in more than a century,<tei:lb/>should go a long way towards solving the problem.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The systematic section of the book contains descriptions and figures of all 271 species<tei:lb/>of marine and brackish water amphipods, in 123 genera and 36 families, recorded<tei:lb/>from British coasts and the adjacent continental shelf to a depth of 200 metres. Keys<tei:lb/>are provided at all levels, as well as relevant synonymies and diagnoses of genera and<tei:lb/>families. The text is illustrated with about 2,300 separate figures which have been<tei:lb/>drawn by the author from Museum and other material, in many cases with reference<tei:lb/>to type specimens.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The work has been carefully edited to bring corresponding descriptions, keys and<tei:lb/>figures into close proximity within the text. The systematic section is supported by<tei:lb/>chapters dealing with morphology, systematics, geographical distribution, biology<tei:lb/>and ecology, the latter being presented in the form of an annotated subject index of<tei:lb/>research literature. Finally, there is an extensive bibliography of about 1,200<tei:lb/>references that includes most of the British marine amphipod literature published to<tei:lb/>date.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>The Bulletin of the British Museum (Natural History), instituted in 1949, is issued in four<tei:lb/>scientific series, Botany, Entomology, Geology (incorporating Mineralogy) and Zoology, and<tei:lb/>an Historical series.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Papers in the Bulletin are primarily the results of research carried out on the unique and<tei:lb/>ever-growing collections of the Museum, both by the scientific staff of the Museum and by<tei:lb/>specialists from elsewhere who make use of the Museum's resources. Many of the papers are<tei:lb/>works of reference that will remain indispensable for years to come.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parts are published at irregular intervals as they become ready, each is complete in itself,<tei:lb/>available separately, and individually priced. Volumes contain about 300 pages and several<tei:lb/>volumes may appear within a calendar year. Subscriptions may be placed for one or more of<tei:lb/>the series on either an Annual or Per Volume basis. Prices vary according to the contents of<tei:lb/>the individual parts. Orders and enquiries should be sent to:</tei:p>

         <tei:p>A review of the Euplotidae (Hypotrichida,<tei:lb/>Ciliophora)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Colin R. Curds &amp; Irene C. H. Wu</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Zoology Department, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London<tei:lb/>SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Contents</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction<tei:lb/>Key to Genera .<tei:lb/>Genus Discocephalus<tei:lb/>Genus Diophrys<tei:lb/>Genus Uronychia .<tei:lb/>Genus Certesia .<tei:lb/>Genus Gastrocirrhus<tei:lb/>Genus Euplotaspis .<tei:lb/>Genus Euplotidium .<tei:lb/>Genus Paraeuplotes .<tei:lb/>Genus Swedmarkia .<tei:lb/>Genus Gruberella<tei:lb/>Genus Cyatharoides.<tei:lb/>References .<tei:lb/>Index .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>191<tei:lb/>192<tei:lb/>193<tei:lb/>197</tei:p>
         <tei:p>215<tei:lb/>227<tei:lb/>228<tei:lb/>232<tei:lb/>234<tei:lb/>239<tei:lb/>240<tei:lb/>242<tei:lb/>243<tei:lb/>244<tei:lb/>247</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Guides to the species of two genera of Euplotid ciliates, Euplotes Ehrenberg in Hemprich<tei:lb/>and Ehrenberg, 1831 and Aspidisca Ehrenberg, 1830, have been published in recent years<tei:lb/>(Curds, 1975; Wu &amp; Curds, 1979). The other genera in the family Euplotidae Ehrenberg,<tei:lb/>1838 contain fewer species but there are still identification problems in some. The present<tei:lb/>work is intended to aid the specific identification of the remaining nine genera which Borror<tei:lb/>(1972) grouped together into the Euplotidae, another more recently described genus and one<tei:lb/>other addition. Although four of these genera only contain single species it was thought<tei:lb/>worthwhile to include them for completeness. The genera included here are in chronological<tei:lb/>order of authority. Discocephalus Ehrenberg in Hemprich and Ehrenberg, 1828; Diophrys<tei:lb/>Dujardin, 1841; Uronychia Stein, 1859; Certesia Fabre-Domergue, 1885; Gastrocirrhus<tei:lb/>Lepsi, 1928; Euplotaspis Chatton and Seguela, 1936; Euplotidium Noland, 1937;<tei:lb/>Paraeuplotes Wichterman, 1942; Swedmarkia Dragesco, 1954; Gruberella Corliss, 1960 and<tei:lb/>Cyatharoides Tuffrau, 1975.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Members of the Euplotidae are hypotrichs with the characteristically prominent adoral<tei:lb/>zone of membranelles (AZM) arranged at the anterior left of the ventral surface bordering a<tei:lb/>wide peristome. In certain genera the AZM may continue over the apical end of the body<tei:lb/>onto the dorsal surface. Unfortunately there is no set of characters which will include all of<tei:lb/>Borror's (1972) genera into the family. This could well indicate that it is not a natural family.<tei:lb/>The cirri on the ventral surface are arranged in distinct groups but they are not the only<tei:lb/>hypotrichs to have this feature. For example, the Oxytrichidae Ehrenberg, 1838 also have<tei:lb/>grouped cirri, and Borror (1972) distinguished them from the Euplotidae by their possession<tei:lb/>of only a few left marginal and no right marginal cirri, even so there are several exceptions to</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. nat. Hist. (Zool.)44(3): 191-247</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Issued 31 March 1983</tei:p>
         <tei:p>191</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>192 C. R. CURDS &amp; I.C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>this rule. Furthermore, while right caudal cirri are present in Diophrys, Discocephalus,<tei:lb/>Euplotes and Uronychia, they are absent in others. However, transverse cirri are present in<tei:lb/>all but one Euplotid genus. With this state of affairs, perhaps it is not surprising that there is<tei:lb/>some disagreement in the literature as to what constitutes the family Euplotidae. Borror<tei:lb/>(1972) included Aspidisca in the family but Stein (1859a), Biitschli (1889), Kahl (1932),<tei:lb/>Faure-Fremiet (1961) and Corliss (1961, 1977, 1979) all maintain it in a separate family the<tei:lb/>Aspidiscidae Ehrenberg, 1838. Corliss (1979) included four genera in the family<tei:lb/>Aspidiscidae; Aspidisca, Euplotaspis, Onychaspis Stein, 1859 and Paraeuplotes (as an<tei:lb/>incertae sedis). The genus Onychaspis differs from Aspidisca only by the former's possession<tei:lb/>of a larger number of transverse cirri and most authors consider it to be a synonym of<tei:lb/>Aspidisca (Borror, 1972; Wu and Curds, 1979). Indeed, Corliss (1979) indicated this<tei:lb/>possibility in his classification. One of the features of the Aspidiscidae is the great reduction<tei:lb/>of oral membranelles, however this description would not fit the AZM of Euplotaspis or<tei:lb/>Paraeuplotes. Corliss (1979) also placed three of the genera being considered here<tei:lb/>(Cirrhogaster Ozaki and Yagui, 1941; Euplotidium and Gastrocirrhus) into the family<tei:lb/>Gastrocirrhidae Faure-Fremiet, 1961 . Here the single species genus Cirrhogaster is regarded<tei:lb/>as a member of the genus Gastrocirrhus as it only differs slightly in cirral number. Borror<tei:lb/>(1972) included Gastrocirrhus in the Euplotidae but noted that its position in that family was<tei:lb/>provisional.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is clear from this brief synopsis that the position of these genera within three or a single<tei:lb/>family group is still in a state of flux and to a large extent a matter of conjecture. It is not<tei:lb/>the purpose of the present paper to attempt to assign the genera to any old, current or new<tei:lb/>classification scheme; more data, particularly concerning their comparative morphogenesis,<tei:lb/>are required before anything useful can be suggested. The aim here is to aid the marine<tei:lb/>ecologist and protozoologist to identify the species of those genera which conveniently fall<tei:lb/>within the single family Euplotidae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Key to Genera</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 AZM conspicuous, in single anterior part 2</tei:p>
         <tei:p>AZM inconspicuous, in 2 parts ASPIDISCA (see Wu&amp; Curds, 1979)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2 Without frontoventral cirri, caudals never extremely large . . . GRUBERELLA(p.242)<tei:lb/>With frontoventral cirri (when frontoventrals reduced and inconspicuous then caudals</tei:p>
         <tei:p>usually very large)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3 With caudal cirri 4</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Without caudal cirri 11</tei:p>
         <tei:p>4 With marginal cirri, caudals sometimes very large 5</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Without marginals, caudals present but never very large</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5 Caudals very large and prominent 6</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Caudals weak EUPLOTES (see Curds, 1975)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>6 5- 10 conspicuous frontoventral cirri present, moves forwards . . DIOPHRYS (p. 197)<tei:lb/>3 inconspicuous cirri present, moves backwards URONYCHIA(p.2l5)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 Elongate with discoid 'head' region DISCOCEPHALUS (p. 193)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ovoid to elongate, when elongate never with discoid 'head' region 8</tei:p>
         <tei:p>8 Ovoid, dorsoventrally flattened 9</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Elongate, rounded in section, often cup-shaped with funnel-shaped peristome opening</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE 193</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus DISCOCEPHALUS Ehrenberg, 1 828</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The genus Discocephalus was erected by Ehrenberg in Hemprich and Ehrenberg (1828) and<tei:lb/>although his description and illustration of the type species D. rotatorius were crude, it was<tei:lb/>clear that the organism had a distinctive discoid 'head' formed by a constriction at the<tei:lb/>anterior end of the elongate oval body. Butschli (1889) expressed some doubt concerning the<tei:lb/>observations of Ehrenberg (1831) but it was not until Kahl (1932), that Discocephalus was<tei:lb/>adequately described. The latter description concerned an organism which Kahl (1932)<tei:lb/>considered to be D. rotatorius Ehrenberg and of which he thought Polycoccon octangularis<tei:lb/>Sauerbrey, 1928 to be a junior synonym. Later, however, Dragesco (1960) isolated an<tei:lb/>organism from Roscoff which although identical to that described by Kahl (1932) was<tei:lb/>different in several respects from D. rotatorius Ehrenberg. Dragesco (1960) therefore named<tei:lb/>the species found at RoscoffZ). ehrenbergi and designated D. rotatorius Kahl to be its junior<tei:lb/>synonym. However, Dragesco (1960) agreed that Polycoccon octangularis Sauerbrey was a<tei:lb/>synonym of D. rotatorius Ehrenberg. In addition to the two species mentioned above two<tei:lb/>others, D. grandis Dragesco, 1954 and D. minimus Dragesco, 1968, have been described.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is a well developed AZM in all four of the above species which borders the small<tei:lb/>ventral peristome on the left of the discoid 'head'. In one species, D. ehrenbergi, there are 5-7<tei:lb/>large membranelle-like structures lying along the right border of the peristome which<tei:lb/>Dragesco (1968) called 'pre-membranelles'. This character enables the diagnosis and<tei:lb/>separation of D. ehrenbergi Dragesco from D. rotatorius Ehrenberg which lacks these<tei:lb/>'membranelles'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The cirral patterns on the ventral surface of Discocephalus differ greatly from those of<tei:lb/>Euplotes and as their morphogenesis is yet to be published fully it is difficult to interpret<tei:lb/>them adequately. Furthermore as the cirri vary considerably from species to species, it is<tei:lb/>possible that Discocephalus is really a polyphyletic group. The present confusion in different<tei:lb/>terms used for the same cirri by different authors can be illustrated by reference to 'marginal'<tei:lb/>cirri. In D. rotatorius and D. ehrenbergi there are two 'marginal' cirri on the left body edge<tei:lb/>just below the peristome. However, in D. grandis, Dragesco (1954) refers to the presence of<tei:lb/>three rows of 'marginal' cirri which is more characteristic of members of the Oxytrichidae<tei:lb/>rather than the Euplotidae. Furthermore, in D. minimus there is one row of cirri on the right<tei:lb/>body edge called ventral cirri and one row on the left edge called 'marginal' cirri. Until<tei:lb/>further morphogenetic information becomes available the present authors prefer to follow in<tei:lb/>part the system used by Hartwig and Parker (1977) which distinguishes left, central and right<tei:lb/>ventral cirri rather than to attempt to distinguish marginal from ventral rows simply on the<tei:lb/>basis of their position on the ventral surface. However, it is preferable to call the most<tei:lb/>posterior cirri, left and right caudal cirri, rather than left and right posterior 'marginals' as<tei:lb/>used in Hartwig and Parker (1977). The dorsal surface of Discocephalus has received less<tei:lb/>attention than that of either Euplotes (Curds, 1975) or Aspidisca (Wu &amp; Curds, 1979) but<tei:lb/>both Dragesco (1965) and Kahl (1932) have illustrated D. ehrenbergi with six kinetics on the<tei:lb/>dorsal surface. Dorsal argyrome patterns as found in Euplotes and Aspidisca (Curds, 1975:<tei:lb/>Wu &amp; Curds, 1979) have yet to be described. This means that the four species must currently<tei:lb/>be distinguished on the basis of their size, numbers and distribution of cirri, the presence or<tei:lb/>absence of membrane-like structures on the right of the peristome and the nature of the<tei:lb/>macronuclear apparatus. Corliss (1979) described this as a curious genus which he included<tei:lb/>only 'tentatively' in the Euplotidae and indeed, the unique body shape, the cirral arrange-<tei:lb/>ment and large number of macronuclear parts may well be considered sufficient for placing it<tei:lb/>in a separate family.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 2).          <tei:p>Genus DIOPHRYS Dujardin, 1841<tei:lb/>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It was Dujardin (1841) who first briefly described an organism called Diophrys marina and<tei:lb/>defined the genus Diophrys as having a discoid shape with groups of long 'cilia' at the two<tei:lb/>extremities of the body which was without a mouth. Later Stein (18590) stated that both D.<tei:lb/>marina and the earlier Stylonychia appendiculata Ehrenberg, 1838 were synonyms of</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>198 C. R. CURDS &amp; I. C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Styloplotes appendiculatus Stein, 1859 (see Stein 1859a) which he characterised by the<tei:lb/>presence of three large right caudal cirri. Biitschli (1889) recognised Diophrys as a distinct<tei:lb/>genus and his list of synonyms included certain species of the genera, Stylonychia Ehrenberg,<tei:lb/>Ploesconia Dujardin, Euplotes Claparede &amp; Lachmann, Schizopus Claparede &amp; Lachmann,<tei:lb/>Styloplotes Stein, Styloplotes Quennestedt, Styloplotes Fresenius, Styloplotes Rees,<tei:lb/>Styloplotes Fabre-Domergue, and Styloplotes Andrusova. Although Biitschli (1889) stated<tei:lb/>that there were two species in the genus, he only illustrated Diophrys (Styloplotes) grandis<tei:lb/>Rees, 1 88 1 and failed to mention D. appendiculata. Over the next few years the combination<tei:lb/>D. appendiculata was used occasionally, for example, by Wallengren (1901) and by Calkins<tei:lb/>(1902) who established the combination Diophrys (Styloplotes) appendiculatus Stein.<tei:lb/>However, it was Kahl (1932) who first traced the taxonomic history of the species correctly.<tei:lb/>Borror (1972) recently listed eleven species of the genus with their synonyms and he<tei:lb/>designated Diophrys scutum (Dujardin, 1841) Kahl, 1932 the type species and listed D.<tei:lb/>marina as a synonym of D. appendiculata (Ehrenberg) Kahl. In view of the evidence given<tei:lb/>above, the present authors are of the opinion that Diophrys (Stylonychia) appendiculata<tei:lb/>(Ehrenberg, 1838) Kahl, 1932 is the correct name and authority for the type species of the<tei:lb/>genus Diophrys.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Kahl (1932) defined the genus Diophrys as those members of the family Euplotidae with<tei:lb/>conspicuous sturdy transverse cirri and a single group of three large caudal cirri. In addition<tei:lb/>to D. appendiculata, Kahl (1932) described, and gave keys, to three other species, Diophrys<tei:lb/>(Ploesconia) scutum (Dujardin, 1841) Kahl, 1932; Diophrys hystrix Buddenbrock 1920 and<tei:lb/>Diophrys irmgard Mansfeld, 1923. Of the eleven species listed by Borror (1972) the present<tei:lb/>authors accept ten nominal species. D. tetramacronucleata Kattar, 1970 and D.<tei:lb/>multinucleata Hartwig, 1973 are two later additions to the genus, characterised by their<tei:lb/>possession of four and over twenty macron uclei respectively. The authors accept the latter as<tei:lb/>a distinct species but suspect that the former is a synonym of D. appendiculata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Two of the species, D. appendiculata and D. scutum, have been described by many authors<tei:lb/>over the past century but most of the other species are relatively recent additions. In most<tei:lb/>cases there are rather few data on the extent of intraspecific variation that might be found in<tei:lb/>potentially useful diagnostic structures. Even so, it was thought worthwhile to present a<tei:lb/>summary of the data that are available and to discuss the possible diagnostic importance of<tei:lb/>the various morphological features.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(a) Shape. The typical body shape of Diophrys is an ovoid in which there is often a posterior<tei:lb/>right lateral indentation where the right caudal cirri are located. The anterior of D. hystrix is<tei:lb/>particularly truncate and the body of D. kahli is elongate. The dorsal surface of Diophrys is<tei:lb/>generally smooth, but in D. irmgard it is described with a 'trapeziform elevation'<tei:lb/>(Mansfeld, 1923). These variations are apparently distinctive although the keys here do not<tei:lb/>rely upon them for identification of these species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(b) Size. Most Diophrys species are between 50 and 120 um long. D. scutum at 1 50-200 (j.m<tei:lb/>long is the largest and D. hystrix at 30-40 um long is the smallest. Size variation within<tei:lb/>species appears to be small (Hartwig, 1973) so the size differences between D. hystrix, D.<tei:lb/>scutum and D. appendiculata (50-100 um long) are likely to be of diagnostic value.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(c) Adoral zone of membranelles (AZM). Diophrys has a wide peristome which extends a<tei:lb/>third to two-thirds down the body length. On the right of the peristome there is a large, wide<tei:lb/>undulating membrane. On the left, there is a well-developed AZM which continues<tei:lb/>anteriorly over onto the dorsal surface: in some species it can return to the ventral surface<tei:lb/>down the right side of the body. Borror (1965a) stated that statistical analysis showed that the<tei:lb/>length of the right portion of the AZM in D. scutum was significantly longer from that of D.<tei:lb/>peloetes. He noted that this 'terminal portion' of the AZM in D. scutum was 0-4 the body<tei:lb/>length but only 0-3 the body length in D, peloetes. The present authors would not<tei:lb/>recommend species recognition on such a small difference as this without resort to statistical<tei:lb/>morphometric analysis but it is a useful feature for distinguishing between certain species.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>199</tei:p>
         <tei:p>For example, in D. appendiculata the AZM hardly extends onto the right of the body while<tei:lb/>in D. scutum it extends almost to the central region. Rees (1883) and Kahl (1932) also<tei:lb/>consider this right extension of the AZM to be an important diagnostic feature by which they<tei:lb/>identified Styloplotes quennerstedti and D. scutum respectively.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(d) Cirri. The type species, D. appendiculata, has 7-8 fronto ventral, 5 transverse, 1-3 left<tei:lb/>marginal and 3 right caudal cirri. The frontoventral cirri are arranged in two distinct groups,<tei:lb/>with 5 in the anterior right frontal group and 2-3 in the ventral group, often much smaller<tei:lb/>than the frontal cirri, lying in close proximity to the first transverse cirrus on the right. This<tei:lb/>distribution pattern is seen in all species but D. hystrix, D. irmgard and D. kahli have 9-10<tei:lb/>frontoventrals in groups of 7 frontals and 2-3 ventrals which the authors consider to be of<tei:lb/>diagnostic importance. Some authors are in agreement with this others are not. For example,<tei:lb/>Agamaliev (1967) established a new species D. scutoides which differs from D. scutum only<tei:lb/>in having five instead of seven frontoventral cirri. However, Borror (1963) identified an<tei:lb/>organism as D. irmgard even though it possessed only five frontoventrals rather than the nine<tei:lb/>in Mansfeld's (1923) original description. Borror (1963) neither established the organism as a<tei:lb/>new species nor did he suggest the cirral difference to be due to intraspecific variation. The<tei:lb/>morphogenesis of cirri in D. appendiculata was described by Wallengren (1901). The<tei:lb/>frontoventral-transverse cirri arise from six streaks of kinetosomes as in Euplotes, with the<tei:lb/>I/I , 11/2, II/3, HI/2 and IV/2 cirri forming the frontals: V/2 and VI/2 the ventrals and II-VI/1<tei:lb/>the transversals (Fig. 5).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The transverse cirri of Diophrys are noticeably larger than those of Euplotes. There are<tei:lb/>almost invariably five transverse cirri but there are only four in D. irmgard and D.<tei:lb/>multinucleata. The presence of large, sickle-shaped, dorso-laterally attached right caudal<tei:lb/>cirri is a characteristic feature of the genus Diophrys. During morphogenesis, these cirri arise<tei:lb/>from basal bodies at the posterior ends of the ciliary rows on the right of the dorsal surface<tei:lb/>(Borror, 1972). Most of the species described have three of these cirri but D. quadricaudatus<tei:lb/>Agamaliev, 1967 has four and D. kahli Dragesco, 1963 has only one. Intraspecific variation<tei:lb/>in the number of these cirri has not yet been reported and so it is thought that this might be a<tei:lb/>useful diagnostic character. In most species, the left marginal cirri are located just posterior</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 5 Arrangement and numbering of cirral streaks in Diophrys appendiculata, after</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Wallengren, 1901.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>200</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I.C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>to the peristome, but in D. irmgard, D. kahli and D. multimicronucleata, they are in the<tei:lb/>'caudal position' as are those in Euplotes and Uronychia. Hartwig (1974) believed that the<tei:lb/>positioning of these cirri in these three species was sufficient to constitute a separate genus.<tei:lb/>While the present authors agree that the caudal positioning of the left marginal cirri appears<tei:lb/>to be a significant character apparently associated with the presence of only four transverse<tei:lb/>cirri, they hesitate to erect a new genus on this alone. Mansfeld (1923) and Kahl (1932)<tei:lb/>distinguished D. irmgard from other species, not by the position of the left marginal cirri but<tei:lb/>by their number. Although most species have two left marginal cirri and D. irmgard has<tei:lb/>three, variation between 1-3 has been observed in D. appendiculata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(e) Nuclear features. The most commonly found nuclear arrangement in Diophrys is two<tei:lb/>macronuclei and 2-6 micronuclei. Exceptions to this include D. quadricaudatus, D.<tei:lb/>tetramacronudeata and D. multinucleata which have three, four and over 20 macronuclei<tei:lb/>respectively, with the diagnosis of the latter two species resting heavily on this feature. The<tei:lb/>macronuclei may be rod-shaped, ovoid or moniliform.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 6 Nuclear arrangement in Diophrys: (a) Diophrys magnus, after Raikov &amp; Kovaleva, 1968;<tei:lb/>(b-d) Diophrys scutoides, after Agamaliev, 1967; (c) Diophrys quadricaudatus, after Agamaliev,<tei:lb/>1967; (e, Diophrys hystrix, after Buddenbrock, 1920.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Raikov and Kovaleva (1968) separated D. magnus from D. scutum principally on the basis<tei:lb/>of its macronuclei being moniliform, however they are similar to those of D. scutum and<tei:lb/>other species which are often shown to be slightly nodular (Fig. 6a-c). Here D. magnus and<tei:lb/>D. scutum are therefore regarded to be synonymous.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Summers (1935) described the reorganisation and division of the macronuclei of D.<tei:lb/>scutum, misidentified as D. appendiculata. He noted (Fig. 7) that in the normal resting stage,<tei:lb/>the species possesses two macronuclei without a visible strand between them and a variable<tei:lb/>number of micronuclei. He observed that fragmentation of the reorganised parts of the<tei:lb/>macronuclei was not uncommon but 'fragments of the macronuclei have never been found<tei:lb/>free in the cytoplasm after the several parts fuse to form the rod-like mass'. One cannot be<tei:lb/>certain if the tripartite macronuclear structure of D. quadricaudatus (Fig. 6d) is the result of<tei:lb/>fragmentation or if it is the true resting stage, but it is likely that the sausage-shaped nucleus<tei:lb/>which Buddenbrock (1920) described in certain specimens of D. hystrix to be a divisional<tei:lb/>stage in the normally ovoid macronuclei (Fig. 6e).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>201</tei:p>
         <tei:p>d</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 7 Division of the macronucleus of Diophrys scutum, after Summers, 1935 (called D.<tei:lb/>appendiculatd): (a) nucleus at rest; (b) first appearance of a reorganisation band at the outer pole<tei:lb/>of the posterior macronucleus; (c) reorganisation bands about to disappear at inner poles of the<tei:lb/>macronuclei; (d) fusion of the macronuclei; (e) after fusion; (0 macronucleus beginning to divide;<tei:lb/>(g) macronuclei completely divided just before daughter cells separate.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Kisselbach (1936) also illustrated various stages in the nuclear development of D.<tei:lb/>appendiculata (Fig. 8a-e) one of which shows a quadripartite stage similar to that illustrated<tei:lb/>by Kattar (1970) in D. tetramacronucleata (Fig. 8f, g). In view of this D. tetramacronucleata<tei:lb/>should be strongly suspected as being a synonym of the type species. However, since one<tei:lb/>illustration by Kattar (1970) and another by Hartwig (1974) show the four ovoid<tei:lb/>macronuclei to be completely distinct (Fig. 8g) the species has been provisionally included<tei:lb/>here awaiting further data. As mentioned above, there are commonly 2-6 micronuclei in<tei:lb/>Diophrys. Borror (19650) accorded little significance to this feature and referred to Ito (1963)<tei:lb/>who stated that macronuclei may vary considerably in number within a species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(/) Dorsal silver-line system. Borror (19650) pointed out that few workers had mentioned the<tei:lb/>dorsal ciliature in Diophrys species descriptions and observed that Kahl (1932) was the first<tei:lb/>to note the presence of five rows of short cilia in D. scutum. Using the Chatton-Lwoff(1930)<tei:lb/>technique, Borror (19650) was able to show that '. . . the kinetosomes in the dorsal rows of<tei:lb/>Diophrys behave during cell division in a manner similar to the behavior of the dorsal<tei:lb/>kinetosomes in Euplotes, hence the proter and opisthe usually have the same number of<tei:lb/>rows. Within a population, with practically no exceptions, all members of the genus present<tei:lb/>have the same number of rows of cilia dorsally, and this is apparently not related with body<tei:lb/>size.'</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>202</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I.C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Several authors have used the number of dorsal kinetics as a diagnostic character (Borror,<tei:lb/>19650, b: Agamaliev, 1967: Raikov &amp; Kovaleva, 1968). However, the dorsal silver-line<tei:lb/>systems of other species including D. hystrix, D. irmgard, D. kahli, D. tetramacronudeata<tei:lb/>and D. multinucleata still remain to be described. For this reason the key here only<tei:lb/>distinguishes between D. oligothrix and D. peloetes on this character. All dorsal argyromes<tei:lb/>that have been described so far consist of a meshwork pattern and are therefore of little value<tei:lb/>for specific identification.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 8</tei:p>
         <tei:p>20yur</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Nuclear arrangement in Diophrys: (a-e) Diophrys appendiculata, after Kisselbach, 1936;<tei:lb/>(f, g) Diophrys tetramacronudeata, after Kattar, 1970.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Diophrys</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Marine hypotrichs 30-200 um in length. Ovoid body usually with prominent right<tei:lb/>posterio-lateral concavity from which arise three large sickle-shaped right caudal cirri. There<tei:lb/>are 5-10 fronto ventral, 4-6 transverse and usually 2-3 left marginal cirri. There are often<tei:lb/>two elongate, sometimes nodular, macronuclei but in some species there may be four or over<tei:lb/>twenty macronuclei. There is a variable number of micronuclei. The dorsal silver-line<tei:lb/>system consists of 4-8 dorsolateral kinetics and a mesh-like argyrome.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Species descriptions</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys appendiculata (Ehrenberg, 1838)Kahl, 1932</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Stylonychia appendiculata Ehrenberg, 1838</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys marina Dujardin, 1 84 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Schizopus norwegicus Claparede &amp; Lachmann, 1858</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Styloplotes appendiculatus Stein, 1 859</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Styloplotes fresenii Rees, 1883</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Styloplotes appendiculatus var. pontica Andrusova, 1 886</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Planiplotes wagneri Andrusova, 1 886</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys appendiculatus (Stein, 18 59) Calkins, 1902</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Figs 9, 10). This, the type species of the genus, is 50-100 (im long. The body<tei:lb/>shape is typically ovoid with the characteristic lateral concavity at the posterior where the<tei:lb/>three sickle-shaped right caudal cirri arise. There are 7-8 frontoventral cirri - 5 anterior and<tei:lb/>2-3 close to the transversals, 5 transverse and 1-3 left marginal cirri. The AZM extends<tei:lb/>down half to two-thirds of the length of the body on the left but hardly at all on the right. The<tei:lb/>two macronuclei are usually elongate to ovoid and may be smooth or nodular. There are 2-4<tei:lb/>micronuclei and 5-6 dorsolateral kinetics each carrying 6-10 cilia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. Some of the nomenclatural history of this species has already been outlined in the<tei:lb/>introduction to the genus. Kahl (1932) transferred Stylonychia appendiculata Ehrenberg,<tei:lb/>1838 to the genus Diophrys and redescribed the species. However, he made no reference to<tei:lb/>the fact that Stein (1859#) had already erroneously redefined the species as Styloplotes<tei:lb/>appendiculatus which combination was subsequently used by many workers (Fresenius,<tei:lb/>1865: Quennerstedt, 1867: Kent, 1881: Rees, 1883: Fabre-Domergue, 1885). Stein (18590)<tei:lb/>suggested that Ehrenberg (1838) had overlooked the frontoventral cirri and suggested that<tei:lb/>Schizopus norwegicus Claparede &amp; Lachmann, 1858 was probably a synonym, even though<tei:lb/>Claparede &amp; Lachmann (1858) had distinguished their species from Stylonychia<tei:lb/>appendiculata Ehrenberg by the absence of marginal cirri. It seems likely that the two left<tei:lb/>marginal cirri were mistaken identified to be satellite transverse cirri. Calkins (1902) later<tei:lb/>transferred Styloplotes appendiculatus Stein to the genus Diophrys.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Rees (1883) briefly 'described a species of Styloplotes which he stated was the same as<tei:lb/>Styloplotes appendiculatus Stein as described by Fresenius (1865) which he (Rees, 1883)<tei:lb/>proceeded to call Styloplotes fresenii. Earlier Rees (1881) had described the new species<tei:lb/>Styloplotes grandis but later (Rees, 1883) concluded that it was identical to Styloplotes<tei:lb/>norwegicus Quennerstedt, 1867 which he then erroneously called Styloplotes quennerstedti.<tei:lb/>In the present authors, opinion, Quennerstedt (1867) was mistaken in making Styloplotes<tei:lb/>norwegicus Quennerstedt a synonym of Schizopus norwegicus Claparede &amp; Lachmann. It is<tei:lb/>here considered that the former species is a synonym of D. scutum because of the extent to<tei:lb/>which the AZM is developed on the right side of the body. Rees (1883) also used this<tei:lb/>character to distinguish Styloplotes quennerstedti, a synonym of D. scutum, from Styloplotes<tei:lb/>fresnii, a synonym of D. appendiculata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys scutum (Dujardin, 1841) Kahl, 1932</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Styloplotes norwegicus Quennerstedt, 1 867<tei:lb/>Styloplotes grand is Rees, 1881<tei:lb/>Styloplotes quennerstedti Rees, 1 883<tei:lb/>Diophrys grandis Butschli, 1889<tei:lb/>Diophrys magnus Raikov &amp; Kovaleva, 1968<tei:lb/>Diophrys kasymovi Agamaliev, 1 97 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Figs 11, 12). This is the largest species (150-200 urn long) of the genus so far<tei:lb/>described. It may be distinguished from the type species, D. appendiculata, by its size and by<tei:lb/>the AZM which extends to the central body region on the right side. The body shape is ovoid<tei:lb/>with an indentation in the posterior right. The dorsal surface is smooth and arched. There<tei:lb/>are 3 large sickle-shaped right caudal cirri, 7-8 fronto ventral, 5 transverse and 2 left marginal<tei:lb/>cirri. The two elongate macronuclei may be curved, nodular or moniliform and are<tei:lb/>accompanied by up to six micronuclei. The dorsal silver-line system consists of 5-6<tei:lb/>dorsolateral kinetics interspersed with mesh-like argyromes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>f</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 12. Diophrys scutum: (a, b) ventral and dorsal surfaces with section showing argyromes, after<tei:lb/>Agamaliev, 1968; (c) after Raikov &amp; Kovaleva, 1968 (called D. magnus); (d, ventral argyrome,<tei:lb/>nuclei and dorsal argyrome after Agamaliev, 1 97 1 (called D. kasymovi).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>

         <tei:p>NOTES. Claparede &amp; Lachmann (1858) and Stein (\S59a) believed that the three illustrations<tei:lb/>of Ploesconia scutum by Dujardin (1841) were of two different species. Claparede &amp;<tei:lb/>Lachmann (1858) thought that one represented a Euplotes species whereas Stein (1859a)<tei:lb/>considered it to be Styloplotes appendiculatus Stein. The present authors doubt both of these<tei:lb/>opinions but agree with Kahl (1932) who identified the species as Diophrys scutum and<tei:lb/>considered the AZM originating in the middle of the right border to be an important<tei:lb/>diagnostic feature. It is mainly by this character that D. scutum can be distinguished from D.<tei:lb/>appendiculata. Raikov and Kovaleva (1968) distinguished D. magnus from D. scutum by the<tei:lb/>difference in the shapes of their macronuclei which is not a significant feature. The recent<tei:lb/>addition D. kasymovi Agamaliev, 1971 has been included here in spite of it being rather<tei:lb/>small for this species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 13 Diophrys hystrix, after Buddenbrock, 1 920: (a) ventral surface; (b, c) nuclear features.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys hystrix Buddenbrock, 1920</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 1 3). Diophrys hystrix is a small (30^0 urn long) species. The body outline<tei:lb/>is generally oval but it is truncated anteriorly and there is the usual concavity on the<tei:lb/>posterior right accommodating three large sickle-shaped right caudal cirri. The dorsal<tei:lb/>surface is strongly arched. The ten frontoventral cirri are arranged in two distinct groups<tei:lb/>with 7 'frontals' at the anterior on the right of the peristome and 3 'ventrals' near the<tei:lb/>transverse cirri. One of the 'ventrals' is adjacent and similar in size to the transversals, but it<tei:lb/>points in the opposite direction and is used in the creeping movements of the animal. There<tei:lb/>are 4 long transverse cirri and 2 small left marginal cirri located just behind the peristome.<tei:lb/>There are two ovoid macronuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. Kahl (1932) gave an almost identical description and illustration of this species as in<tei:lb/>Buddenbrock's (1920) original. Kattar (1970) identified a small (35-40 urn) species with two<tei:lb/>ovoid macronuclei as D. hystrix but this is a dubious identification since the author gave an<tei:lb/>inadequate description and the illustration was of a different shape and cirral number to that<tei:lb/>of Buddenbrock (1920).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys irmgard Mansfeld, 1 923</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 14). This is a medium sized (75-135 urn long) marine species. The body<tei:lb/>shape is characteristic, being broadly oval in outline but tending to be rectangular. The wide<tei:lb/>peristome is approximately half the body length and there is a prominent undulating</tei:p>

         <tei:p>1 963 ;(f) after Borror, 1963.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>membrane on the right. The AZM bordering its left edge curves around the anterior end but<tei:lb/>only just extends to the right side of the body. There are 3 right caudal, 9 frontoventral, 4-5<tei:lb/>transverse and 3 left marginal cirri. The two ovoid macronuclei are 8 urn in diameter and<tei:lb/>each is associated with a micronucleus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. This species may be distinguished from the type species, D. appendiculata, by the<tei:lb/>presence of 9 instead of 7 frontoventral cirri and because the left marginal cirri are in the<tei:lb/>'caudal' position. Horror (1963) described an organism which he called D. irmgard but as it had<tei:lb/>only 5 frontoventral cirri perhaps future studies will show this to be a separate species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys salina Ruinen, 1938</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 1 5). This is a small (3CMO um long) species. The outline shape of the<tei:lb/>body is characteristically oval without any obvious lateral concavities but there is a posterior<tei:lb/>indentation where the 3 large caudal cirri arise. The dorsal surface is strongly convex, but the<tei:lb/>ventral surface is flattened. The peristome region extends to the centre of the body, with an<tei:lb/>AZM on the left and a well developed undulating membrane on the right. The frontoventral<tei:lb/>cirri are arranged in two groups with 4 right anterior 'frontals' and 4 'ventrals' situated<tei:lb/>immediately behind, and to the right of, the peristome. There are no left marginal cirri. Five<tei:lb/>transverse cirri lie between the caudal and 'ventral' cirri. The original description did not<tei:lb/>include any details concerning the nuclear apparatus. Feeds on diatoms, algae and bacteria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 15 Diophrys salina: (a, b) ventral surface and lateral view, after Ruinen, 1 938.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys kahli Dragesco, 1 963</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 16). This species is about 80 um long, the body is elongate with a short<tei:lb/>posterior narrowed tail region. The peristome is small, bordered on the right by a well<tei:lb/>developed undulating membrane and on the left by a small AZM. The frontoventral cirri<tei:lb/>consist of a group of 7 long 'frontals' and 2 small 'ventrals'. The 5 transverse cirri are long,<tei:lb/>the 2 left marginal cirri are in the 'caudal' position and there is only one right caudal cirrus.<tei:lb/>The two oval macronuclei are connected by a nuclear membrane and there are 4-6<tei:lb/>micronuclei. A few cilia have been observed along the left border of the animal.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. Dragesco (1963) identified an organism that he considered to be identical to one<tei:lb/>incompletely studied by Kahl (1932) which he called D. kahli. Untypically, the body of this<tei:lb/>species is rather elongate and its left marginal cirri emerge caudally instead of immediately<tei:lb/>posterior to the peristome. Hartwig (1974) grouped this species with D. irmgard and D.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>210</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I.C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 16 Diophrys kahli: (a, b) ventral surface and dorsal plaques, after Dragesco, 1963; (c) dorsal<tei:lb/>surface, after Kahl, 1932 (called an 'incompletely studied Diophrys species').</tei:p>
         <tei:p>multinucleata because of this latter feature. In contrast to other species in the genus, D. kahli<tei:lb/>has only one right caudal cirrus, its peristome is rather small and the AZM is under-<tei:lb/>developed. Dragesco (1963) described the presence of small oval plaques on the dorsal<tei:lb/>surface (Fig. 14b) which he suspected to be 'protrichocysts'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys oligothrix Borror, 1 965</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 17). Diophrys oligothrix is a medium sized (79-106 urn long), ovoid<tei:lb/>species. The concavity at the posterior right edge, so typical of the genus, is here incon-<tei:lb/>spicuous and the dorsal surface smooth. The AZM bordering the left edge of the large<tei:lb/>peristome continues dorsally along the anterior region to the right side of the body. The<tei:lb/>ciliature and nuclear features are also typical of the genus, there are 7 fronto ventral, 5<tei:lb/>transverse, 2 left marginal and 3 right caudal cirri. The two irregular, elongate macronuclei<tei:lb/>are usually accompanied by four micronuclei. The silver-line system consists of 3 dorsal<tei:lb/>kinetics, one lateral kinety on the right and a fine mesh-like argyrome. The dorsal kineties<tei:lb/>bear, from left to right, 13-20, 16-24, 12-18 cilia and the ventral kinety 9-13 cilia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. This species is distinguished from D. scutum on the basis of its different number of<tei:lb/>kineties.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys peloetes Borror, 1965</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 18). D. peloetes is a medium sized (95-135 urn long) species typical of the<tei:lb/>genus. It has a wide peristome which is about two-thirds of the body length, bordered by a<tei:lb/>well developed AZM on the left and an undulating membrane on the right. The AZM travels<tei:lb/>along the anterior dorsal edge onto the right side of the body where it extends about 3/10 of<tei:lb/>the body length. There are 3 prominent right caudal cirri, 5 long transverse, 7 frontoventral<tei:lb/>and 2 left marginal cirri. The two macronuclei are elongate. There are 8 dorsolateral</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>211</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 17 Diophrys oligothrix, after Borror, 19656: (a) ventral surface; (b) dorsal surface; (c) frontal</tei:p>
         <tei:p>section showing nuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>kinetics, each bearing 6-17 cilia. The number of kinetics distinguishes this species from D.<tei:lb/>scutum which has only five.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. Borror (1965a) established D. peloetes after a morphometric comparison with D.<tei:lb/>scutum. He stated that the new species was 'extremely similar to D. scutum except for having<tei:lb/>eight dorsal rows of cilia instead of five, having fewer cilia per row, and having a significantly<tei:lb/>shorter AZM'. He also pointed out that he had identified it incorrectly as D. scutum in an<tei:lb/>earlier study (Borror, 1963).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys quadricaudatus Agamaliev, 1967</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 19). This is a medium sized (100-1 10 urn long) species, characterised by<tei:lb/>the presence of 4 right caudal cirri arising from a particularly prominent concavity on the<tei:lb/>posterior right of an otherwise oval body. The wide peristome is about 48 um long and on its<tei:lb/>right there is a conspicuous undulating membrane. The AZM is composed of 50-60<tei:lb/>membranelles which extend forward over to the right side of the body. There are 7<tei:lb/>fron to ventral, 5 transverse, and 3 left marginal cirri. The dorsal silver-line system consists of<tei:lb/>5 or 6 dorsolateral kinetics and a finely-meshed dorsal argyrome. There are two anterior and<tei:lb/>one posterior macronuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>212</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I. C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 18 Diophrys peloetes: (a, b) ventral and dorsal surfaces, after Borror, 1963 (called D.<tei:lb/>scutum); (c-e) ventral and dorsal silver-line systems, and nuclei, after Borror, 1965a.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. Agamaliev (1967) considered the presence of four right caudal cirri and three<tei:lb/>macronuclei to be the diagnostic features of this species. The uneven number of macron uclei<tei:lb/>is rather strange and could perhaps represent an interdivisional state.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys scutoides Agamaliev, 1967</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 20). D. scutoides is 1 10-120 urn long. The body is oval with a prominent<tei:lb/>concavity on the posterior right edge. The AZM of 75-80 membranelles borders the left edge<tei:lb/>of a wide peristome and continues along the anterior edge to extend a third of the body length</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>213</tei:p>
         <tei:p>20yum</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 19 Diophrys quadricaudatus, after Agamaliev, 1967: (a) ventral surface of living cell (b-d)<tei:lb/>stained ventral surface, dorsal surface and nuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>down the right side. There are 3 right caudal, 5 transverse and 2 left marginal cirri but only 5<tei:lb/>frontoventral cirri. The two elongate macronuclei are nodular and are accompanied by three<tei:lb/>micronuclei. The silver-line system consists of 5 dorsolateral kinetics with mesh-like dorsal<tei:lb/>and ventral argyromes.</tei:p>
