         <tei:p>NOTES. Agamaliev (1967) described this species from the Caspian Sea. Apart from being<tei:lb/>slightly smaller in size, it differs from D. scutum in having five instead of seven frontoventral<tei:lb/>cirri.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys tetramacronucleata Kattar, 1970</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 21). This is a small (55-65 urn long) species. The body is oval except for<tei:lb/>the concavity at the posterior right edge. The AZM consists of about 36 membranelles which<tei:lb/>extend about halfway down the body on the left but hardly at all on the right. There are 7<tei:lb/>frontoventral, 5 transverse, 2 left marginal and 3 right caudal cirri. There are 5 dorsolateral<tei:lb/>kinetics and 4 ovoid macronuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. The original description of this species was brief and only the presence of four<tei:lb/>macronuclei distinguished it from the type species. Although it is suspected that Kattar<tei:lb/>(1970) described an interdivisional specimen, it is provisionally included here as a nominal<tei:lb/>species. Kattar (1970) stated that protargol impregnation showed the presence of three<tei:lb/>median and two dorsolateral kinetics he provided no diagram of these structures. The</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>215</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 21 Diophrys tetramacronucleata, after Kattar, 1 970: (a) ventral surface; (b) nuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>redescription and photographs of this species by Hartwig (1974) are also incomplete<tei:lb/>although the presence of four macronuclei was clearly demonstrated.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diophrys multinucleata Hartwig, 1973</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 22). D. multinucleata is a medium sized (76-11 Sum long) highly<tei:lb/>thigmotactic species. The body is oval to rectangular in outline with the dorsal surface being<tei:lb/>arched and the ventral surface flattened. There is a small but distinct projection on the<tei:lb/>anterior right corner of the body. The peristome is about two-thirds of the body length. The<tei:lb/>AZM consists of about 30 membranelles lying along the left of the peristome and a few larger<tei:lb/>membranelles along the anterior edge of the body. The frontoventral cirri are arranged in<tei:lb/>two distinct groups: the 5-6 large 'frontals' are in the anterior and the two very small<tei:lb/>'ventrals' lie close to the transverse cirri. There are 4 long transverse, 3 right caudal and 3 left<tei:lb/>marginal/caudal cirri. There are at least 20 macronuclei present.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. This species is characterised by the presence of over twenty macronuclei which is<tei:lb/>unique in the genus, otherwise it is similar to D. irmgard in cirral arrangement, particularly<tei:lb/>in the caudal positioning of the left marginal cirri which Hartwig (1973, 1974) considered to<tei:lb/>be a feature of diagnostic importance.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus URONYCHIA Stein, 1859</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Stein (1859#, 18596) established and described the genus Uronychia and transferred<tei:lb/>Trichoda transfuga Muller, 1786 to the genus as the type species. Between 1901 and 1928,<tei:lb/>five new species were described and Kahl (1932) included a key to them in his classical<tei:lb/>compendium. Since then only one addition, Uronychia bivalvorum Fenchel, 1965, has been</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>216</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I.C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 22 Diophrys multinucleata, after Hartwig, 1973: (a) dorsal surface; (b) ventral surface</tei:p>
         <tei:p>showing nuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>made which he found in the mantle cavity of certain lamellibranch molluscs. Uronychia is<tei:lb/>characterised by its enormous peristome region occupying half of the ventral surface with<tei:lb/>conspicuous peristomial membranes, the three posterior groups of large cirri, the apparent<tei:lb/>absence of frontoventral cirri and by its rapid backward motion (Bullington, 1940). How-<tei:lb/>ever, there are few features which clearly distinguish the described species from one another.<tei:lb/>Many of the features used in the past are variable so that here only four nominal species are<tei:lb/>recognised.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(a) Shape. The typical Uronychia body is oval. Dorsal ribs, ridges or striations are often<tei:lb/>mentioned in descriptions but there are few data on the variability of these structures.<tei:lb/>Ventrally, there are two large posterior cavities which accommodate the transverse and left<tei:lb/>marginal cirri. In general, the right caudal cirri of Uronychia are attached more dorsally than<tei:lb/>those in Diophrys, so the oval body outline is without the dorsolateral concavity at the<tei:lb/>posterior right which is characteristic of the latter genus. However, Buddenbrock (1920)<tei:lb/>described U. heinrothi with an 'Ausschnitt' (notch) on the posterior right edge which can<tei:lb/>make the posterior border S-shaped. Similarly, Taylor (1928) described an 'uncinus' on U.<tei:lb/>uncinata which he stated apparently owes its origin in part to an attenuation of the remnant<tei:lb/>which holds proter to opisthe during the final stages of division.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It seems likely that the 'Ausschnitt' and 'uncinus' are the same structure and as<tei:lb/>Buddenbrock (1920) noted that it was variable in extent and absent from some individuals<tei:lb/>then it cannot be used as a reliable diagnostic character. Indeed if it is formed during division<tei:lb/>then it could only be present in the proter of a dividing cell.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(b) Size. The distinctive sizes of U. magna and U. setigera are here considered to be</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>217</tei:p>
         <tei:p>diagnostically significant. The former at 450 um long is the largest species so far described in<tei:lb/>the family Euplotidae, and the latter at 40-50 jim long is the smallest Uronychia species. The<tei:lb/>sizes of the remaining species fall within the range of 70-250 um long. The size of the type<tei:lb/>species U. transfuga has been noted to vary between 50-1 50 um long (Kahl, 1932) but<tei:lb/>Bullington (1940) separated U. heinrothi from U. transfuga by the larger size (129-264 um<tei:lb/>long) of the species he observed. However, Buddenbrock (1920) described U. heinrothi as<tei:lb/>having the range 70-250 urn in length. It is apparent that the size variation of these two<tei:lb/>species is great and their ranges overlap. Since there are no other significant distinguishing<tei:lb/>features, these two species are here regarded as being synonymous.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(c) Adoral Zone of Membranelles. The ventral surface of Uronychia is dominated by an<tei:lb/>enormous peristome with conspicuous membranelles and membranes. Unlike the rest of the<tei:lb/>Euplotidae, the AZM of Uronychia consists of large membranelles along the anterior of the<tei:lb/>body and 4-5 smaller paroral membranelles at the posterior left of the peristome and there<tei:lb/>are undulating membranes along both sides of the peristome. Fenchel (1965) was of the<tei:lb/>opinion that it was the fusion of the adoral membranelles which forms the membrane along<tei:lb/>the left peristomial border. These peristomial membranes and membranelles may be<tei:lb/>observed to be closed over the peristome region or spread out and wing-like. The paroral<tei:lb/>membranes are often found in a pocket-like invagination of the peristomial wall.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buddenbrock (1920) described the presence of two large peristomial membranes in U.<tei:lb/>heinrothi but these appear to be split into five parts in one of his diagrams. Bullington (1940)<tei:lb/>observed four peristomial membranes on his specimens of U. heinrothi plus another large<tei:lb/>membrane close to the posterior right edge of the body. This marginal membrane has been<tei:lb/>described only on this occasion so it has not been used here as a diagnostic character.<tei:lb/>Although the peristomial membranes of U. heinrothi appear to be larger and more numerous<tei:lb/>than has been observed in U. transfuga, it is not considered to be a reliable character. On the<tei:lb/>other hand, Calkins (1902) described 'flagella-like' cirri in the peristome of U. setigera which<tei:lb/>have subsequently been noted by other authors (Buddenbrock, 1920; Young, 1922; Kahl,<tei:lb/>1932; Kattar, 1970). This feature is apparently distinctive and consistent and is therefore<tei:lb/>useful in the identification of U. setigera.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 23 Arrangement and numbering of cirral streaks in Uronychia transfuga, after Wallengren,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1901.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>218</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I.C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(d) Cirri. One of the most distinctive generic features of Uronychia is the apparent absence of<tei:lb/>frontoventral cirri. Wallengren (1901) studied cirral morphogenesis in U. transfuga and was<tei:lb/>first to number the cirral streaks (Fig. 23). He found that there was a small residuum of cirral<tei:lb/>elements I/I, II/2 and 111/2 grouped closely together at the anterior right near the adoral<tei:lb/>membranelles which he described as membranous cirri. Young (1922) noted these in three<tei:lb/>Uronychia species and described them as cirri-like membranelles, but it was Buddenbrock<tei:lb/>(1920) who illustrated three similar small cirrus-like structures at the anterior left as well as<tei:lb/>on the right. Wallengren (1901) showed that the cirri V/2 and VI/2 which form the 'ventrals'<tei:lb/>in Euplotes and Diophrys lie among the transversals in U. transfuga and they appear like<tei:lb/>'satellites' in this group of cirri. These cirri have not yet been observed by other authors but<tei:lb/>Buddenbrock (1920), Young (1922) and Taylor (1928) described the presence of one slender<tei:lb/>cirrus at the right of the transversals in U. setigera, U. heinrothi and U. uncinata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>All species of Uronychia have 4 or 5 transverse cirri. Young (1922) considered the<tei:lb/>possession of four transversals in U. binucleata to be a specific character even though he<tei:lb/>recorded that the number of transversals in U. transfuga varied between 4 and 5. To the left<tei:lb/>of the ventral cavity from which the transversals emerge, there is another cavity which<tei:lb/>accommodates the two enormous left marginal cirri with a variable number of small cirri or<tei:lb/>cilia. The right caudal cirri emerge dorsally on the posterior right and are invariably sickle-<tei:lb/>shaped. All of the described species have three right caudals except U. magna which was<tei:lb/>shown as having two by Pierantoni (1909), although he also observed only two right caudals<tei:lb/>in U. transfuga which others have shown to possess three. Bullington (1940) discussed the<tei:lb/>attachment and movement of the right caudal cirri in Uronychia (Fig. 24).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In addition to these three groups of large cirri, two small right marginal cirri have been<tei:lb/>observed in most Uronychia species. Fenchel (1965) distinguished U. bivalvorum from U.<tei:lb/>transfuga partly by the absence of these two small cirri but it should be noted that both U.<tei:lb/>transfuga and U. setigera have also been described without right marginals.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 24 Arrangement of the right caudal cirri of Uronychia transfuga, after Bullington, 1940<tei:lb/>(called U. heinrothi): (a) showing two large cirri attached in the mid-dorsal region of the posterior<tei:lb/>body end with a cirrus on the right; (b) showing two defimbriated cirri attached to either side of<tei:lb/>the mid-line and one cirrus attached in a pocket near the right edge and slightly below the other<tei:lb/>two cirri.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>219</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 25 Stages in cell division of Uronychia transfuga, after Calkins, 1911: (a) resting stage; (b)<tei:lb/>condensation of the macronucleus; (c, d) later stages in division; (e) just before cell separation;<tei:lb/>(f-h) daughter cells immediately, 15 minutes and 1 hour after separation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Kahl (1932) noted the presence of a long slender right caudal cirrus in U. binucleata which<tei:lb/>he considered to be of diagnostic value, similarly Young (1922) described such a cirrus in U.<tei:lb/>setigera although no others have reported its presence. It is evident that cirral numbers and<tei:lb/>arrangements in this genus are highly variable and therefore of little diagnostic importance.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(e} Nuclear features. Kahl (1932) relied heavily on the nuclear features of Uronychia in his<tei:lb/>key to the species. For example, he stated that U. transfuga had, without exception, two<tei:lb/>sausage-shaped macronuclei with a micronucleus and that U. magna and U. heinrothi had<tei:lb/>their nuclei split into many fragments. However, U. transfuga had already been seen with its<tei:lb/>macronucleus in many fragments. Calkins (1911) described the nuclear reorganisation of U.<tei:lb/>transfuga and showed that the macronucleus just after cell division was in two parts but that<tei:lb/>it was a fragmented C-shape when at the resting stage (Fig. 25). The macronucleus of U.<tei:lb/>transfuga has been described as having a fragmented C-shape, like a string of beads or<tei:lb/>partially fused, and in two parts (Fig. 26) (Buddenbrock, 1920; Bullington, 1940). Borror<tei:lb/>(1972) has also noted that the macronuclear shape and number were variable and for this<tei:lb/>reason considered U. heinrothi to be synonymous with the type species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Calkins (1902) described U. setigera with a single ovoid macronucleus but Young (1922)<tei:lb/>observed two irregular macronuclei in his specimens of the same species. Fenchel (1965)<tei:lb/>distinguished U. bivalvorum from the type species partly on the basis of its nucleus which he<tei:lb/>described as 'an irregular structure divided into two or more parts of unequal size': this<tei:lb/>would lie within the range of variability already noted above for U. transfuga. Kattar(1970)<tei:lb/>distinguished between U. transfuga and U. setigera by their possession of 9-15 and 2 nodular<tei:lb/>macronuclei respectively, although he also concluded that the six species recognised by Kahl<tei:lb/>(1932) including U. setigera could be varieties of U. transfuga. Although the number and<tei:lb/>shape of the macronuclei in Uronychia appear to be highly variable this is not the case with</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>220</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I. C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p/>
         <tei:p>^0</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 26 Nuclear variation in Uronychia transfuga, after Buddenbrock, 1920 (called V. heinrothi).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>the micronucleus. Most species possess a single micronucleus but U. binucleata is charac-<tei:lb/>terised by the presence of two micronuclei. Only one other species, U. magna, has been<tei:lb/>recorded with two micronuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(/) Dorsal silver-line system. Kattar (1970) noted the presence of four kinetics on the dorsal<tei:lb/>surface of U. transfuga and five on U. setigera, while Reiff (1968) illustrated five dorsolateral<tei:lb/>kinetics on the former species. The kinetics which have only recently been described appear<tei:lb/>to correspond in number and position with the dorsal ridges and striations that are<tei:lb/>sometimes described by earlier authors. However, far more data are required before the<tei:lb/>diagnostic importance of the silver-line systems of Uronychia can be assessed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Uronychia</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Marine hypotrichs of variable size, mostly within the range 50-250 urn long, but up to<tei:lb/>450 um long. Body oval and smooth in outline, dorsal surface smooth or with ridges.<tei:lb/>Peristome large with conspicuous membranes. The AZM is limited to the anterior border<tei:lb/>and the paroral region. There are 4-5 transverse and 2 left marginal cirri which emerge from<tei:lb/>prominent ventral cavities. The 3 large right caudal cirri are attached to the dorsal surface<tei:lb/>and there are sometimes 2 right marginal cirri present. The frontoventral cirri are reduced to<tei:lb/>a field of 3 near the origin of the AZM. The macronuclei are variable in number and shape<tei:lb/>and there are 1 or 2 micronuclei. Characteristically moves backwards rapidly.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Key to the species of Uronychia</tei:p>

         <tei:p>With a single micronucleus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With two micronuclei</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Peristomial cirri present</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Peristomial cirri absent</tei:p>
         <tei:p>40-50 um long</tei:p>
         <tei:p>About 450 um long</tei:p>
         <tei:p>About 450 urn long, macronucleus moniliform and C-shaped<tei:lb/>60-80 um long, macronucleus in 3-5 pieces ....</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>224</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I.C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Figs 27, 28 &amp; 29). This is a cosmopolitan species. The oval shaped body is<tei:lb/>variable in size (50-260 urn long), its dorsal surface is arched and may be smooth or with 3-4<tei:lb/>ridges. At the posterior right there are the 3 characteristically sickle-shaped right caudal cirri.<tei:lb/>The large peristome and two posterior cavities occupy most of the ventral surface.<tei:lb/>Prominent adoral membranelles are situated along the anterior edge of the body and these<tei:lb/>emerge dorsally. At the posterior left of the peristome, there are 4-5 paroral membranelles<tei:lb/>lying in a pocket-like invagination. Large undulating membranes border two sides of the<tei:lb/>peristome. There are 4-5 large transverse cirri implanted in the larger posterior cavity on the<tei:lb/>right, sometimes with 1 or 2 satellite cirri. In the posterior left cavity there are 2 large<tei:lb/>marginal cirri sometimes accompanied by a few, usually 2, small cirri or cilia. The<tei:lb/>macronucleus varies from being a moniliform (5-13 segments) C-shape at resting stage, to<tei:lb/>being 2 irregular masses immediately after cell division. The silver-line system consists of<tei:lb/>3-5 dorsolateral kinetics. It has been observed in the mantle cavities of the lamellibranch<tei:lb/>molluscs Thyasiraflexuosa and T. sarsi.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>10JJ</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 30 Uronychia setigera: (a) after Calkins, 1902; (b-d) after Young, 1922; (e) after Kattar,</tei:p>

         <tei:p>NOTES. Stein (1859&lt;2, b) established the genus Uronychia and redescribed U. (Trichoda)<tei:lb/>transfuga Muller as the type species. U. transfuga has since been identified by many workers<tei:lb/>from various localities (see Hartwig 1973, 1974). As discussed earlier U. heinrothi, U.<tei:lb/>uncinata and U. bivalvorum were established on characters that are now known to be<tei:lb/>variable and unreliable. These three species are here regarded as synonyms of the type<tei:lb/>species until more convincing data become available.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Uronychia setigem Calkins, 1 902</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 30). This is the smallest (40-50 urn long) species yet described. The dorsal<tei:lb/>surface is arched and is sculptured longitudinally by 3-4 ridges. There are 2-3 flagella-like<tei:lb/>cirri in the large peristome and there are about 4 paroral membranelles. Wide undulating<tei:lb/>membranes lie on each side of the peristome. There are 3 curved right caudal cirri attached<tei:lb/>dorsally and a long thin satellite cirrus has been observed. In the two ventral cavities there<tei:lb/>are 4-5 transverse cirri and 2 large left marginal cirri. The macronucleus is usually in 2 band-<tei:lb/>like parts with a micronucleus between them but a single spherical macronucleus has also<tei:lb/>been observed in this species. There are 5 dorsolateral kinetics.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. This species is characterised by the presence of flagella-like cirri extending from the<tei:lb/>posterior left margin into the peristome (Calkins, 1902). This author further distinguished it<tei:lb/>from the type species by its single ovoid macronucleus; however later authors (Young, 1922;<tei:lb/>Kattar, 1970) reported two macronuclei. Kattar (1970) impregnated the cell with protargol<tei:lb/>but failed to clearly illustrate the silver-line system.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Uronychia magna Pierantoni, 1909</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 3 1 ). This is the largest species yet recorded (450 um long). The body shape<tei:lb/>is approximately oval in outline but is ornamented at either end. The anterior dorsal edge is<tei:lb/>serrated and there are two short spines on the posterior left dorsal border. The dorsal surface<tei:lb/>is longitudinally striated. The peristome is extensive, occupying most of the ventral surface.<tei:lb/>The AZM is located anteriorly in a posterior pocket in the peristome. There are two large<tei:lb/>undulating membranes, one on each side of the peristome. There are 6 transverse, 2 left<tei:lb/>marginal and 3 right caudal cirri. The macronucleus is in many pieces forming a string of<tei:lb/>beads which follow the curve of the left side of the body so that it is C-shaped. Anteriorly<tei:lb/>there are 2 micronuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. This species has apparently been described on a single occasion.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Uronychia binucleata Young, 1 922<tei:lb/>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 32). Uronychia binucleata is 60-80 urn long. The dorsal surface is arched</tei:p>

         <tei:p>and decorated with many small pits. The peristome is bordered on either side by a large<tei:lb/>undulating membrane and at the anterior left there are 3 delicate sickle-shaped<tei:lb/>membranelles. About 4 paroral membranelles lie in the 'buccal' pocket. There is a long<tei:lb/>slender cirrus accompanying the 3 curved right caudal cirri, 4 transverse, 2 left marginal and<tei:lb/>2 right marginal cirri. The macronucleus is in 3-5 fragments and 2 micronuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. Young (1922) established this species which he showed to be almost identical to U.<tei:lb/>setigera in his comparative study. He distinguished his species by the absence of peristomial<tei:lb/>cirri, the presence of 4 or less transverse cirri, and the presence of a slender right caudal cirrus<tei:lb/>in addition to the two micronuclei. Although Young (1922) described a satellite cirrus in U.<tei:lb/>setigera which he considered to be significant, curiously he failed to mention it in U.<tei:lb/>binucleata even though he illustrated it. Here the presence of two micronuclei is used to<tei:lb/>characterise the species: should future studies show that the number of micronuclei to be<tei:lb/>variable, then it would become synonymous with U. transfuga.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus CERTESIA Fabre-Domergue, 1885</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fabre-Domergue (1885) described an organism that was very similar to Euplotes except that<tei:lb/>it had a row of left marginal cirri, no caudal cirri and the macronucleus was in four parts. In<tei:lb/>his view this was sufficient to create the new genus Certesia although later both Biitschli<tei:lb/>(1 889) and Sauerbrey (1928) considered it to be a subgenus of Euplotes. Since the description<tei:lb/>of the type species, Certesia quadrinucleata Fabre-Domergue, 1885, only one other species,<tei:lb/>Certesia ovata Vacelet, 1960, has been described. Vacelet (1960) distinguished it from the<tei:lb/>type species on account of its smaller size, its more oval shape and its two curved left<tei:lb/>transverse cirri. Here, these differences are not considered to be sufficient to treat C. ovata as<tei:lb/>a separate species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Certesia</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Oval marine hypotrichs with an anterior nose-like projection on the right anterior body<tei:lb/>edge. There is a conspicuous AZM which extends a third to halfway down the left side of the<tei:lb/>body. There are 1 1-13 frontoventral, 5 large transverse, and a variable number (6-1 1) of left<tei:lb/>marginal cirri. There are no caudal cirri. Macronucleus in several parts. Single species genus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>20/jm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 33 Certesia quadrinucleata: (a) after Fabre-Domergue, 1885; (b) after Sauerbrey, 1928; (c)<tei:lb/>after Kahl, 1932;(d, e) after Vacelet, 1960 (called C. ovata).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>228 C. R. CURDS &amp; I. C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species description</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Certesia quadrinucleata Fabre-Domergue, 1885<tei:lb/>Certesia ovata Vacelet, 1 960</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 33). Certesia quadrinucleata is a medium sized (75-80 fim long), oval,<tei:lb/>rigid and colourless marine hypotrich. The dorsal surface is arched and smooth: the ventral<tei:lb/>surface flat. The right body border is convex and the left may be slightly concave. There is a<tei:lb/>distinct nose-like projection on the right of the extreme anterior body edge. The peristome is<tei:lb/>a third to a half of the body length. The AZM consists of about 5 large membranelles along<tei:lb/>the anterior border and 1 5-20 smaller ones along the left edge of the peristome. There are<tei:lb/>1 1-13 frontoventral cirri, 5 large transverse cirri, a row of 6-1 1 left marginal cirri but no<tei:lb/>caudal cirri. Four ovoid macronuclei are located in pairs, one pair on the right anterior and<tei:lb/>one pair on the left below the AZM.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus GASTROCIRRHUS Lepsi, 1928</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lepsi (1928) created the genus Gastrocirrhus and stated that the type species Gastrocirrhus<tei:lb/>intermedium Lepsi, 1928 possessed characters that were intermediate between the oligotrichs<tei:lb/>and the hypotrichs. The organism had ventral cirri arranged in groups like those of<tei:lb/>hypotrichs, but its large anterior funnel-shaped peristome and spiral AZM were more like<tei:lb/>those of oligotrich genera such as Stentor. Kahl (1932) thought that the organism was<tei:lb/>probably a piece of a fragmented Oxytricha, but soon after Bullington (1940) established<tei:lb/>another species Gastrocirrhus stentoreus Bullington, 1940. This has been followed by several<tei:lb/>other species descriptions.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The six cirri along the right side of G. intermedius are here interpreted to be frontoventrals<tei:lb/>and the remaining ten cirri are called caudals. Bullington (1940) recorded the presence of<tei:lb/>four marginals, four ventral and two oral/anterior cirri on G. stentoreus. These are<tei:lb/>considered to be frontoventral cirri so that this species may be diagnosed by the presence of<tei:lb/>only five caudal cirri. In Gastrocirrhus adhaerens Faure-Fremiet, 1954, there are 16 cirri<tei:lb/>arranged in two rows on the right of the peristome, and 12 in a semi-circle at the posterior.<tei:lb/>Here, these are interpreted to be frontoventral and caudal cirri respectively. Gastrocirrhus<tei:lb/>trichocystus Ito, 1958 has 18 frontoventral and 13 caudal cirri arranged similarly to those in<tei:lb/>G. adhaerens but the species is characterised by the presence of zones of trichocysts<tei:lb/>(mucocysts?) on the dorsal and ventral surfaces.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Faure-Fremiet (1961) created the family Gastrocirrhidae and included the single species<tei:lb/>genus Cirrhogaster Ozaki &amp; Yagui, 1942 in the family. The type species, Cirrhogaster<tei:lb/>monilifer Ozaki &amp; Yagui, 1942 is similar to G. adhaerens, but the former species has 10<tei:lb/>rather than 16 frontoventral cirri. Dragesco (1965) suggested that these two species were<tei:lb/>synonymous but here, although C. monilifer is considered to be a species of Gastrocirrhus it<tei:lb/>is not considered to be a synonym of G. adhaerens.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Gastrocirrhus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Marine hypotrichs that may be dorso vent rally flattened or cup-shaped. There is a large<tei:lb/>anterior funnel-shaped peristome which opens both anteriorly and ventrally. A well<tei:lb/>developed AZM borders the C-shaped anterior body edge and winds anti-clockwise down the<tei:lb/>left of the peristome. There are 5-18 frontoventral cirri, usually arranged in two oblique<tei:lb/>rows on the right of the peristome: 5-13 caudal cirri along the posterior pole of the body<tei:lb/>which in some species curve forwards ventrally on the left to give the appearance of<tei:lb/>transverse cirri. Macronucleus may be oval or moniliform in 11-15 pieces.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Gastrocirrhus intermedius Lepsi, 1928</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 34). This species is a dorso ventral ly flattened cup-shape, approximately<tei:lb/>70 jim long. The well developed AZM borders the anterior funnel-shaped peristome. There<tei:lb/>are 16 cirri in three distinct groups. Near the right body margin there are 6 frontoventral<tei:lb/>cirri. The caudals are situated along the posterior pole, comprised of a group of 3 sickle-<tei:lb/>shaped cirri, rather like the right caudal cirri in Diophrys, and 7 others to their left which are<tei:lb/>smaller and straighten The macron ucleus is oval.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. There is only a single description of this, the type species of the genus. The<tei:lb/>observations of Lepsi (1928) appear to have been made exclusively on living specimens. The<tei:lb/>arrangement of the cirri and nuclear features do not appear to be as typical as those of the<tei:lb/>species described later.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gastrocirrhus stentoreus Bullington, 1940</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 35). Gastrocirrhus stentoreus is a medium sized (100 urn long, 70-80 um<tei:lb/>wide) perfectly cup-shaped species. The large funnel-shaped peristome has a ventral,<tei:lb/>elongate opening about two-thirds of the body length and is bordered by a prominent AZM.<tei:lb/>There are 1 1 frontoventral cirri on the right of the peristome in groups of two, four and five<tei:lb/>cirri. At the posterior right margin, there are 5 sickle-shaped caudal cirri.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Gastrocirrhus trichcystus Ito, 1958</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 38). Gastrocirrhus trichocystus is 90-103 um long, 82-90 urn wide and<tei:lb/>cup-shaped. The ventral surface becomes flattened when starved. The large peristome is<tei:lb/>bordered by numerous long adoral membranelles. There are 18 frontoventral and 13 caudal<tei:lb/>cirri separated by longitudinal ridges on the ventral surface. The sickle-shaped macronucleus<tei:lb/>is moniliform with 10-12 pieces. There are usually 8 micronuclei. The species is<tei:lb/>characterised by bands of 'trichocysts' (mucocysts?) along the anterior and right margin of<tei:lb/>the dorsal surface, also they are found along the left margin and at the anterior right on the<tei:lb/>ventral surface.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Genus EUPLOTASPIS Chatton &amp; Seguela, 1936</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Chatton &amp; Seguela (1936) described a Euplotes-like hypotrich found in the branchial cavity<tei:lb/>of the sea squirt, dona intestinalis. They noted that the organisms were never observed on<tei:lb/>or around their host and they did not survive without their host for more than 36 hours in a<tei:lb/>medium which had been used successfully for the culture of many marine Euplotes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This species was found to have many characters similar to those of Euplotes but Chatton &amp;<tei:lb/>Seguela (1936) noted that its body shape and very thick short cirri were more like those of<tei:lb/>Aspidisca. Furthermore, the AZM was entirely ventral so that it did not border the anterior<tei:lb/>dorsal surface as in Euplotes, and four of the frontoventral cirri were split into two parts<tei:lb/>which is a distinctive feature. Corliss (1961) placed this genus along with Paraeuplotes in the<tei:lb/>family Paraeuplotidae but later (Corliss, 1977) he transferred it to the family Aspidiscidae.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>233</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Euplotaspis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Marine oval hypotrichs living in sea squirts. There is a prominent AZM restricted to the<tei:lb/>ventral surface. There are 9 frontoventral, 5 transverse and 34 caudal cirri. The four<tei:lb/>frontoventral cirri towards the right body border are split longitudinally into two parts.<tei:lb/>Macronucleus C-shaped. The dorsal argyrome is like that of Euplotes vannus (see Curds,<tei:lb/>1975).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species description</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Euplotaspis cionaecola Chatton &amp; Seguela, 1936</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 39). Euplotaspis cionaecola is a 60-70 um long marine hypotrich found in</tei:p>

         <tei:p>the branchial cavity ofCiona intestinalis. The body outline is perfectly oval, convex dorsally<tei:lb/>and flattened or slightly concave ventrally. The AZM of about 50 membranelles, is a<tei:lb/>prominent band parallel to, and at a short distance from, the left body margin. There are 10<tei:lb/>short, wide frontoventral cirri: the four on the right being split longitudinally. The 5<tei:lb/>transverse cirri are separated by marked ridges on the ventral surface. The 3-4 caudal cirri<tei:lb/>are small and indistinct in vivo but can be seen in silver impregnated specimens. The dorsal<tei:lb/>silver-line system consists of 7-8 dorsal kinetics, each carrying about 1 5 cilia, with simple<tei:lb/>cross-links between the kinetics.          <tei:p>Genus EUPLOTIDIUM Noland, 1937</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Noland (1937) described a hypotrich found in sponges from the Gulf of Mexico and stated<tei:lb/>that 'the organism differs from Euplotes, its nearest relative, in the absence of caudal cirri,<tei:lb/>and in the more cylindrical shape of the body'. Consequently, he erected the genus<tei:lb/>Euplotidium Noland, 1937 and called the organism Euplotidium agitatum Noland, 1937<tei:lb/>because of its erratic movements. Ito (1958) also described a cylindrical Euplotes-\ike<tei:lb/>organism, but in this species there was one left caudal cirrus and more frontoventral and<tei:lb/>transverse cirri than in E. agitatum, this he called Euplotidium itoi Ito, 1958. A further<tei:lb/>species with different numbers of frontoventral and transverse cirri, Euplotidium arenarium<tei:lb/>Magagnini &amp; Nobili, 1964, was later described and Borror (1972) transferred Euplotes<tei:lb/>psammophilus Vacelet, 1961 to the genus. More recently Hartwig (1980) added another<tei:lb/>species to the genus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Euplotidium</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Small to large (65-200 um long) marine hypotrichs. Outline shape slightly elongated oval.<tei:lb/>Rounded in cross-section, never dorso ventrally flattened. The peristome is a wide funnel-<tei:lb/>shape with a prominent AZM that borders both the anterior semi-circular body edge and the<tei:lb/>left margin of the peristome. There are 7-12 frontoventral, 5-6 transverse and, when present,<tei:lb/>a reduced number (1-2) of caudal cirri.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Key to the species of Euplotidium</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 With less than 10 frontoventral cirri 2</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With 10 or more frontoventral cirri 4</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2 With 9 frontoventral cirri but without caudal cirri E. agitatum</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With 7 frontoventral and 1 or 2 caudal cirri 3</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3 With 1 caudal cirrus, macronucleus in many parts E. helgae</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With 2 caudal cirri, macronucleus elongate E. psammophilus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>4 With 10 frontoventral and more than 1 caudal cirri E. arenarium</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With 12 frontoventrals and 1 caudal cirrus E. itoi</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species descriptions</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Euplotidium agitatum Noland, 1937</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 40). This, the type species, is 65-95 um long and its cylindrical body<tei:lb/>shape is most apparent when viewed from the anterior. There is a funnel-shaped peristome<tei:lb/>which is lined around its anterior rim and left side by a prominent AZM. There are<tei:lb/>approximately 40 membranelles on the anterior part and about the same number in the</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>

         <tei:p>ventral part of the AZM. There are 9 frontoventral cirri, aligned in 2 rows, near the right<tei:lb/>body edge and 5 conspicuous transverse cirri but caudal cirri are not present.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Euplotidium itoi Ito, 1958</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 41). This is a medium sized (89-95 um long), cylindrical hypotrich with a<tei:lb/>slightly flattened ventral surface. The AZM borders the anterior and left edges of the<tei:lb/>peristome forming a sigmoid shape. Nine of the 12 frontoventral cirri are in 2 oblique rows<tei:lb/>situated near the anterior right of the peristome while the other 3 are scattered along the right<tei:lb/>body edge. There are 6 large transverse cirri and a single small left caudal cirrus. The<tei:lb/>macronucleus is in 2 ribbon-like parts with 4 micronuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. The position of the reorganisation band in Ito's (1958) diagram indicates that it could<tei:lb/>have been at some stage of division. Originally found in seaweed in the Inland Sea of Japan.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Euplotidium arenarium Magagnini &amp; Nobili, 1964<tei:lb/>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 42). This is a medium sized (71-120 um long) oval species with a wide</tei:p>

         <tei:p>236</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I. C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 41 Euplotidium itoi, after Ito, 1958.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>triangular peristome. The AZM consists of about 75 membranelles and it extends around the<tei:lb/>anterior semi-circular rim of the peristome down to the left side. There are 10 fron to ventral s,<tei:lb/>5 transversals and a single left caudal cirrus. The transverse cirri are not conspicuous as in<tei:lb/>the other species of the genus and could be overlooked. The macronucleus is moniliform, in<tei:lb/>5-10 pieces, and is curved towards the right. The silver-line system consists of 2 marginal<tei:lb/>kinetics confluent posteriorly and there are dorsal and ventral mesh-like argyromes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. Originally isolated from sand in the Gulf of Naples.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Euplotidium psammophilus (Vacelet, 1961)Borror, 1972<tei:lb/>Euplotes psammophilus Vacelet, 1 96 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 43). This is a large (125 urn long) species in which the wide peristome<tei:lb/>extends about two-thirds down the length of the body. There are 7 frontoventral cirri<tei:lb/>arranged in 2 groups on the right of the peristome. Three are closely packed together at the<tei:lb/>anterior and 4 are in a row behind them. The 5 transverse cirri are long and there are 2<tei:lb/>caudal cirri. Vacelet (1961) also described the presence of a row of short cilia along the<tei:lb/>posterior right body edge. The elongated curved macronucleus is rod-like with an adjacent<tei:lb/>micronucleus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTES. This was originally described as a species of Euplotes', Borror (1972) transferred it to<tei:lb/>Euplotidium on account of the shape of the body and peristome and because of the reduced<tei:lb/>number of caudal cirri.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus PARAEUPLOTES Wichlerman, 1942</tei:p>
         <tei:p>239</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Wichterman (1942) described a hypotrichous ciliate which he found in abundance on the<tei:lb/>coral Eunicea crassa in the Tortugas. The species resembled Euplotes in that it had a well<tei:lb/>developed AZM and a C-shaped macronucleus, but its rather peculiar ciliature bore no<tei:lb/>resemblance to that of Euplotes. He called the genus Paraeuplotes Wichterman, 1942 and<tei:lb/>placed it in a new family, the Paraeuplotidae Wichterman, 1942. Here, following Horror<tei:lb/>(1972) and Corliss (1977) the genus is provisionally included in the Euplotidae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Paraeuplotes</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discoid marine hypotrich with a well-developed AZM which originates on the dorsal surface<tei:lb/>and curves down three-quarters of the body length on the ventral surface. Undulating<tei:lb/>membrane absent. There are no marginal cirri but there is a small group of short caudal cirri.<tei:lb/>There is an extensive arc of transverse cirri parallel with the right body edge. Anteriorly,<tei:lb/>there is an arc of cilia parallel with the apical body edge and an isolated pair of cirri in the<tei:lb/>midventral position. The macronucleus is C-shaped. Contains numerous zooxanthellae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>20&gt;um</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 45 Paraeuplotes tortugensis, after Wichterman, 1942: (a) ventral surface; (b) dorsal surface;</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(c) nucleus.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>240 C. R. CURDS &amp; I. C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species description</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Paraeuplotes tortugensis Wichterman, 1942</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 45). Small to medium discoid species measuring on average 80 um in<tei:lb/>diameter. The well-developed AZM forms a collar-like structure on the anterior dorsal<tei:lb/>surface which extends ventrally down about three-quarters of the body length. There is no<tei:lb/>undulating membrane present. Anteriorly there is an arc of cilia parallel with the body edge<tei:lb/>and a pair of isolated cirri lying in a midventral position. There is an extensive arc of<tei:lb/>transverse cirri parallel with the right body edge and a group of 5-6 short caudal cirri slightly<tei:lb/>to the right of the posterior body pole. The macronucleus is C-shaped. The cell is packed<tei:lb/>with numerous yellow-brown zooxanthellae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. Originally isolated from the coral Eunice crassa in the Tortugas.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus SWEDMARKIA Dragesco, 1954</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dragesco (1954, 1960, 1965) studied and described this genus over a period of several years.<tei:lb/>He (Dragesco, 1960, 1965) likened the genus to Euplotidium and Gastrocirrhus and<tei:lb/>considered it to be a possible evolutionary link between the Holostichidae Faure-Fremiet,<tei:lb/>1961 and the Euplotidae. Both Faure-Fremiet (1961) and Corliss (1977) placed Swedmarkia<tei:lb/>in the family Gastrocirrhidae but it is here provisionally included in the Euplotidae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Swedmarkia</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Medium sized (100-1 10 jim long) marine hypotrichs with conspicuous AZM arranged<tei:lb/>around a wide triangular peristome. There are two particularly long membranelles at the left<tei:lb/>of the apex of the peristome. A wide undulating membrane lies on the right of the peristome.<tei:lb/>There are many (54-58) cirri present with the following distribution. Numerous<tei:lb/>frontoventrals include a row along the right peristome edge, an apical group and an irregular<tei:lb/>midventral row. There are 5 large transverse and rows of right and left marginal cirri that are<tei:lb/>confluent posteriorly. The macronucleus is divided into numerous (about 100) portions and<tei:lb/>there are 5-9 micronuclei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species description</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Swedmarkia arenicola Dragesco, 1954</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 46). This is a medium sized (100-1 10 urn long) marine hypotrich, similar<tei:lb/>in shape to Euplotes. The body is oval to triangular in shape and there is a large triangular<tei:lb/>peristome which extends down three-quarters of the body length where it occupies about half<tei:lb/>of the ventral surface. The AZM consists of 54-68 membranelles of which two, at the<tei:lb/>extreme left apex of the peristome, are particularly long. There is a wide undulating<tei:lb/>membrane on the right peristome border. The total of 54-58 cirri are arranged as follows, a<tei:lb/>row of fron to ventral (peristomial) cirri is spaced evenly along the right peristomial edge. A<tei:lb/>group of 6 frontoventral cirri are situated around the extreme apex of the cell and there is an<tei:lb/>irregular row of 5 frontoventrals lying in the midventral position. Additionally, a short row<tei:lb/>of left marginal cirri is present with an irregularly spaced row of right marginals, of which the<tei:lb/>anterior half arise from the dorsal surface. The marginals are continuous along the posterior<tei:lb/>border. The macronucleus is divided into numerous (about 100) spherical pieces and there<tei:lb/>are 5-9 micronuclei. The three dorsal kinetics bearing double cilia are illustrated in Fig. 46b.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>241</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 46 Swedmarkia arenicola, after Dragesco, 1965: (a) ventral surface; (b) dorsal surface; (c, d)<tei:lb/>ventral surface showing variations in form, number and arrangement of certain cirri.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>242</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I. C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus GRUBERELLA (Gruber, 1884) Corliss, 1960</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Stylocoma Gruber, 1 884<tei:lb/>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gruber (1884) described the marine hypotrich Stylocoma oviformis Gruber, 1884 which<tei:lb/>Kahl (1932) redescribed and placed in the family Euplotidae. Soon after, another species,<tei:lb/>Stylocoma adriatica Kiesselbach, 1936, which lacked transverse cirri, was described. Corliss<tei:lb/>(1960) pointed out that the generic name was preoccupied by Stylocoma Lioy, 1864 a<tei:lb/>dipteran insect, and proposed that it should be replaced by Gruberella Corliss, 1960. Borror<tei:lb/>(1972) considered the genus to be of questionable status but Corliss (1977) included it as an<tei:lb/>incertae sedis in the Sporadotrichina. Here, the genus is provisionally included in the<tei:lb/>Euplotidae on account of its funnel-like peristome, which is reminiscent of Gastrocirrhus,<tei:lb/>and the arrangement of cirri which, although reduced, are more like those in the Euplotidae<tei:lb/>than in any other family of hypotrichs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Gruberella</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ovoid, marine hypotrichs, rounded in cross-section. There is a centrally placed funnel-<tei:lb/>shaped peristome bordered by a conspicuous AZM anteriorly and down the left side. Cirri<tei:lb/>reduced. Caudal cirri present, transverse cirri may or may not be present. Frontoventral and<tei:lb/>marginal cirri absent. Macronucleus in two parts.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Key to the species of Gruberella</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 With 6 caudal and 7 transverse cirri .<tei:lb/>With 6 caudal but without transverse cirri</tei:p>
         <tei:p>G. oviformis<tei:lb/>G. adriatica</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species descriptions</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gruberella oviformis (Gruber, 1 884) Corliss, 1960<tei:lb/>Stylocoma oviformis Gruber, 1 884</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 47). Ovoid marine hypotrich with wide, centrally placed, funnel-shaped<tei:lb/>peristome which has a slightly raised edge forming an anterior collar-like region. The</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 47 Gruberella oviformis: (a) after Gruber, 1 884; (b) after Kisselbach, 1936.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>REVIEW OF EUPLOTIDAE 243</tei:p>
         <tei:p>prominent AZM consisting of many large membranelles originates on the ventral surface on<tei:lb/>the right of the peristome and continues around the apex down the left side. There are only 2<tei:lb/>groups of cirri, 7 transverse and 6 long caudal cirri. No frontoventral or marginal cirri are<tei:lb/>present. Shape of macronucleus unrecorded.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gmberella adriatica (Kisselbach, 1936) Corliss, 1960<tei:lb/>Stylocoma adriatica Kiesselbach, 1936</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 48). Medium sized (80 um long) triangular shaped marine hypotrich with<tei:lb/>funnel-shaped, centrally positioned peristome. Prominent AZM borders the anterior and left<tei:lb/>peristomial edges. Cirri reduced to a single group of 6 long caudals. There are no transverse,<tei:lb/>frontoventral or marginal cirri present. Macronucleus divided into 2 ovoid pieces with a<tei:lb/>micronucleus between the pair.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 48 Gruberella adriatica, after Kisselbach, 1 936.<tei:lb/>Genus CYATHAROIDESTuffrau, 1975</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Tuffrau (1975) described a species from Antarctica which closely resembled Euplotes in<tei:lb/>certain respects but not in others. It is characterised by the presence of a row of 12 large right<tei:lb/>marginal cirri in addition to the usual frontoventral and transverse cirri. Furthermore, there<tei:lb/>is an extensive paroral membrane composed of a single kinety of long cilia which is unlike<tei:lb/>the undulating membrane of Euplotes and there is also endoral ciliature which is not found<tei:lb/>in Euplotes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Cyatharoides</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Irregularly oval marine hypotrich with a very large peristomial funnel occupying much of<tei:lb/>the ventral surface onto which it opens. The peristome is surrounded by a collar-like<tei:lb/>swelling of the anterior left body edge, with a characteristic 'niche' or invagination of the<tei:lb/>right anterior peristome region. The AZM is composed of many membranelles lining the left<tei:lb/>peristomial border. On the right, at the bottom of the peristomial funnel there is a paroral<tei:lb/>membrane composed of a single kinety of long cilia and an arc of endoral cilia. There are 10<tei:lb/>frontoventral, 5 transverse and a row of 1 2 right marginal cirri along the right body edge.<tei:lb/>Dorsally there are 12-26 kinetics of cilia and the macronucleus is C-shaped.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>244</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. R. CURDS &amp; I. C. H. WU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species description</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cyatharoides balechi Tuffrau, 1 975</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 49). Irregularly oval, large (140-200 urn long) marine, planktonic<tei:lb/>hypotrich with a very large peristomial funnel opening over much of the ventral cell surface.<tei:lb/>Anteriorly the peristome is surrounded by a collar-like swollen ridge of the anterior left body<tei:lb/>edge, with a characteristic invagination of the peristome on the right anterior edge. The<tei:lb/>AZM is prominent and composed of many large membranelles. There are paroral and<tei:lb/>endoral membranes on the posterior right peristomial edge. With 10 frontoventral, 5<tei:lb/>transverse and 12 right marginal cirri. Dorsal surface with 12-26 kinetics of many cilia.<tei:lb/>Macronucleus large, open C-shaped.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. Originally isolated from plankton sample taken from Antarctic Ocean near base of<tei:lb/>Argentina by Prof. Balech.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>An Atlas of Freshwater Testate Amoebae</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. G. Ogden &amp; R. H. Hedley</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1980, Hardcovers, 222pp, 17.50 (18.00 by post). Co-published by British Museum<tei:lb/>(Natural History) and Oxford University Press.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This book illustrates, using scanning electron micrographs, most of the common<tei:lb/>species of testate amoebae that are found in freshwater habitats. Information on<tei:lb/>the biology, ecology, geographical distribution and a classification are followed by<tei:lb/>descriptions of ninety-five species. Each of these is illustrated by several views of<tei:lb/>the shell.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The text is designed not only to enable biologists to identify species of testate<tei:lb/>amoebae, but to serve as an introduction to students interested in the taxonomy<tei:lb/>and biology of these freshwater protozoa. It will be of special interest to<tei:lb/>protozoologists, ecologists, limnologists, water treatment specialists and<tei:lb/>micropalaeontologists interested in recent sediments.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>The Bulletin of the British Museum (Natural History), instituted in 1949, is issued in four<tei:lb/>scientific series, Botany, Entomology, Geology (incorporating Mineralogy) and Zoology, and<tei:lb/>an Historical series.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Papers in the Bulletin are primarily the results of research carried out on the unique and<tei:lb/>ever-growing collections of the Museum, both by the scientific staff of the Museum and by<tei:lb/>specialists from elsewhere who make use of the Museum's resources. Many of the papers are<tei:lb/>works of reference that will remain indispensable for years to come.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parts are published at irregular intervals as they become ready, each is complete in itself,<tei:lb/>available separately, and individually priced. Volumes contain about 300 pages and several<tei:lb/>volumes may appear within a calendar year.          <tei:p>The Ophthalmotilapia assemblage of cichlid fishes<tei:lb/>reconsidered</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Peter Humphry Greenwood^</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Roa<tei:lb/>SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Contents</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Synopsis 249</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction 249</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Material examined 250</tei:p>

         <tei:p>The Ophthalmotilapia assemblage reconsidered 262</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Relationships within the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage .... 263<tei:lb/>The nature of the apophysis for the upper pharyngeal bones in the</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ophthalmotilapia assemblage 274</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Ophthalmotilapia assemblage 275</tei:p>

         <tei:p>problem in these and other lake cichlids 278</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Cyathopharynx Regan, 1920 284</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Synopsis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Ophthalmotilapia assemblage of cichlid fishes from Lake Tanganyika, first defined by Liem<tei:lb/>(1981), originally comprised the genera Asprotilapia, Ectodus, Lestradea, Cunningtonia and<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia (with which was synonymized Ophthalmochromis). The characters on which the<tei:lb/>assemblage was based are reviewed and revised in the light of information derived from large-scale<tei:lb/>outgroup comparisons.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As a result of this review, five additional genera can be included in the assemblage (viz.<tei:lb/>Aulonocranus, Grammatotria, Callochromis, Xenotilapia and Cyathopharynx (with which Cardio-<tei:lb/>pharynx is synonymized). Two lineages within the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage are defined.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Previous schemes of supposed relationships for these taxa are discussed, as is the problem of<tei:lb/>identifying the sister-group for the assemblage. No sister-group can be identified amongst the endemic<tei:lb/>taxa of Lake Tanganyika, but the possibility of an endemic taxon from Lake Malawi being the sister-<tei:lb/>group is explored.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Problems arising from possible homoplasy and thus the misidentification of sister-groups are<tei:lb/>illustrated by examples involving cichlid species from Lakes Tanganyika and Malawi, and from these<tei:lb/>lakes and Lake Fwa (Zaire drainage basin).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>During the last thirty years there has been a marked increase in our knowledge of taxonomic<tei:lb/>and ecological diversity amongst African cichlid fishes. Unfortunately there has been less<tei:lb/>progress made in our understanding of phyletic relationships between the various elements<tei:lb/>of those faunas, or even amongst members of the so-called species flocks of the major African<tei:lb/>lakes (see Greenwood, 1980).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mm. nat. Hist. (Zool.) 44 (4): 249-290 Issued 28 April 1 983</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>250 P- H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Two recent papers are welcome and important contributions to the field of phyletic<tei:lb/>studies since both are concerned with interrelationships in a single species flock, that of Lake<tei:lb/>Tanganyika (Liem &amp; Stewart, 1976;Liem, 1981).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem's (1981) paper is directed at establishing the monophyletic origin of five endemic<tei:lb/>genera from that lake, viz. Asprotilapia, Ectodus, Lestradea, Cunningtonia and Ophthalmo-<tei:lb/>tilapia. Although Liem was able to argue a case for recognizing the monophyly of<tei:lb/>these taxa (the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage), and the intragroup relationships of its<tei:lb/>constituent genera, he was unable to recognize a sister-group for the whole assemblage. He<tei:lb/>did, however, suggest that among the endemic Tanganyika genera, Aulonocranus,<tei:lb/>Xenotilapia, Callochromis and Cardiopharynx share some of the derived features<tei:lb/>characterizing the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage (Liem, 1981 : 206; 208).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>My interest in these species, and the Ophthalmotilapia lineage, stems from my current<tei:lb/>research into the levels of relationship existing between the endemic cichlid genera of Lakes<tei:lb/>Victoria, Malawi and Tanganyika (Greenwood, 1979; 1980). Using information acquired in<tei:lb/>this search, it seemed that Liem's concept of the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage could be<tei:lb/>extended to include several other Tanganyika taxa, including those suspected of such<tei:lb/>relationship by Liem. It was also apparent that the group characters for the assemblage<tei:lb/>should be reviewed in the light of more extensive outgroup comparisons than were employed<tei:lb/>originally.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Finally, my interest was aroused by what seemed to be the unusually clear light that some<tei:lb/>members of the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage could throw on the longstanding and often<tei:lb/>intractable problem of homoplasy and its effects on hypotheses of relationship amongst<tei:lb/>African cichlid fishes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Like Liem, I have been unable to identify a sister-group for the Ophthalmotilapia<tei:lb/>assemblage from amongst the Tanganyika cichlids. A very tentative suggestion can be made,<tei:lb/>however, for a possible sister-group relationship between the assemblage and certain<tei:lb/>members of the Lake Malawi flock.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Material examined</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dissections were made of the dorsal gill-arch musculature in one, or usually 2, specimens of<tei:lb/>each genus now included in the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage (see p. 278). The specimen of<tei:lb/>Asprotilapia leptura (BMNH 1906.9.6: 157) was that used by Liem (1981) but the jaw<tei:lb/>muscles of the left side were freshly dissected.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The nature of the gut and its coiling pattern were checked in several specimens of each<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia assemblage species (except Asprotilapia leptura where only the type and<tei:lb/>the specimen noted above could be used).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Jaw and dorsal gill-arch muscles were dissected in unregistered specimens of Astatotilapia<tei:lb/>elegans and in A. burtoni.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>All available dry skeletal material in the BM(NH) collections was examined, in particular<tei:lb/>that prepared for the revisions of the Lake Victoria, Edward-George, Kivu, and Turkana<tei:lb/>haplochromine species (see Greenwood, 1980). Additional material, prepared for this<tei:lb/>paper, and alizarin transparencies not previously listed, are given below. The taxa are<tei:lb/>first grouped geographically, and then alphabetically, within the categories: Dry skeleton<tei:lb/>(DS) and Alizarin transparency (AT).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>A review of group characters for the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As originally defined by Liem (1981), the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage 1 comprised the<tei:lb/>following genera: Asprotilapia Blgr (1901), Ectodus Blgr (1898), Lestradea Poll (1943),<tei:lb/>Cunningtonia Blgr (1906) and Ophthalmotilapia Pellegrin (1904). Ophthalmochromis Poll<tei:lb/>(1956) was shown by Liem (1981 : 210) to be a synonym of Ophthalmotilapia, a decision<tei:lb/>with which I fully agree.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem's concept of the OA, and his grounds for considering it a monophyletic group, were<tei:lb/>based on eight uniquely congruent apomorphic characters present in all the constituent taxa<tei:lb/>(Liem, 1981 : 207-208). With one possible exception, however, Liem believed that none of<tei:lb/>these characters is an autapomorphy for the group (Liem's character 2, the outline shape of<tei:lb/>the palatine bone, is the exception).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The apomorphic status of the eight group-characters was justified by Liem (198 1 : 205) on<tei:lb/>the grounds of their being derived relative to the character state found in various generalized<tei:lb/>taxa (such as Astatotilapia burtoni and A. elegans) and in other taxa from Lake Tanganyika.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>I have been able to extend Liem's outgroup comparisons to include the cichlid genera of<tei:lb/>Lakes Malawi, Victoria and Edward, taxa from various river systems and their associated<tei:lb/>small lakes, and additional genera from Lake Tanganyika itself. Wherever possible, several<tei:lb/>species of a genus were examined.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The review of the eight OA group-characters which follows takes this extra material into<tei:lb/>account. It should be noted, however, that the new members of the 0A (see p. 262) are, for<tei:lb/>the purposes of the review, not treated as elements of the assemblage. Thus, unless otherwise<tei:lb/>indicated, all references to the OA in this section of the paper are to the assemblage as<tei:lb/>originally defined by Liem.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Since the sequence in which group apomorphies are treated here differs somewhat from<tei:lb/>that used by Liem (1981 : 207), the number he gave to a character is given, in square<tei:lb/>brackets, after the number used in this review. The same convention is followed throughout<tei:lb/>the paper.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(1) [1]. The entopterygoid is widely separated from the palatine (see Liem,<tei:lb/>1981: 205-206, 207; fig. 4; and Figs 1 A-F below).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 For the sake of brevity, Liem's shorthand term for the assemblage, the OA, will be used in this paper as well.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAP1A ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES</tei:p>
         <tei:p>255</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Scales '3 mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 1 Suspensoria (left) of: A, Callochromis macrops melanostigma; B, Xenotilapia<tei:lb/>tenuidentata; C, Cyathopharynx furcifer; D, Cyathopharynx schoutedeni (see p. 282); E.<tei:lb/>Grammatotria lemairei; F, Aulonocranus dewindti.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As far as I can determine, this character, except for its occurrence in the Malawian genus<tei:lb/>Lethrinops (and those species from Tanganyika which I propose to include in the 6&gt;A) is<tei:lb/>unique to the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The gap is created, in part, by a reduction in the depth of the entopterygoid, and in part by<tei:lb/>a reduction of its extension above the quadrate. As a. result of these proportional and<tei:lb/>positional changes, the anterior entopterygoidal margin lies below the level of the palatine's<tei:lb/>posterior margin, and below the upper part of the ectopterygoid as well (with both of which<tei:lb/>bones it would otherwise articulate). The space between the three bones (that is, the 'gap') is<tei:lb/>actually filled by dense, translucent connective tissue.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>256 P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It could be argued (and has been argued by Barel, pers. comm.} that the palatopterygoid<tei:lb/>gap is a correlate of the large and often ovoid eye characterizing all members of the OA. This<tei:lb/>is a complex problem to resolve since the influence of eye size and shape on cranial<tei:lb/>architecture is the result of several interacting factors, and there are few data available on the<tei:lb/>dynamics of syncranial ontogeny. In particular we have no information on the influence of<tei:lb/>ultimate eye size on the ontogeny of the whole suspensorium, of which the palatine and<tei:lb/>entopterygoid bones are but a part (see below p. 257).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Taking the maximum diameter of the eye (expressed as a proportion of head length) as a<tei:lb/>measure of eye size it is difficult to find a simple correlation between that ratio and the nature<tei:lb/>of the palatine-entopterygoid articulation. For example, in Hemibates, Trematocara,<tei:lb/>Reganochromis, certain ' 'Limnochromis'' species and in Perissodus (all from Lake<tei:lb/>Tanganyika) the eye is as large as that in members of the OA, and the eye in Trematocara is<tei:lb/>as markedly ovoid as it is in any member of the OA. Yet, in none of these species is there a<tei:lb/>palatopterygoid gap. (Recently, Poll (1981) has revised the genus Limnochromis which he<tei:lb/>now divides into four genera. Since several of the older references in the literature are just to<tei:lb/>the genus Limnochromis, I have adopted the convention of referring to the taxon as<tei:lb/>'Limnochromis', except when reference is made to species retained by Poll (1981) in that<tei:lb/>genus.)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Conversely, in many species of the Malawian genus Lethrinops (Fig. 15) there is a<tei:lb/>palatopterygoid gap (albeit a less extensive one than in some though not all OA species), but<tei:lb/>the eye is smaller than in members of the OA.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Since, amongst the taxa with enlarged eyes only the OA species have developed a<tei:lb/>palatopterygoid gap, the gap would seem to be a shared derived character for the assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>That argument would hold even if future research shows the 'gap' to be one of several<tei:lb/>correlated features associated with the evolution of a large eye. The presence of a gap in<tei:lb/>Lethrinops (which does not have enlarged eyes), on the other hand, fails to support any<tei:lb/>argument suggesting that eye size and a palatopterygoid gap are necessarily correlated.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(2) [3]. The slender hyomandibula has a long symplectic process and no, or a very<tei:lb/>reduced, hyomandibular flange.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>On the basis of extensive outgroup comparisons I cannot agree with Liem on the relative<tei:lb/>length of the symplectic process. In fact, most OA species have a process which is no longer<tei:lb/>than that in Astatotilapia burtoni, A. elegans (or in other Astatotilapia species), or that in<tei:lb/>other Tanganyika genera (Fig. 2). Unfortunately this character is difficult to quantify and is<tei:lb/>one affected by the level to which the anterior margin of the hyomandibular flange is<tei:lb/>produced ventrally. To this extent the apparent length of the process is an optical illusion. It<tei:lb/>is also affected by the stoutness of the flange which, in this region of the bone, is easily<tei:lb/>damaged; if partially broken its absence may add to the apparent length of the symplectic<tei:lb/>process.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As compared with the hyomandibular flange in many taxa (both within and outside Lake<tei:lb/>Tanganyika) that in all OA species is reduced. But, I would question that it is ever absent,<tei:lb/>even in those species with the greatest reduction in flange area.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A reduction in flange area comparable with that found in OA species does occur in several<tei:lb/>other species as well, and these are mostly taxa with large eyes. Thus, amongst the Lake<tei:lb/>Tanganyika endemics a reduced OA-like flange is found in Hemibates, Trematocara,<tei:lb/>Haplotaxodon, Grammatotria, Callochromis, Xenotilapia, Cardiopharynx, Cyathopharynx<tei:lb/>and Aulonocara. In contrast, Reganochromis calliurus, whose eye is as large as that in some<tei:lb/>OA species, has a moderately well-developed flange which is larger than that in any OA<tei:lb/>species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The hyomandibular flange is not reduced in Lethrinops (Lake Malawi) nor in any of the<tei:lb/>species examined whose modal eye size (i.e. eye diameter as a proportion of head length) is<tei:lb/>less than that of any OA species.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES</tei:p>
         <tei:p>257</tei:p>
         <tei:p>i mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 2 Hyomandibula (right), in lateral view, of: A, Ectodus descampsi; B, Astatotilapia</tei:p>
         <tei:p>macropsoides.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is thus some suggestion that a reduction in hyomandibular flange area may be<tei:lb/>correlated with a large eye size. This correlation could result, ontogenetically, from the<tei:lb/>developing eye preempting some of the space which otherwise would be available for the<tei:lb/>levator arcus palatini muscle whose origin is principally from the flange. It is noteworthy<tei:lb/>that in those species with a reduced flange, the levator arcus palatini has, relatively speaking,<tei:lb/>a reduced volume, and that its origin has shifted largely to the metapterygoid (Liem,<tei:lb/>1981 : 19 5-6; personal observations).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Incidentally (pace Liem, 1981 : 207), in three of the four Oreochromis niloticus specimens<tei:lb/>examined, hyomandibular flange area is proportionately equivalent to that in the OA<tei:lb/>species; in the fourth specimen it is noticeably larger. The two smallest O. niloticus (ca. 42<tei:lb/>and 52 mm standard length) have the narrowest flanges of the four specimens examined,<tei:lb/>again suggesting that flange area may be related to eye size since the smallest fishes have<tei:lb/>proportionately the largest eyes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(3) [5] The vertical depth of the metapterygoid is shallow.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is difficult to assess the value of this character as an indicator of phyletic relationships.<tei:lb/>The metapterygoid is noticeably shallow in all the OA species recognized by Liem, and in<tei:lb/>those which I would add to the assemblage. However, an equally shallow or even shallower<tei:lb/>metapterygoid is known from several Tanganyika taxa (for example, in species of<tei:lb/>Lamprologus, Reganochromis, Trematocara, 'Limnochromis', and also in Perissodus<tei:lb/>hecqui, P. microlepis and P. eccentricus). In some of these species the eye is large (equivalent<tei:lb/>in size to those of the OA species), in others it is smaller. From this information it is<tei:lb/>impossible to decide whether a shallow metapterygoid is a correlate of eye enlargement, a<tei:lb/>homoplastic feature, or one which might be indicative of phyletic relationship at a higher<tei:lb/>level than that under consideration.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(4) [2] The posterior and dorsal margins of the palatine form a 90 angle. Elsewhere,<tei:lb/>Liem (1981: 206) expands this statement and notes that The 90 posterodorsal angle<tei:lb/>surrounding a posterodorsal expansion of the palatine is not found in any other<tei:lb/>Tanganyika cichlids and deviates from the condition in generalized cichlids (e.g.<tei:lb/>Astatotilapia burtoni, Liem and Osse, 1975 and A. elegans, Barel, et al, 1976).'</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>258 P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem (1981 : 208) places particular importance on this character, considering it as possibly<tei:lb/>the only autapomorphic feature of the assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>My observations on the OA species indicate that the posterodorsal angle is not always<tei:lb/>rectangular and that it shows some intra- and interspecific variability. Also, I would contest<tei:lb/>the statement that the OA type of palatine shape is not found in other Tanganyika species. It<tei:lb/>does occur, for example, in Limnochromis abeelei, and is closely approached in other species<tei:lb/>as well. Furthermore it occurs in species outside the lake (e.g. Astatotilapia macropsoides<tei:lb/>[Lakes Edward and George] and in some Lethrinops species [Lake Malawi]).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>I would agree, however, that the overall type of palatine morphology in OA species is<tei:lb/>relatively uncommon amongst African cichlids, combining as it does a straight or virtually<tei:lb/>straight posterior margin meeting the dorsal margin at, or almost at, an angle of 90, and with<tei:lb/>a posteriorly expanded body of the bone. In combination these features give to the elongate<tei:lb/>vertical part of the bone a distinctive and near rectangular outline.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Like character (3) [5], the palatine shape is difficult to evaluate as an indicator, or potential<tei:lb/>indicator, of close phylogenetic relationship. However, since a similarly shaped bone is<tei:lb/>rarely present amongst taxa other than members of the OA (and in those genera where it does<tei:lb/>occur it is not manifest by all member species), and since non-OA taxa with this type of<tei:lb/>palatine are not closely related to the OA, it might well indicate a shared common ancestry<tei:lb/>for the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(5) [4]. The anterior margin of the pterosphenoid is notched.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This character is so widely distributed amongst African cichlids (including the most<tei:lb/>generalized taxa) that it cannot be treated as an apomorphy at this level of phyletic analysis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>That the notch, or rather the tongue which delimits one aspect of the notch, has not been<tei:lb/>commented upon before, or been shown in figures of cichlid neurocrania, may well be due to<tei:lb/>its fragility and hence loss during preparation of the skull. (It is of course absent in some<tei:lb/>species and is not invariably present in all members of a genus.) Also, the process can be<tei:lb/>rather small and is then virtually invisible unless the skull is carefully cleaned of connective<tei:lb/>tissue.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pace Liem (1981 : 207), the ligament connecting the sclerea with the pterosphenoid or its<tei:lb/>notch is present in generalized cichlids. In fact, it is present in all the cichlids I have<tei:lb/>dissected, and also in several other teleostean groups as well (including non-percoids).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(6) Liem's two myological characters, viz. [7] the transversus dorsalis is reduced, and [8]<tei:lb/>the obliquus posterior is enlarged, may be taken together.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>At the outset of any discussion it must be made clear that neither character is easily<tei:lb/>assessed, partly because of insufficient comparative data from outgroups, and partly because<tei:lb/>they are not readily quantified and are thus particularly subjective.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the discussion which follows, Asprotilapia is excluded from any generalizations about<tei:lb/>the transversus dorsalis in the OA; Asprotilapia does show unequivocally clear-cut<tei:lb/>reduction of the transversus dorsalis anterior and the posterior head of the muscle is not<tei:lb/>developed at all.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>From my observations on dorsal gill-arch muscles in OA taxa, and in other cichlids from<tei:lb/>Tanganyika and elsewhere (including generalized species such as Astatotilapia elegans, and<tei:lb/>'derived' taxa such as Bathybates, Hemibates and Trematocara (see also Stiassny, 1981), I<tei:lb/>would not consider the condition of the transversus dorsalis or the obliquus posterior in OA<tei:lb/>species to be trenchantly distinct. For example, although Liem (1981 : 207) considers the<tei:lb/>transversus dorsalis complex in OA species to be reduced relative to that in A. elegans, and<tei:lb/>the obliquus posterior to be hypertrophied, I could see no obvious differences when making<tei:lb/>the same comparisons.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>That there are differences in the extent to which these and other dorsal gill-arch muscles<tei:lb/>are developed amongst African cichlids cannot be denied (see for example Liem, 1973; Liem<tei:lb/>&amp; Osse, 1975; Stiassny, 1981). But, the differences are rarely trenchant and in some cases</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOT1LAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES 259</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(including manifestly trenchant ones) are the result of environmentally induced individual<tei:lb/>variations (see Liem &amp; Osse, 1975 : 442, fig. 11; for environmental effects, see Greenwood<tei:lb/>1965).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Thus, until considerably more comparative data are available, and until some means of<tei:lb/>quantifying apparent differences is employed, the use of relative muscle size would seem to<tei:lb/>be of very limited value, and could even be misleading. For those reasons I would not use<tei:lb/>either of the muscle characters in attempting to unravel the phylogeny of the OA (except, as<tei:lb/>noted earlier, with regard to Asprotilapia; see below, p. 263).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(7) [6] The operculum has a distinct auricular process.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although Liem (1981 : 207) notes that a well-developed auricular process occurs on the<tei:lb/>operculum in several taxa, it is only in Perissodus hecqui, 'Limnochromis' dhanisi and the<tei:lb/>six genera now included in the OA (see p. 262) that I would consider the process equivalent<tei:lb/>to that found in the OA species. In all members of the assemblage, the opercular process,<tei:lb/>although interspecifically variable in form and size, is always a noticeable feature of the<tei:lb/>bone. That fact, coupled with the infrequent occurrence of a distinct process amongst other<tei:lb/>genera (and its mosaic interspecific distribution in those taxa), would seem to enhance its<tei:lb/>value as an indicator of monophyletic origin for the OA.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In brief, of the eight supposedly apomorphic characters cited by Liem as suggesting a<tei:lb/>monophyletic origin for the OA, only three would seem to fulfil the necessary requirements<tei:lb/>for such features, and then mainly because of their unique congruence in the species con-<tei:lb/>cerned. The characters, as numbered above, are: the palatopterygoid gap (1), the morphology<tei:lb/>of the palatine bone (4), and the presence of an auricular process on the posterodorsal<tei:lb/>margin of the operculum (7).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The remaining characters (i.e. 2, 3, 5 &amp; 6) are either of no value, are possible homoplasies,<tei:lb/>or cannot be fully evaluated within the scope of our present knowledge.</tei:p>
