         <tei:p>There are, however, two other characters noted by Liem, and used by him at a different<tei:lb/>level of universality (the intragroup level), which I consider to be group apomorphies for<tei:lb/>the whole assemblage. These are:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(8) [15] The morphology of the lachrymal (1st infraorbital) bone.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The lachrymal in members of the OA has a very distinctive appearance (see Liem, 1981,<tei:lb/>fig. 5; and Figs 3A-H), one which, apparently, is not replicated in any other African cichlid.<tei:lb/>It differs from the generalized condition (as seen, for example, in Astatotilapia macropsoides,<tei:lb/>Fig. 3J) in its overall protraction and relative shallowness. Because the anterior portion is<tei:lb/>noticeably deeper than the posterior part, the bone has almost the appearance of a short but<tei:lb/>deep handle extending from its expanded and near rhomboidal anterior region (Figs 3A-H);<tei:lb/>although the anterior region is much deeper than the posterior part, it is by no means as<tei:lb/>expansive as it is in the majority of African cichlids.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>An elongate lachrymal occurs in a few other taxa (e.g. certain 'Limnochromis' species; Fig.<tei:lb/>31) but here the bone is uniformly protracted and so lacks the contrast between its shallower<tei:lb/>posterior third to half and the deeper anterior part, which is so characteristic of the OA type<tei:lb/>lachrymal. Also, in these non-OA taxa with an elongate, or relatively elongate lachrymal,<tei:lb/>the dorsal margin of the bone is but slightly concave, whereas in the OA species it is<tei:lb/>markedly so.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The anterior margin of the lachrymal is straight or very slightly concave, its anteroventral<tei:lb/>angle produced into a slight but distinctive peak (which is lacking in most species with an<tei:lb/>elongate lachrymal but is present in some, e.g, 'Limnochromis' permaxillaris and 'L'.<tei:lb/>pfefferi).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>When the bone is in situ its anterior margin slopes upwards at an angle of 50-60 to the<tei:lb/>horizontal (the 'modal' slope in African cichlids would seem to be one close to the<tei:lb/>horizontal, but there are several and apparently unrelated species where the slope is between<tei:lb/>45 and 60).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>260</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 3 Lachrymal (left), in lateral view, of: A, Cyathopharynx furcifer; B, Callochromis macrops<tei:lb/>melanostigma; C, Xenotilapia tenuicaudata; D, Xenotilapia boulengeri; E, Grammatotria<tei:lb/>lemairei; F, Cyathopharynx schoutedeni (see p. 282); G, Asprotilapia leptura; H, Aulonocranus<tei:lb/>dewindti: I, Limnochromis auritus; J, Astatotilapia macropsoides.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem's figure of the OA species Cunningtonia longiventralis (and his comments on the<tei:lb/>bone, Liem, 1981 : 206, fig. 5C) represents an unusual condition in that species since the<tei:lb/>anterior lachrymal margin is shown as markedly concave and aligned almost horizontally. In<tei:lb/>all 12 specimens I examined, the orientation of the bone and the shape of its anterior margin<tei:lb/>(including the anteroventral peak) are typically of the OA type (see Fig. 4).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There are, with very few individual exceptions, six lateral line canal pores in the<tei:lb/>lachrymal of OA species (but see p. 263 below). The modal number of these pores in all<tei:lb/>African cichlids is five; the occasional departure from that number seems to be attributable<tei:lb/>to individual variability. Thus, both in its outline shape (including the anteroventral peak)<tei:lb/>and in having six lateral line pores, the lachrymal appears to be a unique apomorphic feature<tei:lb/>for the OA.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem (1981 : 208) used the presence of an anteroventral peak on the lachrymal as a<tei:lb/>synapomorphy differentiating Ectodus, Lestradea, Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia<tei:lb/>from Asprotilapia which, by implication, lacked this process. In the three specimens of<tei:lb/>Asprotilapia leptura (the sole species) I examined, a typical OA peak is present and the<tei:lb/>outline shape of the bone also conforms with that of other OA species (see Fig. 3G).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOT1LAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES 261</tei:p>
         <tei:p>omm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 4. Cunningtonia longiventralis, left lateral view of lachrymal in situ.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(9) [9]. The presence on the anguloarticular bone of a well-developed, anteroposteriorly<tei:lb/>aligned fossa for the insertion of the adductor mandibulae muscle A 2 .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This well-developed fossa is noted by Liem (1981 : 195) in his anatomical description of<tei:lb/>Ectodus descampsi, and is illustrated in the six species depicted in his figure 4. Liem did not,<tei:lb/>however, use the feature as a major apomorphy for the OA as a whole. Indeed, he used the<tei:lb/>presence of an extensive fossa in Asprotilapia as an autapomorphic character for that genus<tei:lb/>(Liem, 1981 : 208).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In all &lt;9A species the fossa is clearly defined, and is limited anteriorly by a prominent,<tei:lb/>near-vertically aligned ridge on the anguloarticular. The principal surface for muscle<tei:lb/>insertion is situated on the lateral aspect of the bone, and is thus unlike the generalized<tei:lb/>condition where it lies on and across the bone's posterior margin. The insertion face, and<tei:lb/>hence the area of the fossa, varies in size amongst members of the OA. It is largest in<tei:lb/>Asprotilapia, smallest in Cunningtonia, with the other species occupying various inter-<tei:lb/>mediate positions in the range. Parenthetically, it may be noted that amongst the species I<tei:lb/>would now include in the 6&gt;A, the fossa is small in Cyathopharynx, of intermediate size in<tei:lb/>Xenotilapia and Grammatotria, and largest in Callochromis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Amongst the outgroup taxa examined, an 0A type fossa is found only in Lethrinops (Lake<tei:lb/>Malawi), Trematocara (Lake Tanganyika), Chromidotilapia batesi and C. kingsleyae (West<tei:lb/>Africa) and, in a poorly developed state, in the following Lake Malawi 'Haplochromis'<tei:lb/>species 'H. ' prostoma, 'H. ' johnstoni, 'H. ' breviceps and 'H. ' tetrastigma. (For the use of the<tei:lb/>name 'Haplochromis' see Greenwood [ 1 979 : 3 1 7]).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In all other outgroup taxa examined the adductor mandibulae A 2 inserts along the some-<tei:lb/>what medially expanded posterior margin of the anguloarticular.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is no obvious evidence to suggest a recent common ancestry shared by the O\ and<tei:lb/>the Chromidotilapia species, nor one between any of the Lake Malawi 'Haplochromis' and<tei:lb/>either the OA or the Chromidotilapia species.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>262 P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the current state of our knowledge one can be less certain about making a similar state-<tei:lb/>ment with respect to the OA and either Trematocara (also from Lake Tanganyika) or the<tei:lb/>Malawian genus Lethrinops. Thus a suspended judgement only can be given on whether the<tei:lb/>presence of an OA-type fossa in these three taxa is a homoplasy, or whether it is a<tei:lb/>synapomorphy for a group of higher universality than the one under discussion (see also<tei:lb/>p. 257).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Because the fossa is present in all OA species (including the new additions, see below) and<tei:lb/>because it is congruent with other apomorphic features shared by them, it can, I would con-<tei:lb/>sider, be taken as further evidence for the monophyly of the lineage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>To summarise this review of group characters in the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage (as<tei:lb/>defined by Liem, 1981), the congruent apomorphic features are:<tei:lb/>(i) The presence of a palatopterygoid gap (see p. 254)<tei:lb/>(ii) The morphology of the palatine bone (see p. 257)<tei:lb/>(iii) The presence of an auricular process on the opercular bone (see p. 259)<tei:lb/>(iv) The shape of the lachrymal bone (1st infraorbital); see p. 259)<tei:lb/>(v) The presence of a well-defined, laterally placed fossa for the insertion of the A 2<tei:lb/>adductor mandibulae muscle on the anguloarticular bone (see p. 261).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Ophthalmotilapia assemblage reconsidered</tei:p>
         <tei:p>On the grounds of all their constituent species sharing the five features listed above, and<tei:lb/>because none possesses any feature which might suggest other relationships, six further<tei:lb/>genera can be included in the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage (see Figs 1,3, 10 and 1 1).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The new additions are:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(i) Xenotilapia Blgr, 1899 (type species X. sima Blgr)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(ii) Callochromis Regan, 1920 (type species Pelmatochromis macrops Blgr)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(iii) Grammatotria Blgr, 1899 (type species G. lemairii Blgr)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(iv) Cyathopharynx Regan, 1920 (type species Tilapia grandoculis Blgr; but see p. 284).<tei:lb/>(v) Cardiopharynx Poll, 1942 (type species C. schoutedeni Poll)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(vi) Aulonocranus Regan, 1920 (type species Paratilapia dewindti Blgr)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although not apparently relevant to the question of their phyletic relationships, it may be<tei:lb/>noted that these six genera also share with members of Liem's original OA all the other<tei:lb/>features discussed in the previous section (i.e. characters 2, 3, 5 &amp; 6).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>For taxonomic and biological details of these genera and their contained species, reference<tei:lb/>should be made to Poll (1946 &amp; 1956). Since I can find no grounds for maintaining<tei:lb/>Cyathopharynx and Cardiopharynx as separate genera (see p. 282), all further references to<tei:lb/>these taxa will be made under the name of the senior synonym, Cyathopharynx.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem (198 1 : 208) has already suggested that Xenotilapia, Callochromis and Aulonocranus<tei:lb/>might be the sister lineage of the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage, but he felt that more<tei:lb/>information was needed before their 'precise relationships' to the others could be<tei:lb/>determined. His reason for making this suggestion was that all three genera share with the<tei:lb/>OA a palatopterygoid gap and various derived features of the hyomandibula, ie characters (1)<tei:lb/>and (2) above. That the three taxa also shared the other six and supposedly apomorphic<tei:lb/>features originally used to define the OA, was not noted by him.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although I would be chary of claiming that the 'precise relationships' of Xenotilapia,<tei:lb/>Callochromis, Aulonocranus, Grammatotria and Cyathopharynx have been determined, I<tei:lb/>would submit that an a priori case can be established for including them within the<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia assemblage itself, and not just as a sister-group to that lineage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Before going on to consider intralineage relationships within the expanded OA, some<tei:lb/>comments must be made about certain features in Aulonocranus and Xenotilapia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In Aulonocranus the lachrymal has the characteristic shape of that bone in other OA<tei:lb/>species (Fig. 3H), but it lacks the anteroventral peak; the anteroventral angle is rounded and</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOT1LAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES 263</tei:p>
         <tei:p>so resembles the condition found in most cichlid taxa. It is possible that this atypical anterior<tei:lb/>profile might be attributed to the greatly inflated laterosensory canals in the lachrymal of<tei:lb/>Aulonocranus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In most Xenotilapia species too, the lachrymal is atypical for the OA because in these<tei:lb/>species there are, modally, five and not six openings to the laterosensory canal system (Figs<tei:lb/>3C &amp; D). There is, however, considerable inter- and intraspecific variation in pore number;<tei:lb/>some species have six pores, others only four, and some individuals have a different number<tei:lb/>of pores on each side of the head. Clearly, pore number is an unstable characteristic in<tei:lb/>Xenotilapia. The genus also differs from all other OA taxa, and all other African cichlids I<tei:lb/>have examined, in having the posterior opening to the lachrymal laterosensory system<tei:lb/>positioned below and not opposite the anterior canal opening in the second infraorbital<tei:lb/>bone.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As in Aulonocranus, the outline shape of the lachrymal in Xenotilapia is a typical OA one<tei:lb/>(Figs 3C &amp; D); not surprisingly, considering the number of species (11), there is rather more<tei:lb/>variation on that basic shape in Xenotilapia than in other members of the assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Relationships within the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In its original form, the assemblage was divided into two major sublineages, one comprising<tei:lb/>only Asprotilapia leptura, the other containing the four remaining genera, Ectodus,<tei:lb/>Lestradea, Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia (see Liem, 198 1 : 208 &amp; fig. 9).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As defining features for the larger sublineage, Liem employed two supposedly<tei:lb/>synapomorphic characters: (i) the morphology of the lachrymal, and (ii), the dominance of<tei:lb/>the A, division of the adductor mandibulae muscle complex (Liem's characters 15 &amp; 16<tei:lb/>respectively).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Asprotilapia lineage was recognized both by the absence of those features, and, more<tei:lb/>importantly, by its having six presumed autapomorphic characters (see Liem, 198 1 : 208).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As argued above (character (8)[15], page 259), the features of the lachrymal must now be<tei:lb/>considered an apomorphic character for the whole OA (including the new additions and<tei:lb/>Asprotilapia itself)-</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The muscle character, according to Liem, has two components. First, that the A, division<tei:lb/>of the adductor mandibulae has become the dominant component of the complex, its cross<tei:lb/>sectional area surpassing '. . . that of the other parts', and second, that '. . . its origin has<tei:lb/>expanded ventrally at the expense of the adductor mandibulae part A 2 ' (Liem, 1981: 208).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is difficult to test the first claim adequately, and my attempts to do so failed to confirm<tei:lb/>Liem's claims, especially if, as his statement implies, the A, division is dominant to both<tei:lb/>the A 2 and A 3 divisions combined. However, the area of origin of A, in the taxon concerned<tei:lb/>is very clearly greater than that of A 2 when measured by its extent along the vertical arm of<tei:lb/>the preoperculum (see fig. 6 in Liem, 1981).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>When, however, the additional OA taxa are taken into account, the second feature shows a<tei:lb/>continuous range of variation from a state where the origins of both A, and A 2 occupy an<tei:lb/>approximately equal depth on the vertical preopercular limb, to one where the origin of A 2<tei:lb/>is virtually excluded from that limb and thereby is almost confined to the horizontal part of<tei:lb/>the bone. Furthermore, even within a single genus (as in Xenotilapia and Callochromis}<tei:lb/>some species have A, and A 2 with almost equal depths of vertical origin (the plesiomorph<tei:lb/>condition in cichlids), others have the depth of A, greater than A 2 , and yet others have A 2<tei:lb/>with a much greater vertical depth of origin than A, (Fig. 5B). Thus there would no longer<tei:lb/>seem to be any grounds for maintaining the unity of the Ectodus- Cunningtonia sublineage<tei:lb/>on the basis of its myological characters.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The peculiar arrangement of the adductor mandibulae muscles in Asprotilapia still stands<tei:lb/>as a well-defined autapomorphy for the genus. Here, division A, is markedly reduced, both<tei:lb/>in the depth of its origin on the preoperculum, and in its overall bulk (Fig. 5A), a condition<tei:lb/>not found elsewhere in the OA. Other features, however, suggest that Asprotilapia is closely<tei:lb/>related to some of the newly incorporated members of the assemblage (p. 265).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>264</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>B</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 5 Superficial adductor mandibulae muscles (left side) of: A, Asprotilapia leptura; B,<tei:lb/>Xenotilapia sima. AM, &amp; AM 2 : adductor mandibulae divisions 1 and 2; TAM, : tendon of<tei:lb/>insertion for AM,.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although a primary dichotomy within the OA cannot be made on the characters<tei:lb/>employed by Liem, such a dichotomy can be established on differences in the length of the<tei:lb/>gut and the manner in which it is arranged within the visceral cavity (see Figs 6 &amp; 7).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem (1981 : 209; character [19]) used intestinal length to define a group of taxa<tei:lb/>(Ophthalmotilapia, Cunningtonia and Lestraded) within the original 0A, but he did not<tei:lb/>comment on the spatial arrangement of the alimentary tract in those species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Within the expanded OA, the member taxa can be grouped into those with an intestinal<tei:lb/>length less than 3 times the standard length of the body, modally 2-3-2-5 times SL, and those<tei:lb/>with an intestinal length 3-6 times the standard length.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species in the first group have the relatively short gut coiled into a few loops whose<tei:lb/>arrangement is in an essentially anteroposterior direction. In contrast, species with a long gut<tei:lb/>have the intestine much coiled and the coils are arranged in an essentially transverse<tei:lb/>direction (see Figs 6 &amp; 7). At first sight this transverse arrangement gives an erroneous<tei:lb/>impression of the intestine actually being coiled around the stomach (Fig. 7).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A long and complexly coiled gut is a derived feature, occurring in several cichlid lineages.<tei:lb/>But, since these lineages are not closely related it can be treated as a synapomorphy at the<tei:lb/>level of universality involved here.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES</tei:p>
         <tei:p>265</tei:p>
         <tei:p>mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 6 Alimentary tract (left lateral view) of an Asprotilapia subassemblage taxon (Grammatotria</tei:p>
         <tei:p>lemairei).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 7 Alimentary tract (left lateral view) of an Ophthalmotilapia subassemblage taxon</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(Ophthalmotilapia boops).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Taxa of the OA belonging to the group with a long and transversely coiled intestine are:<tei:lb/>Lestradea, Ophthalmotilapia, Cunningtonia and Cyathopharynx - hereafter referred to as<tei:lb/>the Ophthalmotilapia subassemblage; their interrelationships will be considered later (see p.<tei:lb/>271).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The group with a short and longitudinally coiled intestine comprises the genera<tei:lb/>Asprotilapia, Callochromis, Xenotilapia, Grammatotria, Ectodus and Aulonocara-<tei:lb/>hereafter referred to as the Asprotilapia subassemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Four genera in the Asprotilapia subassemblage, viz. Asprotilapia, Callochromis,<tei:lb/>Xenotilapia and Grammatotria, share an apomorphic feature which suggests their shared<tei:lb/>common ancestry. This character is the presence of a fully developed pharyngeal hanging<tei:lb/>pad, with its associated modifications to the superficial anatomy of the gill-rakers; see Figs 8<tei:lb/>&amp;9.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The pad is an hypertrophied and well-circumscribed, forwardly directed, and turgid fold of<tei:lb/>the buccopharyngeal tissues. It lies immediately anterior to the upper pharyngeal bones and<tei:lb/>extends forward and downward, as a visor-like projection, for a short distance in front of the<tei:lb/>first gill-arch. Posterolaterally the pad is fused with the thickened tissue covering the<tei:lb/>epibranchial gill-rakers of the first gill-arch, but over most of its width the visor-like part is</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>266</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 8 Pharyngeal hanging pad in Xenotilapia boulengeri. Left side, seen from a slightly</tei:p>
         <tei:p>dorsolateral viewpoint.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 9 Ventral portions of the gill-arches, and the lower pharyngeal bone of Xenotilapia<tei:lb/>boulengeri, viewed from above to show the nature of the gill-rakers.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>separated from the buccal roof by a distinct transverse groove. This groove is open anteriorly<tei:lb/>and laterally. In the buccal midline the left and right halves of the visor-like portion are<tei:lb/>confluent with the buccopharyngeal roof. When the mouth is closed, and the ventral<tei:lb/>gill-arch skeleton is adducted, the visor occludes the pharynx, leaving only a narrow channel<tei:lb/>coincident with the medial area of confluence between the two halves of the visor and the<tei:lb/>buccopharyngeal epithelium.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES 267</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The pharyngeal surface of the visor is thrown into a large number of broad-based but<tei:lb/>terminally acute papillae. When the gill-arches are adducted the papillose area is brought<tei:lb/>into close contact with the upper surface of the cerato- and hypobranchial regions of the<tei:lb/>gill-arches. These, in turn, are covered by a greatly thickened and soft epithelium. The inner<tei:lb/>and outer gill-rakers on the ceratobranchial of each arch are joined transversely by anvil-<tei:lb/>shaped folds of thickened tissue so that, superficially, there appears to be but a single series of<tei:lb/>rakers, with each raker extending across the breadth of the arch (Fig. 9).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A pharyngeal hanging pad is known from two other, and very dissimilar, African genera,<tei:lb/>Chromidotilapia and Tylochromis, and in the South American Geophagus (see Trewavas,<tei:lb/>1 974 : 389-392). Since there are several features strongly indicating that none of these taxa is<tei:lb/>closely interrelated, and that none is closely related to the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage, the<tei:lb/>repeated occurrence of a hanging pad can only be interpreted as the result of convergent<tei:lb/>evolution. Any other explanation would be most unparsimonious. It would be equally<tei:lb/>unparsimonious to assume that its occurrence in four of the OA taxa was the result of<tei:lb/>independent evolution in each genus. This is particularly so since three of the four genera<tei:lb/>share another apomorphy, a high number of caudal vertebrae in Xenotilapia, Asprotilapia<tei:lb/>and Grammatotria, and a second derived character, the shape of the dentary, is shared by<tei:lb/>Xenotilapia, Callochromis and Grammatotria, of which taxa Callochromis alone does not<tei:lb/>share the vertebral apomorphy.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Thus, on the basis of their all possessing a pharyngeal hanging pad, Xenotilapia,<tei:lb/>Callochromis, Grammatotria and Asprotilapia 1 are taken to form a natural group within the<tei:lb/>&lt;9A. The group can be further subdivided on the distribution within its members of certain<tei:lb/>other derived features.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Xenotilapia, Callochromis and Grammatotria all have a peculiarly shaped dentary (Fig.<tei:lb/>10). When viewed laterally, the dorsal margin of the bone is seen to dip downwards<tei:lb/>immediately behind the last tooth in the outer row. It continues posteriorly at this lower<tei:lb/>level until it curves upwards to form the anterior margin of the coronoid process. The<tei:lb/>alveolar surface is confined to that part of the bone preceding the step, behind which it is<tei:lb/>edentulous. The dentary of Asprotilapia will be discussed below.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This type of dentary, as far as I am aware, is not found in any other cichlid from Lake<tei:lb/>Tanganyika, or for that matter from Lake Victoria either. It is, however, closely approached<tei:lb/>by the dentary in the Malawian genus Lethrinops and in some 'Haplochromis' species from<tei:lb/>that lake. The significance of this similarity, and the occurrence in Lethrinops of a<tei:lb/>palatopterygoid gap, is discussed on page 279.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Within the group comprising Xenotilapia, Callochromis and Grammatotria it is<tei:lb/>impossible to determine which two genera are the more closely related since no clear-cut<tei:lb/>linking synapomorphies can be recognized. It is accepted that the diagnostic 'generic'<tei:lb/>characters for each genus are autapomorphies for that taxon. At present the trio can only be<tei:lb/>treated as an unresolved trichotomy, but with the suggestion that further research may show<tei:lb/>Xenotilapia and Grammatotria to be sister taxa.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Asprotilapia, the fourth member of the group, is a most distinctive taxon, in which Liem<tei:lb/>(1981 : 208) identified six autapomorphies. These must now be reviewed in the context of<tei:lb/>the expanded Ophthalmotilapia assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As in earlier discussions, Liem's apomorphy number is given in square brackets.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(i) [9]. The elongate, slender mandible has an expanded adductor fossa for the A 2<tei:lb/>division of the adductor mandibulae muscle.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As noted earlier (p. 261) the adductor fossa is expanded in several members of the OA,<tei:lb/>particularly in species of the group to which Asprotilapia belongs. However, even amongst<tei:lb/>those species the fossa is most expansive in Asprotilapia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>'Microbranchiospines are present in all four of these Tanganyika genera; in this respect they resemble Tylochromis<tei:lb/>and differ from Chromidotilapia (see discussion in Trewavas, 1 973 : 1 7 &amp; 1 974 : 388).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>268</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ADDF</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 10 Dentary and anguloarticular in two species of the Asprotilapia subassemblage : A,<tei:lb/>Grammatotria lemairei (lateral view); B &amp; C, Xenotilapia boulengeri (lateral and ventral views<tei:lb/>respectively). ADDF : adductor fossa. NF : nerve foramen.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 11 Left dentary and anguloarticular of Asprotilapia leptura in : A, lateral view; B, occlusal<tei:lb/>view. In B the teeth have been restored (based on a spirit specimen), but in A only the tooth scars<tei:lb/>are shown.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAP1A ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES 269</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The mandible is not, in my view, especially elongate, particularly when it is compared<tei:lb/>with that element in other members of the Asprotilapia subassemblage (cf. Figs 10 &amp; 1 1). Its<tei:lb/>gross morphology differs from that in Xenotilapia, Callochromis and Grammatotria since it<tei:lb/>lacks a 'stepped' alveolar margin, and because each ramus of the jaw has a more abrupt and<tei:lb/>stronger medial curvature towards the symphysis. Also, in Asprotilapia, immediately before<tei:lb/>the dentary curves inwards, the alveolar surface is produced laterally so as to form a<tei:lb/>noticeable, shelf-like overhang of the underlying ramus (Fig. 1 1).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Overall, the dentary in Asprotilapia bears a fairly close resemblance to that bone in<tei:lb/>Labeotropheus of Lake Malawi, a resemblance enhanced by the tricuspid, slender-necked,<tei:lb/>procumbent and movably implanted teeth present in both genera. Asprotilapia has, how-<tei:lb/>ever, a shallower dentary, and the anguloarticular is taller and more expansive than in<tei:lb/>Labeotropheus. Parenthetically it may be noted that Asprotilapia, like Labeotropheus, has<tei:lb/>an enlarged and similarly shaped cartilaginous meniscus underlying the premaxillary<tei:lb/>ascending process, and a similar fleshy medial projection overlying the broad palato-<tei:lb/>premaxillary ligaments. Observations made from radiographs of the two species, and from<tei:lb/>manipulation of preserved specimens, indicate that the protrusile mechanisms in the two<tei:lb/>species are very similar. There are, however, no reasons to doubt that these similarities<tei:lb/>should be treated as homoplasies.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(ii) [10]. The posterior head of the transversus dorsalis anterior muscle is absent.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This seems to be a clear-cut autapomorphy, but should be checked in more examples than<tei:lb/>the single specimen available to Liem and myself.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(iii) [11]. Lateral ethmoids greatly enlarged.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This again is an apparently good autapomorphy.<tei:lb/>(iv) [12]. Interorbital width greatly reduced.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The interorbital width is reduced in some Xenotilapia species, but in none is it as narrow<tei:lb/>as in Asprotilapia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(v) [13]. The reduced articular process of the premaxilla is in a more forward position.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is considerable and continuous variation in the relative position of this process in<tei:lb/>Xenotilapia species, and indeed within the taxa of the entire OA. It would not, therefore,<tei:lb/>seem to be a character of particular value.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(vi) [14]. The greatly enlarged cranial condyle and the premaxillary process constitute<tei:lb/>the bulk of the maxilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The maxilla of Asprotilapia is less outstanding when compared with that bone in other<tei:lb/>members of the subassemblage, especially Xenotilapia (Fig. 12). In X. boulengeri, for<tei:lb/>example, the process is larger than in Asprotilapia. The cranial condyle, however, is largest<tei:lb/>in Asprotilapia and, as compared with all other &lt;9A species, the whole bone is relatively<tei:lb/>foreshortened.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the context of the expanded &lt;9A, I would consider that at least three of the<tei:lb/>autapomorphies originally proposed (i.e. nos. i, ii and iii above) retain their validity (if, that<tei:lb/>is, the apomorphic features of the lower jaw are interpreted as has been done here).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although not listed as an autapomorphy by Liem (198 1), the peculiar condition of the A,<tei:lb/>division of the adductor mandibulae muscle in Asprotilapia would seem deserving of that<tei:lb/>status. In Asprotilapia, as compared with all other OA taxa, A, is a very short and narrow<tei:lb/>muscle with an extremely long tendon of insertion (Liem, 1981; fig. 6; also Fig. 5), and an<tei:lb/>area of origin much smaller in all respects than that of the A 2 division. No other species in<tei:lb/>the OA has this arrangement of the adductor mandibulae muscles.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>270</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 12 Right maxilla, in lateral view, of: A, Xenotilapia boulengeri; B, Asprotilapia leptura (the<tei:lb/>damaged ventral margin of the premaxillary saddle is indicated by a broken line).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>When commenting on the unusual A, muscle in Asprotilapia, Liem (1981:203)<tei:lb/>makes particular reference to a tendon stemming from the muscle's principal tendon of<tei:lb/>insertion, and which joins the tendinous part of adductor division A^. This emphasis might<tei:lb/>give an impression that the vertically directed interconnecting tendon is a unique (or<tei:lb/>unusual) feature of Asprotilapia. That is not so because the tendon is present in all cichlids<tei:lb/>whose jaw musculature has been examined (see for example Liem &amp; Osse, 1975: fig. 6;<tei:lb/>Stiassny, 1981 : 80, and figs 8 &amp; 1 1 [tA.b]; also personal observations on species other than<tei:lb/>those studied by these authors). Asprotilapia is, however, unusual in having a discrete,<tei:lb/>ligament-like condensation in the connective tissue between the premaxilla and dentary,<tei:lb/>inserting on the lateral (and not the medial) face of the premaxilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Interestingly, despite the resemblances in lower jaw morphology between Asprotilapia and<tei:lb/>Labeotropheus (see above p. 269), the pattern of adductor muscles in the two genera is quite<tei:lb/>different. Labeotropheus has the typical generalized cichlid arrangement.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The various autapomorphies of Asprotilapia serve to indicate the taxon's isolation within<tei:lb/>its group. For the moment it can only be treated as the sister taxon to the other three genera<tei:lb/>(i.e. Callochromis, Xenotilapia and Grammatotria) combined.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The two remaining taxa in the Asprotilapia subassemblage (see p. 265), Ectodus and<tei:lb/>Aulonocranus, are 'interrelated' only at the level of their sharing with their supposed sister-<tei:lb/>group, Asprotilapia, Callochromis, Xenotilapia and Grammatotria, the plesiomorphic<tei:lb/>feature of a short intestine. With one possible exception, neither Ectodus nor Aulonocranus<tei:lb/>has any uniquely shared derived features suggestive of their recent common ancestry.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The exceptional feature concerns the marked elongation of the first, and to a lesser extent<tei:lb/>the second pelvic fin rays in adult males. In Aulonocranus the first ray extends to about the<tei:lb/>middle of the anal fin, and in Ectodus to a point beyond its spinous part, but never as far as<tei:lb/>the middle of the fin. Other members of the Asprotilapia subassemblage have variously<tei:lb/>modified relative proportions of different pelvic fin rays (see Poll, 1956) but none has the first<tei:lb/>ray elongated to the degree found in Ectodus and Aulonocranus. The significance of this<tei:lb/>feature is, however, somewhat questionable (see p. 272).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES 271</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In Liem's scheme (1981 : 208-9, fig. 9), Ectodus was allied with Lestradea, Ophthalmo-<tei:lb/>tilapia and Cunningtonia (now considered to be a separate sublineage, see below)<tei:lb/>and not with Asprotilapia. Liem's grounds for this alliance were based on Ectodus sharing<tei:lb/>two apomorphic characters with Lestradea, Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia, namely a<tei:lb/>distinct antroventral process on the lachrymal bone, and a dominant Aj division in the<tei:lb/>adductor mandibulae muscle complex.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As was argued above (pp. 262-263) the former character is one shared by all members of<tei:lb/>the OA (except Aulonocranus) and the latter is a variable feature of little value as an<tei:lb/>indicator of relationships within the OA.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Aulonocranus was not included in the original OA, although Liem (1981 : 206 &amp; 208) did<tei:lb/>suggest that it might be related to that assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The genus is readily identified by one outstanding apomorphy, the hypertrophy of its<tei:lb/>cephalic laterosensory canal system, and as a probable correlate, the enlarged saccular bulla<tei:lb/>in the basioccipital and prootic bones.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ectodus has one autapomorphy, viz. the vertical and horizontal limbs of the pre-<tei:lb/>operculum are of equal or almost equal length (see Liem, 1981 : 209). Liem also ranked the<tei:lb/>enlarged saccular bulla as an autapomorphy, but the bulla is enlarged to an equal extent in<tei:lb/>Lestradea and Ophthalmotilapia amongst members of the original OA, and in Aulonocranus<tei:lb/>and Cyathopharynx among the new additions to the assemblage. Indeed, apart from<tei:lb/>Callochromis, Xenotilapia and Grammatotria the saccular bulla is noticeably enlarged in all<tei:lb/>OA species, the degree of enlargement showing a continuous increase from the condition in<tei:lb/>Asprotilapia through to that in Aulonocranus, with Lestradea, Ectodus and Ophthalmo-<tei:lb/>tilapia all close to Aulonocranus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Turning now to the second lineage of the primary dichotomy, the Ophthalmotilapia<tei:lb/>subassemblage (p. 265), one again finds difficulty in establishing intragroup relationships,<tei:lb/>but little difficulty in recognizing autapomorphies for the constituent genera, viz. Lestradea,<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia, Cunningtonia and Cyathopharynx.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Since Lestradea lacks the various derived features shown by its congeners in the<tei:lb/>subassemblage it would appear to be the plesiomorph sister taxon to the other three genera<tei:lb/>combined.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem (1981 : 209) singled out two autapomorphies for Lestradea (i) the edentulous<tei:lb/>anterior process of the lower pharyngeal bone is only half as long as the toothed part (his<tei:lb/>apomorphy 20), and (ii), the body of the maxilla is stout, and has a prominent postmaxillary<tei:lb/>process (his apomorphy 2 1 ).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>I cannot agree with Liem's statement about the relative proportions of the anterior process<tei:lb/>of the lower pharyngeal bone* The Lestradea specimens I have examined all have this<tei:lb/>process as long as, or almost as long as the toothed part, a condition approaching that in<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia. This condition must be considered derived relative to<tei:lb/>that in Astatotilapia and many other African cichlids. In these various taxa the edentulous<tei:lb/>anterior process of the bone is indeed half or less than half as long as the toothed part.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The body of the maxilla in Lestradea is stout and the posterior process is prominent, but<tei:lb/>in both features the bone does not differ from the maxilla in Ophthalmotilapia where the<tei:lb/>process is a little less prominent. A relatively short maxilla with a prominent posterior<tei:lb/>process is, it would seem, a common feature in the whole Ophthalmotilapia assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cyathopharynx is readily distinguished by its autapomorphous lower pharyngeal bone<tei:lb/>(Fig. 23), with its deeply concave occlusal surface, heart-shaped dentigerous area, and greatly<tei:lb/>inflated body below that surface. There are, however, no synapomorphies (except group<tei:lb/>ones) shared by Cyathopharynx and any other member of the subassemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem (1981:209) recognized three synapomorphies which suggested to him that<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia were members of a monophyletic unit. The presumed<tei:lb/>synapomorphies were (i) jaw teeth with long stalks, and movably implanted, (ii) the first<tei:lb/>pelvic ray greatly elongate (Liem's apomorphies 22 &amp; 23 respectively), and (iii) the posterior<tei:lb/>margin of the vertical preopercular limb is straight and forms a 90 angle with the horizontal<tei:lb/>limb.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>272 P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The teeth in all members of the OA (sensu lato) are movably implanted, and in all species<tei:lb/>the teeth could be described as having 'long stalks', albeit with specifically distinct but<tei:lb/>varying degrees of slenderness. For example, in both these features the outer jaw teeth in<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia are very like those in Lestradea, but those in Cunningtonia have a much<tei:lb/>more slender and elongate neck than do the teeth in any other OA species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The first and second pelvic rays are elongate (much more so in males than in females) in<tei:lb/>both Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia, with, in the former, the first ray somewhat longer<tei:lb/>than the second. But, when other species in the expanded OA are taken into account one<tei:lb/>finds that the condition of the two rays in Cyathopharynx furcifer is like that in<tei:lb/>Cunningtonia longiventralis, and that Aulonocranus also has elongate rays but which extend<tei:lb/>only to the middle of the anal fin base and not to the posterior margin, or slightly beyond, as<tei:lb/>they do in Cunningtonia and Cyathopharynx furcifer. Cyathopharynx schoutedeni has<tei:lb/>elongate first and second pelvic rays too, but in this species it is the second ray which is the<tei:lb/>longer and, unlike the elongate first ray in C. furcifer, it extends posteriorly only a short<tei:lb/>distance beyond the spinous part of the anal fin.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Thus it is difficult to treat marked elongation of the first, or first and second pelvic rays as a<tei:lb/>synapomorphy for Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia. That it is a character showing<tei:lb/>continuous variation and one that is incongruent with other apomorphies would also<tei:lb/>preclude its use as an indicator of recent shared common ancestry for the genera exhibiting<tei:lb/>it, namely Aulonocranus, Ophthalmotilapia, Cunningtonia and Cardiopharynx (see below).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Some comment on the spatulate, bifid tips to the first ray in males of Ophthalmotilapia<tei:lb/>species would be appropriate here.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The feature appears to be a unique apomorphy for Ophthalmotilapia, indeed it is one of<tei:lb/>the reasons given by Liem (1981 :210) for synonymizing Ophthalmochromis with that<tei:lb/>genus. Spatulate tips have not been mentioned in the formal descriptions of any other<tei:lb/>species, nor have I seen such modifications in any of the taxa, other than Ophthalmotilapia,<tei:lb/>which I have examined. However, Brichard (1978 : 187) describes the pelvic fin in<tei:lb/>Cyathopharynx furcifer as having '. . . a long filament tipped with a yellow double spatula'.<tei:lb/>On page 190 of the same book he comments on 'The ventral filaments, reaching the end of<tei:lb/>the anal fin are each tipped with a double yellow-orange spatula'. Finally, Brichard<tei:lb/>(1978 : 148 &amp; 149) provides two illustrations of a live individual (or individuals) showing<tei:lb/>what certainly looks like a spatulate tip to the protracted pelvic fin rays.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Brichard is an experienced underwater-naturalist and one is hesitant to suggest he has<tei:lb/>misidentified his material. On the other hand, in his description (Brichard, 1978 : 326) of<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia ventralis (as Ophthalmochromis ventralis) he remarks that The males . . .<tei:lb/>have very long pelvic filaments also forked like O. nasutus, but the tips are without spatulae<tei:lb/>(although they are pale yellow like those of O. nasutusY; bold type added. Clearly there is<tei:lb/>some terminological confusion here since none of the male O. ventralis specimens I have<tei:lb/>examined, or those which have been described elsewhere, lacks well-defined spatulae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The tips of the elongate pelvic rays in Cyathopharynx are brightly coloured, and seem to<tei:lb/>serve the same ethological function as the spatulae in Ophthalmotilapia. Perhaps the<tei:lb/>confusion stems from that colour similarity rather than a structural one? Until spatulate<tei:lb/>Cyanopharynx are described formally, the uncertainty will remain.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In itself, very marked elongation of the first and second pelvic rays is a derived feature (see<tei:lb/>below), but in the context of the OA it is not clear at what level of universality it can be<tei:lb/>recognized as a synapomorphy. For example, if used to unite Aulonocranus, Cyathopharynx,<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia, pelvic fin length would be incongruent with the group<tei:lb/>synapomorphy of an elongate and complexly folded intestine shared by all except<tei:lb/>Aulonocranus, which has a short and simply folded intestine. If, on the other hand, because<tei:lb/>of its short gut Aulonocranus was taken to be the plesiomorph sister taxon of the others, then<tei:lb/>where would Lestradea which has a long gut but short pelvic fins be placed?</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The problem is further compounded by Ectodus which has a short and simply folded gut<tei:lb/>but elongate first and second pelvic rays produced to a degree almost comparabale with those<tei:lb/>in Aulonocranus.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOT1LAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES 273</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In no case are there any known synapomorphies which are uniquely congruent with<tei:lb/>protracted pelvic fin length, and which would thereby establish a strong case for arguing that<tei:lb/>the gut character is a homoplasy. Finally, it must be recalled that some relative elongation of<tei:lb/>the first and second pelvic rays is of relatively common occurrence in African cichlids (see<tei:lb/>Greenwood, 1981), and that the degree of elongation must be treated as a continuous<tei:lb/>variable, albeit one rarely reaching the extremes found in Cunningtonia and Ophthalmo-<tei:lb/>tilapia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem's third synapomorphy (involving the shape of the preoperculum; see page 271) is<tei:lb/>also difficult to substantiate, particularly in the context of the expanded OA. In all<tei:lb/>constituent species of the O\ sensu law the vertical and horizontal limbs of the preopercular<tei:lb/>bone meet at, or very nearly at, a right angle, and in all except Ophthalmotilapia and<tei:lb/>Cunningtonia the posterior margin of the vertical limb curves inwards for a short distance<tei:lb/>near its dorsal extremity. Because Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia have the bony<tei:lb/>flange behind the vertically aligned laterosensory canal tube narrowing imperceptibly, rather<tei:lb/>than abruptly, the entire posterior margin of the preoperculum does give the impression of<tei:lb/>being straight in these two species. The difference between the two types of posterior margin<tei:lb/>is, however, very slight and is almost obliterated by the condition in Cyathopharynx. Here<tei:lb/>the dorsal extremity of the margin is slightly indented, but less noticeably so than in most<tei:lb/>other OA taxa.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Thus, of the three apomorphies under review, only the nature of the preopercular margin,<tei:lb/>a not particularly trenchant character, would seem to be a synapomorphy linking<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>On the morphological evidence alone, it is thus impossible to hypothesize precise<tei:lb/>intragroup relationships for the Ophthalmotilapia subassemblage, except to note that on a<tei:lb/>simple summation of derived features Lestradea would seem to be the most plesiomorph<tei:lb/>taxon. At the generic level the other taxa are readily identified by their particular<tei:lb/>autapomorphies (see below).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is possible that these currently obscure and therefore uncertain intralineage relation-<tei:lb/>ships will be clarified when more data are available on the breeding habits of its constituent<tei:lb/>species. Brichard (1978 : 108), for example, groups Cyathopharynx with Ophthalmotilapia<tei:lb/>as polygamous spawners in which there is no contact between the sexes at the nest site. The<tei:lb/>import of Brichard's statement is not really clear, and the phyletic importance of most<tei:lb/>ethological characters still awaits evaluation. Nevertheless it is suggestive that Brichard did<tei:lb/>single out these taxa as forming a distinctive reproductive class.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Returning now to the autapomorphic features of the genera constituting the sub-<tei:lb/>assemblage, one finds that Ophthalmotilapia (sensu Liem, 1981 : 210) is distinguished by<tei:lb/>the bifid spatulae in which each elongate first pelvic ray terminates, and the subdivision of<tei:lb/>the retractor dorsalis muscle of the upper gill-arches into two distinct heads (see Liem,<tei:lb/>1981 :201,fig.8D).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cunningtonia has as its principal autapomorphies the nature of its oral dentition, the stout<tei:lb/>foreshortened dentary (Fig. 1 3) and the stout premaxilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 13 Left dentary and anguloarticular of Cunningtonia longiventralis in lateral view.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>274 P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Most of the jaw teeth are very slender and tall, with strongly recurved, tricuspid and broad<tei:lb/>crowns. The teeth are arranged in wide bands over the entire alveolar surface of the<tei:lb/>premaxilla and on the transverse part of the dentary, but on the lateral alveolar surface the<tei:lb/>teeth are much stouter and are unicuspid.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The palatine of Cunningtonia is also unique in having its facet for articulation with the<tei:lb/>lateral ethmoid expanded medially into a shelf-like projection. When viewed from the side,<tei:lb/>especially in specimens prepared as alizarin transparencies, the shelf has a spine-like<tei:lb/>appearance (see Liem, 198 1 : 2 10; apomorphy 26).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem considered that the symplectic in Cunningtonia was '. . . very elongate' (his<tei:lb/>apomorphy 28), but I find that it is of virtually equal relative length in all members of the<tei:lb/>OA.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In Cyathopharynx the principal autapomorphy is the peculiar, heart-shaped and deeply<tei:lb/>concave dentigerous surface of the lower pharyngeal bone and, of course, the correlated<tei:lb/>changes in the shape of the upper pharyngeal elements (see p. 288).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The nature of the apophysis for the upper pharyngeal bones in the Ophthalmotilapia<tei:lb/>assemblage</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Like Liem (1981), I have not taken into account the nature of the pharyngeal apophysis<tei:lb/>when analysing intragroup relationships amongst members of the OA (see Greenwood,<tei:lb/>1978, for an evaluation of this character in determining phyletic relationships; also Fryer &amp;<tei:lb/>lies, 1972:504^5^.).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Based on material examined personally, the distribution of apophyseal types (defined as in<tei:lb/>Greenwood, 1978) within the two major subassemblages of the 0A is as follows:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Asprotilapia subassemblage Ophthalmotilapia subassemblage</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hap. 1 Troph. Tilapia Hap. Troph. Tilapia</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Callochromis Xenotilapia Cyatho- Lestradea</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(a few spp.) pharynx</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Xenotilapia Aulono-</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(most spp.) cranus Ophthalmotilapia</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Grammatotria* Ectodus Cunningtonia</tei:p>
         <tei:p>l ffap. = Haplochromis type; Troph. = Tropheus type; Tilapia = Tilapia type.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>*In all specimens the apophysis is of the Haplochromis type on one side, and the Tropheus type on the other.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is interesting that no true Tilapia type apophysis occurs amongst members of the<tei:lb/>Asprotilapia subassemblage, but that three of the four taxa in the Ophthalmotilapia<tei:lb/>subassemblage do have an apophysis of that type. In contrast, a Haplochromis type<tei:lb/>apophysis occurs in three members of the Asprotilapia subassemblage but not in any taxa of<tei:lb/>the Ophthalmotilapia subassemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It must be borne in mind, however, that the Tropheus apophyseal type is structurally<tei:lb/>intermediate between the Haplochromis and Tilapia types (see Greenwood, 1978) and that<tei:lb/>in the Asprotilapia subassemblage certain taxa have more than one type of apophysis.<tei:lb/>Indeed, the three specimens of Grammatotria lemairei examined have a Haplochromis type<tei:lb/>apophysis on one side of the skull and a Tropheus type on the other.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is also interesting to note that if the Tropheus category (of Greenwood, 1978), which<tei:lb/>Regan did not recognize, is ignored and the taxa in that category are returned to the one in<tei:lb/>which they were placed by Regan (1920), then the entire Asprotilapia subassemblage is of the<tei:lb/>Haplochromis type, and the Ophthalmotilapia subassemblage becomes exclusively of the<tei:lb/>Tilapia type.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES 275</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Such a pattern might well be taken to argue against my earlier criticisms of the pharyngeal<tei:lb/>apophysis as an indicator of phyletic relationships at a high level of universality<tei:lb/>(Greenwood, 1978) and that, on the contrary, African cichlids can be divided into 'Tilapicf<tei:lb/>and 'Haplochromis" 1 supralineages as suggested by Regan (1920).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>If the latter argument is accepted, then the synapomorphies delimiting the Ophthalmo-<tei:lb/>tilapia assemblage as a whole must be considered as homoplasies developed independently<tei:lb/>in the two subassemblages recognised here. That assumption would produce a scheme of<tei:lb/>relationships less parsimonious than the one proposed above and earlier by Liem<tei:lb/>(1981). Also, the existence of intermediate conditions (i.e. the Tropheus type) in the<tei:lb/>structure of the apophysis, and of other cases where a classification based on apophyseal<tei:lb/>structure is incongruent with different and apparently synapomorphic characters (see<tei:lb/>Greenwood, 1978; Liem &amp; Stewart, 1976) would seem to support the rejection of apophyseal<tei:lb/>structure as an indicator of phyletic relationships at the level proposed by Regan.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A review of other schemes of relationship suggested for members of the<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia assemblage</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Regan (1920 : 52) did not present detailed arguments for his views on the interrelationships<tei:lb/>of the Lake Tanganyika genera, which were strongly influenced by his assumption of there<tei:lb/>being a fundamental dichotomy of African cichlids into those with a 'TilapicC type<tei:lb/>apophysis, and those with a 'Haplochromis'' type (see above). As a result of this basic<tei:lb/>difference in approach, it is difficult to make direct comparisons between Regan's ideas and<tei:lb/>those put forward in this paper. However, some comments can be made on certain of Regan's<tei:lb/>suggested relationships involving OA members and taxa outside that assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>For example, Ophthalmotilapia was grouped with Cyathopharynx, Cunningtonia,<tei:lb/>Asprotilapia, Petrochromis and the Malawian genus Petrotilapia (then considered a species<tei:lb/>of Petrochromis); furthermore, Regan suggested that Ophthalmotilapia was closely related to<tei:lb/>Limnotilapia (now synonymised with Simochromis, see Greenwood, 1979) and that it had<tei:lb/>'. . . given rise to Cyathopharynx'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Petrochromis and Petrotilapia were included in this grouping because, in their dental<tei:lb/>morphology and pattern, they are strikingly similar to Cunningtonia (see p. 280 below).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>No reasons were given for including Asprotilapia, a taxon quite unlike the others in its<tei:lb/>gross morphology and in its dentition; presumably the reason lay in Regan's (1920 : 42)<tei:lb/>belief that the 'Skeleton (is) essentially similar to that of Ophthalmotilapia ventralis . . .'. The<tei:lb/>skeletal features noted by Regan (1920 : 41 &amp; 42) were, it should be emphasised, not those<tei:lb/>used in this paper; most can be treated as plesiomorph characters when used at the level of<tei:lb/>analysis involved here.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Presumably it was the same suite of skeletal characters which led Regan to suggest a close<tei:lb/>relationship between Limnotilapia (i.e. Simochromis) and Ophthalmotilapia, a relationship<tei:lb/>which I cannot accept since ''Limnotilapia'' apparently shares no derived features with any<tei:lb/>members of the OA..</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ectodus (as a putative ancestral morphotype) was grouped by Regan (1920: 53) with<tei:lb/>Callochromis, Xenotilapia and Grammatotria,\hQ taxa being given that order of increasing<tei:lb/>morphological derivation. Again no detailed reasons are given for this grouping, save that<tei:lb/>all its taxa have a 'Haplochromis'' type pharyngeal apophysis and small conical teeth. In<tei:lb/>effect, however, it approximates closely to the arrangement proposed in this paper.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Regan (1920 : 53) also included, albeit implicitly rather than explicitly, Aulonocranus and<tei:lb/>Trematocara with those genera listed in the previous paragraph. The association of<tei:lb/>Aulonocranus with Trematocara was, presumably, based on both genera having hyper-<tei:lb/>trophied cephalic laterosensory canal systems (Regan, 1920:47); no other relationship<tei:lb/>with Aulonocranus was suggested, save that it is 'Intermediate between Haplochromis and<tei:lb/>Trematocara.'' The linking of Aulonocranus and Trematocara with the Ectodus-<tei:lb/>Grammatotria group (see above) was apparently based on the common possession of small<tei:lb/>conical teeth and a 'Haplochromis' type of pharyngeal apophysis.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>276 P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>That Regan did not consider there to be any relationship between his Asprotilapia-<tei:lb/>Ophthalmotilapia and his Ectodus-Grammatotria ( + Aulonocranus) groups is doubtless due<tei:lb/>to his basic assumption that the endemic genera of Lake Tanganyika were derived from<tei:lb/>'. . .two ancestral types, one nearly related to Limnotilapia and the other to Haplochromis'<tei:lb/>(Regan, 1920:53).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>After Regan's initial analysis of the Tanganyika cichlids, no further attempt to interrelate<tei:lb/>the endemic genera of the lake was made for more than fifty years. In 1972 Fryer &amp; lies paid<tei:lb/>considerable attention to this problem, in particular to the assumption that there was a basic<tei:lb/>diphyletic origin of the flock. However, despite their professed uncertainty about the value of<tei:lb/>the pharyngeal apophysis as an indicator of phyletic relationships, Fryer &amp; lies (1972 : 506,<tei:lb/>fig. 337) virtually followed Regan's (1920) scheme. They were, of course, able to include<tei:lb/>three genera described since that time, namely Lestradea, Cardiopharynx and Ophthalmo-<tei:lb/>chromis. Cardiopharynx is now considered a synonym of Cyathopharynx (see p. 282)<tei:lb/>and Ophthalmochromis was synonymised with Ophthalmotilapia by Liem (1981: 210-21 1).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As would be expected, Fryer &amp; lies considered Ophthalmochromis and Cardiopharynx to<tei:lb/>be the sister taxa of Ophthalmotilapia and Cardiopharynx respectively. Interestingly, they<tei:lb/>associated Lestradea with Asprotilapia, Cunningtonia and the Ophthalmotilapia-<tei:lb/>Ophthalmochromis pair, but gave no reasons for doing so. Their tentative alliance of<tei:lb/>Cyathopharynx -{-Cardiopharynx with Lobochilotes and Limnotilapia is not explained<tei:lb/>either, and only partly follows Regan who implied some relationship between Limnotilapia<tei:lb/>and Lobochilotes, but also included Gephyrochromis, Simochromis and Tropheus in the<tei:lb/>same group -again without a detailed explanation (Regan 1920:52). I can find no<tei:lb/>synapomorphic characters to support the idea of a close relationship between Lobochilotes<tei:lb/>and any member of the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Like Regan, Fryer &amp; lies (1972 : fig. 337) treat Aulonocranus and Trematocara as close<tei:lb/>relatives, but give the two genera an origin separate from that of the taxa currently grouped<tei:lb/>in the 0A.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Leptochromis (now renamed Reganochromis, see Whitley, 1928), a genus not mentioned<tei:lb/>in Regan's analysis, is included by Fryer &amp; lies as a member of their Ectodus, Callochromis,<tei:lb/>Xenotilapia lineage, but again no reasons are given. This suggested relationship is discussed<tei:lb/>on p. 278 below.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In effect, the main difference between the schemes proposed by Regan (1920) and Fryer &amp;<tei:lb/>lies (1972) lies in the latter authors not portraying any taxa in an ancestor-descendent<tei:lb/>relationship, as was implied, or stated explicitly, in Regan's treatment. Also, Fryer &amp; lies<tei:lb/>indicate a more distant relationship than did Regan between Limnotilapia (i.e.<tei:lb/>Simochromis) and other members of the latter author's Ophthalmotilapia group (see above,<tei:lb/>p. 275).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In their final analysis Fryer &amp; lies are less definite in their suggested relationships than was<tei:lb/>Regan (see figure 337 in Fryer &amp; lies, 1972 : 507); their phylogram was to be '. . . regarded as<tei:lb/>extremely tentative'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem's (1981) wide ranging review of the 0A is, in its treatment of anatomical and<tei:lb/>morphological detail, far more thorough than either of the other two reviews. It was also the<tei:lb/>first to employ a basically cladistic (sensu Hennig, 1966, phylogenetic sensu Wiley, 1981)<tei:lb/>methodology.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Liem brought together certain taxa from Regan's two major groups (see above p. 275),<tei:lb/>namely Ectodus with some elements of Regan's Ophthalmotilapia- Asprotilapia group, but<tei:lb/>excluded other taxa from his Ectodus-Grammatotria assemblage (see p. 275 above).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The present analysis (also cladistically based) finally brings together, in a single lineage, all<tei:lb/>but one pair of taxa from Regan's two groups, the exceptions being Petrochromis and the<tei:lb/>Malawian genus Petrotilapia (see above, p. 275). It also includes Aulonocranus (but not<tei:lb/>Trematocara) from a third group which Regan implied had some relationship with his<tei:lb/>Callochromis-Grammatotria lineage (Regan, 1920 : 53). Effectively it hypothesizes that the<tei:lb/>two major Regan groups are sister lineages within a larger taxon which, following Liem<tei:lb/>(1981), can be named, informally, the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage (see Fig. 1 4).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
