      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>278 P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Summary of the taxonomic conclusions and a discussion of the sister-group problem</tei:p>
         <tei:p>in these and other lake cichlids</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Ophthalmotilapia assemblage, originally comprising the genera Ectodus, Lestradea,<tei:lb/>Asprotilapia, Cunningtonia and Ophthalmotilapia (Liem, 1981) can now be expanded to<tei:lb/>include Xenotilapia, Callochromis, Grammatotria, Aulonocranus and Cyathopharynx (with<tei:lb/>which is synonymized Cardiopharynx).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Taxonomically, the assemblage is recognized as a monophyletic lineage on the grounds of<tei:lb/>its member species sharing a unique congruence of five apomorphic characters (pages<tei:lb/>254-262; Fig. 14).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Two major sublineages can be recognized within the assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>One, characterized by having a short and simple coiled intestine, comprises the genera<tei:lb/>Ectodus, Aulonocranus, Asprotilapia, Xenochromis, Callochromis and Grammatotria. It can<tei:lb/>be further subdivided on the basis of various synapomorphies shared by four of its members<tei:lb/>(see pages 263-274 and Fig. 14).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The other major sublineage contains Lestradea, Ophthalmotilapia, Cunningtonia and<tei:lb/>Cyathopharynx, species in which the intestine is long and complexly coiled (see pages<tei:lb/>264-265). Attempts to subdivide this lineage have not proved satisfactory, but it would seem<tei:lb/>that Lestradea is the plesiomorph sister-group of the other three genera (pages 271-274 and<tei:lb/>Fig. 14); possibly amongst these three genera Ophthalmotilapia and Cunningtonia are sister<tei:lb/>taxa, but the entire group is, for the moment, probably best treated as an unresolved<tei:lb/>polychotomy.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>No sister-group, or even a single taxon, has so far been satisfactorily identified amongst the<tei:lb/>cichlids of Lake Tanganyika. Regan's (1920 : 52) suggestion of close relationship between<tei:lb/>Limnotilapia (i.e. Simochromis, see Greenwood, 1979) and Ophthalmotilapia (and hence<tei:lb/>Cyathopharynx, Asprotilapia and Cunningtonia as well) cannot be corroborated on the basis<tei:lb/>of shared synapomorphies.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>When comparisons are made with other Tanganyika taxa the results are equally<tei:lb/>unproductive except that two OA group synapomorphies occur, singly, in some species of<tei:lb/>' Limnochromis* and Trematocara.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>For example, in Trematocara marginata, but in no other species, there is a poorly<tei:lb/>differentiated OA-like projection from the anteroventral angle of the lachrymal (see p. 259).<tei:lb/>A similar projection occurs in 'Limnochromis'' permaxillaris and 'L.' pfefferi, both of which<tei:lb/>are now placed in Poll's genus Gnathochromis. Both the ' 'Limnochromis* species and<tei:lb/>Trematocara marginata have only 5 pores in the lachrymal, but other 'Limnochromis'<tei:lb/>species which lack the projection, V otostigma, L. auritus and L. abeeli, have 5 or 6 pores.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Neither Trematocara nor ' ' Limnochromis'' has the characteristically shaped lachrymal of<tei:lb/>the OA species, and the distribution pattern of the two OA group-features indicates an<tei:lb/>independent (i.e. homoplastic) origin in the two genera.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Regan's (1920:53) suggested relationship between Aulonocranus and Trematocara<tei:lb/>apparently stems from the hypertrophied laterosensory canals, and pores, present in both<tei:lb/>taxa. Apart from that feature, and the weak OA-type lachrymal peak in one species of<tei:lb/>Trematocara, there are no derived features uniquely shared by the two genera. Admittedly<tei:lb/>there is a trend amongst the OA species for there to be some degree of hypertrophy in the<tei:lb/>laterosensory canal system of the lachrymal. But, in the absence of other synapomorphies it<tei:lb/>would be unrealistic to use a trend character as a basis for suggesting group relationships,<tei:lb/>especially when that trend occurs in several other lineages as well. In this particular instance,<tei:lb/>too, the lowest degree of canal enlargement is found in those OA species with the least<tei:lb/>number of derived features (i.e. Ectodus and Lestradea), and which are therefore taken to be<tei:lb/>the plesiomorph members of their respective subassemblages.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The tentative phylogenetic schemes proposed by Fryer &amp; lies (1972 : 507, fig. 337) are not<tei:lb/>always arranged so as to suggest sister-group relationships with taxa outside the OA. They<tei:lb/>do, however, show Leptochromis (i.e. Reganochromis) as a sister taxon of Ectodus,<tei:lb/>Callochromis, Xenotilapia and Grammatotria, and Aulonocranus is paired with</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOT1LAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES</tei:p>
         <tei:p>279</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Trematocara. Once again, there are no shared derived features to substantiate such relation-<tei:lb/>ships, and none which might indicate that either Reganochromis or Trematocara is the<tei:lb/>sister-group, or part of the sister-group, to the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The only taxon which consistently shows more than a single OA group synapomorphy<tei:lb/>amongst a number of its species is the Malawian genus Lethrinops.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>I have examined alizarin preparations and dry skeletons of five Lethrinops species, the<tei:lb/>type species Lethrinops lethrinus, and L. praeorbitalis, L. parvidens, L. auritus and L.<tei:lb/>longimanus. In all there is a distinct palatopterygoid gap and a well-defined, although not<tei:lb/>extensive OA type adductor fossa on the anguloarticular bone (Figs 1 5 &amp; 16).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The palatopterygoid gap is relatively smaller than in most OA species, but in none of the<tei:lb/>Lethrinops species examined is there any contact between the palatine and the<tei:lb/>entopterygoid. Unlike members of the OA, the Lethrinops species have a much deeper<tei:lb/>entopterygoid, and one that either rests along the upper margin of the quadrate or slightly<tei:lb/>overlaps that bone medially. In OA species, most of the entopterygoid lies medial to the<tei:lb/>quadrate, and is thus largely obscured by it in lateral view. As a consequence of this spatial<tei:lb/>relationship the dorsal margin of the entopterygoid in Lethrinops lies at a level nearer the<tei:lb/>palatine head than it does in OA species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>* mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 15 Left suspensorium of Lethrinops lethrinus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The shape of the palatine (Fig. 15) in Lethrinops differs somewhat from that in the OA<tei:lb/>taxa(see p. 257 above). Its posterior margin is slightly concave, and the angle between this<tei:lb/>margin and the head of the bone is less nearly rectangular; the posterodorsal margin<tei:lb/>contributing to the angle is also less acute in Lethrinops; indeed, in some specimens and<tei:lb/>species it is almost rounded. But, as in the OA species the body of the bone is expanded<tei:lb/>posteriorly so that the bone's proportions are nearer those of the OA type than that<tei:lb/>commonly found amongst African cichlids.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The occurrence of this particular palatine shape in association with a palatopterygoid gap<tei:lb/>raises the question of whether or not the two characters are correlated. That a similarly<tei:lb/>shaped palatine does occur in at least two species without a palatopterygoid gap (viz.<tei:lb/>Limnochromis abeeli [Lake Tanganyika] and Astatotilapia macropsoides [Lakes Edward<tei:lb/>and George]), would seem to argue against correlation, but the possibility requires further<tei:lb/>testing.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The adductor fossa in Lethrinops (Fig. 16) is well defined but, as compared with the fossa<tei:lb/>in members of the Asprotilapia subassemblage amongst the OA (p. 261) it is less extensive. It<tei:lb/>is, however, comparable with the fossa in members of the Ophthalmotilapia subassemblage<tei:lb/>(p. 265).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>280 P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>omm<tei:lb/>Fig. 16 Left dentary and anguloarticular ofLethrinops lethrinus, lateral view.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>An adductor fossa of this type is not restricted to members of the OA, but also occurs in<tei:lb/>several seemingly unrelated taxa, including some 'Haplochromis* species from Malawi (see<tei:lb/>p. 261). Thus, in itself, the fossa cannot be considered a unique apomorphy; its value as a<tei:lb/>group synapomorphy stems solely from its congruence with other apomorphic characters.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is a third derived character found in the five Lethrinops species which is also present<tei:lb/>in one subgroup of the OA, namely a stepped dorsal margin to the dentary, with the teeth<tei:lb/>confined to the higher level of the step (Fig. 16). Amongst the OA taxa this feature is found<tei:lb/>only in Xenotilapia, Callochromis and Grammatotria (all members of the Asprotilapia<tei:lb/>subassemblage; see p. 267). Its restricted distribution within the OA considerably reduces its<tei:lb/>potential significance as a character indicative of a possible relationship between Lethrinops<tei:lb/>and the OA. That it apparently occurs only in these OA species and in Lethrinops, and that<tei:lb/>both groups have a palatopterygoid gap is, nevertheless, intriguing and requires further<tei:lb/>investigation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Attempts to evaluate the two apomorphic features shared by Lethrinops and the OA in its<tei:lb/>entirety (i.e. the palatopterygoid gap and the adductor fossa) are hampered by lack of<tei:lb/>comparative data from the Malawi cichlids as a whole. A relatively superficial survey shows,<tei:lb/>however, that the adductor fossa is present only in some haplochromine species (see p. 26 1 ).<tei:lb/>Whether or not these haplochromines are closely related to Lethrinops has not been<tei:lb/>adequately tested, but preliminary investigations do not suggest that this is the case.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The situation regarding the palatopterygoid gap is different. I have examined specimens of<tei:lb/>all the described genera of Malawi fishes, but by no means all their included species. In none<tei:lb/>is there a palatopterygoid gap. Thus, the character appears restricted to Lethrinops and the<tei:lb/>OA (see also p. 255).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In summary, it seems that no group of Lake Tanganyika cichlids consistently shows one or<tei:lb/>more of the group synapomorphies for the OA. On the other hand, in Lake Malawi at least<tei:lb/>some species of Lethrinops share two derived features with all members of the OA, and<tei:lb/>only one of these features (the adductor fossa) occurs in other Malawi taxa. The Lethrinops<tei:lb/>species also have a third derived feature, the shape of the dentary, which is present in one<tei:lb/>lineage amongst the OA.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As it stands, this indication of a possible sister-group relationship between the OA and<tei:lb/>Lethrinops is no more than suggestive. It could well be refuted as an example of convergence<tei:lb/>when more is known about the interrelationships of the cichlids from Lake Malawi and those<tei:lb/>of Lake Tanganyika.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There are several cases of close similarity existing between certain features in endemic taxa<tei:lb/>of each lake, but few between species in those lakes and endemic taxa from Lake Victoria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As examples one may mention that the dentition in Cunningtonia is virtually identical<tei:lb/>with that in Petrotilapia and there are marked similarities in the jaw anatomy of the two<tei:lb/>genera (Petrochromis from Lake Tanganyika can also be included in this example); or, the<tei:lb/>morphology of the dentary in Asprotilapia which is like that of Labeotropheus a Malawian<tei:lb/>genus also sharing several features with Tropheus from Lake Tanganyika. Finally, one can<tei:lb/>cite the many similarities in syncranial architecture existing between Simochromis of<tei:lb/>Tanganyika and the Pseudotropheus species complex of Lake Malawi.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES</tei:p>
         <tei:p>281</tei:p>
         <tei:p>These are by no means the only cases that can be, or have been, cited of supposed<tei:lb/>convergence or parallelism between the cichlid faunas of the two lakes (see Fryer &amp; lies,<tei:lb/>1 972). Adequate explanations for these similarities are, however, far more difficult to find.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>An example of similarity involving much greater geographical separation than that<tei:lb/>between Tanganyika and Malawi, and one which incorporates a greater number of species<tei:lb/>as well as a mosaic distribution of similar characters amongst the species, involves two<tei:lb/>members of the OA, another, unrelated, genus from Lake Tanganyika, a Malawian taxon<tei:lb/>and Neopharynx schwetzi, a monotypic genus from the lower Fwa (Kasai drainage in south-<tei:lb/>western Zaire; for a full description of N. schwetzi, see Poll, 1948).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>&gt;mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 17 Lower pharyngeal bone of Neopharynx schwetzi, occlusal view (from a specimen in</tei:p>
         <tei:p>paratypical series, MRAC 7 1 29 1-7 1 299).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Neopharynx schwetzi has a lower pharyngeal bone morphologically and dentally almost<tei:lb/>identical with that of Cyathopharynx (see Figs 17 &amp; 24). Its oral dentition, in contrast, is<tei:lb/>virtually identical with that in Petrochromis (Tanganyika) and Petrotilapia (Malawi), and is<tei:lb/>quite unlike that in Cyathopharynx. As far as the morphology of the teeth is concerned, but<tei:lb/>not their distribution on the dentary, Neopharynx also closely resembles Cunningtonia of<tei:lb/>Lake Tanganyika. It differs from all three taxa in the shape of its premaxilla, but the<tei:lb/>morphology of the premaxillary teeth is, as might be expected, very similar in all four genera.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The relationships of Neopharynx have yet to be established; probably they lie with two<tei:lb/>other Fwa endemics, Cyclopharynx and Callopharynx, genera having an extreme<tei:lb/>development of the Neopharynx-Cyathopharynx type of lower pharyngeal bone (for details<tei:lb/>see Poll, 1948).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Neopharynx has neither an OA type lachrymal, a palatopterygoid gap, nor an OA-type of<tei:lb/>adductor fossa, and the shape of its palatine bone is close to the generalized form. Thus the<tei:lb/>similarities between Neopharynx, Cyathopharynx and Cunningtonia are undoubtedly<tei:lb/>homoplastic, as most probably are the similarities shared with Petrochromis and<tei:lb/>Petrotilapia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Neopharynx example underlines the problems involved in attempting to work out<tei:lb/>interrelationships amongst cichlid fishes, as do the repeated appearances of certain derived<tei:lb/>features shown by members of the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage in species which appear to<tei:lb/>be but distantly related to the OA.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Surmises about the interrelationships of African lake cichlids have, I believe, been unduly<tei:lb/>influenced, perhaps even inhibited, by three major factors. Firstly, the idea that there are two</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>282 P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>basic lineages, a ' Tilapia" line and a 'Haplochromis' one (see discussion in Greenwood, 1 978;<tei:lb/>also p. 274 above). Secondly, that the major lakes are, faunistically, closed basins and have<tei:lb/>been so almost since their inception, with the result that the cichlids of a lake are presumed<tei:lb/>to have evolved from one or a few ancestral species originally trapped there (see discussion in<tei:lb/>Fryer &amp; lies, 1972; and Greenwood, 1974). In other words, assumed histories for the lakes<tei:lb/>have been given too great a weight in deciding whether a feature was the result of<tei:lb/>convergence, parallelism, or the consequence of common ancestry.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The third, and overriding, factor is a paucity of specific and critical studies on the<tei:lb/>phylogeny of the fishes. Overall resemblances, or the use of characters without adequate<tei:lb/>outgroup comparisons, are often major weaknesses influencing decisions on relationships.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The effect of these three factors has resulted in a tendency to restrict the search for sister-<tei:lb/>groups to a single lake and to the appropriate 'Tilapia' or 'Haplochromis' lineage.<tei:lb/>Admittedly, at lower levels of universality sister species are generally to be found within the<tei:lb/>same lake or proto-lake system (Greenwood, 1980); the problems arise when attempting to<tei:lb/>establish relationships at somewhat higher taxonomic levels. The Ophthalmotilapia<tei:lb/>assemblage, and the search for its sister-group are good examples of problems encountered at<tei:lb/>these two levels.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The existence of endemic species flocks, the superficially close similarity of species in<tei:lb/>different flocks, and indeed of many different taxa outside the lakes, all help to complicate<tei:lb/>the issue. Under such circumstances the possibility and probability of homoplasy are<tei:lb/>theoretically enhanced, as in practice are the problems associated with their resolution.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>What is needed to resolve these problems are tests of the assumption that a so-called<tei:lb/>species flock is really of monophyletic origin. If a lake's cichlid fauna was derived from a few<tei:lb/>species which are not true sister species, and if the true sister taxa were the ancestors of<tei:lb/>another flock, then the situation suggested by the apparent relationship of Lethrinops<tei:lb/>(Malawi) and the 0A (Tanganyika) could well be a real one. The idea first put forward by<tei:lb/>Regan (1922) that the Malawi 'flock' carries indicators of its monophyly must be seriously<tei:lb/>questioned (Greenwood, in press). No indicators of monophyly have been suggested for<tei:lb/>the Tanganyika 'flock' (even at the levels of the supposed 'Tilapia' and "Haplochromis" type<tei:lb/>basic stocks). The possibility of a close relationship between the faunas of the two lakes<tei:lb/>deserves very careful examination.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The status of Cyathopharynx Regan, 1920 and Cardiopharynx Poll, 1942</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In his original description of the monotypic genus Cardiopharynx, Poll (1942 : 346) noted<tei:lb/>the great similarity between its peculiarly shaped lower pharyngeal bone and the lower<tei:lb/>pharyngeal of Cyathopharynx. He differentiated the two genera because Cardiopharynx has,<tei:lb/>as compared with Cyathopharynx:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(i) Larger scales (36-38 c/48-64 in a longitudinal series (see Poll, 1956:1 27).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(ii) Jaw teeth in two rows, the teeth, in both jaws, of equal size (c/3-5 rows; teeth in the</tei:p>
         <tei:p>outer row larger than those of the inner rows),<tei:lb/>(iii) Dentigerous surface of the lower pharyngeal bone cardiform (cf rounded in</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cyathopharynx)<tei:lb/>(iv) Supraoccipital extending forward to a level above the anterior margin of the orbit</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(c/to the mid-orbital region only)<tei:lb/>(v) Parietal crests ending above the centre of the orbit (c/extending to a point above the</tei:p>
         <tei:p>posterior part of the orbit)<tei:lb/>(vi) More vertebrae (36, i.e. 17+19) c/32-34 (i.e. 1 6 or 1 7 + 1 6 or 1 7)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Poll gives no reasons why these characters should be used to separate the taxa at a generic<tei:lb/>level, and neither does he indicate why the great similarity in pharyngeal morphology, and<tei:lb/>its uniqueness, should be outweighed as an indicator of close phyletic relationship by the<tei:lb/>diagnostic characters he enumerates.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES 283</tei:p>
         <tei:p>None of these latter features can be considered uniquely apomorphic for Cardiopharynx,<tei:lb/>and now that more material is available several are found to be less trenchant than was first<tei:lb/>thought to be the case, as the following comments show.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The teeth in both jaws of all Cardiopharynx specimens I examined are invariably arranged<tei:lb/>in two rows, but in Cyathopharynx there is a greater variation than was intimated by Poll<tei:lb/>(1942 &amp; 1956). In the majority of specimens examined, the inner premaxillary row is, in<tei:lb/>places, irregularly arranged so as to give the appearance of a double row wherever the<tei:lb/>irregularities occur. Occasionally there are specimens in which the inner tooth row is clearly<tei:lb/>and regularly double, thus giving a total count of three tooth rows; also occasionally it is<tei:lb/>distinctly single, giving a total of two rows. In none of the specimens is there a total of more<tei:lb/>than three premaxillary rows. The inner row of teeth in the dentary is generally single; in a<tei:lb/>few fishes, however, it is somewhat irregular and so comes to resemble the modal condition<tei:lb/>in the premaxilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Contrary to Poll (1946), I can find no marked difference between the taxa in the relative<tei:lb/>size of inner and outer teeth. In both genera the outer teeth, in both jaws, are clearly taller<tei:lb/>and stouter than those of the inner row or rows, and not of equal size in Cardiopharynx as<tei:lb/>claimed by Poll. The most that can be said is that in Cardiopharynx the size difference<tei:lb/>between outer and inner row teeth is a little less marked than in Cyathopharynx.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In a later redescription of both taxa, Poll (1956) commented on the outer teeth of<tei:lb/>Cardiopharynx being more or less tricuspid in young fishes, but he gave no size-range over<tei:lb/>which tricuspid teeth are found. In specimens 60-105 mm SL I have examined, there are no<tei:lb/>outer tricuspids, whereas in specimens of Cyathopharynx of a comparable size range and up<tei:lb/>to 1 12 mm SL, distinctly, and also weakly, tricuspid teeth do occur. In larger Cyathopharynx<tei:lb/>specimens the teeth are exclusively and clearly unicuspid.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The difference in the shape of the dentigerous surface of the lower pharyngeal bone (iii<tei:lb/>above, p. 282) is due entirely to a marked median depression in the posterior face of the bone<tei:lb/>and of the toothed area in Cardiopharynx. In Cyathopharynx this margin of the bone is<tei:lb/>slightly and more broadly indented, and there is no indentation of the posterior tooth row<tei:lb/>(Fig. 23).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In all other respects the lower pharyngeal bone in both taxa is identical. As noted above (p.<tei:lb/>27 1 ) it represents a uniquely derived condition amongst the cichlids of Lake Tanganyika.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The shape of the pharyngeal teeth, tall and slender, with spatulate crowns that are not<tei:lb/>broader than the neck, and their cardiform pattern on the alveolar surface of the bone, is the<tei:lb/>same in both genera (Fig. 24). Likewise, the morphology of the principal upper pharyngeal<tei:lb/>bones is identical, as is the morphology and pattern of their teeth. Unlike the lower teeth,<tei:lb/>those on the upper pharyngeal bones do have a slight, shoulder-like cusp at the base of the<tei:lb/>spatulate crown surface.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The anterior point reached by the supraoccipital and parietal crests (iv &amp; v above) is<tei:lb/>variable intragenerically. In the skulls I have examined there are specimens from each genus<tei:lb/>showing conditions intermediate between those originally used as diagnostic features for the<tei:lb/>two genera.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Finally, there are the supposedly intergeneric differences in vertebral number (vi above). I<tei:lb/>have examined 15 specimens of Cardiopharynx schoutedeni (13 from radiographs, 2 as<tei:lb/>alizarin preparations), and 12 of Cyathopharynx furcifer (10 from radiographs and 2 dry<tei:lb/>skeletons), and obtained the following counts:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cardiopharynx: Total number (excluding the fused Ui-PUi centra) 33 (f5) and 34<tei:lb/>(flO), comprising 1 5 (fl), 16 (f7) or 17 (f7) abdominal and 16 (fl), 17(f9) or 18 (f5) caudal<tei:lb/>elements.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cyathopharynx: Total number (excluding the fused Ui-PUi centra) 32 (f5) 33 (f6) or 34<tei:lb/>(fl ), comprising 1 6 (f9) or 1 7 (D) abdominal and 1 6 (f7) or 1 7 (f5) caudal elements.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The differences in range are not very marked and there is a complete overlap in other<tei:lb/>counts, but with a slight difference in the modal number for total [34 c/33] and abdominal<tei:lb/>counts [1 7 cf\ 6] for Cardiopharynx and Cyathopharynx respectively.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Thus, of Poll's (1942) original diagnostic features, only the difference in scale size remains.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>284 P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There are, however, other differences which were noted in the original diagnosis.<tei:lb/>Cyathopharynx has proportionately longer pelvic fins, in males the tip of this fin reaches the<tei:lb/>last anal fin ray or even to as far as the caudal fin fork; in Cardiopharynx it reaches only to<tei:lb/>about the middle of the anal fin. Also, in Cyathopharynx the first pelvic ray is clearly the<tei:lb/>longest whereas in Cardiopharynx either the first and second rays are equally protracted or<tei:lb/>the second ray may be the longest.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Other differences involve neurocranial shape (Figs 18 &amp; 19). Cardiopharynx has a<tei:lb/>shallower skull than does Cyathopharynx, the prootic portion of its otic bulla is more<tei:lb/>inflated, and the interorbital region is much narrower.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Undoubtedly the two taxa are distinguishable. The problem is to decide at what<tei:lb/>taxonomic level their separation should be recognized.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>To recognize two genera on the basis of the differences discussed above is to obscure the<tei:lb/>fact that, amongst the Lake Tanganyika cichlids, Cyathopharynx and Cardiopharynx share a<tei:lb/>unique apomorphy (the form of the lower pharyngeal bone) which would indicate a common<tei:lb/>ancestry not shared with any other taxon. That relationship is, I believe, best indicated by<tei:lb/>treating the two species as members of a single genus, Cyathopharynx Regan, 1920.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cyathopharynx Regan, 1 920</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cyathopharynx Regan, 1920. Ann. Mag. nat. Hist. (9), 5: 42-43.<tei:lb/>Cardiopharynx Poll, 1942. Revue Zool. Bot. afr., 36: 346-347.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE SPECIES. Tilapia grandoculis Boulenger, 1899. Trans, zool. Soc. Lond. 15: 94, pi. XIX,<tei:lb/>fig. 6.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Poll (1946 : 283^) has synonymized this species with C.furcifer (Blgr), 1 898.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. A member of the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage, distinguished from other<tei:lb/>members of that group by having a cardiform dentigerous surface to the lower pharyngeal<tei:lb/>bone, the body of which is inflated and nearly cardiform in outline.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cyathopharynx is distinguished from other species with a cardiform alveolar surface to the<tei:lb/>lower pharyngeal bone by, among other features, its &lt;9A group characters (see p. 262) and by<tei:lb/>the marked elongation of the first, or first and second pelvic fin rays in adult male fishes;<tei:lb/>these protracted rays extend to at least the middle of the anal fin, and in one species, some-<tei:lb/>times as far as the fork of the caudal fin.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Description</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Neurocranium (Figs 1 8 &amp; 1 9). In its general outline, the skull differs little from that of the<tei:lb/>generalized haplochromine type in which the preorbital profile is slightly decurved. The<tei:lb/>orbit, however, is relatively larger than in a generalized skull, and the otico-occipital region<tei:lb/>is shorter, a correlate, probably, of the enlarged orbit since the ethmoid region retains the<tei:lb/>same proportions as in a generalized skull.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The ventral apophysis for the upper pharyngeal bones is of a weak 'Tropheus 1 type, in<tei:lb/>which the basioccipital barely contributes to the articular surface (see Greenwood, 1978; also<tei:lb/>p. 274 above).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Suspensorium (Figs 1 C &amp; D). As in other members of the Ophthalmotilapia assemblage,<tei:lb/>there is a distinct palatopterygoid gap (see p. 255), and the entopterygoid is shallow, with<tei:lb/>only one-third to one-quarter of its depth visible above the quadrate margin. The<tei:lb/>hyomadibula has a narrow flange anterior and dorsal to the symplectic process. The shape<tei:lb/>and proportions of the symplectic are typically those of an OA species (see p. 256).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Infraorbital series (Figs 3 A &amp; F). The lachrymal bone (1st infraorbital) has the typical<tei:lb/>outline shape, and the anteroventral process, of an OA taxon (see p. 259). There are six<tei:lb/>pores opening from the laterosensory canal system, the tubular part of which is somewhat</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES</tei:p>
         <tei:p>285</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 18 Neurocranium of Cyathopharynx furcifer in : A, left lateral view; B, dorsal view.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>inflated. The pores, however, do not show a corresponding enlargement (i.e. they do not<tei:lb/>deviate noticeably from the generalized condition). The other infraorbital bones are little<tei:lb/>more than tubular ossifications around the sensory canal, but do have low dorsal and ventral<tei:lb/>keels.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Myology. Division I of the adductor mandibulae complex has an extensive origin along<tei:lb/>the vertical limb of the preoperculum, but division II has its preopercular origin mainly from<tei:lb/>the horizontal limb of that bone; only a small area extends onto the vertical limb.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The dorsal gill-arch muscles compare closely, in most respects, with those of other 0A<tei:lb/>species (see Liem, 1981 : 196-7 &amp; 205, &amp; fig. 8; also p. 258 above). The retractor dorsalis<tei:lb/>muscles are especially well developed with, in some individuals of both species, indications<tei:lb/>of a subdivison into dorsal and ventral components.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dentition (Fig. 20). Some aspects of the oral dentition have been commented upon already<tei:lb/>(p. 283 above). The outer row teeth in both jaws are tall and slender. In C. furcifer the crown<tei:lb/>is slightly broader than the neck and shaft of the tooth, whereas in C. schoutedeni the crown<tei:lb/>is no wider than the shaft. The crowns are slightly recurved in both species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Premaxillary outer row teeth are aligned vertically to the alveolar surface, but in the<tei:lb/>dentary the teeth situated anteriorly and anterolaterally are procumbent. The posterior<tei:lb/>dentary teeth are vertical, those of C. furcifer continuing for some distance up the coronoid<tei:lb/>process.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Inner row teeth in both jaws, and both species, are noticeably smaller than those of the<tei:lb/>outer row, and are implanted so as to lie almost horizontally.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Mouth. The lips are thin, and the gape is horizontal.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>286</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 19 Neurocranium of Cyathopharynx schoutedeni in : A, left lateral view; B, dorsal view.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 20 Outer row jaw teeth (drawn in situ) from : A &amp; B, Cyathopharynx schoutedeni, specimen<tei:lb/>103 mm SL (premaxillae and dentary respectively); C &amp; D, C. furcifer, specimen 1 13 mm SL<tei:lb/>(premaxilla and dentary, respectively). Dentary teeth viewed from below.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Jaws. The dentary in both species is a moderately slender bone, shallower and less robust in<tei:lb/>C. schoutedeni than in C. furcifer (Fig. 2 1 ). Anteriorly and anterolaterally the dorsal aspect of<tei:lb/>the bone is expanded into a broad surface which extends outwards to overhang, as a narrow<tei:lb/>shelf, the body of the bone. The tooth rows occupy only the outermost part of the surface,<tei:lb/>with the result that there is a wide expanse of bone lying medial to them. The laterosensory<tei:lb/>canal system and its openings in C. furcifer are more cavernous than those in C. schoutedeni.<tei:lb/>The anguloarticular has a well-defined but short fossa for the adductor mandibulae<tei:lb/>muscle, with the ridge delimiting its anterior margin particularly deep and prominent.<tei:lb/>The premaxilla (Fig. 22) has no especially outstanding features.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES</tei:p>
         <tei:p>287</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 21 Dentary and anguloarticular, in left lateral and occlusal views respectively, of : A &amp; B,<tei:lb/>Cyathopharynxfurcifen C &amp; D, C. schoutedeni.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 22 A &amp; B, premaxilla ofCyathopharynxfurcifer, anterior and lateral view; C, maxilla (left)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>seen from a slightly ventrolateral viewpoint.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>288</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. H. GREENWOOD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As compared with the generalized type of maxilla, that in Cyathopharynx is foreshortened<tei:lb/>and has a well-developed, long-based posterior process (see also p. 269).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The pharyngeal bones. The shape and other peculiar features of the lower pharyngeal bone<tei:lb/>(Fig. 23) are described on page 283.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The upper pharyngeal bones differ less markedly from the usual condition seen in African<tei:lb/>cichlids. The outline of the alveolar surface of the major element (pharyngobranchial 3) is<tei:lb/>noticeably ovoid, but otherwise differs little from the generalised condition. Their principal<tei:lb/>difference lies in the relatively greater alveolar surface area, and its more elongate<tei:lb/>proportions. Other differences are found in the less prominent facets for articulation with the<tei:lb/>3rd and 4th epibranchials, and in the lower summit facet (nomenclature following Barel et<tei:lb/>al, 1976: 214, fig. 26).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The lower pharyngeal teeth (Fig. 24) are slender, near cylindrical in cross-section and are<tei:lb/>closely packed. Those at the periphery of the dentigerous area are weakly curved, the others</tei:p>
         <tei:p>i mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 23 Lower pharyngeal bone, in occlusal view of: A, Cyathopharynx furcifer, B,<tei:lb/>C. schoutedeni, and, in ventral view, C, of C. furcifer.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>B</tei:p>
         <tei:p>I \</tei:p>
         <tei:p>0.25mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 24 Cyathopharynx furcifer, pharyngeal teeth (lateral and semiocclusal views) from : A, the</tei:p>
         <tei:p>posterior; and B, anterior dental fields.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE OPHTHALMOTILAPIA ASSEMBLAGE OF CICHLID FISHES 289</tei:p>
         <tei:p>erect. There is a noticeable and rapid increase in the height of the teeth forming the posterior<tei:lb/>5 or 6 rows, with the teeth of the posterior row tallest and stoutest. The elongate crown of<tei:lb/>each tooth is flat, and slopes gently upwards and backwards. There is no indication of a low<tei:lb/>shoulder-like projection or cusp at the base of the crown.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The shape and dense arrangement of the upper pharyngeal teeth are very similar to those<tei:lb/>of the lower bone, the teeth differing only in having a small shoulder or cusp at the base of the<tei:lb/>posteriorly directed crown.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Vertebrae. Regan (1920 : 43), in his original description of Cyathopharynx, noted that the<tei:lb/>third vertebra lacks an inferior apophysis, from which the retractor dorsalis muscles<tei:lb/>originate. In four of the five dry skeletons and alizarin preparations I examined, a low<tei:lb/>apophysis is present on the fourth centrum in one fish and on the fifth centrum in three<tei:lb/>others, but none is present in the fifth specimen.<tei:lb/>Vertebral counts for the two species are given on page 283.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Squamation. Scales on the body are weakly ctenoid except for the cycloid scales on the chest<tei:lb/>and belly. Those covering the thoracic region are small, and are fairly abruptly demarcated<tei:lb/>from the larger scales on the ventral flanks and the belly.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The two Cyathopharynx species differ, disjunctly, in the size of their body scales, with<tei:lb/>C. schoutedeni having 36-38 scales in a longitudinal series, and C. furcifer 48-64. The<tei:lb/>species also differ, slightly, in the posterior extension of the upper lateral line pore scales,<tei:lb/>those in C. furcifer usually extending almost to the caudal fin base, whereas in C. schoutedeni<tei:lb/>the pore scales terminate at a level 3 or 4 scale rows anterior to the caudal base. There is,<tei:lb/>however, some interspecific overlap in this feature.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fins. Little can be added to the description (p. 272) of the protracted first or first and second<tei:lb/>pelvic fin rays in adult males. In females and juvenile males these rays are also noticeably<tei:lb/>longer than the others, but usually do not extend beyond the level of the anus; exceptionally<tei:lb/>they may reach the spinous part of that fin.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The caudal fin is forked; adult males have the two upper- and lowermost principal<tei:lb/>branched rays produced into fine filaments. Rows of small, barely overlapping scales are<tei:lb/>present on the fin membrane (except between the three middle rays), and extend to the level<tei:lb/>of the fork.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gut. The intestine is long (ca 3 to 4 times SL) and complexly coiled in a predominantly<tei:lb/>transverse direction (see p. 264).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Contained species</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cyathopharynx furcifer (Blgr) 1898<tei:lb/>Cyathopharynx schoutedeni (Poll) 1 942</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Both are lacustrine species endemic to Lake Tanganyika; for detailed descriptions, figures<tei:lb/>and biological data see Poll (1956: 1 30-1 37 &amp; 1 25-1 30 for the species respectively).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Synopsis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Acanthodactylus is reassessed, supplementing the external features previously used with new data from<tei:lb/>the skeleton and hemipenis. The genus appears to be closely related to Eremias and Mesalina rather<tei:lb/>than to Latastia as was previously thought, and 'Eremias' guineensis is confirmed as an<tei:lb/>Acanthodactylus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is suggested that genitalia have an enhanced propensity to 'store' evidence of shared evolutionary<tei:lb/>experience in the form of common characters among descendants, when compared with other organ<tei:lb/>systems. Such characters deserve relatively high weight in assessing relationships. However, not all<tei:lb/>genital characters can be interpreted in this way for some seem likely to have evolved as physical<tei:lb/>isolating mechanisms between similar species and, in such cases, closely related forms may have<tei:lb/>radically different genitalia. Differences of this kind, together with some osteological features, have<tei:lb/>proved important in establishing the species status of several forms usually regarded as subspecies or<tei:lb/>varieties. Thus the four subspecies of A. cantoris are now accorded full species status as A. cantoris, A.<tei:lb/>blanfordii, A. schmidti and A. arabicus and a similar upgrading may well be appropriate for two taxa<tei:lb/>usually subsumed in A. tristrami: A. (/.) tristrami and A. (t.) orientalis. Within the A. pardalis complex,<tei:lb/>A. pardalis, A. maculatus and A. spinicauda are regarded as separate species and the form listed by</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mm. nat. Hist. (Zool.) 44(5): 29 1-339</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Issued 26 May 1983</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>292 E. N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Boulenger (1921) as var. bedriagai is treated as a subspecies of A. pardalis; a similar un-named West<tei:lb/>Moroccan population is also assigned to this species. Recently available material makes it probable<tei:lb/>that A. grandis and A. fraseri are closely related and perhaps allocatable to the same highly variable<tei:lb/>species. Within the A. scutellatus group the following taxa recognized by Bons and Girot (1962) are<tei:lb/>assigned to A. scutellatus itself: A. s. scutellatus, A. s. audouini, A. s. hardyi, A. i. inornatus and A.<tei:lb/>dumerilii. A. longipes is retained as a separate species and the same status is given to A. aureus which<tei:lb/>Bons and Girot regarded as a subspecies of A. inornatus. Geographical variation requires considerable<tei:lb/>further study in a number of taxa including the A. grandis complex, A. boskianus and the A. scutellatus<tei:lb/>group but a number of currently recognized subspecies are invalid such as A. tristrami iracensis<tei:lb/>Schmidt, 1939 ( = A. (/.) orientalis), A. pardalis latastii ( = A. maculatus) and probably several of the<tei:lb/>forms in the A. scutellatus group.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>An attempt has been made to estimate a phylogeny for the species of Acanthodactylus, although<tei:lb/>considerable character conflict exists.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The lacertid lizard genus Acanthodactylus Wiegmann, 1834 contains about 26 species. It<tei:lb/>occurs from Spain and Portugal across the Sahara desert and its periphery to the Red Sea,<tei:lb/>over most of Arabia and as far north as Cyprus and the Syrian-Turkish border; it also<tei:lb/>extends through Iraq, south and east Iran, south Afghanistan, Pakistan and northwest India.<tei:lb/>All its members are quite small, maximum body sizes of populations varying from about<tei:lb/>52 mm to around 105 mm from snout to vent. They are diurnal and essentially ground-<tei:lb/>dwelling lizards usually found in relatively flat, often sandy situations and occurring in a<tei:lb/>wide variety of dry habitats which usually have at least some vegetation and range from open<tei:lb/>woodland to the borders of quite severe desert. Substrates occupied vary from quite hard<tei:lb/>loess and clay to aeolian sand. All species appear to be mainly active hunters and feed largely<tei:lb/>on small invertebrates including ants. Body temperatures of normally active animals are<tei:lb/>usually in the range 36 to 41 C (personal observations; Duvdevani &amp; Borut, 1974a). Like<tei:lb/>most open-country lizards, Acanthodactylus species are probably very prone to bird<tei:lb/>predation and are also taken by other vertebrates such as snakes and monitor lizards<tei:lb/>(Varanus). Predator avoidance seems to depend largely on crypsis (colour match with<tei:lb/>substrate is often very good), fleeing, tail autotomy and the use of burrows. All species are<tei:lb/>oviparous and usual clutch size varies from two to about seven eggs, females in many<tei:lb/>populations appearing to produce more than one clutch annually. The majority of forms<tei:lb/>seem to mature within a year of hatching although some, like A. erythrurus in Spain and<tei:lb/>Portugal, may take two seasons.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The most detailed synopsis of the genus to date is that of Boulenger (192 1) which although<tei:lb/>inevitably to some extent out dated, remains extremely useful, providing detailed descrip-<tei:lb/>tions of many forms. However, the amount of material available for study has increased<tei:lb/>massively over the past fifty years. In particular, specimens from many areas previously<tei:lb/>unsampled, such as much of the Sahara desert and Arabia, have been obtained. Furthermore,<tei:lb/>both Boulenger and subsequent workers have depended almost entirely on external<tei:lb/>characters. As there are reasons for believing that these on their own can be misleading when<tei:lb/>judging lacertid relationships (see for example Arnold, 1973) it seems worthwhile to increase<tei:lb/>the range of characters considered and, in the present paper, osteology and the structure of<tei:lb/>the hemipenis are taken into account.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>What follows is not a formal revision but an overview of Acanthodactylus is given paying<tei:lb/>especial attention to the objective reality of the genus, species boundaries and, so far as they<tei:lb/>can be judged, inter-relationships of its members. Although the results presented here differ<tei:lb/>considerably from previously held opinions, the genus still requires a more detailed appraisal<tei:lb/>based on the large but scattered collections available in continental Europe and North<tei:lb/>America.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS 293</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In this paper, the methods of phylogeny estimation employed are those discussed by<tei:lb/>Arnold (1981 a). They depend largely but not entirely on Hennig's (1950, 1 966) precept that<tei:lb/>joint possession of a derived character state is prima Jade evidence of relationship. The word<tei:lb/>relationship is used in its genealogical sense: two species are more closely related to each<tei:lb/>other than to a third if they share a common ancestor not shared by that form. The following<tei:lb/>terms coined by Hennig are used, together with the adjectives derived from them:<tei:lb/>apomorphy a derived character state; synapomorphy a derived character state shared by<tei:lb/>two or more species and possibly indicating their relationship to each other; plesiomorphy<tei:lb/>a primitive character state; symplesiomorphy a primitive character state snared by two or<tei:lb/>more species, it does not indicate their relationship. Monophyletic and holophyletic are used<tei:lb/>in the sense of Ashlock (1974).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Abbreviations used</tei:p>
         <tei:p>BM(NH) British Museum (Natural History), London; CAS California Academy of<tei:lb/>Sciences, San Francisco; CM Carnegie Museum, Pittsburgh; EBD Estacion Biologica de<tei:lb/>Donana, Seville); INHM Iraq Natural History Museum, Baghdad; JUM Jordan<tei:lb/>University Museum; MCZ Museum of Comparative Zoology, Harvard; NMW<tei:lb/>Naturhistorisches Museum, Vienna; RSM Royal Scottish Museum; USNM United<tei:lb/>States National Museum, Washington.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The reality of the genus Acanthodactylus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>On the basis of external features, Boulenger (19 18a) described the genus Acanthodactylus as<tei:lb/>'un des plus naturels et des plus nettement delimites de la famille des Lacertides'. Exam-<tei:lb/>ination of skeletal and hemipenial characters provides additional support for Boulenger's<tei:lb/>view and Acanthodactylus is confirmed as a clearly denned assemblage that shows no<tei:lb/>integration into other genera. Most of its features are found in the lizard usually known as<tei:lb/>Eremias guineensis but it is certain that this form should really be included in Acantho-<tei:lb/>dactylus (see p. 296). Features present in all or most species are listed below.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 . Frontal bones completely fused in adults and at least largely so in juveniles.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2. Often a fontanelle in the anterior wall of the orbit between the frontal and prefrontal</tei:p>
         <tei:p>bones.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3 . A backwardly directed spur of jugal bone absent.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>4. Parietal fontanelle present,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5. Parietal bone not projecting backwards over the supraoccipital.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>6. Postorbital and postfrontal bones usually separate (fused only in A. cantoris).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>7. Postorbital bone not filling supratemporal foramen.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>8. Parietal and squamosal bones not usually in contact.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>9. Pterygoid teeth present or absent (often considerable intraspecific variation).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 0. Epipterygoid not usually in direct contact with pro-otic bone.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 1 . Fourteen scleral ossicles present in each eye.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>12. Scleral ossicle number 14 (following numbering system of Gugg, 1939) lacks a</tei:p>
         <tei:p>radially directed peripheral section (see Fig. 1).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>13. Number of presacral vertebrae ranges from 23 to 27, in most cases 23-26.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>14. In the post-thoracic series of free dorsal ribs, the longer anterior ribs are fewer than</tei:p>
         <tei:p>the shorter posterior ones.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 5. Ribs on last presacral vertebra very reduced or absent.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>294 E. N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 1 Scleral ossicles of Acanthodactylus; arrow indicates scleral ossicle 14 which lacks a radially</tei:p>
         <tei:p>directed peripheral section.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>16. Sternum with a more or less heart-shaped fontanelle, which is sometimes divided</tei:p>
         <tei:p>into left and right sections.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 7. Sternal: xiphisternal rib formula 3 : 2 or less commonly 3:1.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>18. Clavicle expanded medially with a large fenestra in this region, so that the bone</tei:p>
         <tei:p>forms an uninterrupted loop.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>19. Interclavicle cruciform, the lateral arms usually directed obliquely forwards.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>20. Only the principal coracoid foramen present.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>21. Caudal vertebrae of the C-type (Arnold, 1973: 305); that is, the most anterior</tei:p>
         <tei:p>autotomic vertebrae have two pairs of transverse processes, the members of<tei:lb/>the posterior one being longer and directed obliquely backwards.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>22. Hemipenis and armature usually asymmetrical with medial side reduced (see</tei:p>
         <tei:p>p. 300).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>23. Hemipenis with an armature consisting of a flat intramuscular plate, one or two</tei:p>
         <tei:p>clavulae and short connectors.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>24. In species where the hemipenis and armature is not very reduced there may be more</tei:p>
         <tei:p>than four connectors.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>25. Nostril closable by a valve hinging on the posterior side of the opening.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>26. Nostril nearly always situated between a postnasal, an internasal and the first upper</tei:p>
         <tei:p>labial (not in A. guineensis, although the situation in this species is derivable<tei:lb/>from that found in other Acanthodactylus, see Fig. 2).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>27. First upper labial broad above but sides converge downwards (not in A. guineensis',</tei:p>
         <tei:p>see comments about previous character).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>28. Occipital scale reduced or, more usually absent.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>29. A distinct collar of imbricate scales beneath neck.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>30. Dorsals small and granular or larger, imbricate and keeled with rounded posterior</tei:p>
         <tei:p>borders.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3 1 . Maximum number of ventral scales in a row across the belly varies from 8 to 1 8.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>32. Toes with three longitudinal rows of scales around them and fingers with three or</tei:p>
         <tei:p>four rows.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>33. Subdigital lamellae keeled.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>34. Lateral rows of scales on the toes and sometimes fingers forming pectinations.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>35. Femoral pores present.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>36. Tail more or less cylindrical, longer than body.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>295</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 2 Right nasal regions of Eremias and Acanthodactylus'. (a) 'Eremias' ( = Acanthodactylus)<tei:lb/>guineensis, adult: (b) Eremias nitida, adult: (c) 'Eremias'' ( = Acanthodactylus) guineensis,<tei:lb/>juvenile: (d) Acanthodactylus erythmrus lineomaculatus, juvenile: (e) A. e. lineomaculatus,<tei:lb/>atypical juvenile, BM 1966.430. Arrows indicate first upper labial scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Of these features, the following are probably derived, rather than primitive, within the<tei:lb/>Lacertidae as a whole: 1,2,3,5,7,8, 10, 12, 15, 16, 21, 22,24, 25,27, 28, 31, 32, 33 and 34.<tei:lb/>Only number 27 appears to be unique to Acanthodactylus but 22, hemipenial asymmetry<tei:lb/>with the medial side of the organ reduced, is found elsewhere only in Philochortus Matschie,<tei:lb/>1893 which, on other grounds, does not seem to be closely related. Monophyly is also<tei:lb/>suggested by (i) the great similarity between the species of Acanthodactylus so that they are<tei:lb/>interconnected by high levels of resemblance; (ii) possession of a unique combination of<tei:lb/>derived features even if they themselves are not unique to the genus; (iii) a coherent geo-<tei:lb/>graphical range.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The relationship of Acanthodactylus to other lacertids is difficult to judge outside the</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>296 E. N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>context of a comprehensive revision of the Lacertidae. Boulenger (1921) suggests that the<tei:lb/>genus is related to Latastia Bedriaga, 1884 but the two share only a couple of the derived<tei:lb/>features listed above, namely 1 and 33. Most derived features are shared with Eremias<tei:lb/>Wiegmann, 1834 (used in the restricted sense of Shcherbak, 1974, for the Palaearctic species)<tei:lb/>and Mesalina Gray, 1838 (used for the north African and southwest Asian species originally<tei:lb/>referred to Eremias see Arnold, 19806). Eremias shares 1, 2, 3, 7, 8, 14, 15, 16, 28, 32, 33<tei:lb/>and often 25 and 34 while Mesalina shares 1, 2?, 3, 5, 7, 8, 10, 15, 16, 21, 33 and sometimes<tei:lb/>28. More characters need to be examined before it can be decided which of these is likely to<tei:lb/>be the sister taxon of Acanthodactylus or whether this is Eremias plus Mesalina.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Systematic position of Eremias guineensis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Eremias guineensis Boulenger, 1887a was described on the basis of a single hatchling,<tei:lb/>supposedly from 'Brass, Mouths of Niger' (Nigeria). In fact, as Schmidt (1919) has already<tei:lb/>suggested, this locality is almost certainly erroneous, or the result of accidental trans-<tei:lb/>portation, for all the specimens collected since have come from places far to the north of<tei:lb/>Brass in the Doka and Sudan woodland areas (vegetation classification of Rosevear, 1965) of<tei:lb/>Nigeria and Ghana (BM(NH) specimens), Niger (Pappenfuss, 1969) and Cameroon. Material<tei:lb/>from the latter country was described as a separate species, Eremias benuensis Monard, 1949<tei:lb/>but Pappenfuss correctly synonymized this form with E. guineensis. The differences noted<tei:lb/>by Monard (p. 740) result largely from comparing adult Cameroon animals with Boulenger's<tei:lb/>description of a single juvenile, as is clearly apparent now that a number of adults are<tei:lb/>available from Nigeria and other more western localities. The ostensible differences in head<tei:lb/>and frontal scale proportions and relative head and leg lengths all result from allometric<tei:lb/>changes during growth, and the supposedly distinctive conditions of the supraocular scales,<tei:lb/>supraciliary granules and subnasal scale in E. benuensis can all be matched in E. guineensis<tei:lb/>from Nigeria. Finally the supposed difference in pattern (six white stripes on dorsum in E.<tei:lb/>benuensis and allegedly five in E. guineensis results from an error in Boulenger's description.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Boulenger placed E. guineensis in Eremias sens. lat. because it has the combination of fine<tei:lb/>dorsal scaling, keeled subdigital lamellae and nostril separated from the first upper labial<tei:lb/>scale that this author regarded as diagnostic of the genus. But he felt that this species<tei:lb/>occupied an isolated position within the assemblage and assigned it to a new subgenus,<tei:lb/>Taenieremias Boulenger, 19186. This separation of E. guineensis from the rest of Eremias<tei:lb/>sens. lat. appears valid, for it differs in a number of features from the three main groups<tei:lb/>included in Boulenger's concept of the genus. It can be distinguished from Eremias proper (a<tei:lb/>Palaearctic assemblage) by having its postorbital and postfrontal bones unfused, by absence<tei:lb/>of a radially directed peripheral section on scleral ossicle number 14, possession of C-type<tei:lb/>caudal vertebrae, a reflectable collar and an asymmetrical hemipenis; from members of<tei:lb/>Mesalina by absence of a radially directed peripheral section of scleral ossicle number 14,<tei:lb/>absence of an occipital scale, possession of three rows of scales around the digits, which are<tei:lb/>pectinate, and an asymmetrical hemipenis; and from the Ethiopian species usually referred<tei:lb/>to Eremias and the probably related Meroles by C-type caudal vertebrae and an asym-<tei:lb/>metrical hemipenis which differs in detail from any found in that group. It also differs from<tei:lb/>the great majority of them in its heart-shaped sternal fontanelle and in absence of a<tei:lb/>peripheral section of scleral ossicle 14 and of an occipital scale.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>On the other hand, E. guineensis agrees with all the features of Acanthodactylus listed on<tei:lb/>pp. 293-294 with the exception of the arrangement of scales around the nostril (features 26 &amp;<tei:lb/>27). Yet even here the situation in E. guineensis is not very like that found in species<tei:lb/>assigned to Eremias sens. lat. and in size, shape and pattern of contact with each other, the<tei:lb/>scales bear a close overall resemblance to those in Acanthodactylus, the only obvious<tei:lb/>difference being the presence of an extra suture in E. guineensis running across the area<tei:lb/>occupied by the first upper labial scale in Acanthodactylus to produce a smaller, nominal<tei:lb/>first upper labial and a subnasal scale that is radically different in form from that found in</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>297</tei:p>
         <tei:p>members of Eremias sens. lat. (Fig. 2). Evidence that the E. guineensis condition is easily<tei:lb/>derived from that found in Acanthodactylus is provided by a juvenile A. erythrurus<tei:lb/>lineomaculatus (BM(NH) 1966.430) which, although normal in other respects, possesses an<tei:lb/>extra suture that virtually duplicates the condition found in E. guineensis (Fig. 2e).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In addition to possessing the overwhelming majority of the features found in all or most<tei:lb/>Acanthodactylus, E. guineensis bears a more detailed resemblance to A. boueti of north<tei:lb/>Dahomy and Ghana and to A. erythrurus and A. savignyi of northwestern Africa (p. 318).<tei:lb/>Given this degree of similarity, it seems best to transfer E. guineensis to Acanthodactylus, as<tei:lb/>suggested elsewhere (Arnold, 1980/?).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Characters varying within Acanthodactylus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The following features may vary between species of Acanthodactylus and are therefore<tei:lb/>potentially useful in defining species and in attempting to work out their inter-relationships.<tei:lb/>Species names mentioned in the course of description are used in the senses employed<tei:lb/>subsequently in this paper. A summary of many of the features is given in Tables 8 and 9.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Osteological characters</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shape ofthepremaxillary region (Fig. 3).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In many Acanthodactylus the outer edge of the premaxilla (viewed from above or below) is<tei:lb/>smoothly continuous with those of the maxillae, but in some species this bone is abruptly<tei:lb/>narrowed and there may be a slight constriction where its lateral surfaces meet the maxillae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 3 Premaxillary regions of Acanthodactylus skulls from beneath: (left) A. erythrurus outer<tei:lb/>edge of maxilla and premaxilla smoothly continuous; (centre) A. schmidti premaxilla abruptly<tei:lb/>narrowed; (right) A. scutellatus premaxilla abruptly narrowed and premaxillary teeth reduced<tei:lb/>to five, pm-premaxilla m-maxilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This condition is strongly developed in A. blanfordii, A. schmidti, A. arabicus, A.<tei:lb/>gongrorhynchatus, A. haasi, A. masirae and especially the members of the A. scutellatus<tei:lb/>group: it is at least indicated in many A. boskianus, A. cantoris and A. opheodurus. A smooth<tei:lb/>lateral border to the anterior part of the skull is the usual condition in lacertids and the<tei:lb/>narrowing found in some Acanthodactylus is almost certainly apomorphic. It is best<tei:lb/>developed in species habitually living on soft sand. These forms usually have very acutely<tei:lb/>pointed snouts and at least some probe the sand when hunting, perhaps in response to tactile<tei:lb/>or aural cues originating from hidden invertebrates. Narrowing of the premaxilla may be<tei:lb/>related to producing the acuminate snout-tip necessary for this activity.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Number of premaxillary teeth.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The usual number of premaxillary teeth in most Acanthodactylus species is seven with<tei:lb/>occasional individual variation to six or eight. The three specimens of A. boueti checked are<tei:lb/>singular in apparently having nine premaxillary teeth while many members of the A.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>298 E. N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>scutellatus group have only five; this is true of the great majority of A. scutellatus and A.<tei:lb/>longipes examined but of only a few A. aureus (see p. 328). As the number<tei:lb/>of premaxillary teeth in most lacertids is about seven to nine, five seems likely to be an<tei:lb/>apomorphic condition within Acanthodactylus. Its development may well be connected with<tei:lb/>the narrowness of the premaxilla in these forms.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Postorbital and postfrontal bones.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>These elements are separate in nearly all Acanthodactylus but they are fused in A. cantoris,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>even juvenile ones.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Number ofpresacral vertebrae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There are from 23 to 27 vertebrae between the skull and the sacrum in Acanthodactylus. The<tei:lb/>interspecific variation encountered during this study is set out in Table 1. From this it will<tei:lb/>be seen that, in most species, males usually have more presacral vertebrae than females, the<tei:lb/>average difference in vertebral number between them varying from about 0-66 to 1-23. The<tei:lb/>main exceptions to this are A. pardalis bedriagai and members of the A. scutellatus group<tei:lb/>where average sexual differences are very small, ranging from 0-07 to 0-28. Although most<tei:lb/>populations of A. schmidti show sexual differentiation in count, this is very reduced in<tei:lb/>populations from the United Arab Emirates, eastern Arabia. In the majority of species, there<tei:lb/>are 24 presacral vertebrae in most males and 25 in most females, but counts are higher in A.<tei:lb/>(t.) tristrami, A. (t.) orientalis, A. robustus, A. erythrurus, A. savignyi, A. boueti and A.<tei:lb/>guineensis: here males typically have about 25 presacral vertebrae against usually 26 or even<tei:lb/>27 in females. A. pardalis bedriagai generally has 26 vertebrae in both sexes while in male<tei:lb/>and female A. schmidti from the United Arab Emirates, A. scutellatus and A. aureus average<tei:lb/>counts approach 24; the average is even lower in A. longipes and in some parts of its range<tei:lb/>may be around 23.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Nearly all other lacertids show marked sexual variation in vertebral number, so its<tei:lb/>reduction is likely to be apomorphic. Counts of 23 in males and 23 or 24 in females are at the<tei:lb/>lower limit for lacertids as a whole and may well be derived conditions. Other counts are well<tei:lb/>within the normal range for the family but the higher ones, 25 in males, 26 or 27 in females,<tei:lb/>may possibly also be apomorphic as they commonly occur only in a minority of forms which<tei:lb/>on other grounds may be closely related.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In general, species with high presacral vertebral counts are found in relatively mesic<tei:lb/>habitats while those with low vertebral numbers occur in drier, more open places with<tei:lb/>sparser vegetation. It may be that the former habitat type requires more body flexion in<tei:lb/>passing through plant cover and more vertebrae facilitate this. Certainly in lacertids as a<tei:lb/>whole there is a distinct if imprecise correlation between vertebral number and habitat<tei:lb/>structure.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The selective pressures that cause loss of sexual differentiation in vertebral number are<tei:lb/>obscure. Possibly the difference in average count found in most lacertids reflect sex-<tei:lb/>correlated difference in microhabitat, males might, for instance, spend more time in open<tei:lb/>situations than females. If this were so, uniformity of vertebral number might result from<tei:lb/>lack of such microhabitat differences, something that seems quite probable in the relatively<tei:lb/>uniform, open environments occupied by A. schmidti and the A. scutellatus group. Another<tei:lb/>possibility is that the extra vertebrae of most female lacertids increase the length of the body<tei:lb/>cavity for carriage of eggs and that loss of sexual differentiation is a correlate of small clutch<tei:lb/>mass. However, although egg number in the A. scutellatus group at least is quite small (n = 2<tei:lb/>or 3) there is no clear evidence that a clutch occupies a smaller proportion of the body cavity<tei:lb/>than in most other species of Acanthodactylus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sternal ribs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the great majority of Acanthodactylus species the usual sternal rib formula is 3 : 2, that is<tei:lb/>three pairs of ribs attach directly to the sternum and two to the xiphisternum. However, in<tei:lb/>nearly all of these, the fifth sternal rib is interrupted on at least one side in a minority of<tei:lb/>individuals, albeit often only briefly. In contrast, interruption is the commoner condition in</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Hemipenial characters</tei:p>

Its basic anatomy and that of the lacertid</tei:p>

         <tei:p>In most species, the medial side of the armature is reduced in size, simpler in form than the<tei:lb/>lateral side and indeed may be entirely absent. The extent to which this reduction occurs<tei:lb/>varies and shows some correlation with reduction of the medial lobe of the hemipenis,<tei:lb/>although how precise this is varies.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sides subequal: A. micropholis, some A. cantoris.</tei:p>
