         <tei:p>In these forms the medial branch of the sulcus is short or absent.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shape of the medial clavula.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In species where a medial clavula is present there are two main patterns.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Clavula fairly broad, flat and blunt-tipped with at least the inner edge turned upwards: A.<tei:lb/>micropholis, A. cantoris, A. blanfordii and some A. schmidti.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Clavula narrow and pointed with a ^ -shaped cross section: A. boskianus, A. schreiberi, A.<tei:lb/>grandis complex, A. (t.) tristrami, A. robustus, A. erythrurus, A. savignyi, A. boueti, A.<tei:lb/>pardalis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In A. (tristrami) orientalis the median clavula is like this or very small and flattened.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shape of lateral clavula.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Substantial differences are found in the form of this structure.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 . Simple, flattened, often with one or both edges turned dorsally: A. micropholis, A.<tei:lb/>cantoris, some A schmidti, A. robustus, A. opheodurus, A.felicis, A. masirae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2. Not flattened, blunt, edges folded dorsally, a central lobe on the upper surface with a<tei:lb/>proximally directed pocket: A. blanfordii, many A. schmidti.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3. Very narrow, hollowed above: A. arabicus, A. gongrorhynchatus (A. haasi has a similar<tei:lb/>but shorter lateral clavula).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>4. Complexly structured with multiple lobes below: A. boskianus, A. schreiberi (most<tei:lb/>medial lobe often single), A. grandis complex (most medial lobe often divided).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5. Complexly structured although not conspicuously lobed below, sometimes very<tei:lb/>slightly bifurcate: A. (t.) tristrami.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>6. Complexly structured and not conspicuously lobed below but clearly bifurcate at tip: A.<tei:lb/>(t.) orientalis, A. erythrurus, A. savignyi, A. boueti, A. pardalis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1. Lateral clavula simple but often bifurcate and folded to give a D-shaped cross section:<tei:lb/>A. maculatus, A. spinicauda.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>8. Lateral clavula flattened and not bifurcate but folded to give a ID-shaped cross section:<tei:lb/>A. aureus, A. scutellatus, A. longipes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Connectors.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In some species the connectors are all relatively thin but in others the most medial one on the<tei:lb/>lateral side is usually thickened. This is found in A. boskianus, A. schreiberi, the A. grandis<tei:lb/>complex, A. (t.) tristrami, A. (t.) orientalis. A. robustus, A. erythrurus, A. savignyi, A. boueti,<tei:lb/>A. guineensis, A. pardalis, A. maculatus and A. spinicauda.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Polarity of hemipenial features</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The only available criterion for the polarity of hemipenial features found in Acanthodactylus<tei:lb/>is outgroup comparison using other lacertids as the outgroup. In these, the hemipenis is</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>304 E. N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>usually fairly large and symmetrical with well developed sulcal lips on both sides. Where<tei:lb/>present the armature is also typically symmetrical with simple, often flattened and rather<tei:lb/>blunt clavulae. On this basis, small size, asymmetry of the lobes, sulcal lips and armature and<tei:lb/>the development of peculiar and often complex clavulae and thickened connectors are all<tei:lb/>likely to be derived features.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Origin of hemipenial differences</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is common in many animal groups for genital structure to vary between taxa and for at<tei:lb/>least some of its features to be considered good indicators of relationship, especially as they<tei:lb/>often correlate with characters from other organ systems. The origin of such differences and<tei:lb/>possible reasons why they should often reflect relationship are discussed at length elsewhere<tei:lb/>(Arnold, 1973; in press). It is suggested that genitals differ from other organ systems in their<tei:lb/>potential to retain changes that develop in them as a result of pleiotropic effects or transient<tei:lb/>selective forces. With most organ systems, any pleiotropically induced change is likely to<tei:lb/>result in a loss of functional efficiency. Provided this lowering or efficiency does not<tei:lb/>outweigh the original selective advantage of the genetic change concerned, the pleiotropic<tei:lb/>alteration will initially become fixed. But there will then be normalizing selection modifying<tei:lb/>the genotype so that the pleiotropic change will tend to be suppressed and the organ will<tei:lb/>return to its original state and level of efficiency. Similarly, if the organ changes in response<tei:lb/>to direct but transient selective pressure, it will tend to revert to its original condition once<tei:lb/>this pressure is relaxed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The situation with genitalia is different since their efficiency cannot be considered in<tei:lb/>isolation but only in relation to their co-ordinated functioning with the genitals of the<tei:lb/>opposite sex. Efficiency does not depend on a particular conformation or size but on good<tei:lb/>match between the sexes. Consequently, if there were, say, a pleiotropic change affecting the<tei:lb/>male organs which reduces copulatory efficiency, there would not only be normalizing<tei:lb/>selection acting on the male genotype to bring the male organ back towards its original<tei:lb/>condition but also, simultaneously, selection acting on the female genotype to produce<tei:lb/>genital changes adapting to the pleiotropic alterations that have already taken place in the<tei:lb/>male system. This means that the two sets of genitals might return to their previous mutual<tei:lb/>efficiency without reverting to their original morphological state. The same sort of effect<tei:lb/>would be expected if transient selective forces acted directly on the genitalia themselves.<tei:lb/>Genitals are thus likely to 'store' changes, which in other organ systems would probably be<tei:lb/>eradicated. So they may be on the whole more likely to incorporate evidence of shared<tei:lb/>evolutionary experience than many other organs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>An important factor in the production of hemipenial differences in lizards, including<tei:lb/>Acanthodactylus, may be the development of physical isolating mechanisms when pre-<tei:lb/>viously allopatric species come into contact. Such isolating mechanisms could reduce the<tei:lb/>loss of reproductive effort which is likely if two species interbreed. That such loss can occur<tei:lb/>is evidenced by the occasional discovery of hybrids between lacertid species (cases in Lacerta<tei:lb/>and its relatives are summarized by Arnold, 1973, and in Acanthodactylus by Mertens,<tei:lb/>1968). Physical isolating mechanisms would be at a premium in situations where similar<tei:lb/>species with no experience of each other come into contact since, in this situation,<tei:lb/>premating isolating mechanisms may not be well developed. Interspecific mating would be<tei:lb/>reduced if considerable mismatch evolved in the shape and dimensions of the genitalia of the<tei:lb/>forms concerned. The often radically different hemipenes of otherwise similar species of<tei:lb/>Acanthodactylus may be examples of this, especially as they correspond in shape and size to<tei:lb/>the cloacal bursa of their own females into which the male organ is inserted. Such differences<tei:lb/>occur between forms which, on other grounds, are likely to be closely related, for instance<tei:lb/>Acanthodactylus pardalis and A. maculatus and, because they reduce the possibility of<tei:lb/>successful copulation between the forms, are prima facie evidence of separate species status.<tei:lb/>Some of the derived features found in the hemipenes of Acanthodactylus can be interpreted<tei:lb/>as contributing to such isolating mechanisms; they include not only small size but also</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS 305</tei:p>
         <tei:p>asymmetry of the hemipenis and armature since loss of its medial side reduces the diameter<tei:lb/>of the organ.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although they are likely to be 'stored' beyond their period of function (presumably<tei:lb/>premating isolating mechanisms are likely to develop in the long run), changes that are<tei:lb/>probably associated with physical isolating mechanisms are not likely to be very reliable<tei:lb/>indicators of relationship. This is partly because, by their nature, they tend to produce<tei:lb/>marked differences between very similar and often closely related species. Also, means of<tei:lb/>producing incompatible genitalia are rather restricted in lizards and it is very likely that<tei:lb/>small size and asymmetry may have been produced several times. This is supported by their<tei:lb/>patchy occurrence in Acanthodactylus, turning up in a number of groups which on other<tei:lb/>grounds do not seem to be closely related. Furthermore these features have developed in<tei:lb/>other lacertid genera. Other hemipenial features may not be subject to such strong<tei:lb/>convergent selective forces and, as such are more likely to be good indicators of relationship<tei:lb/>for the reasons given earlier in this discussion.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Description of the hemipenes of different species</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Figures in parentheses refer to the number of individual organs examined.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. micropholis (2). Lobes more or less symmetrical or medial rather reduced, sulcus<tei:lb/>bifurcate, the proximal borders of its branches formed by backwardly directed flaps;<tei:lb/>armature fairly symmetrical although one or other clavula larger than the other, clavulae<tei:lb/>rather expanded and obtusely pointed, the medial one with its inner edge turned upwards,<tei:lb/>lateral one with both edges turned upwards; often three lateral connectors and two medial<tei:lb/>ones, all narrow.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. cantoris (7). Size reduced; medial lobe usually rather smaller than lateral, lobes not<tei:lb/>complexly folded, the walls often corrugated and lack regular plicae, stem of hemipenis long,<tei:lb/>sulcus bifurcate, the proximal borders of its branches formed by a soft flap in at least some<tei:lb/>cases although this is not always clear; armature symmetrical or medial side somewhat<tei:lb/>reduced, slender, the clavulae expanded and laminar, medial with inner edge turned<tei:lb/>upwards, lateral with both edges turned upwards, at least one connector on each side.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. blanfordii (10). Lateral lobe large and medial one reduced, sulcus bifurcate, proximal<tei:lb/>border of lateral but not medial side formed by a backwardly directed flap; armature clearly<tei:lb/>asymmetrical, the median side reduced with a flattened clavula of which the inner edge is<tei:lb/>turned upwards, lateral clavula large and obtusely pointed, not flattened, edges turned<tei:lb/>upwards and a central lobe present on the upper side below which there is a proximally<tei:lb/>directed pocket (the lobe is least developed in southern populations), connectors as in A.<tei:lb/>micropholis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. schmidti (10). Lateral lobe large and fan-shaped, medial lobe absent or minute, sulcus<tei:lb/>bifurcate but medial branch very short; medial side of armature absent or reduced to a thread<tei:lb/>in most cases; lateral clavula large, either rather pointed, slightly bifurcate or rather blunt,<tei:lb/>edges turned upwards and clavula may be rolled to some extent, a central lobe present in<tei:lb/>many cases on the upper side, below which is often a proximally directed pocket as in A.<tei:lb/>blanfordii but is less well developed. In one case (BM(NH) 1931.7.16.48) there is a flattened<tei:lb/>medial clavula.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. arabicus (6). Essentially like A. schmidti but intramuscular part of armature much<tei:lb/>narrower and lateral clavula very narrow, simple and pointed with a concavity above or the<tei:lb/>medial edge rolled upwards.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. gongrorhynchatus ( 1 ). Similar to A. arabicus but medial edge of clavula rolled onto dorsal<tei:lb/>surface.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>306 E- N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. haasi (1). Like A. gongrorhynchatus but clavula shorter, broader and more dorso-<tei:lb/>ventrally flattened.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. (tristrami) tristrami (3). Lobes more or less symmetrical, sulcus bifurcate, the proximal<tei:lb/>borders of its branches formed by distinct flaps; armature asymmetrical, the medial side<tei:lb/>reduced with a pointed clavula that has a ^ -shaped cross section; lateral clavula expanded<tei:lb/>and, when spread out, essentially flat with a very weakly bifurcate tip formed of overlapping<tei:lb/>laminae, lower surface fairly smooth but with a strap-shaped area with free edges towards<tei:lb/>outer side, upper surface with a medially directed pocket and edges rolled upwards; three<tei:lb/>lateral and two medial connectors, the more medial of the lateral ones thickened.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. (tristrami) orientalis (4). Different from A. (t.) tristrami: lateral lobe very large and medial<tei:lb/>strongly reduced (as in Fig. 5c or d), sulcus bifurcate, proximal borders of branches formed<tei:lb/>by distinct flaps, the median one rather small; armature very asymmetrical, medial clavula<tei:lb/>quite small, either pointed with a &lt;= -shaped cross-section or blunt and flattened; lateral<tei:lb/>clavula rather similar to that of A. (t.) tristrami and has free strap-shaped area on lower<tei:lb/>surface but is smaller with a more bifurcate tip and no clear pockets.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. robustus (2). Generally like A. (t.) tristrami but lateral clavula much simpler with edges<tei:lb/>turned on to dorsal surface.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. boskianus (10), A. schreiberi (5). Generally like A. (t.) tristrami but proximal border of<tei:lb/>medial branch of sulcus is a relatively weak, soft fold rather than a flap; lateral clavula has<tei:lb/>similar general form but is more swollen and lobed and there is an area with free edges<tei:lb/>towards the medial side of the ventral surface; upper surface has medial edge rolled upwards<tei:lb/>and greatly swollen. In A. schreiberi and A. boskianus the median area of the ventral surface<tei:lb/>may be divided by a longitudinal cleft.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. erythrurus erythrurus (3), A. e. lineomaculatus (3), A. savignyi savignyi (2), A. s. bland<tei:lb/>(2). Similar to A. (t.) tristrami but lateral clavula usually clearly bifurcate at tip and the more<tei:lb/>median cleft in A. (t.) tristrami, that separates a strap shaped area with free edges in this form,<tei:lb/>is usually not clearly marked.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. e. belli (6). As A. e. erythrurus but lateral clavula is smaller, flatter and usually simpler.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. boueti (1). Size reduced; lobes subequal; armature asymmetrical, the medial side reduced<tei:lb/>with a pointed clavula that has a &lt;c -shaped cross section; lateral clavula expanded and<tei:lb/>flattened, bifurcate at tip, with lateral edge turned upwards.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. guineensis (1). Lateral lobe very large, medial lobe absent, sulcus lacks medial branch,<tei:lb/>proximal border of its lateral branch formed by a backwardly directed flap; medial side of<tei:lb/>armature absent, lateral clavula superficially narrow but in fact laminar and tightly rolled,<tei:lb/>lower surface smooth but with a cleft near each margin, dorsum with a medially directed<tei:lb/>pocket; two connectors, the more medial one broad.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. pardalis (20). Lateral lobe large and medial small but plicate, sulcus bifurcate, the<tei:lb/>proximal border of its branches formed by backwardly directed flaps; armature very like that<tei:lb/>of A. erythrurus and A. savignyi.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. maculatus (20), A. spinicauda (2). Small; lateral lobe large, medial lobe absent; medial<tei:lb/>branch of sulcus very short, proximal border of lateral branch only of sulcus formed by back-<tei:lb/>wardly directed flap; medial side of armature absent or reduced to a thread, lateral side<tei:lb/>narrow and clavula small but sometimes bifurcate; compared to condition in A. pardalis,<tei:lb/>lateral clavula has been rotated and folded to give a ^-shaped cross section so that the<tei:lb/>bifurcation, if present, is in the vertical plane; two or three connectors, the most medial one<tei:lb/>usually thickened.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. aureus (3). Lateral lobe large and fan-shaped, medial lobe absent or very small and<tei:lb/>confluent with lateral one, medial branch of sulcus absent or very short; medial side of</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS 307</tei:p>
         <tei:p>armature absent or reduced to a thread, lateral side broad; lateral clavula flattened but<tei:lb/>rotated sideways, as in A. maculatus, and folded to give a D -shaped cross section, with two<tei:lb/>connectors.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. scutellatus (5), A. longipes (3). Generally similar to A. aureus but folded clavula usually<tei:lb/>broader and sometimes apparently only one connector present.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. opheodurus (10), A. felicis (4), A. masirae (2). Lateral lobe extremely large, medial one<tei:lb/>very reduced, sulcus with relatively short medial branch, proximal border of lateral branch<tei:lb/>usually visible as a rather weak flap or fold; medial side of armature reduced to a thread or<tei:lb/>absent, lateral clavula usually flattened with the margins rolled over onto dorsal surface;<tei:lb/>lateral connectors usually two or sometimes three, slender.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>External features</tei:p>
         <tei:p>These have nearly all been used by Boulenger (1918a, 1921) and subsequent authors and,<tei:lb/>until now have formed the basis of Acanthodactylus classification.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Scales around nostrils.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. guineensis has a peculiar perinasal scale arrangement that seems to be derived from the<tei:lb/>typical Acanthodactylus condition, it also occurs as a rare variant in A. erythrurus (see<tei:lb/>p. 296).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Frontonasal scale.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This is usually intact but is frequently divided in two by a longitudinal suture in some</tei:p>
         <tei:p>populations of A. erythrurus and A. savignyi. The condition occurs as a rarity in a few other</tei:p>
         <tei:p>species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Azygos scales between prefrontals.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>One or more azygos scales between the prefrontals occurs commonly in A. boueti, A.<tei:lb/>savignyi and in some populations of A. erythrurus. This arrangement is also found as an<tei:lb/>uncommon variant in A. guineensis, A. schreiberi and A. boskianus and even more rarely in<tei:lb/>some other forms.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Supraocular scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is considerable variation in the extent to which the supraocular scales roofing the orbit<tei:lb/>are fragmented in Acanthodactylus. 1. A. schreiberi has four large, more or less intact scales;<tei:lb/>this condition is approached by members of the A. grandis complex. 2. In the A. cantoris, A.<tei:lb/>scutellatus and A. opheodurus groups and in A. gongrorhynchatus, A. haasi and many A.<tei:lb/>boskianus there is usually an area of granules wedged between the third and fourth supra-<tei:lb/>oculars and, in some cases, between the first and second too; the fourth supraocular may also<tei:lb/>be broken up, at least to some extent. A. boskianus from north Egypt and A. haasi may<tei:lb/>have the first supraocular divided into two or three sections. 3. Members of the A.<tei:lb/>pardalis group have the first supraocular sometimes divided into two or even three and the<tei:lb/>fourth is very fragmented. 4. In the A. tristrami and A. erythrurus groups the first supraocular<tei:lb/>is divided into at least three sections and often into many granules and this usually applies to<tei:lb/>the fourth too. 5. A. boueti has the first, second and third supraoculars all fragmented. There<tei:lb/>is however a significant amount of variation within taxa.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is probable that the plesiomorphic condition is four more or less undivided supraocular<tei:lb/>scales, as this is by far the commonest state in the Lacertidae as a whole, but fragmentation<tei:lb/>seems to have occurred independently in some forms assigned to Eremias and in Ichnotropis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Subocular scale.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A number of species have a subocular scale that extends ventrally to the edge of the mouth,<tei:lb/>separating the, in these cases, usually four anterior upper labial scales from the remainder.<tei:lb/>This condition is found in A. (t.) tristrami, A. (/.) orientalis, A. masirae, A. boueti and A.<tei:lb/>guineensis, in many A. micropholis, A. erythrurus and A. savignyi, in a few A. boskianus<tei:lb/>from the Iraq region and in occasional examples of some other species such as members of</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>308 E. N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>the A. pardalis group and A. opheodurus. Elsewhere the subocular is shallower and the upper<tei:lb/>labials form a continuous series beneath it. The former state is likely to be plesiomorphic as<tei:lb/>it is very widespread in the Lacertidae as a whole, but the latter occurs in Eremias proper, in<tei:lb/>some species usually assigned to Eremias from the Ethiopian region and in Meroles Gray,<tei:lb/>1838, Aporosaura Boulenger, 18876 and Ichnotropis Peters, 1854. The supposed apomor-<tei:lb/>phic condition may apparently arise by the subocular becoming increasingly narrowed<tei:lb/>ventrally or by splitting to produce a small ventral scale that forms an extra upper labial.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In general, the subocular is separated from the lip in forms from drier, more open habitats<tei:lb/>which tend to have particularly large eyes and the character may be at least partly a<tei:lb/>functional correlate of this size increase. In species where the subocular scale usually borders<tei:lb/>the mouth, the lateral surface below the anterior eye, formed from the jugal and maxillary<tei:lb/>bones, tends to be fairly vertical, so that the scale can cover it but still remain roughly in the<tei:lb/>same plane as the upper lip of which its lower section forms part. But, with increase in eye<tei:lb/>size, the jugal arch bulges outwards and its outer face and that of the section of the maxilla<tei:lb/>below it is directed more obliquely downwards. In this situation, a single scale covering this<tei:lb/>surface and forming part of the lip would be strongly bent and possibly not able to provide<tei:lb/>the necessary flexibility for labial movement. In forms living in dry areas, the need for such<tei:lb/>movement is increased, for the upper lip projects further ventrally and is more bulky, so it<tei:lb/>and the scales covering its outer surface are displaced more during closure of the mouth,<tei:lb/>presumably producing a more efficient seal against water loss and incursion of sand particles<tei:lb/>than is present in more mesic species. Because of these changes, the replacement of the<tei:lb/>original subocular by two functional parts would probably be an advantage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Number of upper labial scales anterior to the centre of the eye.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the majority of forms there are typically four upper labials anterior to the centre of the eye<tei:lb/>but in some, such as the members of the A. cantoris group, A. scutellatus and A. longipes, five<tei:lb/>is the usual number. As four is found in forms which on other grounds seem primitive, it may<tei:lb/>be the plesiomorphic condition for Acanthodactylus. When present, the extra upper labial<tei:lb/>sometimes seems to develop by being split off the subocular but this may not always be its<tei:lb/>origin. In many species exceptions to the usual number occur.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parietal scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. boueti is singular in having the parietal scales reduced and the areas normally occupied</tei:p>
         <tei:p>by their lateral and posterior parts replaced by small scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ear opening.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This is usually quite large but in A. gongrorhynchatus and A. haasi it is reduced in size, being<tei:lb/>occluded by skin from above and from the front. Similar reduction is found in some other<tei:lb/>lacertids that live on loose sand such as Aporosaura and some species of Meroles.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dorsal body scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is great variation in size and shape of the dorsal scales. In many species they are fine<tei:lb/>and more or less granular and either smooth or carinate, but in others size increases<tei:lb/>posteriorly and the scales become flat, more keeled and strongly imbricate. In such cases, the<tei:lb/>lateral scales tend to be smaller than the more medial ones. Transverse counts at mid-body<tei:lb/>may be as high as 100 in the most fine-scaled species, A. longipes, or as low as 18 in some A.<tei:lb/>boskianus asper. There is often substantial variation within and, more obviously, between<tei:lb/>populations of a species; thus counts vary from about 35 to 80 in A. scutellatus and from 1 8<tei:lb/>to 52 in A. boskianus. This suggests that dorsal scalation is very labile so that the assessment<tei:lb/>of a primitive condition for the genus is impossible. All that can be said is that the extremes<tei:lb/>encountered in Acanthodactylus are near the extremes for the Lacertidae as a whole and that<tei:lb/>the primitive condition is perhaps more likely to be somewhere in between.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In some other sections of the Lacertidae, relatively fine granular scaling is usually<tei:lb/>associated with open environments while, large, keeled, strongly imbricate scales are<tei:lb/>typically found in species that live, or at least take refuge in, dense often spiny vegetation.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS 309</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The latter scale arrangement provides much better mechanical protection against damage to<tei:lb/>the flexible skin between the scales, something that is potentially likely to occur when<tei:lb/>running in densely vegetated habitats. The same selective pressure may well act on<tei:lb/>Acanthodactylus; the most fine-scaled forms, like many populations of the A. scutellatus<tei:lb/>group, tend to live in the most open habitats, whereas forms like A. boskianus do frequently<tei:lb/>seem to take refuge in spiny vegetation (see p. 3 1 5).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dorslateral tracts of enlarged scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>These are found on the posterior body of A. schmidti and are at least indicated in some A.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>blanfordii.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ventral scale number.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The number of ventral scales in the longest transverse row across the belly varies from about<tei:lb/>8 to 18, 10 being the commonest figure. As the majority of lacertids have low numbers, these<tei:lb/>are likely to be primitive in Acanthodactylus. In general low numbers are most frequent in<tei:lb/>forms occupying more mesic habitats and higher ones in species living in relatively arid<tei:lb/>areas, but this correlation is imprecise. Intraspecific variation occurs, especially in forms<tei:lb/>with higher counts.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Tessellation of ventral scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Forms with high transverse counts of ventrals often have the scales tessellated, that is, they<tei:lb/>are staggered so that they do not form regular longitudinal rows. In many cases, this<tei:lb/>staggering is confined to the sides of the belly, but it may affect the median areas too. It is best<tei:lb/>and most extensively developed in most populations of the A. scutellatus group and in the A.<tei:lb/>grandis complex; it is found to a lesser extent in other members of these assemblages and is<tei:lb/>at least indicated in the A. cantoris group and A. gongrorhynchatus. There is significant<tei:lb/>variation in the extent of staggering within species where it is present. Tessellation is found<tei:lb/>in some other lacertid groups such as Eremias proper, Meroles and Aporosaura.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Inter gradation of dorsal and ventral scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In most Acanthodactylus, dorsal and ventral scales are easily distinguished from each other,<tei:lb/>but in A. gongrorhynchatus they intergrade. This trend is also apparent in A. haasi and to a<tei:lb/>lesser extent in some A. arabicus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Number of rows of scales running along fingers.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In many species there are three rows of scales running along fingers one to four, the same<tei:lb/>number that runs along each toe. One row is dorsal, one ventral and the third posterior. On<tei:lb/>finger five, and sometimes at the base of the others, there may be four rows, the additional<tei:lb/>one being anterior. In contrast, a number of forms have four regular and continuous rows of<tei:lb/>scales on all fingers; these include the A. cantoris and A. scutellatus groups, the A. grandis<tei:lb/>complex, A. gongrorhynchatus and A. haasi. In fact, these two conditions are not as clear cut<tei:lb/>as they appear, for a number of individuals of species that usually have three scale rows<tei:lb/>possess additional scales forming an irregular fourth row on at least some fingers; this occurs<tei:lb/>in A. micropholis, A. robustus and A. opheodurus. A. masirae habitually has four rows but<tei:lb/>these are often irregular, and A. cantoris in which four regular rows are usually present<tei:lb/>occasionally has the anterior one strongly reduced.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It seems likely that three scale rows running along the fingers is the primitive condition in<tei:lb/>Acanthodactylus for it comes closest to the two rows typical of the majority of Lacertidae and<tei:lb/>other scincomorph lizards. Three or four scale rows on the fingers also occur in Eremias<tei:lb/>proper, Meroles and Aporosaura. The presence of a regular fourth row is largely associated<tei:lb/>with sandy habitats. It may well allow better development of a pectination on the anterior<tei:lb/>face of the digit that matches the posterior one. This results in a wider digit that is more<tei:lb/>efficient for digging in light, unconsolidated substrates such as fine sand where<tei:lb/>Acanthodactylus often scrabbles for food as well as excavating burrows.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>310 E.N.ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pectination of the digits.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The scales along the posterior surfaces of the digits are pointed and project to form a comb-<tei:lb/>like fringe or pectination. In some instances, this is inconspicuous but in others the scales<tei:lb/>become very long and flattened in the horizontal plane and in extreme cases, such as A.<tei:lb/>longipes, the fringe on the toes may be wider than the digits themselves. The scales forming<tei:lb/>the row running along the upper surfaces of the digits may project slightly forwards to<tei:lb/>produce a weak anterior fringe as well, but in species where there is an anterior scale row on<tei:lb/>the fingers, this may form a fringe approaching the posterior one in development.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is a continuum between the weakest fringes and the most pronounced ones. Degree<tei:lb/>of development correlates very closely with what is known about the ecology of the species<tei:lb/>concerned, being least in forms living on relatively hard ground and greatest in forms from<tei:lb/>soft sand. The relationship is so strong in taxa for which some ecological information is<tei:lb/>available that it seems reasonable to use degree of pectination to predict substrate type in<tei:lb/>cases where this is unknown. The importance of fringes on the fingers for digging in loose<tei:lb/>sand has already been mentioned; fringes also enable lizards to travel across soft sand<tei:lb/>surfaces efficiently by increasing the area covered so that unit pressure is low and effort is not<tei:lb/>wasted pushing the sand backwards as the lizard moves forwards.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Keeling on upper caudal scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Most Acanthodactylus, like the majority of lacertids, have keels on the dorsal scales of the tail<tei:lb/>base but these are absent in A. (/.) tristrami, A. (t.) orientalis and A. robustus. The restricted<tei:lb/>distribution of this feature in the Lacertidae suggests it is an apomorphy, although it has<tei:lb/>developed independently elsewhere, for instance in Mesalina rubropunctata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lateral processes on tail base.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In A. spinicauda the lateral scales at the base of the tail are greatly enlarged, especially in</tei:p>
         <tei:p>males, with their keels produced to form tubercles. This feature is unique in the Lacertidae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Tail length.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In most Acanthodactylus the tail is more than 1-5 times the length of the head and body</tei:p>
         <tei:p>together but in A. (t.) tristrami, A. (t.) orientalis and A. robustus it is less.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pattern.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the great majority of Acanthodactylus species, juveniles have a striking pattern of narrow,<tei:lb/>dark and light longitudinal stripes. The only certain exceptions are A. schmidti, A. longipes<tei:lb/>and some A. scutellatus in which the newly hatched young are uniform or dappled. This<tei:lb/>condition is likely to be derived as striped patterns are very widespread among juvenile<tei:lb/>lacertids. When present, the number of dark stripes varies considerably and there may be as<tei:lb/>many as six pairs, although not all these run the whole length of the body. In the no-<tei:lb/>menclature of Lantz (1928), developed for Palaearctic Eremias, these are 1. the spinal<tei:lb/>originating near the mid-line and usually fusing to produce a single stripe on the body, 2. the<tei:lb/>occipital arising from the hind edge of the parietal scale, 3. the parietal arising from the<tei:lb/>lateral edge of that scale, 4. the temporal beginning behind the eye and passing over the<tei:lb/>upper edge of the ear (equivalent to Lantz's upper and lower temporals), 5. the maxillary<tei:lb/>passing through the ear and 6. the costal passing below it.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is usually acknowledged that a high number of stripes is plesiomorphic in the Lacertidae<tei:lb/>(Eimer, 1881; Boulenger; 1921; Lantz, 1928) and this condition is certainly commoner in<tei:lb/>forms which on other grounds seem primitive. Reduction in number seems to take place<tei:lb/>most usually by loss or fusion of bands at the mid-line. As we have seen, a few Acantho-<tei:lb/>dactylus have a pair of spinal bands on the neck that fuse on the body; reduction from this<tei:lb/>state appears to follow a clear sequence: 1. the spinals fuse throughout their length; 2. the<tei:lb/>resultant composite stripe shortens while the occipital stripes fuse posterior to it; 3. the<tei:lb/>spinal stripe is reduced to a vestige or disappears and fusion of the occipitals extends for-<tei:lb/>wards; 4. the occipitals fuse completely to produce a simple mid-line stripe. This means of<tei:lb/>course that mid-line stripes in Acanthodactylus are not always homologous as they can be</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS 3 1 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>composed either of fused spinal bands or of occipitals. There may also be loss of the outer-<tei:lb/>most bands, especially the costals on the body.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Presence of a pair of spinal stripes on the neck is largely confined to some A. micropholis,<tei:lb/>A. erythrurus and A. pardalis. The partial development of a mid-dorsal stripe made up of the<tei:lb/>occipitals occurs in some individuals of the following species: A. boskianus, A. schreiberi, A.<tei:lb/>pardalis, A. scutellatus, A. opheodurus, A. felicis and A. masirae, but complete fusion seems<tei:lb/>to be confined to some A. boskianus and A. scutellatus and most A. opheodurus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. micropholis retains a simple striped pattern throughout life. In other forms this is often<tei:lb/>modified, but predominantly striped individuals, or ones with longitudinal rows of spots, are<tei:lb/>common in most species. However not all the stripes present in juveniles may be discernible<tei:lb/>in these adults.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. pardalis and A. maculatus are singular in that some individuals of each have spots of<tei:lb/>brownish red pigment on their backs that do not fade or disappear in alcohol.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In A. (t.) tristrami, A. (t.) orientalis and A. robustus some individuals have two rows of large<tei:lb/>ocellar markings along the back.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species boundaries and species groups</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The introduction of osteological and especially hemipenial characters provides a partial test<tei:lb/>of the homogeneity of the species presently recognized within Acanthodactylus. For even if<tei:lb/>the external features on which these nominal taxa are based show little obvious variation,<tei:lb/>discontinuity in characters from the new sources may indicate that more than one form is<tei:lb/>involved. The species are mostly discussed below in groups that consist of species with a high<tei:lb/>level of overall similarity and which, in most cases, probably form holophyletic assemblages,<tei:lb/>although there are exceptions.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. micropholis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. micropholis Blanford, 1874a, occurs in southeast Iran and southwest Pakistan, in the<tei:lb/>latter country occupying sandy places along water courses in hilly areas (Minton, 1966). It<tei:lb/>has a high proportion of primitive features including the following: premaxilla with about<tei:lb/>seven teeth and not abruptly narrowed, 24 presacral vertebrae in males and 25 in females,<tei:lb/>sternal ribs usually intact; hemipenis and armature fairly symmetrical, clavulae flattened and<tei:lb/>simple, connectors slender; subocular often reaching mouth, frequently four upper labials<tei:lb/>anterior to centre of eye, eight or ten ventrals in longest row across belly, ventrals arranged in<tei:lb/>straight longitudinal rows, usually three longitudinal rows of scales on fingers, pectination on<tei:lb/>digits rather weak, upper caudal scales keeled, young with numerous dorsal stripes which are<tei:lb/>retained by adults. On the other hand, the first and fourth supraoculars are broken up and, in<tei:lb/>some individuals, the subocular may be separated from the mouth, five upper labials are<tei:lb/>sometimes present anterior to the centre of the eye and there may be indications of a fourth<tei:lb/>scale row on the fingers.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The A. cantons group, A. gongrorhynchatus and A. haasi</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. cantons Giinther, 1864ft: NW. India, Pakistan, E. Afghanistan.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. blanfordii Boulenger, 191 8a: S. Afghanistan, SW. Pakistan, SE, Iran, Oman (Muscat area).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. schmidti Haas, 1957: Arabia except extreme west; SW. Iran (Anderson, 1963, 1974).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. arabicus Boulenger, 1918a: SW. Arabia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. gongrorhynchatus Leviton &amp; Anderson, 1967: E. and SE. Arabia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. haasi Leviton &amp; Anderson, 1967: E. Arabia (Dhahran).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The first four forms, which constitute the A. cantoris group, are all associated with loose<tei:lb/>sand habitats (A. cantoris Minton, 1966; Mertens, 1969, A. blanfordii Blanford, 18746;<tei:lb/>Anderson, 1963; Clark et ai, 1969; M. D. Gallagher, pers. comm. A. schmidti Anderson,<tei:lb/>1963; personal observations, A. arabicus Anderson, 1895). Soft ground types are also</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>312 E.N.ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>suggested by the extensive pectination of the digits. Members of the A. cantoris group share<tei:lb/>the following features: premaxilla with about seven teeth and usually abruptly narrowed<tei:lb/>(least in A. cantoris), usually 24 presacral vertebrae in males, 25 in females (less sexual<tei:lb/>differentiation in some populations of A. schmidti), fifth sternal rib often intact; hemipenis<tei:lb/>and armature usually showing marked asymmetry (not in most A. cantoris), clavulae not<tei:lb/>very complex, connectors relatively slender; first supraocular intact or not much divided and<tei:lb/>the fourth large or rather fragmented with a group of granules wedged between it and the<tei:lb/>third, subocular nearly always separated from the mouth, usually five upper labials anterior<tei:lb/>to the centre of the eye, posterior dorsals coarse and carinate, 12-18 ventrals in longest row<tei:lb/>across belly, ventrals arranged in fairly straight longitudinal rows, although sometimes<tei:lb/>staggered at sides, nearly always four longitudinal rows of scales on fingers (anterior row<tei:lb/>sometimes reduced in A. cantoris), pectination on digits strong, upper caudal scales keeled,<tei:lb/>young usually with numerous dorsal stripes (not in A. schmidti) that are typically lost in<tei:lb/>adults.<tei:lb/>In the past it has been usual to treat these four forms as subspecies of A. cantoris. But,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Table 2 Variation in the A. cantoris group</tei:p>

         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS 3 1 3</tei:p>
         <tei:p>although they have a strong overall resemblance to each other and, so far as is known, are<tei:lb/>allopatric, each has a number of distinctive characters that are often more pronounced than<tei:lb/>those separating accepted species of Acanthodactylus (see Table 2). Because of this, it seems<tei:lb/>more consistent to give them full species status.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. cantoris is widespread in northwest India and in Pakistan is found in the southeast and<tei:lb/>in the Indus drainage whence it reaches east Afghanistan (Kabul River area, CAS 96200-01 ,<tei:lb/>120358-60); it also extends westwards along the Pakistan coast at least to Ormara (BM(NH)<tei:lb/>1904.12.7.2; Minton, 1966, records A. blanfordii from this locality but his specimens, RSM<tei:lb/>1 964.58.2/6, are in fact A. cantoris). There is some geographical variation, for instance in the<tei:lb/>Punjab the anterior row of scales on the fingers is incomplete in some animals. This is true of<tei:lb/>a female from Cambellpur (BM(NH) 1933.5.16.8) which is also peculiar in having a rather<tei:lb/>blunt snout and elongate, strongly overlapping dorsal scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. blanfordii reaches its easternmost limit in the Helmand drainage of south Afghanistan<tei:lb/>and the neighbouring border area of Pakistan (BM(NH) 86.9.21.77-80; Mertens, 1969; it is<tei:lb/>also probable that the specimens listed by Clark et al, 1969, and Minton, 1966 are A.<tei:lb/>blanfordii). A. blanfordii and A. cantoris may be separated by intervening high ground in this<tei:lb/>area but it is possible that they meet on the coast for A. blanfordii extends eastwards as far as<tei:lb/>Pasni (Mertens, 1969), only about 130 km from the nearest known A. cantoris locality. The<tei:lb/>differences in dorsal and gular scale counts on which these two forms were originally<tei:lb/>separated do not always hold but they can be distinguished by the fusion of postorbital and<tei:lb/>postfrontal bones in A. cantoris and by hemipenial features. A. blanfordii extends into<tei:lb/>eastern Iran at least as far as the Bandar Abbas region (Anderson, 1963) and an isolated<tei:lb/>population has recently been found by M. D. Gallagher near Muscat, southeast Arabia<tei:lb/>(BM(NH) 1973.723-26, 1976. 1462-64, 1977.68-69); here all the adults examined retain at<tei:lb/>least traces of dorsal striping.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. schmidti is widespread in Arabia where it shows substantial regional variation in body<tei:lb/>size (Arnold, 1981/7). It also extends into southwest Iran (Anderson, 1974) but without<tei:lb/>apparently contacting A blanfordii.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. arabicus is restricted to the littoral area of southwest Arabia and seems to be separated<tei:lb/>from A. schmidti by hilly, not very sandy country occupied by such species as A. boskianus,<tei:lb/>A. felicis and A. opheodurus. Although A. arabicus and A. schmidti both have the medial<tei:lb/>side of the hemipenis and armature very reduced, they differ in their other apparent<tei:lb/>apomorphies (A. arabicus very narrow clavula; A. schmidti often very large size,<tei:lb/>dorsolateral tracts of enlarged scales on hind back, lack of stripes in juvenile and adult<tei:lb/>patterns) and in dorsal scale number. In fact A. arabicus is more similar to A. blanfordii,<tei:lb/>especially Muscat specimens, differing mainly in its more slender build, greater hemipenial<tei:lb/>asymmetry, narrower clavula and higher number of ventral scales across the belly (usually<tei:lb/>1416 as against 11-13(14) at Muscat). A. arabicus from Aden and its vicinity are<tei:lb/>comparatively large and usually retain stripes or spots when adult but animals from futher<tei:lb/>east (as far as Gischin ( = Qishn), NMW 11814/1-16) are smaller and often become uniform<tei:lb/>in colouring with maturity.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Relationships within the A. cantoris group are not easy to discern with great certainty,<tei:lb/>since most of the apomorphies available for judging affinities are rather weak (see p. 324), but<tei:lb/>A. blanfordii, A. schmidti and A. arabicus all differ from A. cantoris in their more narrowed<tei:lb/>premaxillae, more asymmetrical hemipenes and usually higher number of ventrals, while<tei:lb/>lacking its fused postorbital and postfrontal bones and its hemipenial peculiarities (p. 305),<tei:lb/>so they may well form a distinct unit with A. blanfordii as the least modified species. Within<tei:lb/>it, A. schmidti and A. arabicus share almost total loss of the medial side of the hemipenis and<tei:lb/>armature, but there are no other apparent synapomorphies joining them, apart from increase<tei:lb/>in ventral scale count. As extreme hemipenial asymmetry has developed a number of times<tei:lb/>within Acanthodactylus, its value as an indicator of relationship is quite low. It is possible<tei:lb/>that A. schmidti is the sister species of A. blanfordii for the latter occasionally shows<tei:lb/>indications of the dorsolateral tracts of enlarged scales on the hind-back that are typical of</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>314 E.N.ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>the former and they often share a peculiar lateral clavula shape in which there is a proxi-<tei:lb/>mally directed pocket. Moreover, A. schmidti occasionally has a medial clavula exactly like<tei:lb/>that of A. blanfordii. Certainly A. arabicus and A. schmidti are more like A. blanfordii than<tei:lb/>like each other and may well have been independently derived from a A. blanfordii-\ike<tei:lb/>stock.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. gongrorhynchatus is sympatric with A. schmidti in east and southeast Arabia and shares<tei:lb/>many features of the A. cantoris group but it differs in its very slender adult build, convex<tei:lb/>pileus, short but abruptly acuminate snout, weakly keeled supratemporal scales that are<tei:lb/>fragmented posteriorly, four upper labials anterior to the centre of the eye, an ear hole that is<tei:lb/>reduced in size and fine dorsal scaling that sometimes grades into the ventral scales. Recent<tei:lb/>observations by Mr W. Ross (personal communication) show that A. gongrorhynchatus lives<tei:lb/>on aeolian sand. Its morphology depressed fingers, strong digital pectination, very pointed<tei:lb/>snout and partly occluded ear opening is appropriate for such a substrate and suggests that<tei:lb/>it is more strictly confined to this habitat than A. schmidti which occupies the same general<tei:lb/>area. A. gongrorhynchatus may well be paedomorphic in some features which are typical of<tei:lb/>juvenile Acanthodactylus, for instance the rounded pileus, short snout and lack of strong<tei:lb/>keeling on the supratemporal scales. In fact it has substantial resemblance to immature<tei:lb/>specimens of A. arabicus and may well be closely related to this species, especially as their<tei:lb/>hemipenes are very similar including the very narrow lateral clavula.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The present situation in Arabia may have arisen by a second stock of the A. cantoris group<tei:lb/>invading to exist alongside one already present. It is possible that much of the peninsula was<tei:lb/>originally occupied by A. arabicus-\ike populations and that the precursor of A. schmidti<tei:lb/>entered the area from the east to become sympatric with them, except in the extreme south-<tei:lb/>west littoral region which is isolated by geographical barriers. Here A. arabicus survives but<tei:lb/>elsewhere coexistence of two species in soft-sand habitats favoured by the A. cantoris group<tei:lb/>would be expected to produce considerable character displacement, the A. arabicus-like<tei:lb/>stock giving rise to A. gongrorhynchatus and the invading one to A. schmidti, the two most<tei:lb/>divergent species in the assemblage. An analagous situation exists among the nocturnal<tei:lb/>ground geckoes of the genus Stenodactylus that occupy soft sand habitats (Arnold, 1980a).<tei:lb/>As in Acanthodactylus there is a single species in the southwestern littoral region of Arabia<tei:lb/>(S. pulcher) which has a close relative widespread in the peninsula (S. arabicus), where it is<tei:lb/>sympatric with another more distantly related species (S. doriae). As with A. gongrorhyn-<tei:lb/>chatus, S. arabicus is more strongly adapted to sandy environments and smaller than its<tei:lb/>southwestern relative whereas S. doriae, like A. schmidti is relatively large.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. haasi is known only from the male holotype obtained at Dahran, eastern Saudi Arabia.<tei:lb/>In spite of being collected in 1946, no further examples have appeared, even though a great<tei:lb/>deal of Acanthodactylus material has been taken in the general area. A. haasi has much in<tei:lb/>common with A. gongrorhynchatus and shares a number of synapomorphies with it that<tei:lb/>occur nowhere else in the genus; these include very slender habitus, similar lateral clavula<tei:lb/>structure and reduced ear opening. It differs in its less acuminate snout and less narrowed<tei:lb/>premaxilla, shorter lateral clavula, five upper labials anterior to the centre of the eye, large<tei:lb/>and weakly keeled dorsal scales, less pectination on the fingers and numerous keels on the<tei:lb/>subdigital lamellae. In most of these features and many others A. haasi resembles members of<tei:lb/>the A. cantoris group from which A. gongrorhynchatus is probably derived. This together<tei:lb/>with the synapomorphies shared with the latter may indicate that A. haasi and A.<tei:lb/>gongrorhynchatus are sister species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Another possibility, suggested partly by the fact that no further specimens have been<tei:lb/>collected, is that A. haasi is a hybrid between A. gongrorhynchatus and one of the other<tei:lb/>Acanthodactylus species in eastern Arabia. Assuming that a hybrid would probably be<tei:lb/>intermediate in many features, the various candidates can be considered in turn. A. schmidti<tei:lb/>is unlikely because its premaxilla is constricted, it has a high number of ventral scales in the<tei:lb/>longest row across the belly (13-18 compared with 10-12 in A. gongrorhynchatus and 12 in<tei:lb/>A. haasi), pectination on the fingers is extensive, multiple carination on the digits is absent</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS 3 1 5</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(present in A. haasi) and, in east Arabia, it is a far larger animal than A. gongrorhynchatus, so<tei:lb/>interspecific copulation is unlikely; size difference would probably also exclude A. boskianus<tei:lb/>as well. A. scutellatus has a narrow premaxilla with only five teeth, dorsal scaling is fine and<tei:lb/>again there is no multiple carination on the digits. A. opheodurus has this feature in at least<tei:lb/>some cases and possesses all the other characteristics to be expected if A. haasi is a hybrid<tei:lb/>between it and A. gongrorhynchatus. But the status of A. haasi will probably only be decided<tei:lb/>by further collection in the area of the type locality.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. schreiberi and A. boskianus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. schreiberi Boulenger, 1878; Cyprus, Lebanon, N. Israel.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. boskianus (Daudin, 1802): Much of north Africa as far south as N. Nigeria and Eritrea, Arabia but<tei:lb/>not the R'ub al Khali, S. Israel, Jordan, Iraq, Syria and adjoining Turkey.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>These species are rarely found on very soft sand surfaces but occur on more stable sand and<tei:lb/>other harder substrates. A. boskianus is often associated with quite dense, sometimes spiny<tei:lb/>vegetation, although it also forages in more open areas (Doumergue, 1901; Flower, 1933;<tei:lb/>field labels attached to BM specimens; personal observations in southeast Arabia). Both<tei:lb/>species share a relatively high number of primitive features including the following:<tei:lb/>premaxilla with about seven teeth and not abruptly narrowed, usually 24 presacral vertebrae<tei:lb/>in males and 25 in females; fifth sternal rib often intact, hemipenis more or less symmetrical,<tei:lb/>medial side of clavula not strongly reduced; supraoculars more or less intact (first sometimes<tei:lb/>divided in A. boskianus), usually four upper labials anterior to centre of eye, typically 10<tei:lb/>ventral scales in longest row across belly (12 in many Arabian A. boskianus), ventrals<tei:lb/>arranged in straight longitudinal rows, three longitudinal rows of scales on fingers,<tei:lb/>pectination on digits not exceptionally strong, upper caudal scales keeled, young with<tei:lb/>numerous dorsal stripes that are sometimes retained in modified form by adults. Apparently<tei:lb/>apomorphic characters include reduction of the proximal lip of the medial branch of the<tei:lb/>hemipenial sulcus, some reduction of medial side of armature, medial clavula pointed with a<tei:lb/>^ -shaped cross section, lateral clavula complexly lobed, most medial of the lateral<tei:lb/>connectors broad; subocular does not usually reach mouth (exceptions in A. boskianus) and<tei:lb/>dorsal scales are often large. The close resemblance of A. schreiberi and A. boskianus<tei:lb/>together with the close match of their hemipenes in apomorphic features makes it very<tei:lb/>probable that they are sister species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. schreiberi has two allopatric populations: A. s. schreiberi on Cyprus and A. schreiberi<tei:lb/>syriacus Boettger, 1879 in Lebanon and north Israel, the latter having coarser dorsal scales<tei:lb/>with sharper keels. A. boskianus usually differs most obviously from A. schreiberi in<tei:lb/>possessing larger dorsal scales. However there is slight overlap in the transverse scale counts<tei:lb/>between the hind limbs, but not in Israel where the two species appear to contact each other<tei:lb/>without introgression (Duvdevani and Borut, 19746). A. boskianus is the most widely<tei:lb/>distributed species of its genus but the range is not continuous in northern Africa where this<tei:lb/>lizard is mainy restricted to the periphery of the Saharan desert and to oases and other<tei:lb/>fairly mesic areas within it. As the species is relatively uniform in this region, its spread<tei:lb/>to these isolated areas may have been quite recent, presumably during one of the episodes in<tei:lb/>the Quaternary when conditions in north Africa were less extreme.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. boskianus has been divided into three subspecies: A. b. boskianus in the Nile delta area<tei:lb/>and parts of Sinai; A. b. euphraticus Boulenger, 1919 described from Ramadieh ( = Ramdi,<tei:lb/>Iraq) and A. b. asper (Audouin, 1829) which covers almost the whole of the species' range.<tei:lb/>This simple, tripartite division is not satisfactory, for some of the supposedly distinctive<tei:lb/>features of A. b. euphraticus are not consistent and there is some differentiation within the<tei:lb/>populations assigned to A. b. asper. An adequate intraspecific treatment of A. boskianus is<tei:lb/>beyond the scope of this paper but variation of some features within the species will be</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>316 E.N.ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>briefly described. Over most of north Africa, the number of dorsal scales in a transverse row<tei:lb/>at mid-body varies from about 26 to 41, although maxima are less in many areas, the first<tei:lb/>supraocular scale is usually intact and the subocular does not reach the mouth in most<tei:lb/>individuals. In the Nile delta and north Sinai, populations assigned to A. b. boskianus have<tei:lb/>high dorsal scale counts (34-52) and the supraocular scale is divided, but these populations<tei:lb/>intergrade with those typical of the rest of north Africa and with similar ones in Israel and<tei:lb/>west Jordan. Animals with high dorsal counts (38-48) also occur in northeast Jordan, north<tei:lb/>and central Iraq, east Syria and adjoining Turkey. In some cases, such as the types of A. b.<tei:lb/>euphraticus, they also have the first supraocular divided and the subocular often meeting the<tei:lb/>mouth, but these conditions are absent in many individuals from neighbouring populations.<tei:lb/>Arabian animals are rather similar to those widespread in north Africa but are characterized<tei:lb/>by low transverse dorsal scale counts at mid-body (usually 18-27 but up to 36 in the south-<tei:lb/>western mountains) and a large body size (up to 93 mm from snout to vent against maxima of<tei:lb/>about 82 elsewhere; specimens from the western periphery of the peninsula tend to be<tei:lb/>smaller). Arabian A. boskianus contrast strongly with those found to the north. In Jordan an<tei:lb/>animal from the southeast of the country (El Inab, JUM R505) is of the Arabian type<tei:lb/>whereas those from elsewhere are less coarsely scaled (31-37 scales across mid-back) and<tei:lb/>relatively small (only up to about 65 mm from snout to vent). Animals of this type occur at<tei:lb/>Wadi Rum (JUM R69), only about 130 km from El Inab. Whether the two forms intergrade<tei:lb/>and, if so, whether the intergradation is abrupt is not known.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Geographical variation in A. boskianus may well reflect differences in niche across its<tei:lb/>range. As stated, this species is often associated with dense vegetation and large dorsal scales<tei:lb/>may well be protective where shrubs are rigid and spiny; the fine-scaled populations are in<tei:lb/>relatively mesic areas (Nile Delta, Tigris-Euphrates river system) where vegetation would be<tei:lb/>expected to be less damaging than in more arid regions. Division of the first supraocular scale<tei:lb/>and a subocular that reaches the mouth are also characters typical of more mesic situations<tei:lb/>(see p. 307). The distinctive features of lowland Arabian A. boskianus may be related to the<tei:lb/>fact that they coexist here with A. opheodurus, a small form originally confused with A.<tei:lb/>boskianus that occupies similar substrates and occurs in strict sympatry with it, at least in<tei:lb/>some areas (Arnold, 19806). The presence of A. opheodurus might have produced displace-<tei:lb/>ment or restriction of the niche available to A. boskianus with consequent morphological<tei:lb/>change (Arnold, 19816). Thus, increase in body size may allow adult A. boskianus to take<tei:lb/>larger prey, reducing competition for small food items; very large scale size could indicate<tei:lb/>that this species spends a higher proportion of time in vegetation than elsewhere. It is<tei:lb/>perhaps significant that in extreme southwest Arabia, where no A. opheodurus are known, A.<tei:lb/>boskianus is smaller with higher dorsal scale counts than elsewhere in the peninsula.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In spite of the considerable variation encountered in A. boskianus, as presently under-<tei:lb/>stood, there is as yet no firm evidence that it consists of more than one species. However, A.<tei:lb/>schreiberi may well have originated as an isolate of A. boskianus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The A. grand is complex</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. grandis Boulenger, 1909 complex (including A. fraseri Boulenger, 1918c): Syria, E. Lebanon,<tei:lb/>Jordan, NW. Arabia, Iraq, SW. Iran (Khuzistan and Pars provinces Anderson, 1974).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Nothing certain is known about the ecology of these lizards but the restricted pectination on<tei:lb/>the digits suggests that they are not usually found on very soft sand surfaces; however there is<tei:lb/>some variation in this feature between populations which may indicate that they occupy a<tei:lb/>variety of ground types. The members of the A. grandis complex share many features with A.<tei:lb/>schreiberi and A. boskianus, especially the former, the most significant differences being<tei:lb/>that, in the A. grandis complex, the ventrals are tessellated and the number in the longest<tei:lb/>row across the belly is higher (14-18), there are four longitudinal series of scales running<tei:lb/>along the fingers and the hemipenis, although generally very similar often differs slightly in<tei:lb/>the pattern of lobing on the upper surface of the lateral clavula (p. 306); also, five upper<tei:lb/>labial scales are more frequently present anterior to the centre of the eye.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS<tei:lb/>Table 3 A. grandis complex: variation between samples</tei:p>
         <tei:p>317</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. grandis was originally described from the Damascus area of Syria while the only other<tei:lb/>nominal species assignable to the complex, A. fraseri, is based on material from Zobeya,<tei:lb/>Shariba ( = Shu'aiba, northeast of Basra) far to the east in southeastern Iraq. Material<tei:lb/>collected subsequently in the intervening areas shows a variety of conditions intermediate<tei:lb/>between the named forms but the pattern of variation appears to be irregular and sometimes<tei:lb/>animals from adjacent localities show considerable differences in such features as adult size,<tei:lb/>strength of keeling on the dorsal scales, and pattern (summarized in Table 3). In general,<tei:lb/>members of the eastern populations tend to be smaller than western animals with fewer,<tei:lb/>more strongly keeled dorsal scales and single points on each subdigital lamella.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is possible that the A. grandis complex is best regarded as a single species but available<tei:lb/>samples are too small and scattered to be certain about this. The irregular variation of<tei:lb/>populations intermediate between typical A. grandis and A. fraseri may reflect the geography<tei:lb/>of Mesopotamia, for here the comparatively arid country favoured by Acanthodactylus is<tei:lb/>divided up by the Tigris and Euphrates rivers and their tributaries which flood seasonally, so<tei:lb/>populations may be substantially discontinuous. Other Acanthodactylus species, especially<tei:lb/>A. boskianus, also show considerable variation in this area.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>318 E.N.ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The A. tristrami group</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. (/.) tristrami (Giinther, 1864): Lebanon, SW. Syria, NW. Jordan.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. (/.) orientalis Angel, 1936: E. Syria, W. and central Iraq.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. robustus Werner, 1929: S. Syria, SW. Iraq, Jordan and N. Arabia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The weak digital pectination in these forms suggests that they are found on fairly hard<tei:lb/>substrates. A. (t.) tristrami seems to be confined to steppe-type habitats on the edge of the<tei:lb/>Anti-Lebanon range. A. (t.) orientalis may also be restricted to relatively mesic habitats since<tei:lb/>most records are from localities on or near the Tigris-Euphrates river system (Angel, 1936;<tei:lb/>Schmidt, 1939; Haas and Werner, 1969), although Angel also records it from Palmyra. A.<tei:lb/>robustus, on the other hand seems to occupy more desertic regions; Riney (1953) gives brief<tei:lb/>ecological notes that refer to this species, although they are attributed to A. (t.) orientalis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Typical A. tristrami has many features in common with A. schreiberi (p. 3 1 5) but there<tei:lb/>are a number of differences: there are more presacral vertebrae, with about 25 in males and 26<tei:lb/>or 27 in females, the proximal lip of the medial branch of the hemipenial sulcus is not<tei:lb/>reduced, the lateral clavula is complex but not lobed and is very slightly bifurcate at its tip,<tei:lb/>the first and fourth supraoculars are fragmented, the subocular reaches the mouth, the dorsal<tei:lb/>and upper caudal scales lack keels and the tail is short. Of these features, fragmentation of the<tei:lb/>supraoculars, unkeeled upper caudals, short tail, clavula shape, and perhaps vertebral<tei:lb/>number may be regarded as apomorphies. Nearly all occur in other populations of the A.<tei:lb/>tristrami group and unkeeled upper caudals and the short tail are confined to it.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Two other subspecies assigned to A. tristrami have been named, A. t. orientalis from<tei:lb/>eastern Syria and A. t. iracensis, Schmidt, 1939, described from Haditha on the Euphrates in<tei:lb/>west Iraq. The latter is said to have a lower mid-body dorsal scale count (45-46 against<tei:lb/>48-56) and a weaker dorsal pattern than A. t. orientalis but other material from the same area<tei:lb/>(Haas &amp; Werner, 1969) and from Najaf, further down the Euphrates (INHM, with 51 dorsal<tei:lb/>scales at mid-body and a bold pattern) suggest that this distinction is illusory. A. t. iracensis is<tei:lb/>therefore referred to the synonymy of orientalis. As understood here, this form is<tei:lb/>distinguished from typical tristrami by its smaller size (up to 63 mm from snout to vent<tei:lb/>instead of up to 92 mm) and fewer dorsal scales (43-56 against 54-65). The hemipenes are<tei:lb/>also distinctive, orientalis having the medial lobe and medial side of the armature very<tei:lb/>reduced. These differences suggest that tristrami and orientalis might be better regarded as<tei:lb/>full species but more information is needed before this can be confirmed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. robustus shares most of the distinctive features of A. (t.) tristrami but the lateral clavula<tei:lb/>is relatively simple, the snout is shorter and more pointed, the subocular frequently<tei:lb/>separated from the mouth, there are often 12 instead of 10 ventrals in the longest row across<tei:lb/>the belly and there may be a rudimentary fourth longitudinal row of scales on the fingers.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. ery thrums group</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. erythrurus (Schinz, 1833): Spain, Portugal, Morocco, N. Algeria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. savignyi(Audou\n, 1829): N. Algeria, N. Tunisia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. boueti Chabanaud, 1 9 1 7: N. Dahomey, N. Ghana.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. guineensis (Boulenger, 1887a): Ghana, Nigeria, Niger and Cameroon.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>These species are typical of relatively mesic areas and usually have weak digital pectination.<tei:lb/>They are similar to A. (t.) tristrami in many features but all lack the short tail and unkeeled<tei:lb/>upper caudal scales of this form and also frequently have an azygos shield between the<tei:lb/>prefrontal scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In A. erythrurus and A. savignyi the lateral clavula of the hemipenis is deeply bifurcate at<tei:lb/>its tip, the frontonasal scale is quite often divided and the subocular may be separated from<tei:lb/>the lip.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. erythrurus has three widespread forms: A. e. erythrurus in Spain and Portugal A. e.<tei:lb/>lineomaculatus Dumeril &amp; Bibron, 1839 in lowland western Morocco and A. e. bellii Gray,</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS 3 1 9</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1845 in the adjoining higher country and in north Algeria. Each of these is well denned on<tei:lb/>external features and A. e. bellii is further distinguished by its usually simple lateral clavula.<tei:lb/>Two other subspecies are sometimes recognized: A. e. mauritanicus, Doumergue, 1901 of<tei:lb/>extreme northern Algeria resembles A. e. bellii in most features including its lateral clavula<tei:lb/>and differs only in its more obviously keeled dorsals; A. e. atlanticus Boulenger, 1918# has a<tei:lb/>restricted distribution in north Morocco between the ranges of A. e. bellii and A. e.<tei:lb/>lineomaculatus and may be an intermediate between them. A. savignyi bland Doumergue,<tei:lb/>1 90 1 of north Tunisia is very similar to A. e. lineomaculatus but A. s. savignyi which coexists<tei:lb/>with A. erythrurus in north Algeria is more distinctive. It has a clearly pointed snout and a<tei:lb/>better developed pectination on the digits than is usual in its close relatives which may be<tei:lb/>related to its occupying more sandy habitats (Doumergue, 1901). It is possible that the<tei:lb/>differences between the two subspecies of A. savignyi are a result of character displacement<tei:lb/>(Arnold, 198 16).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. boueti and A. guineensis are generally like the above but the frontonasal is undivided<tei:lb/>and the subocular scale is never separated from the lip. Each species has a number of<tei:lb/>distinctive features. In A. boueti there are nine premaxillary teeth, the hemipenis is very<tei:lb/>small, the second supraocular scale is broken up and the parietal scales are partly<tei:lb/>fragmented. A. guineensis has the medial side of the hemipenis and armature absent and a<tei:lb/>peculiar arrangement of nasal scales (p. 296), a feature that occurs rarely also in A.<tei:lb/>erythrurus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The A. pardalis group</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. p. pardalis (Lichtenstein, 1823): Cyrenaica, north Egypt, Israel.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. p. bedriagai Lataste, 1881: High plateaux of north Algeria (provinces of Oran, Algiers and</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Constantine); closely related populations in west Morrocco and west Tunisia.<tei:lb/>A. maculatus (Gray, 1838): northeast Morocco, north Algeria, Tunisia, Tripolitania.<tei:lb/>A. spinicauda Doumergue, 1901: Arba Tahtani and El Abiodh Sidi Sheikh, northwest Algeria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Members of the A. pardalis group are largely confined to relatively hard compact substrates<tei:lb/>such as clayey-sandy soils, clay, loess and salt flats (Blanc, 1980; Gauthier, 1967; Mosauer,<tei:lb/>1 934; data on labels attached to BM(NH) specimens).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>They all agree in the following characters: premaxilla with about seven teeth, not<tei:lb/>narrowed and separated from the maxillae by a constriction, presacral vertebrae usually<tei:lb/>24-26 in males and 25-26 in females, fifth sternal rib often interrupted; hemipenis and<tei:lb/>armature asymmetrical, often markedly so, lateral clavula often bifurcate at tip, lateral<tei:lb/>connectors often thickened; first supraocular intact or not very much divided and the fourth<tei:lb/>fragmented, subocular separated from mouth, four or five upper labials anterior to centre of<tei:lb/>eye, dorsals small and often more or less smooth, 12 or more ventrals in longest row across<tei:lb/>belly, ventrals arranged in fairly straight longitudinal rows, three longitudinal rows of scales<tei:lb/>on fingers, pectination on digits relatively weak although somewhat variable, young with<tei:lb/>numerous dorsal stripes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Boulenger (1918a, 1921) treated the A. pardalis group as a single species within which he<tei:lb/>recognized five varieties: pardalis (his forma typica), bedriagai, maculatus, latastii<tei:lb/>Boulenger, 1918a and spinicauda. Pasteur &amp; Bons (I960) regarded spinicauda as a distinct<tei:lb/>species but felt that the other varieties were no more than individual variations and placed<tei:lb/>them in the synonymy of the monotypic species A. pardalis. In fact, the introduction of<tei:lb/>osteological and hemipenial data helps to confirm the validity of the majority of Boulenger's<tei:lb/>divisions and it appears that at least five entities within the A. pardalis group can be<tei:lb/>recognized (see Table 4 &amp; Fig. 7), A. maculatus, as understood here, includes Boulenger's<tei:lb/>var. latastii. This author restricted maculatus to often relatively small-bodied populations in<tei:lb/>which the posterior dorsal scales are frequently keeled and some individuals have light<tei:lb/>stripes or rows of light spots. These are distributed along the coastal areas of Tripolitania and</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>

         <tei:p>Tunisia and in northeast Algeria. The name latastii was applied to inland populations lying<tei:lb/>mainly to the south in which body size is sometimes greater, dorsal scales are usually more or<tei:lb/>less smooth, the dorsal pattern tends to lack light stripes or spots and the snout is more<tei:lb/>pointed. However, there is considerable variation and many intermediates occur, so it seems<tei:lb/>best to regard these populations as a single unit. The geographical differences within this<tei:lb/>may reflect change in vegetation and substrate conditions; the pattern of maculatus-type<tei:lb/>animals is more likely to be cryptic where vegetation is relatively dense whilst that typical of<tei:lb/>latastii is probably cryptic in more open areas.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The relationships between the forms described in Table 4 are not entirely clear, but it is<tei:lb/>certain that they cannot all be assigned to a single species. Although no areas of certain<tei:lb/>sympatry are known, bedriagai, the form of the high plateaux of north Algeria, approaches<tei:lb/>the more southern maculatus near Biskra (bedriagai Aures Mountains north of Biskra,<tei:lb/>BM(NH) 91.5.4.70-72; maculatus near Biskra, BM(NH) 1907.4.6.10-25, BM(NH)<tei:lb/>1920.1.20.791, BM(NH) 1969.2121-23). The two localities are probably within 100 km of<tei:lb/>each other but the forms retain their differences in hemipenial structure, usual number of<tei:lb/>presacral vertebrae and of ventral scales in the longest row across the belly, and in pattern. It<tei:lb/>seems likely therefore that bedriagai and maculatus are distinct species, especially as the<tei:lb/>hemipenial differences between them could act as an isolating mechanism.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The un-named west Moroccan population (north of Agadir, BM(NH) 1970.246^7; 20 km<tei:lb/>north of Tiznit, BM 1970.248; 30km southwest of Goulmime, BM(NH) 1970.249-50) is<tei:lb/>generally similar to bedriagai and is probably conspecific with it but differs in the following<tei:lb/>features: snout often more pointed, posterior dorsal scales more pointed and keeled, ventrals<tei:lb/>in longest row across belly often 12 (not 14), upper labials anterior to centre of eye often four<tei:lb/>(frequently five in bedriagai), range of dorsal patterns somewhat different. It is possible that<tei:lb/>this population also contacts A. maculatus although evidence is lacking.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Another A. bedriagai-\ike population occurs at Tamesmida (33.05N 8.23E) in western<tei:lb/>Tunisia (BM(NH) 1920.1.20.3018, discussed by Boulenger, 1921, p. 67). The two male<tei:lb/>specimens available resemble this form very closely in osteological features and externals<tei:lb/>but the hemipenis is single-lobed and the median side of the armature is lacking.
