         <tei:p>322 E. N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Furryanah (34.57N 8.35E), A. maculatus occurs (BM(NH) 1920.1. 20.30 18e) but, as the<tei:lb/>Tamesmida specimens are not like this form in other respects, a hybrid origin for them seems<tei:lb/>unlikely. Possibly they represent an isolate of the A. bedriagai stock that has undergone<tei:lb/>hemipenial modification in response to some previous threat of introgression, as seems to<tei:lb/>have happened several times in Acanthodactylus (p. 304). Tamesmida is on the edge of the<tei:lb/>eastern extension of the high ground on which A. bedriagai is found.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The form spinicauda is known only from Arba Tahtani ( = Arbaouat, 33.05N 00.35E) and<tei:lb/>nearby El Abior Sidi Sheikh ( = E1 Abiodh Sidi Cheikh, 32.53N 00.34E), oases at the<tei:lb/>southern foot of the Saharan Atlas in northwest Algeria. In general form, hemipenial<tei:lb/>structure and pattern, it resembles A. maculatus populations in the region (Mecheria and<tei:lb/>Laghouat) but differs in having the lateral scales at the base of the tail greatly enlarged,<tei:lb/>especially in males, with their keels produced to form tubercles, this condition occurring in<tei:lb/>no other lacertid. The hind legs are also rather longer than in nearby A. maculatus<tei:lb/>populations, although this is partly due to the fact that relative hind-leg length decreases<tei:lb/>with size and spinicauda is quite small; three females radiographed all have 26 presacral<tei:lb/>vertebrae instead of the 25 usual in A. maculatus. The two forms do not appear to be<tei:lb/>sympatric, so a direct test of the species status of spinicauda is not possible. It is certainly<tei:lb/>very like neighbouring A. maculatus but the three distinctive features, especially the unique<tei:lb/>tail structure, suggest it may be best to regard it as a full species, at least for the present. In the<tei:lb/>south of its range, maculatus appears to have a disjunct distribution, occurring mainly<tei:lb/>around oases such as Ouargla, Ghardia and Leghouat. Presumably its range was once more<tei:lb/>continuous but has become fragmented since the climate of the area has become more<tei:lb/>extreme. It is probable that A. spinicauda originated from such an oasis isolate.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Typical A. pardalis show some variation, for instance animals from the Jebel el Akhdar<tei:lb/>region of Cyrenaica appear to be smaller than samples to the south and east. Also, although<tei:lb/>Egyptian animals usually have 24 presacral vertebrae in males and 25 in females, in<tei:lb/>Cyrenaica 25 is common in males and females sometimes have 26. While there is evidence<tei:lb/>that bedriagai and maculatus are specifically distinct, it is less clear how they are related to<tei:lb/>typical A. pardalis. This is partly because neither is known to contact this form, there being a<tei:lb/>gap in the known range of the A. pardalis group as a whole south of the Gulf of Sirte, Libya,<tei:lb/>although whether this is real or a collecting artifact is uncertain. Typical A. pardalis<tei:lb/>resembles bedriagai in hemipenial structure but differs in usual number of presacral<tei:lb/>vertebrae and ventral scales in the longest row across the belly (although the bedriagai-\ike<tei:lb/>population in west Morocco matches pardalis in this last characteristic). On the other hand,<tei:lb/>A. pardalis resembles maculatus in these features and the nearest populations of each form<tei:lb/>are quite similar, although coastal maculatus west of the Gulf of Sirte are smaller and have<tei:lb/>more strongly keeled dorsal scales than the closest pardalis. However, these two forms differ<tei:lb/>radically in hemipenial structure which, coupled with size-difference in this area might well<tei:lb/>preclude interbreeding. This being so, it seems reasonable to regard maculatus as specifically<tei:lb/>distinct from pardalis, although it cannot be ruled out that connecting populations may yet<tei:lb/>be found in north Libya with intermediate hemipenes. Typical bedriagai and the similar<tei:lb/>west Moroccan population are tentatively regarded as conspecific with pardalis as they are<tei:lb/>hemipenially similar and the differences between them are mainly in features showing<tei:lb/>substantial variation within each population. On hemipenial evidence, the Tamesmida<tei:lb/>population may be specifically distinct from A. bedriagai but it seems safest to leave its status<tei:lb/>undecided until more is known about it.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The 1. scutellatus group</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. scutellatus (Audouin, 1829): North Africa south to Senegal, Mauretania, Mali, Niger, N. Chad and</tei:p>
         <tei:p>N. Sudan; S. Israel, N. Arabia, S. and central Iraq.<tei:lb/>A. longipes Boulenger, 1918a: North Africa including parts of S. Morocco, Algeria, Libya, Mauretania,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Mali, Niger and Chad.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS 323</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. aureus Gunther, 1903; Coasatal areas of NW. Africa from S. Morocco to Mauretania; also some<tei:lb/>neighbouring inland areas.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The A. scutellatus group is largely associated with soft-sand habitats (Anderson, 1898;<tei:lb/>Doumergue, 1901; Flower, 1933; Mosauer, 1934; Gauthier, 1967; Blanc, 1980; field labels<tei:lb/>on BM(NH) material); this is also suggested by the extensive pectination usually present on<tei:lb/>the toes. However occasional populations are found on harder substrates (Scortecci, 1946).<tei:lb/>In north Africa the group occupies many desert areas and, unlike A. boskianus in this region,<tei:lb/>is not largely confined to relatively mesic enclaves. A. scutellatus and its relatives have few<tei:lb/>features plesiomorphic within Acanthodactylus but many apomorphic ones including the<tei:lb/>following: premaxilla often with only five teeth and abruptly narrowed, usual number of<tei:lb/>presacral vertebrae 23 or 24 with very little sexual variation, fifth sternal rib interrupted in a<tei:lb/>high proportion of individuals; medial lobe of hemipenis, medial branch of sulcus and<tei:lb/>medial side of armature absent or greatly reduced, clavula with a ID -shaped cross section;<tei:lb/>subocular scale separated from mouth, five or six upper labial scales anterior to centre of eye<tei:lb/>(four in A. aureus), (10)12-18 ventral scales in longest row across belly; ventrals tessellated,<tei:lb/>four longitudinal rows of scales on fingers, pectination on digits usually strong; young often<tei:lb/>uniform above.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The populations that constitute the A. scutellatus group show great variation in some<tei:lb/>features: maximum adult size ranges from about 50 mm from snout to vent to nearly 80 mm,<tei:lb/>dorsal scales may be coarse and keeled or fine and smooth, transverse counts at mid-body<tei:lb/>ranging from 36 to 100, number and degree of tessellation of ventral scales varies as does<tei:lb/>amount of pectination on digits and dorsal pattern ranges from stripes or rows of spots to<tei:lb/>reticulation or uniformity. From analogy with other species in the genus, keeled dorsal<tei:lb/>scales, low scale counts, reduced ventral tessellation, limited pectination and striped patterns<tei:lb/>are all likely to be associated with habitats that have harder substrates and more extensive<tei:lb/>vegetation, whereas contrasting conditions may be typical of soft, open sand.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although the A. scutellatus group is easily defined, recognition of taxa within it is difficult.<tei:lb/>Boulenger, (19180, 1921) treated it as a single species with six varieties. Bons &amp; Girot (1962)<tei:lb/>pointed out that, as some of these were sympatric, they could not be regarded as mere<tei:lb/>subspecies and suggested the following arrangement for Boulenger's taxa; they also included<tei:lb/>a form named by Haas (1957) and another described by themselves.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. scutellatus scutellatus (Audouin, 1829): Egypt, Israel, Sudan.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. scutellatus hardyi Haas, 1957: Arabia, S. Iraq.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. scutellatus audouini Boulenger, 1918#: S. Egypt, Libya, S. Tunisia, S. Algeria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. longipes longipes Boulenger, 1918#: S. Libya, Algerian Sahara.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. longipes panousei Bons &amp; Girot, 1962: SE. Morocco.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. inornatus inornatus (Gray, 1838): N. Libya, Tunisia, N. Algeria, S. Morocco.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. inornatus aureus Gunther, 1903: Atlantic coast from S. Morocco to Port Etienne.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. dumerilii (Milne-Edwardes, 1829): Senegal, Mauretania, Niger.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>But this system too presents problems for there is no real evidence that A. scutellatus, A.<tei:lb/>inornatus and A. dumerilii act as good species, nor is it clear that A. i. inornatus and A. i.<tei:lb/>aureus are conspecific. Difficulties in the interpretation of the A. scutellatus group arise<tei:lb/>partly because intrapopulational variation is great and there are large areas in its huge range<tei:lb/>where material is unavailable. Problems also stem from the geographical structure of the<tei:lb/>group. The soft-sand habitats favoured by these lizards are by no means continuous and<tei:lb/>consist of a series of isolates and semi-isolates which have almost certainly had a complicated<tei:lb/>history of contraction and expansion, and contact and isolation (see, for instance Sarnthein,<tei:lb/>1 978). The lizard populations associated with these different, partly discrete habitat units are<tei:lb/>likely to have been subjected to differing selective regimes and even neighbouring<tei:lb/>populations may show differences. However, although the A. scutellatus group exhibits great</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>324 E. N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>variation, it is mainly in a few characters that are likely to be ecologically correlated and it<tei:lb/>seems possible that similar morphotypes may have developed independently in some cases.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sometimes, populations may have evolved differences while isolated that enable them to<tei:lb/>act as good species if contact is restored. Such areas of sympatry or parapatry would provide<tei:lb/>the best evidence of speciation within the A. scutellatus group but, in the stringent environ-<tei:lb/>ments inhabited by these lizards, the niche space they usually occupy may often be able to<tei:lb/>support only one species, so that areas of extensive sympatry may be uncommon and regions<tei:lb/>of contact will frequently be very restricted. Given the poor sampling available, places where<tei:lb/>two species occur together will not be very easy to find. Nevertheless they do exist and there<tei:lb/>are others where quite different populations approach each other very closely. On the basis<tei:lb/>of such localities, the A. scutellatus group is best divided into three species A. scutellatus,<tei:lb/>A. longpipes and A. aureus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. scutellatus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As understood here, this widespread species consists of populations in which there are<tei:lb/>typically less than 70 dorsal scales in a transverse row at mid-body and not usually more than<tei:lb/>1 4 ventral scales in the longest row across the belly (exceptions to both frequent in Egypt and<tei:lb/>neighbouring areas), five or six upper labial scales anterior to the centre of the eye,<tei:lb/>premaxilla typically with five teeth, most usually 24 presacral vertebrae and dorsal<tei:lb/>coloration highly variable. Variation is summarized in Table 5. The name A. scutellatus was<tei:lb/>originally applied to the often large, frequently reticulated, fine scaled populations of<tei:lb/>northern Egypt. The var. audouini of Boulenger is said to differ in usually having spots or<tei:lb/>vermiculations and in its coarser, keeled dorsal scales but individuals assignable to these two<tei:lb/>forms occur in the region of Wadi Haifa, Sudan, alongside intermediates and animals from<tei:lb/>Kufra cannot be convincingly assigned to one form or the other. Other examples attributed<tei:lb/>by Boulenger to var. audouini occur on the coast of Tripolitania but extensive material now<tei:lb/>in the British Museum (Natural History) shows that these intergrade with other samples<tei:lb/>assignable to the small var. inornatus. The snout-length difference between these forms<tei:lb/>mentioned by Boulenger is very difficult to demonstrate and is complicated by allometric<tei:lb/>changes. It seems very improbable, on present evidence, that two species are represented by<tei:lb/>these samples.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Animals from Mauretania and Senegal were assigned by Boulenger to var. dumerilii,<tei:lb/>regarded as a full species by Bons and Girot. This form is characterized by usually small size,<tei:lb/>coarse dorsal scales, low ventral number and sometimes striped dorsal patterns. But, if<tei:lb/>samples are compared, there is a gradual change from south to north the numbers of dorsals<tei:lb/>and ventrals increasing and striping disappearing (Table 5, localities N-J). The most<tei:lb/>northern animals are extremely similar to the next available samples in northern Algeria and<tei:lb/>there are certainly no differences that would suggest they are not conspecific.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In summary, the forms scutellatus, audouini, inornatus and dumerilii are not discrete and,<tei:lb/>on present evidence, cannot be separated at the species level. The pattern of geographical<tei:lb/>variation in A. scutellatus seems too complex to assign subspecies names in any consistent<tei:lb/>way, at least at present.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. longipes</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This species can be distinguished from sympatric or parapatric A. scutellatus by its higher<tei:lb/>mid-dorsal transverse scale count (usually over 70 and always over 65), typically greater<tei:lb/>number of ventral scales in the longest row across the belly (often more than 14) and<tei:lb/>frequently lower presacral vertebral count (usually 23); the dorsum is typically very pale,<tei:lb/>either plain or with a reticulation on the flanks. These differences, together with its usually<tei:lb/>greater pectination on the toes, suggest that it occupies softer, more open sand than A.<tei:lb/>scutellatus, where they occur together. A number of contact or approach areas are known.<tei:lb/>In northern Algeria, there may be considerable geographical overlap but in Mauretania only<tei:lb/>a narrow abutment seems to be present with A. scutellatus in the west and A. longipes in the</tei:p>

         <tei:p>east. In south Libya sympatry may be quite extensive, as indicated by Scortecci, 1946 (his<tei:lb/>group A is apparently A. scutellatus and group B is A. longipes). This author's observations<tei:lb/>give some support to the ecological separation between the two species suggested above.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Localities for A. longipes are very scattered and, given the disjunct nature of the sort of<tei:lb/>habitat that it occupies, this species may consist of several quite isolated populations.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. aureus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The populations found along the Atlantic coast of northwest Africa are all rather similar and<tei:lb/>in many respects resemble neighbouring A. scutellatus (referred by Bons and Girot to A. i.<tei:lb/>inornatus). They differ most obviously from this species in having only four upper labial<tei:lb/>scales anterior to the centre of the eye, seven teeth in the premaxilla and the snout is often<tei:lb/>more acuminate. Such animals, typical of A. aureus, may occur some way from the coast<tei:lb/>(e.g. at Uedi Taamia, 26.0 IN 13.12W; EBD 2450) but at present they are not known to<tei:lb/>contact A. scutellatus. However, at two localities in Mauretania, the latter occurs alongside<tei:lb/>another form that may well be conspecific with A. aureus. If so this provides some evidence<tei:lb/>that the latter is a good species. The animals concerned are from Fort Gouraud (cfcf,<tei:lb/>BM(NH) 1982.292-93) and Atar (9, BM(NH) 1982.294) and are compared with sympatric<tei:lb/>A. scutellatus in Table 7. They are very similar to typical A. aureus in most features and it<tei:lb/>seems likely that they are referrable to this species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The inter-relationships of the three species that make up the A. scutellatus group are<tei:lb/>unclear. A priori, A. aureus might be considered the most primitive, often having seven<tei:lb/>premaxillary teeth and four upper labials anterior to the centre of the eye. But, if the<tei:lb/>Mauretanian specimens with five premaxillary teeth are assignable to A. aureus, this<tei:lb/>apomorphic feature cannot be used to unite A. scutellatus and A. longipes as sister species.<tei:lb/>Also, the presence of four, instead of five or six upper labials, may be secondary, for other<tei:lb/>species with acuminate snouts (A. gongrorhynchatus, A. masirae] have lower labial scale<tei:lb/>counts than expected from their phylogenetic relationships judged by other features.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>The A. opheodurus group</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. opheodurus Arnold, 19806: Arabia (but not apparently the southern desert or northern Oman),</tei:p>
         <tei:p>S. Israel, Jordan, parts of Iraq.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A.felicis Arnold, 19806: S. Arabia (Republic of South Yemen and Dhofar, S. Oman)<tei:lb/>A. masirae Arnold, 19806: S. Oman.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This assemblage consists of three small species, all wholly or largely confined to Arabia. All<tei:lb/>have only recently been described for, although A. opheodurus and A. felicis have been<tei:lb/>present in museum collections for some time, they have been confused with A. boskianus.<tei:lb/>These lizards appear to be associated with relatively hard substrates and have restricted<tei:lb/>pectination on the digits.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The group possesses a high proportion of plesiomorphic features: premaxilla with about<tei:lb/>seven teeth and not abruptly narrowed (except in A. masirae), usually 24 presacral vertebrae<tei:lb/>in males and 25 in females, fifth sternal rib usually intact; lateral clavula simple, hemipenial<tei:lb/>connectors unthickened; first supraocular more or less intact, typically four upper labial<tei:lb/>scales anterior to the centre of the eye, eight or ten ventral scales in longest row across belly,<tei:lb/>ventrals arranged in straight longitudinal rows, three longitudinal rows of scales around<tei:lb/>fingers (a fourth row in A. masirae), pectination on digits not strong, upper caudal scales<tei:lb/>keeled, young striped dorsally, the stripes sometimes persisting in adults. However the<tei:lb/>hemipenis is highly modified in the A. opheodurus group, the medial lobe and medial side of<tei:lb/>the armature being greatly reduced, the fourth supraocular scale is at least partly broken up<tei:lb/>and the subocular scale is often separated from the mouth (not in A. masirae) and the<tei:lb/>number of stripes in the juvenile pattern shows some reduction. Dorsal scaling is relatively<tei:lb/>coarse, there being 25-42 scales in a transverse row across the mid-body.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The three species are grouped together largely on the grounds of overall similarity.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Inter-relationship of the species of Acanthodactylus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The approach used for estimating the phylogeny of the species of Acanthodactylus is briefly<tei:lb/>discussed on p. 293. Probably derived character states shared by two or more species<tei:lb/>(provisional synapomorphies) are listed in Table 8 and their distribution shown in Table 9.<tei:lb/>Polarity, that is which state of a character is primitive and which state or states derived, has<tei:lb/>been decided largely by outgroup comparison, using the rest of the Lacertidae as the<tei:lb/>outgroup. Some assessment of the relative reliability of characters as indicators of relation-<tei:lb/>ship (character weighting) was attempted employing the indicators mentioned by Arnold<tei:lb/>(198 la). No features scored very well on this basis and most scored quite badly but among<tei:lb/>the better ones were the following (numbers refer to Tables 8 &amp; 9, and Fig. 9): premaxillary<tei:lb/>teeth reduced to five (1); hemipenial features not thought to be connected directly with the<tei:lb/>development of physical isolating mechanisms against interbreeding discussed on p. 304 (9,<tei:lb/>1 1-1 7); reduction in size of the ear opening (24); no keeling on proximal dorsal caudal scales<tei:lb/>(30). These features are consequently given some precedence in situations where the<tei:lb/>evidence of different derived character states conflicts. Because many features are poly-<tei:lb/>morphic, with both the primitive and a derived state occurring together in the same species,<tei:lb/>Le Quesne's (1969) method for determining compatible characters could not be used. As well<tei:lb/>as joint possession of derived character states, strong overall resemblance is also taken as<tei:lb/>prima facie evidence of close relationship. Using these indicators, a provisional partial<tei:lb/>phylogeny of Acanthodactylus was constructed and is shown in Fig. 9.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The relationships of the A. cantoris group, A. gongrorhynchatus and A. haasi are discussed<tei:lb/>on p. 3 1 1 . By and large, the hypothesis of their relationships shown in Fig. 9 involves a<tei:lb/>pattern of shared apomorphies that does not include much homoplasy. As stated, the main<tei:lb/>conflict involves the position of A. schmidti: is it more closely related to A. arabicus or to A.<tei:lb/>blanfordiil The former relationship is supported by total loss of the medial side of the<tei:lb/>hemipenis and armature (8d, lOc) and some increase in the number of ventral scales (26b),</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>

         <tei:p>1 7. Most medial connector on lateral side of hemipenis thickened.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 8. Nostril separated from first upper labial scale by a subnasal.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>19. Frontonasal scale longitudinally divided.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>20. One or two azygos scales present between the prefrontal scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2 1 . Supraocular scales not all intact: a. an area of granules wedged between the third and fourth<tei:lb/>supraoculars and the latter sometimes divided; b. first supraocular divided into two or three, fourth<tei:lb/>supraocular very fragmented; c. first and fourth supraoculars very fragmented; d. first, second and<tei:lb/>fourth supraoculars very fragmented.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>22. Subocular scale usually separated from mouth.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>23. Upper labial scales anterior to centre of eye more than four.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>24. Ear opening reduced in size.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>25. Dorsolateral tracts of enlarged scales on posterior body.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>26. Maximum number of ventral scales in a transverse row: a. 12; b. 14 or more.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>27. Ventral scales tessellated: a. at sides only; b. generally.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>28. Ventral scales grade into dorsals: a. to a small extent; b. more generally.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>29. Four longitudinal rows of scales on the fingers: a. anterior row irregular; b. anterior row regular<tei:lb/>and continuous.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>30. No keeling on proximal dorsal caudal scales.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3 1 . Tail less than 1-5 times snout-vent distance.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>32. Occipital stripes fuse to form a mid-dorsal band (often not visible in adults): a. fusion does not<tei:lb/>extend to occiput; b. fusion extends to occiput.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>33. Young without pattern of light and dark stripes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>34. Reddish-brown spots present in pattern that do not fade in alcohol.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>35. Two rows of large ocellar markings along back.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>while affinity to A. blanfordii is suggested by similarity in lateral clavula structure (12) and<tei:lb/>the presence of dorsolateral tracts of enlarged scales (25). The former characters appear<tei:lb/>weaker indicators of relationship, especially as they have developed several times in the<tei:lb/>Lacertidae, while characters 12 and 25 are known nowhere else. A. schmidti is consequently<tei:lb/>regarded as the sister species of A. blanfordii. Whether A. haasi is the sister species of A.<tei:lb/>gongrorhynchatus, which is suggested by its slender habitus, reduced ear opening (24) and<tei:lb/>some features of the lateral clavula (p. 306), or a hybrid between this species and another<tei:lb/>such as A. opheodurus, is discussed elsewhere (p. 314).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>331</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>332</tei:p>
         <tei:p>E. N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>333</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V V ""X /</tei:p>
         <tei:p>T-1b,2.3.5. 19,22\ /</tei:p>
         <tei:p>27b. 29, 33 \ /</tei:p>
         <tei:p>16 -30.31.35 \ /</tei:p>
         <tei:p>. 15, 21 c</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 9 Tentative hypothesis of the relationships of the species of Acanthodactylus. Figures refer to<tei:lb/>derived characters listed in Tables 8 and 9. Lines above species names join forms that have a<tei:lb/>very close overall resemblance, vindicates alternative position for the A. scutellatus group (A.<tei:lb/>aureus, A. scutellatus and A. longipes).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A number of Acanthodactylus species share a distinctive hemipenial structure which<tei:lb/>incorporates two derived features not found elsewhere: the medial clavula is narrow and<tei:lb/>pointed with a ^ -shaped cross section (1 1) and the most medial connector on the lateral side<tei:lb/>is often thickened (17); in addition there is moderate asymmetry of the armature (lOa). This<tei:lb/>pattern occurs in A. boskianus, A. schreiberi, the A. grandis complex, A. (t.) tristrami, A. (t.)<tei:lb/>orientalis, A. robustus, A. erythrurus, A. savignyi, A. boueti and A. pardalis. It seems<tei:lb/>probable that these forms constitute a monophyletic group in Ashlock's (1974) sense within<tei:lb/>Acanthodactylus. Among them A. boskianus, A. schreiberi and the A. grandis complex all<tei:lb/>have a reduced proximal lip to the medial branch of the hemipenial sulcus (9) and a lateral<tei:lb/>clavula that is complexly lobed (14). Within this trio, A. boskianus and A. schreiberi may<tei:lb/>well be sister species, for some populations are extremely similar. A. grandis shows conflict<tei:lb/>of evidence as to its relationships. It has some similarity in derived features to members of the<tei:lb/>A. cantoris group, viz. high number of ventral scales (26) which are tessellated (27b) and four<tei:lb/>longitudinal rows of scales along the fingers (29b). However, weighting criteria (especially<tei:lb/>frequent occurrence in groups not closely related to Acanthodactylus} suggest these features<tei:lb/>are relatively weak indicators of relationship, whereas it has been argued (p. 329) that most of<tei:lb/>the conflicting hemipenial features (9, 11, 14, 17) that ally the A. grandis complex to A.<tei:lb/>boskianus and A. schreiberi are likely to be of greater reliability. Moreover, any detailed<tei:lb/>placement of the A. grandis complex in the A. cantoris group would involve additional<tei:lb/>homoplasies.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Members of the A. tristrami group share features not found elsewhere in the genus, viz. no<tei:lb/>keeling on the proximal, dorsal caudal scales (30), a short tail (31) and an often distinctive<tei:lb/>pattern (35). The very similar A. (t.) tristrami and A. (t.) orientalis could well be sister species.<tei:lb/>A. robustus has some features that suggest relationships elsewhere: subocular scale separated<tei:lb/>from the mouth (22), increased number of ventrals (26a) and an irregular fourth scale row on<tei:lb/>the fingers (29a), but none of these derived states is very strongly developed and all seem<tei:lb/>likely to be relatively weak indicators of relationship compared with those allying A.<tei:lb/>robustus with A. (t.) tristrami and A. (t.) orientalis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The A. tristrami and A. erythrurus groups share some distinctive features, in particular a<tei:lb/>high number of presacral vertebrae (4) and fragmented first and fourth supraocular scales</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>334 E. N. ARNOLD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(2 1 c). Furthermore, some species of each have a lateral clavula that is bifurcate at its tip ( 1 5),<tei:lb/>so it seems reasonable to regard the two assemblages as closely related. The members of the<tei:lb/>A. erythrurus group are all generally similar and at least some individuals of all species have<tei:lb/>one or more azygos shields between the prefrontal scales (20) although this condition also<tei:lb/>occurs occasionally in other forms. It is suggested that A. erythrurus and A. savignyi are sister<tei:lb/>species on the evidence of their great similarity and frequent division of the frontonasal scale<tei:lb/>(19). They are also the only members of the group in which the subocular scale may be<tei:lb/>separated from the lip (22). A. boueti and A. guineensis both have distinctive features of their<tei:lb/>own (A. boueti nine premaxillary teeth, fragmented parietal scales, small hemipenis (7),<tei:lb/>supraocular scales very fragmented (2 Id); A. guineensis very asymmetrical hemipenis and<tei:lb/>armature (8d, lOc), lateral clavula rolled) but their precise relationships to each other and to<tei:lb/>A. erythrurus and A. savignyi are not clear. All A. guineensis examined (n = 20) have a<tei:lb/>peculiar arrangement of nasal scales (18) which occurs as a rarity in A. erythrurus (p. 296).<tei:lb/>However, this feature cannot be regarded as strong evidence of the close relationship of these<tei:lb/>species within the group, for its rarity in A. erythrurus raises the possibility that its apparent<tei:lb/>absence in A. savignyi and A. boueti may be due to inadequate sampling or to pseudoreversal<tei:lb/>(Arnold, 198 la).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The A. pardalis group seems likely to be a monophyletic assemblage on the basis of the<tei:lb/>strong overall similarity of its members. They also have more hemipenial asymmetry (8b or<tei:lb/>8c) than the species placed in the A. grandis complex and the A. boskianus, A. tristrami and<tei:lb/>A. erythrurus groups, share a high incidence of interruption of the fifth sternal rib (6) and the<tei:lb/>number of ventral scales is also regularly high (26). The main reason for allying the A.<tei:lb/>pardalis assemblage with these groups is that the species with the least modified hemipenis,<tei:lb/>A. pardalis, has an armature just like the others with derived features lOa, 11 and 17. The<tei:lb/>absence of these features in the other two species in the A. pardalis group, A. maculatus and<tei:lb/>A. spinicauda, may be secondary, the result of the development of physical isolating<tei:lb/>mechanisms (p. 304). The A. pardalis group shares with the A. tristrami and A. erythrurus<tei:lb/>groups frequent bifurcation of the tip of the lateral clavula (15), some fragmentation of the<tei:lb/>supraocular scales (2 Ib) and some tendency for high presacral vertebral counts (4). However,<tei:lb/>the last feature is not universal and the supraoculars are less broken up than in these forms.<tei:lb/>Because of this, the A. pardalis group is tentatively placed as a sister assemblage to them.<tei:lb/>Within the A. pardalis group, A. maculatus and A. spinicauda may be closely related having<tei:lb/>strongly asymmetrical hemipenes and armatures (8d, lOc) with the lateral clavula folded<tei:lb/>sideways (16).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Holophyly of the A. scutellatus group is supported by the close similarity of its members<tei:lb/>and their possession of a unique synapomorphy; premaxillary teeth reduced to five (2). Its<tei:lb/>members have several other derived features that occur elsewhere and these suggest<tei:lb/>conflicting hypotheses as to the closest relatives of the group. The main candidates are A.<tei:lb/>schmidti, particularly the populations in the United Arab Emirates that have little sexual<tei:lb/>variation in the number of presacral vertebrae, and all or part of the A. pardalis group.<tei:lb/>Derived features that these share with the A. scutellatus assemblage are set out in Table 10;<tei:lb/>the A. grandis complex shares a much smaller number, namely 26, 27 and 29. Most derived<tei:lb/>features, eleven, are shared with A. schmidti populations from the United Arab Emirates and<tei:lb/>only seven with the A. pardalis group of which no more than six occur in any one species.<tei:lb/>Thus on simple count of shared characters A. schmidti would be considered clearly the more<tei:lb/>likely sister taxon, but the likely quality of the features as indicators of relationship should<tei:lb/>also be taken into account. In general, the features shared with A. schmidti seem rather weak<tei:lb/>as they score badly on the weighting criteria listed by Arnold (198 la). For instance, numbers<tei:lb/>3, 5, 6, 8d, lOc, 23, 26, 27, 29 and 33 occur in a number of other lacertid stocks; there are<tei:lb/>additional functional reasons for thinking 8d and lOc may have evolved more than once<tei:lb/>(p. 304); if the argument on p. 333 that the A. grandis complex is more closely related to the<tei:lb/>A. boskianus group than to the A. cantoris assemblage is accepted, then characters 23, 26, 27<tei:lb/>and 29 must have evolved twice within Acanthodactylus and a third independent origin</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>

         <tei:p>would not seem unlikely; a number of features seem to be functionally related to the<tei:lb/>problems of living in the open, soft-sand habitats occupied by A. schmidti and the A.<tei:lb/>scutellatus group, this is true of Ib, 3, 29, 33 and strong pectination on the digits; the coercive<tei:lb/>selective forces likely to produce convergence of these features are discussed in the relevant<tei:lb/>character descriptions. Finally, two features, 3 and 5, are confined in A. schmidti to a very<tei:lb/>small part of the geographical range, the United Arab Emirates. The restricted distribution<tei:lb/>of these characters suggest they may well have developed independently within the species. If<tei:lb/>so, the number of features to be considered is reduced to nine. On the other hand the<tei:lb/>relationship of the A. scutellatus assemblage to the A. pardalis group is supported by an<tei:lb/>apparently unique hemipenial feature (16) and the high incidence of interrupted sternal ribs,<tei:lb/>a condition not found elsewhere in the genus. Assessing such conflicting evidence is<tei:lb/>inevitably difficult but, on balance, the author is inclined to believe that the A. scutellatus<tei:lb/>group is most closely related to the A. pardalis assemblage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As stated, the members of the A. opheodurus group are placed together largely on the<tei:lb/>grounds of overall similarity which is especially marked between A. opheodurus and A.<tei:lb/>felicis. Shared derived features are 8d, lOc and in some individuals of each species 32a; none<tei:lb/>of these are unique to the group. Evidence for the inter-relationship of the species is<tei:lb/>conflicting: A. felicis and A. opheodurus share 5a and 22 while the latter species and A.<tei:lb/>masirae share 1 and 29, although 29 occurs only in a reduced form in A. opheodurus and<tei:lb/>only in a minority of individuals.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The lack of strong derived characters makes it difficult to relate the A. opheodurus group<tei:lb/>to other Acanthodactylus, but absence of a thickened hemipenial connector (1 7) suggests that<tei:lb/>it is not directly related to the A. grandis-A. guineensis sequence (see Fig. 9). Nor is it clear<tei:lb/>how this sequence relates to A. micropholis and the A. cantoris assemblage.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Addendum</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Since this paper was submitted for publication, I have had the opportunity to see the recent<tei:lb/>article on Acanthodactylus by Dr Alfredo Salvador. (1982). In the main, we are in agreement<tei:lb/>over species boundaries within the genus and a number of the divergencies that exist can be<tei:lb/>attributed to using different criteria for deciding if allopatric populations merit species status<tei:lb/>or not, always a rather subjective decision. These and other more important disagreements<tei:lb/>are briefly considered below.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1. The A. yemenicus group of Salvador is the same as the A. opheodurus group of Arnold<tei:lb/>(19806 and this paper) but with the addition of a new taxon, A. yemenicus. This form has<tei:lb/>much in common with A. felicis. Unlike the other members of the group, its specific status<tei:lb/>cannot be confirmed by sympatry, as its range is completely separate from the others. There<tei:lb/>are considerable differences between the two populations from which A. yemenicus has been<tei:lb/>described, Ta'izz and the Sheikh Osman-Aden area, even although these are only separated<tei:lb/>by some 1 30 km.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2. A. erythrurus group. Salvador treats bland as a full species rather than a subspecies of A.<tei:lb/>savignyi. Given the differences between this form and typical savignyi, this course is not<tei:lb/>unreasonable.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3. A. pardalis group. A. pardalis, as understood here, is divided by Salvador into three full<tei:lb/>species: A. p. pardalis becomes monotypic as A. pardalis, A. p. bedriagai becomes A.<tei:lb/>bedriagai and the Moroccan population related to bedriagai becomes A. busacki n. sp.<tei:lb/>These forms overlap in many morphological features.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>4. A. tristrami sens. lat. Salvador regards A. tristrami, in its wide sense, as consisting of two<tei:lb/>subspecies: A. t. tristrami, with which A. t. orientalis is synonymized, and A. t. iracensis. This<tei:lb/>contrasts with the arrangement adopted here where orientalis is regarded as separable from<tei:lb/>tristrami and iracensis is synonymized with orientalis. A careful examination of the types of<tei:lb/>all three forms and of most other available material convinces me that the latter course is<tei:lb/>more appropriate. A. t. iracensis cannot be separated from orientalis on the basis of the</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF ACANTHODACTYLUS 339</tei:p>
         <tei:p>features mentioned by Schmidt (1939), namely lower transverse dorsal count, less vivid<tei:lb/>colour pattern and more sharply pectinate toes; nor does it differ significantly in any of the<tei:lb/>other features investigated. On the other hand, typical tristrami differs from orientalis,<tei:lb/>including iracensis, not only in its larger size, more numerous dorsal scales and hemipenial<tei:lb/>structure, but usually also in its deeper snout with convex upper profile, absence of a<tei:lb/>denticulation on the anterior edge of the ear and virtual lack of pectination on the toes. In<tei:lb/>addition, the first supraocular is more fragmented (tristrami-n 10, average number of<tei:lb/>fragments on each side 5.4, range 3-10; orientalis-n= 17, average number of fragments on<tei:lb/>each side 2.8, range 1-6).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5. A. scutellatus group. Salvador divides A. scutellatus, as understood here, into A.<tei:lb/>scutellatus in the east and A. dumerilii in the west. The two supposed species are said to<tei:lb/>approach each other in Mali and Algeria but not to integade. I have re-examined the<tei:lb/>BM(NH) material from Mali that Salvador assigns to A. scutellatus and A. dumerilii and can<tei:lb/>find no differences in the features that are said to separate them, namely transverse ventral<tei:lb/>count, snout shape and number of rows of supraciliary granules. Where A. scutellatus and A<tei:lb/>dumerilii approach each other around the Algerian-Tunisian border, I again find no<tei:lb/>consistent differences in snout shape as mentioned by Salvador, and although there are<tei:lb/>average differences in degree of fragmentation of the fourth supraocular scale, there is<tei:lb/>substantial overlap. I consequently regard the separation of A. dumerilii from A. scutellatus<tei:lb/>as unproven.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Salvador divides his A. dumerilii into two subspecies: A. d. dumerilii in Senegal, southwest<tei:lb/>Mauretania and Mali and A. d. exiguus in Algeria and Morocco. The former is said to have<tei:lb/>12 instead of 14 longitudinal rows of ventral scales, two instead of one row of supraciliary<tei:lb/>granules and longer legs. In material I have examined, I find that two rows of supraciliary<tei:lb/>granules are often absent in the area where A. d. dumerilii is reported and leg length is<tei:lb/>variable. Furthermore, there is a very large intergrade area between the two subspecies where<tei:lb/>other characters vary clinally. It scarcely seems worth while naming such poorly defined<tei:lb/>entities, especially when A. scutellatus varies so substantially elsewhere in its large North<tei:lb/>African range and no subspecies names are assigned.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>6. The diagnosis of Acanthodactylus given on p. 8 does not fully differentiate the genus<tei:lb/>from Meroles or Eremias.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Salvador, A. 1982. A revision of the lizards of the genus Acanthodactylus (Sauria: Lacertidae). Bonn,<tei:lb/>zool. Monogr. 16: 1-167.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>In the first volume, The Evolving Earth, twenty scientists have been asked to review<tei:lb/>the present state of knowledge in their particular field, ranging from the origin of<tei:lb/>the Earth, through ocean sediments and soils to continental drift and palaeogeography.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the companion volume, The Evolving Biosphere, museum scientists have chosen<tei:lb/>an evolutionary concept speciation, coevolution, biogeography etc. and related<tei:lb/>this to the group of animals or plants in which they are specialising. Thus beetles<tei:lb/>and birds exemplify sympatric and allopatric speciation, butterflies mimicry and<tei:lb/>certain fishes explosive evolution.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Morphological studies on some Difflugiidae from<tei:lb/>Yugoslavia (Rhizopoda, Protozoa)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Colin G. Ogden</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London<tei:lb/>SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Andjelija Zivkovic</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Contents</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction 341</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Materials and methods 341</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Systematic descriptions 342</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia 342</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cucurbitella 369</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pontigulasia 369</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Recent studies (Ogden, 1979, 19800, b, 1983; Ogden &amp; Fairman, 1979) on the shell<tei:lb/>morphology of specimens belonging to the family Difflugiidae, have shown that detailed<tei:lb/>examination of these structures aid identification at the species level. It is now possible using<tei:lb/>the scanning electron microscope to demonstrate differences in shell structure and to<tei:lb/>examine more easily the type and arrangement of materials used in these complex con-<tei:lb/>structions. Earlier workers were handicapped in similar examinations by the limited<tei:lb/>resolution of reflective optical microscopy. This made comparisons of shell structure<tei:lb/>difficult due to the different densities of the shell ranging from opaque to transparent, and<tei:lb/>sometimes the shape made a complete survey impracticable, ovoid or circular shells being<tei:lb/>particularly awkward. The present work is based on specimens collected in Serbia,<tei:lb/>Yugoslavia, from an area of peat bogs located on the high plateau at 1200 m, now submerged<tei:lb/>by the artificial lake 'Vlasina', which was formed by damming the river Vlasina and flooding<tei:lb/>a depression. A limnological study of this lake by Milovanovic &amp; Zivkovic (1956) gives<tei:lb/>information relating to the chemical and biological conditions during the initial formation of<tei:lb/>this feature.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There are several reasons for our interest in the Difflugiidae of Yugoslavia, no previous<tei:lb/>records of testate amoebae are available for this region, there appears to be a similarity of this<tei:lb/>fauna with that reported from Africa by Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas (1958) and specimens of<tei:lb/>the so-called 'cosmopolitan' species are available for comparison with those already<tei:lb/>described from the British Isles. Furthermore, the presence of several compressed species of<tei:lb/>Difflugia is unusual, as these forms appear to be rare.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Materials and methods</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Samples were collected in September, 1947 from a pond 'Godzina bistrica' located at the</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. A/MS. nat. Hist. (Zool.) 44(6) : 34 1-375 Issued 30 June 1 983</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>342 C. G. OGDEN &amp; A. 2lVKOVIC</tei:p>
         <tei:p>margin of a peat bog, and preserved in formalin. The samples were divided into two in 1980,<tei:lb/>one half was deposited in the Zoology Department, British Museum (Natural History) and<tei:lb/>the other was retained in Yugoslavia. This report is based mainly on the BM(NH) specimens<tei:lb/>but reference is also made to those in the other sample. Selected shells from the sample were<tei:lb/>washed in several changes of distilled water, then individuals were prepared for scanning<tei:lb/>electron microscopy using the technique described by Ogden (1979). The prepared stubs<tei:lb/>were examined using a Cambridge Stereoscan SI 80 operating at 10 kV and the results<tei:lb/>recorded on Ilford HP5 film.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Systematic descriptions</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The species ofDifflugia are listed in alphabetical order, except for the new species which are<tei:lb/>described last and D. gramen which is described with D. schurmanni for comparative<tei:lb/>purposes. The single species of the genus Cucurbitella and two species of Pontigulasia are<tei:lb/>described after the species of Dijflugia. The measurements for total body length includes<tei:lb/>aboral processes, unless otherwise stated, breadth and diameter of aperture are taken as the<tei:lb/>widest point, the latter is an internal measurement.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus DIFFLUGIA Leclerc, 1815</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difftugia acuminata Ehrenberg, 1838</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A single specimen, 199 urn long, 85 urn in breadth, with an aperture diameter of 37 urn, was<tei:lb/>examined and seen to be identical to those already described (Ogden, 1979). Significantly the<tei:lb/>distinctive organic cement pattern of this species, a network in which each mesh is further<tei:lb/>divided by a smaller network, was present.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia acutissima Deflandre, 193 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent, pyriform with the sides tapering evenly from about<tei:lb/>the mid-body region and terminating usually with a sharp point (Fig. la). It is composed<tei:lb/>mainly of flattish pieces of quartz to give a smooth surface, with organic cement sometimes<tei:lb/>seen as part of the shell matrix (Fig. Ic). The cement is usually in the form of a network about<tei:lb/>400 nm in diameter with walls 200 nm thick, and each mesh is covered by a smooth<tei:lb/>membrane (Fig. Id). The aperture is circular and surrounded by small to medium particles<tei:lb/>(Fig. Ib). One specimen had an oblique aperture and a misaligned aboral protuberance (Fig.<tei:lb/>le), the latter being only partially sealed (Fig. 10, but was considered to agree with the<tei:lb/>general description of this species in all other respects.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). Four specimens: body length 178-217, breadth, 80-107, diameter<tei:lb/>of aperture 47-51.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Argentina (Dioni, 1970), Chad (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas,<tei:lb/>1958), Congo (Chardez, 1964), Gold Coast, Morocco (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958),<tei:lb/>United States of America (Leidy, 1879), Venezuela (Deflandre, 1931).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species was initially described by Deflandre (1931) who considered that it<tei:lb/>was distinct from D. acuminata and its varieties by the sharpness of the aboral extremity. It<tei:lb/>differs from D. ventricosa, also described by Deflandre (1926) from Venezuela, by having a<tei:lb/>much wider body and aperture. The general body dimensions are similar to those given for<tei:lb/>D. distenda by Ogden (1983), which was a variety of D. acuminata but this species is curved<tei:lb/>aborally to a small tubular horn. The problems of differentiating between species with aboral<tei:lb/>horns or spines are emphasized in two publications by Chardez (1961 &amp; 1973), which show<tei:lb/>several figures supposedly pertaining to the same species. It is clear that concise specific</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>DIFFLUGIDAE FROM YUGOSLAVIA</tei:p>
         <tei:p>343</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 1 Difflugia acutissima : a, lateral view x600; b, apertural view x360; c, detail of organic<tei:lb/>cement pattern x 14000; d, portion of shell surface to show organic cement between particles<tei:lb/>X 6600; e, specimen with oblique aperture and non-central aboral spine x 420; f, detail of partial<tei:lb/>closure at extremity of aboral spine x 1900.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>344 C. G. OGDEN &amp; A. 2IVKOVIC</tei:p>
         <tei:p>identification of specimens must await morphogenetic evidence of natural variation of this<tei:lb/>feature within a species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia bicornis Penard, 1890</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent, ovoid or spherical with usually two, but occasionally<tei:lb/>one aboral spines (Figs 2a &amp; b). Of the three specimens examined only one had a single long<tei:lb/>aboral spine. The surface is rough, thin and composed of a mixture of small to medium<tei:lb/>particles of quartz, with the occasional addition of either a portion of diatom frustule or a<tei:lb/>siliceous flagellate cyst. Each aboral spine is roughly pointed and composed of small<tei:lb/>particles. A network of organic cement is sometimes seen between particles (Fig. 2d). It is in<tei:lb/>the form of a sheet with each mesh being separated by small walls, about 80 nm thick. The<tei:lb/>mesh is irregular, about 300-400 nm in diameter, and each enclosure often has a small inner<tei:lb/>circular wall with arms connecting it to the mesh wall (Fig. 2e). The aperture is usually<tei:lb/>circular and surrounded by a mixture of particles (Fig. 2c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (nm). Three specimens: body length 75-85, breadth 39-53, diameter of<tei:lb/>aperture 19-23.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Germany (Penard, 1890; Jung, 1936), Guatemala, Mexico<tei:lb/>(Laminger, 1973), Switzerland (Penard, 1902).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species was initially described as a distinct species by Penard (1890) but was<tei:lb/>later considered (Penard, 1902) to be a small form of D. elegans, and he illustrated the vari-<tei:lb/>ability of the shell in this form with several figures, including specimens with either one or<tei:lb/>two aboral spines. The specimens reported here have a fragile shell and are in good agree-<tei:lb/>ment with the original description of D. bicornis, the body lengths without the spines or<tei:lb/>horns being about 60 um which is near to Penard's 50-60 urn. The contrast between these<tei:lb/>specimens and those of D. elegans studied recently (Ogden, 1979), which had robust shells<tei:lb/>and varied in body length between 1 1 3-1 58 um, is marked. In the absence of information on<tei:lb/>other small forms identified as D. elegans, we have decided to use the earlier description of<tei:lb/>D. bicornis and consider the present specimens as a distinct species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia bryophila (Penard, 1902)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A single specimen, 124 um in body length, 53 um broad with an aperture diameter of 1 7 um.<tei:lb/>It was identical to those described by Ogden (1 983).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia capreolata Penard, 1 902</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is opaque, thick, pyriform with a restriction of the neck at a position<tei:lb/>about one-third of the total body length, before it swells into the main body (Fig. 3a). It is<tei:lb/>composed of small to medium pieces of angular quartz, with small areas of organic cement as<tei:lb/>part of the matrix (Fig. 3c). This cement is in the form of a smooth sheet with irregular<tei:lb/>perforations (Fig. 3d), the reason that there is no apparent pattern to these perforations may<tei:lb/>be due to these areas being in thin strips rather than a more open arrangement. Each<tei:lb/>perforation has a mean diameter of 200 nm. The aperture is circular and surrounded by a<tei:lb/>regular distribution of medium particles (Fig. 3b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). One specimen: body length 225, breadth 128, diameter of aperture<tei:lb/>58.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Argentina (Dioni, 1970; Lena &amp; Zaidenwerg, 1975),<tei:lb/>Germany (Schonborn, 1965), Russia (Kourov, 1925), Tunisia (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas,<tei:lb/>1958), Sudan (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Switzerland (Penard, 1902).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species although it is large and has a distinctive outline, does not appear to be<tei:lb/>common and is rarely found in large numbers.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia corona Wallich, 1864</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is brown, spherical or ovoid with distinct cone-like spines projecting<tei:lb/>from the aboral half of the body (Fig. 4a-d). The main body is composed of a mixture of<tei:lb/>small to medium particles of quartz arranged to give a relatively smooth shell. The spines are<tei:lb/>randomly arranged, varying between two and eight in number, made of small particles and<tei:lb/>are usually finely pointed. Organic cement in the form of a network is sometimes seen where<tei:lb/>particles meet. The aperture is circular and surrounded by a distinct denticular collar. There</tei:p>

         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species is truly 'cosmopolitan' having been reported from most continents,<tei:lb/>but these sightings are probably due to the ease in observing the prominent features of this<tei:lb/>large, distinctive shell in any sample. Although Jennings (1916, 1937) showed that variation<tei:lb/>of both teeth and spines occurred under cultural conditions, subsequent authors have<tei:lb/>continued to designate forms and varieties based on these structures. It could be argued that<tei:lb/>Jenning's observations were made on rough cultures and that clonal cultures would behave<tei:lb/>differently, or that the medium used was limiting in some way. Nevertheless, some of the<tei:lb/>reported features could easily be considered to represent natural variation.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia decloitrei Godeanu, 1 972</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A single specimen similar to those recently described by Ogden (1983) was found. It is<tei:lb/>possibly an encysted form as the aperture was blocked with several flat pieces of quartz<tei:lb/>bound by organic cement.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). One specimen: body length 79, breadth 52, diameter of aperture 22.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia difficilis Thomas, 1954</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent, ovoid, thin with a small apertural collar (Fig. 5a). It is<tei:lb/>composed of small to medium pieces of angular quartz arranged to make a relatively smooth<tei:lb/>surface, with the occasional projection of some particles. A network of organic cement is<tei:lb/>frequently seen as part of the shell matrix (Fig. 5c). The mesh of this network is open, each<tei:lb/>opening being about 350 nm wide with dividing walls 200 nm thick (Fig. 5d). A short collar<tei:lb/>made mainly of regularly arranged smallish particles, surrounds the circular aperture (Figs<tei:lb/>5b &amp; c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENT (in um). One specimen: body length 8 1 , breadth 56, diameter of aperture 18.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Algeria (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Argentina (Lena<tei:lb/>&amp; Zaidenwerg, 1975), Congo (Chardez, 1964), France (Thomas, 1954), Ivory Coast<tei:lb/>(Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Roumania (Godeanu et al, 1975), Sudan (Gauthier-<tei:lb/>Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This specimen has similar dimensions to those given for D. difficilis and D.<tei:lb/>kabylica by Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas (1958). It differs from D. kabylica in having a<tei:lb/>smaller aperture which does not have a flattened irregular outline. It is most similar to D.<tei:lb/>difficilis, although it does not have 'une excoissance peu proeminente' as initially described<tei:lb/>by Thomas (1954). However this species was later reported (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas,<tei:lb/>1958) to vary a great deal in body outline and often the aboral protuberance is absent. The<tei:lb/>feature that is characteristic of this species is the small collar and in the absence of further<tei:lb/>specimens the present specimen is considered to be D. difficilis.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia oblonga forma lata Jung, 1942</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is opaque, pyriform having a rough surface composed of a mixture of<tei:lb/>mainly small to medium pieces of quartz, with an occasional large particle added (Fig. 6a).<tei:lb/>One specimen has a curved or malformed aboral extremity (Fig. 6c), but in all other respects<tei:lb/>is similar to the original description. Organic cement in the form of a network is seen as part<tei:lb/>of the shell matrix (Fig. 6d). The mesh of the network is open and has a mean diameter of<tei:lb/>350 nm with walls 200 nm thick, although the walls often fuse to give larger areas of cement<tei:lb/>(Fig. 6e). The aperture is circular and surrounded by both small and medium particles<tei:lb/>arranged to give a somewhat irregular outline (Fig. 6b).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia levanderi Playfair, 1918</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is ovoid or almost spherical, composed of flattish and angular pieces<tei:lb/>of quartz to give a regular outline (Fig. 7a). The particles appear to overlap and produce a<tei:lb/>robust structure with a network of organic cement, which is seen infrequently, binding the<tei:lb/>particles (Fig. 7c). Details of the surface are restricted because all the examined specimens<tei:lb/>have a slight covering of small debris, this latter material is clearly no part of the shell<tei:lb/>structure. The aperture is circular, well defined and surrounded by small particles (Fig. 7b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). Five specimens: body length 95-104, breadth 76-92, diameter of<tei:lb/>aperture 32-40.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Algeria (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Australia<tei:lb/>(Playfair, 1918), Chile (Jung, 1942), Morocco and Tunisia (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas,<tei:lb/>1958).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The descriptions of both Levander (1894) and Playfair (1918) refer to specimens<tei:lb/>having a similar shape but differing in size and composition. The larger being robust with a<tei:lb/>coarse appearance, whilst the smaller had a chitinous shell with a scattering of particles.<tei:lb/>Unable to differentiate them Playfair (1918) described them as a new species D. levanderi.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>

         <tei:p>C. G. OGDEN &amp; A. 2IVKOVIC</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Fortunately, he created a precedence by initially describing the larger specimens, which are<tei:lb/>now considered to represent D. levanderi. Specimens similar to D. levanderi except for being<tei:lb/>smaller and having a thin shell whose particles did not overlap, were described by Godeanu<tei:lb/>(1972) as a new species D. decloitrei, and these are considered to be identical to the smaller<tei:lb/>animals described by Levander (1894), Playfair (19 18) and redescribed by Ogden (1983).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia lismorensis Playfair, 1918</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia lismorensis var. quinquelobata Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958<tei:lb/>Difaugia lismorensis var. elongata Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1 958</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DIFFLUGIIDAE FROM YUGOSLAVIA 353</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is either elongate or ovoid, thick, composed of small to medium<tei:lb/>pieces of quartz arranged to give a rough surface (Fig. 8a). All the specimens examined had a<tei:lb/>proportion of the surface covered with an overlay of small extraneous material (Fig. 8c),<tei:lb/>nevertheless, the portions of actual surface visible had only strands of organic cement as part<tei:lb/>of the shell matrix. The aperture has five lobes which are equally spaced, strong, blunt,<tei:lb/>tooth-like extensions (Fig. 8b). Often the strengthening around these lobes, usually with<tei:lb/>small particles, gives it a collar-like appearance (Fig. 8b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). Three specimens: body length 132-144, breadth 96-104, diameter<tei:lb/>of aperture 3 6-4 5.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Argentina (Vucetich, 1970), Australia (Playfair, 1918), Brazil<tei:lb/>(Green, 1973), Chad (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Congo (Chardez, 1964; Gauthier-<tei:lb/>Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Gold Coast, Morocco and Sudan (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas,<tei:lb/>1958).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. Playfair (1918) described D. lismorensis as having an ovoid or sub-globular shell<tei:lb/>with a six-lobed aperture, and added two varieties trilobulata and crucifera which had three<tei:lb/>and four lobes respectively. Since that time two more varieties with five lobes, from Africa,<tei:lb/>have been described by Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas (1958). They suggested the name<tei:lb/>quinquelobata for the ovoid variety and elongata for the specimens with an elongate body,<tei:lb/>the former has subsequently been reported again in Africa by Chardez (1964). Vucetich<tei:lb/>(1970) after examining about 50 specimens with seven lobes from Argentina, concluded that<tei:lb/>these were otherwise identical with D. lismorensis and she did not consider that differences<tei:lb/>in the number of lobes warranted specific designation. Until examples of this species from<tei:lb/>Australia, Africa and South America are compared, we agree with Vucetich (1970) that her<tei:lb/>specimens, plus those of Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas are best described as D. lismorensis.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia nodosa (Leidy, 1879) comb. nov.<tei:lb/>Difflugia pyrif or mis var. nodosa Leidy, 1879 and in Penard, 1902<tei:lb/>Dijflugia oblonga var. nodosa Leidy, 1 879 in Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is thick, opaque, pyriform with the main body compressed to give<tei:lb/>two large lateral wings and an aboral extension or protuberance (Figs lOb &amp; c).          <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). One specimen: body length 367, breadth 241, depth 140, diameter<tei:lb/>of aperture 53.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Algeria (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), Belgium<tei:lb/>(Chardez, 1980; Chardez &amp; Gaspar, 1976), Congo (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958),<tei:lb/>Germany (Laminger, 1973; Schonborn, 1962), Ivory Coast (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas,<tei:lb/>1958), Netherlands (Hoogenraad &amp; Groot, 1940), Roumania (Godeanu et al, 1973), Russia<tei:lb/>(Kourov, 1925), Sudan (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), United States of America (Leidy,<tei:lb/>1879).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. This species has consistently been described as a variety of/), oblonga, initially<tei:lb/>Leidy (1879) remarked that it was a 'striking variety' distinguished by its unusually large size,<tei:lb/>compressed body and three conical eminences which varied a great deal in their develop-<tei:lb/>ment. Although Leidy (1879) reported it as being abundant on one particular occasion, so<tei:lb/>that one drop of ooze contained several dozen individuals, since that time no other reports<tei:lb/>have been so fruitful with specimens. Nevertheless, we consider that this variety is best<tei:lb/>treated as a distinct species which differs from the typical D. oblonga in the features<tei:lb/>described above and the organic cement pattern.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia gramen Penard, 1902</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent or light brown, spherical tapering towards the<tei:lb/>aperture (Fig. 1 la). It is composed of a mixture of small to medium pieces of quartz, bound<tei:lb/>by an organic cement network (Fig. lie). A ring of small pores surrounds the aperture (see<tei:lb/>Ogden, 1 980b) which is trilobed and bordered by an irregular raised rim (Fig. 1 1 b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). See Table 1 .</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia schurmanni Oye, 1 932</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent, elongate ovoid, tapering equally to the aperture and<tei:lb/>aboral extremity (Fig. 1 Id). It is composed mainly of flattish pieces of quartz arranged to give<tei:lb/>a smooth and rather fragile appearance. The particles are bound by organic cement, similar<tei:lb/>to that described for D. gramen, but because the particles fit closely together cement is not<tei:lb/>seen as frequently as in that species. The aperture is trilobed with the dividing projections<tei:lb/>less pronounced in apertural view (Fig. lie) compared with those of D. gramen, and this<tei:lb/>gives it a more open outline especially as the shell has a smaller breadth (see Table 1). In<tei:lb/>addition, the surrounding ridge is often not as well defined although in lateral view the lobes<tei:lb/>are more prominent (Fig. 1 Id). A ring of small pores surrounds the aperture, similar to those<tei:lb/>described for D. gramen (see Ogden, 19806).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). See Table 1.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>GEOGRAPHICAL DISTRIBUTION. Brazil (Green, 1975), Congo (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas,<tei:lb/>1958), Guatemala (Laminger, 1973a), Senegal (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958), South<tei:lb/>Africa (Oye, 1932), Sudan (Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia smilion Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas, 1958</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Unfortunately the only specimen was broken during preparation. The shell is<tei:lb/>transparent, elongate with a distinct aboral conical protuberance. It is composed mainly of<tei:lb/>medium to large, angular or flat pieces of quartz to give a rough surface. Organic cement is<tei:lb/>seen at the junctions of these particles as enclosed spheres, or spheres with small openings, or<tei:lb/>as a thick rimmed circle with four equal openings (Fig. 12b &amp; c). The spheres are about<tei:lb/>600 nm in diameter, the walls of the circle are about 1 50 nm thick and the internal openings<tei:lb/>vary between 1 50-220 nm. The aperture is circular and surrounded mainly by medium sized<tei:lb/>particles (Fig. 12a).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Dijflugia tuberculata (Wallich, 1864)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Specimens with the typical protuberances were examined and the measurements are<tei:lb/>included in Table 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dijflugia wailesi Ogden, 1980<tei:lb/>Difflugia tuberculata var. minor Wailes, 1919</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Two distinct forms of this species are present in the examined material, both<tei:lb/>are figured and described.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the typical form the shell is transparent, ovoid but tapering slightly near the aperture<tei:lb/>(Fig. 13a). It is composed mainly of flattish pieces of quartz and siliceous elements arranged<tei:lb/>to give a smooth surface (Fig. 1 3a). The aperture is polygonal with usually five but occasion-<tei:lb/>ally six indentations and bordered by a small lip (Fig. 13b). A ring of about ten small pores is<tei:lb/>often seen just posterior to the apertural rim.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the other form the shell is ovoid but more markedly tapered towards the aperture (Fig.<tei:lb/>13c). The aperture is circular with no indentations (Fig. 13d), but with a similar small ridge<tei:lb/>as in the typical form. There are nine or more small tooth-like projections inside the<tei:lb/>apertural rim on a level with the main body of the shell (Fig. 13e), these tooth-like structures<tei:lb/>are similar to those sometimes seen at the apex of each apertural projection in the polygonal<tei:lb/>aperture specimens.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). See Table 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The similarity of dimensions between the present specimens and those reported<tei:lb/>recently from Norfolk, England, highlight the difficulty of differentiating the two species D.<tei:lb/>tuberculata and D. wailesi. A further division of D. wailesi is not desirable unless supported<tei:lb/>by strong morphological evidence, therefore, although the specimens are reported as two<tei:lb/>groups no particular significance is attached to these differences.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia balcanica sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent, ovoid or roughly circular with an aboral cone or spine<tei:lb/>and laterally compressed, the shape resembles either an arrow-head or a leaf (Figs 14a &amp; c).<tei:lb/>It is composed of a mixture of small to large pieces of mainly flattish quartz, arranged to give<tei:lb/>a relatively smooth but irregular surface, unfortunately the illustrated specimen has some<tei:lb/>overlying debris scattered on the anterior third of the body. An open network of organic<tei:lb/>cement is seen between the particles. The aperture is roughly circular and surrounded by<tei:lb/>small or medium pieces of quartz (Fig. 14b).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). Two specimens: body length 1 1 1-1 14, breadth 79-82, depth 50,<tei:lb/>diameter of aperture 3 1 .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. There does not appear to be any prior descriptions of any similar compressed<tei:lb/>species of Difflugia, and D. balcanica is considered to be distinct in having a leaf-like shape,<tei:lb/>with an angular surface and a pointed aboral extremity.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia bistrica sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is ovoid or roughly circular, thin and laterally compressed (Fig. 15a<tei:lb/>&amp; b). It is composed of medium flattened pieces of quartz with smaller particles between to<tei:lb/>give a smooth surface (Fig. 15d). Small areas of organic cement occur infrequently, and are<tei:lb/>usually in the form of a network (Fig. 1 5e). The aperture is a regular oval and surrounded by<tei:lb/>small particles (Fig. 1 5c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). One specimen: body length 104, breadth 84, depth 54, diameter of<tei:lb/>aperture 38.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. Although this specimen has an encrustation of small debris, which is not part of<tei:lb/>the shell matrix (see Fig. 15d), it is still possible to describe the surface as smooth. This<tei:lb/>species is similar to D. balcanica in dimensions and degree of compression, but differs signifi-<tei:lb/>cantly in having a rounded, smooth shell which is curved gently at the aboral extremity.<tei:lb/>Notwithstanding that this description is based on a single specimen, it is still considered to<tei:lb/>represent a distinct species and is so designated.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Difflugia dragana sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is opaque, elongate ovoid, thick, tapering gradually from the<tei:lb/>mid-body position to a gracefully curved aboral extremity and anteriorly to the suggestion of<tei:lb/>a small apertural collar, it is laterally compressed (Figs 16a &amp; b). Medium pieces of quartz<tei:lb/>appear to make up most of the surface with smaller particles added. Only small strands of<tei:lb/>organic cement have been seen (Fig. 16d) between these particles. The aperture is circular<tei:lb/>and surrounded by mainly small particles of quartz (Fig. 16c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). One specimen: body length 195, breadth 1 19, depth 96, diameter of<tei:lb/>aperture 48.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia serbica sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is opaque, shaped like a spear-head, tapering evenly from the body<tei:lb/>to the pointed aboral extremity and gradually, anteriorly towards the short apertural collar,<tei:lb/>it is laterally compressed (Figs 1 7a-e). The body is composed mainly of medium to large<tei:lb/>flattish pieces of quartz with a mixture of small to medium pieces at the aperture and aboral<tei:lb/>extremity. Although the surface is usually smooth, two of the specimens are covered with a<tei:lb/>layer of small particular debris which appears to have been added after the shell was con-<tei:lb/>structed (Fig. 1 7e). The organic cement is not frequently seen but is in the form of a network,<tei:lb/>the mesh being about 400 nm in diameter with walls 50 nm thick, each mesh being covered<tei:lb/>(Fig. 1 70- The aperture is circular and usually surrounded by small particles (Fig. 1 7d).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 18 Difflugia serbica sp. nov. Diagrams of four specimens to show the variation in outline.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MEASUREMENTS (in um). Five specimens: body length 169-189, breadth 110-113, depth<tei:lb/>68-79, diameter of aperture 31-41.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The dimensions of five specimens from the Yugoslavian sample were somewhat<tei:lb/>larger: body length 180-280, breadth 1 10-160, depth 75-1 15 (only two measured), diameter<tei:lb/>of aperture 40-70. Nevertheless, they demonstrate a similar variability in general shape and<tei:lb/>structure (Fig. 18). This species is similar to D. soudanensis Gauthier-Lievre &amp; Thomas<tei:lb/>(1958) and D. kempyi Stepanek, 1953 in general dimensions, but both of these species have a<tei:lb/>distinct apertural collar, are curved aborally and have a median pronounced spine or horn.<tei:lb/>Furthermore, of these two species only D. soudanensis is compressed. Difflugia serbica is<tei:lb/>considered to be distinct in having an irregular spear-shaped shell which is laterally<tei:lb/>compressed, with the suggestion of a small apertural collar and usually pointed aborally.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Difflugia styla sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. The shell is transparent, ovoid with a distinct aboral spine (Fig. 20a). It is<tei:lb/>composed of small to medium flattish pieces of quartz, and a network of organic cement is<tei:lb/>often seen around the aperture as part of the shell matrix. Elsewhere on the surface the<tei:lb/>organic cement is seen as strands between particles. The aperture is circular and surrounded<tei:lb/>by smallish particles (Fig. 20b).</tei:p>
